Growing as they did, however, out of the old earth,\r\nthe flowers still sent a fresh and sweet incense up to their Creator;\r\nnor could it have been the less pure and acceptable because Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nyoung breath mingled with it, as the fragrance floated past the window.\r\nHastening down the creaking and carpetless staircase, she found her way\r\ninto the garden, gathered some of the most perfect of the roses, and\r\nbrought them to her chamber.\r\n\r\nLittle Ph\xc5\x93be was one of those persons who possess, as their exclusive\r\npatrimony, the gift of practical arrangement. It is a kind of natural\r\nmagic that enables these favored ones to bring out the hidden\r\ncapabilities of things around them; and particularly to give a look of\r\ncomfort and habitableness to any place which, for however brief a\r\nperiod, may happen to be their home. A wild hut of underbrush, tossed\r\ntogether by wayfarers through the primitive forest, would acquire the\r\nhome aspect by one night\xe2\x80\x99s lodging of such a woman, and would retain it\r\nlong after her quiet figure had disappeared into the surrounding shade.\r\nNo less a portion of such homely witchcraft was requisite to reclaim,\r\nas it were, Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s waste, cheerless, and dusky chamber, which had been\r\nuntenanted so long\xe2\x80\x94except by spiders, and mice, and rats, and\r\nghosts\xe2\x80\x94that it was all overgrown with the desolation which watches to\r\nobliterate every trace of man\xe2\x80\x99s happier hours. What was precisely\r\nPh\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s process we find it impossible to say. She appeared to have no\r\npreliminary design, but gave a touch here and another there; brought\r\nsome articles of furniture to light and dragged others into the shadow;\r\nlooped up or let down a window-curtain; and, in the course of half an\r\nhour, had fully succeeded in throwing a kindly and hospitable smile\r\nover the apartment. No longer ago than the night before, it had\r\nresembled nothing so much as the old maid\xe2\x80\x99s heart; for there was\r\nneither sunshine nor household fire in one nor the other, and, save for\r\nghosts and ghostly reminiscences, not a guest, for many years gone by,\r\nhad entered the heart or the chamber.\r\n\r\nThere was still another peculiarity of this inscrutable charm. The\r\nbedchamber, no doubt, was a chamber of very great and varied\r\nexperience, as a scene of human life: the joy of bridal nights had\r\nthrobbed itself away here; new immortals had first drawn earthly breath\r\nhere; and here old people had died. But\xe2\x80\x94whether it were the white\r\nroses, or whatever the subtile influence might be\xe2\x80\x94a person of delicate\r\ninstinct would have known at once that it was now a maiden\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbedchamber, and had been purified of all former evil and sorrow by her\r\nsweet breath and happy thoughts. Her dreams of the past night, being\r\nsuch cheerful ones, had exorcised the gloom, and now haunted the\r\nchamber in its stead.\r\n\r\nAfter arranging matters to her satisfaction, Ph\xc5\x93be emerged from her\r\nchamber, with a purpose to descend again into the garden. Besides the\r\nrosebush, she had observed several other species of flowers growing\r\nthere in a wilderness of neglect, and obstructing one another\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndevelopment (as is often the parallel case in human society) by their\r\nuneducated entanglement and confusion. At the head of the stairs,\r\nhowever, she met Hepzibah, who, it being still early, invited her into\r\na room which she would probably have called her boudoir, had her\r\neducation embraced any such French phrase. It was strewn about with a\r\nfew old books, and a work-basket, and a dusty writing-desk; and had, on\r\none side, a large black article of furniture, of very strange\r\nappearance, which the old gentlewoman told Ph\xc5\x93be was a harpsichord. It\r\nlooked more like a coffin than anything else; and, indeed,\xe2\x80\x94not having\r\nbeen played upon, or opened, for years,\xe2\x80\x94there must have been a vast\r\ndeal of dead music in it, stifled for want of air. Human finger was\r\nhardly known to have touched its chords since the days of Alice\r\nPyncheon, who had learned the sweet accomplishment of melody in Europe.\r\n\r\nHepzibah bade her young guest sit down, and, herself taking a chair\r\nnear by, looked as earnestly at Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s trim little figure as if she\r\nexpected to see right into its springs and motive secrets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCousin Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, at last, \xe2\x80\x9cI really can\xe2\x80\x99t see my way clear to\r\nkeep you with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese words, however, had not the inhospitable bluntness with which\r\nthey may strike the reader; for the two relatives, in a talk before\r\nbedtime, had arrived at a certain degree of mutual understanding.\r\nHepzibah knew enough to enable her to appreciate the circumstances\r\n(resulting from the second marriage of the girl\xe2\x80\x99s mother) which made it\r\ndesirable for Ph\xc5\x93be to establish herself in another home. Nor did she\r\nmisinterpret Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s character, and the genial activity pervading\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x94one of the most valuable traits of the true New England\r\nwoman,\xe2\x80\x94which had impelled her forth, as might be said, to seek her\r\nfortune, but with a self-respecting purpose to confer as much benefit\r\nas she could anywise receive. As one of her nearest kindred, she had\r\nnaturally betaken herself to Hepzibah, with no idea of forcing herself\r\non her cousin\xe2\x80\x99s protection, but only for a visit of a week or two,\r\nwhich might be indefinitely extended, should it prove for the happiness\r\nof both.\r\n\r\nTo Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s blunt observation, therefore, Ph\xc5\x93be replied as frankly,\r\nand more cheerfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear cousin, I cannot tell how it will be,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I really\r\nthink we may suit one another much better than you suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a nice girl,\xe2\x80\x94I see it plainly,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Hepzibah; \xe2\x80\x9cand it is\r\nnot any question as to that point which makes me hesitate. But, Ph\xc5\x93be,\r\nthis house of mine is but a melancholy place for a young person to be\r\nin. It lets in the wind and rain, and the snow, too, in the garret and\r\nupper chambers, in winter-time, but it never lets in the sunshine. And\r\nas for myself, you see what I am,\xe2\x80\x94a dismal and lonesome old woman (for\r\nI begin to call myself old, Ph\xc5\x93be), whose temper, I am afraid, is none\r\nof the best, and whose spirits are as bad as can be! I cannot make your\r\nlife pleasant, Cousin Ph\xc5\x93be, neither can I so much as give you bread to\r\neat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will find me a cheerful little body\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be, smiling, and\r\nyet with a kind of gentle dignity, \xe2\x80\x9cand I mean to earn my bread. You\r\nknow I have not been brought up a Pyncheon. A girl learns many things\r\nin a New England village.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, sighing, \xe2\x80\x9cyour knowledge would do but\r\nlittle for you here! And then it is a wretched thought that you should\r\nfling away your young days in a place like this. Those cheeks would not\r\nbe so rosy after a month or two. Look at my face!\xe2\x80\x9d and, indeed, the\r\ncontrast was very striking,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cyou see how pale I am! It is my idea that\r\nthe dust and continual decay of these old houses are unwholesome for\r\nthe lungs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is the garden,\xe2\x80\x94the flowers to be taken care of,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Ph\xc5\x93be.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should keep myself healthy with exercise in the open air.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, after all, child,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Hepzibah, suddenly rising, as if to\r\ndismiss the subject, \xe2\x80\x9cit is not for me to say who shall be a guest or\r\ninhabitant of the old Pyncheon House. Its master is coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean Judge Pyncheon?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ph\xc5\x93be in surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJudge Pyncheon!\xe2\x80\x9d answered her cousin angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cHe will hardly cross\r\nthe threshold while I live! No, no! But, Ph\xc5\x93be, you shall see the face\r\nof him I speak of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went in quest of the miniature already described, and returned with\r\nit in her hand. Giving it to Ph\xc5\x93be, she watched her features narrowly,\r\nand with a certain jealousy as to the mode in which the girl would show\r\nherself affected by the picture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you like the face?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hepzibah.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is handsome!\xe2\x80\x94it is very beautiful!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be admiringly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\nas sweet a face as a man\xe2\x80\x99s can be, or ought to be. It has something of\r\na child\xe2\x80\x99s expression,\xe2\x80\x94and yet not childish,\xe2\x80\x94only one feels so very\r\nkindly towards him! He ought never to suffer anything. One would bear\r\nmuch for the sake of sparing him toil or sorrow. Who is it, Cousin\r\nHepzibah?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you never hear,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered her cousin, bending towards her, \xe2\x80\x9cof\r\nClifford Pyncheon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever. I thought there were no Pyncheons left, except yourself and our\r\ncousin Jaffrey,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet I seem to have heard the name\r\nof Clifford Pyncheon. Yes!\xe2\x80\x94from my father or my mother; but has he not\r\nbeen a long while dead?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well, child, perhaps he has!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah with a sad, hollow\r\nlaugh; \xe2\x80\x9cbut, in old houses like this, you know, dead people are very\r\napt to come back again! We shall see. And, Cousin Ph\xc5\x93be, since, after\r\nall that I have said, your courage does not fail you, we will not part\r\nso soon. You are welcome, my child, for the present, to such a home as\r\nyour kinswoman can offer you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith this measured, but not exactly cold assurance of a hospitable\r\npurpose, Hepzibah kissed her cheek.\r\n\r\nThey now went below stairs, where Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x94not so much assuming the office\r\nas attracting it to herself, by the magnetism of innate fitness\xe2\x80\x94took\r\nthe most active part in preparing breakfast. The mistress of the house,\r\nmeanwhile, as is usual with persons of her stiff and unmalleable cast,\r\nstood mostly aside; willing to lend her aid, yet conscious that her\r\nnatural inaptitude would be likely to impede the business in hand.\r\nPh\xc5\x93be and the fire that boiled the teakettle were equally bright,\r\ncheerful, and efficient, in their respective offices. Hepzibah gazed\r\nforth from her habitual sluggishness, the necessary result of long\r\nsolitude, as from another sphere. She could not help being interested,\r\nhowever, and even amused, at the readiness with which her new inmate\r\nadapted herself to the circumstances, and brought the house, moreover,\r\nand all its rusty old appliances, into a suitableness for her purposes.\r\nWhatever she did, too, was done without conscious effort, and with\r\nfrequent outbreaks of song, which were exceedingly pleasant to the ear.\r\nThis natural tunefulness made Ph\xc5\x93be seem like a bird in a shadowy tree;\r\nor conveyed the idea that the stream of life warbled through her heart\r\nas a brook sometimes warbles through a pleasant little dell. It\r\nbetokened the cheeriness of an active temperament, finding joy in its\r\nactivity, and, therefore, rendering it beautiful; it was a New England\r\ntrait,\xe2\x80\x94the stern old stuff of Puritanism with a gold thread in the web.\r\n\r\nHepzibah brought out some old silver spoons with the family crest upon\r\nthem, and a china tea-set painted over with grotesque figures of man,\r\nbird, and beast, in as grotesque a landscape. These pictured people\r\nwere odd humorists, in a world of their own,\xe2\x80\x94a world of vivid\r\nbrilliancy, so far as color went, and still unfaded, although the\r\nteapot and small cups were as ancient as the custom itself of\r\ntea-drinking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour great-great-great-great-grandmother had these cups, when she was\r\nmarried,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah to Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cShe was a Davenport, of a good\r\nfamily. They were almost the first teacups ever seen in the colony; and\r\nif one of them were to be broken, my heart would break with it. But it\r\nis nonsense to speak so about a brittle teacup, when I remember what my\r\nheart has gone through without breaking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe cups\xe2\x80\x94not having been used, perhaps, since Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s youth\xe2\x80\x94had\r\ncontracted no small burden of dust, which Ph\xc5\x93be washed away with so\r\nmuch care and delicacy as to satisfy even the proprietor of this\r\ninvaluable china.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a nice little housewife you are!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the latter, smiling,\r\nand at the same time frowning so prodigiously that the smile was\r\nsunshine under a thunder-cloud. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you do other things as well? Are\r\nyou as good at your book as you are at washing teacups?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot quite, I am afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, laughing at the form of\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s question. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I was schoolmistress for the little children\r\nin our district last summer, and might have been so still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! \xe2\x80\x99tis all very well!\xe2\x80\x9d observed the maiden lady, drawing herself up.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut these things must have come to you with your mother\xe2\x80\x99s blood. I\r\nnever knew a Pyncheon that had any turn for them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is very queer, but not the less true, that people are generally\r\nquite as vain, or even more so, of their deficiencies than of their\r\navailable gifts; as was Hepzibah of this native inapplicability, so to\r\nspeak, of the Pyncheons to any useful purpose. She regarded it as an\r\nhereditary trait; and so, perhaps, it was, but unfortunately a morbid\r\none, such as is often generated in families that remain long above the\r\nsurface of society.\r\n\r\nBefore they left the breakfast-table, the shop-bell rang sharply, and\r\nHepzibah set down the remnant of her final cup of tea, with a look of\r\nsallow despair that was truly piteous to behold. In cases of\r\ndistasteful occupation, the second day is generally worse than the\r\nfirst. We return to the rack with all the soreness of the preceding\r\ntorture in our limbs. At all events, Hepzibah had fully satisfied\r\nherself of the impossibility of ever becoming wonted to this peevishly\r\nobstreperous little bell. Ring as often as it might, the sound always\r\nsmote upon her nervous system rudely and suddenly. And especially now,\r\nwhile, with her crested teaspoons and antique china, she was flattering\r\nherself with ideas of gentility, she felt an unspeakable disinclination\r\nto confront a customer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not trouble yourself, dear cousin!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Ph\xc5\x93be, starting lightly\r\nup. \xe2\x80\x9cI am shop-keeper to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, child!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat can a little country girl know\r\nof such matters?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I have done all the shopping for the family at our village store,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I have had a table at a fancy fair, and made better\r\nsales than anybody. These things are not to be learnt; they depend upon\r\na knack that comes, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d added she, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cwith one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s blood. You shall see that I am as nice a little saleswoman as\r\nI am a housewife!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old gentlewoman stole behind Ph\xc5\x93be, and peeped from the passageway\r\ninto the shop, to note how she would manage her undertaking. It was a\r\ncase of some intricacy. A very ancient woman, in a white short gown and\r\na green petticoat, with a string of gold beads about her neck, and what\r\nlooked like a nightcap on her head, had brought a quantity of yarn to\r\nbarter for the commodities of the shop. She was probably the very last\r\nperson in town who still kept the time-honored spinning-wheel in\r\nconstant revolution. It was worth while to hear the croaking and hollow\r\ntones of the old lady, and the pleasant voice of Ph\xc5\x93be, mingling in one\r\ntwisted thread of talk; and still better to contrast their figures,\xe2\x80\x94so\r\nlight and bloomy,\xe2\x80\x94so decrepit and dusky,\xe2\x80\x94with only the counter betwixt\r\nthem, in one sense, but more than threescore years, in another. As for\r\nthe bargain, it was wrinkled slyness and craft pitted against native\r\ntruth and sagacity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas not that well done?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ph\xc5\x93be, laughing, when the customer was\r\ngone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNicely done, indeed, child!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cI could not have gone\r\nthrough with it nearly so well. As you say, it must be a knack that\r\nbelongs to you on the mother\xe2\x80\x99s side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is a very genuine admiration, that with which persons too shy or too\r\nawkward to take a due part in the bustling world regard the real actors\r\nin life\xe2\x80\x99s stirring scenes; so genuine, in fact, that the former are\r\nusually fain to make it palatable to their self-love, by assuming that\r\nthese active and forcible qualities are incompatible with others, which\r\nthey choose to deem higher and more important. Thus, Hepzibah was well\r\ncontent to acknowledge Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s vastly superior gifts as a shop-keeper;\r\nshe listened, with compliant ear, to her suggestion of various methods\r\nwhereby the influx of trade might be increased, and rendered\r\nprofitable, without a hazardous outlay of capital. She consented that\r\nthe village maiden should manufacture yeast, both liquid and in cakes;\r\nand should brew a certain kind of beer, nectareous to the palate, and\r\nof rare stomachic virtues; and, moreover, should bake and exhibit for\r\nsale some little spice-cakes, which whosoever tasted would longingly\r\ndesire to taste again. All such proofs of a ready mind and skilful\r\nhandiwork were highly acceptable to the aristocratic hucksteress, so\r\nlong as she could murmur to herself with a grim smile, and a\r\nhalf-natural sigh, and a sentiment of mixed wonder, pity, and growing\r\naffection:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a nice little body she is! If she only could be a lady; too\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s impossible! Ph\xc5\x93be is no Pyncheon. She takes everything from her\r\nmother!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs to Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s not being a lady, or whether she were a lady or no, it\r\nwas a point, perhaps, difficult to decide, but which could hardly have\r\ncome up for judgment at all in any fair and healthy mind. Out of New\r\nEngland, it would be impossible to meet with a person combining so many\r\nladylike attributes with so many others that form no necessary (if\r\ncompatible) part of the character. She shocked no canon of taste; she\r\nwas admirably in keeping with herself, and never jarred against\r\nsurrounding circumstances. Her figure, to be sure,\xe2\x80\x94so small as to be\r\nalmost childlike, and so elastic that motion seemed as easy or easier\r\nto it than rest, would hardly have suited one\xe2\x80\x99s idea of a countess.\r\nNeither did her face\xe2\x80\x94with the brown ringlets on either side, and the\r\nslightly piquant nose, and the wholesome bloom, and the clear shade of\r\ntan, and the half dozen freckles, friendly remembrances of the April\r\nsun and breeze\xe2\x80\x94precisely give us a right to call her beautiful. But\r\nthere was both lustre and depth in her eyes. She was very pretty; as\r\ngraceful as a bird, and graceful much in the same way; as pleasant\r\nabout the house as a gleam of sunshine falling on the floor through a\r\nshadow of twinkling leaves, or as a ray of firelight that dances on the\r\nwall while evening is drawing nigh. Instead of discussing her claim to\r\nrank among ladies, it would be preferable to regard Ph\xc5\x93be as the\r\nexample of feminine grace and availability combined, in a state of\r\nsociety, if there were any such, where ladies did not exist. There it\r\nshould be woman\xe2\x80\x99s office to move in the midst of practical affairs, and\r\nto gild them all, the very homeliest,\xe2\x80\x94were it even the scouring of pots\r\nand kettles,\xe2\x80\x94with an atmosphere of loveliness and joy.\r\n\r\nSuch was the sphere of Ph\xc5\x93be. To find the born and educated lady, on\r\nthe other hand, we need look no farther than Hepzibah, our forlorn old\r\nmaid, in her rustling and rusty silks, with her deeply cherished and\r\nridiculous consciousness of long descent, her shadowy claims to\r\nprincely territory, and, in the way of accomplishment, her\r\nrecollections, it may be, of having formerly thrummed on a harpsichord,\r\nand walked a minuet, and worked an antique tapestry-stitch on her\r\nsampler. It was a fair parallel between new Plebeianism and old\r\nGentility.\r\n\r\nIt really seemed as if the battered visage of the House of the Seven\r\nGables, black and heavy-browed as it still certainly looked, must have\r\nshown a kind of cheerfulness glimmering through its dusky windows as\r\nPh\xc5\x93be passed to and fro in the interior. Otherwise, it is impossible to\r\nexplain how the people of the neighborhood so soon became aware of the\r\ngirl\xe2\x80\x99s presence. There was a great run of custom, setting steadily in,\r\nfrom about ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock until towards noon,\xe2\x80\x94relaxing, somewhat, at\r\ndinner-time, but recommencing in the afternoon, and, finally, dying\r\naway a half an hour or so before the long day\xe2\x80\x99s sunset. One of the\r\nstanchest patrons was little Ned Higgins, the devourer of Jim Crow and\r\nthe elephant, who to-day signalized his omnivorous prowess by\r\nswallowing two dromedaries and a locomotive. Ph\xc5\x93be laughed, as she\r\nsummed up her aggregate of sales upon the slate; while Hepzibah, first\r\ndrawing on a pair of silk gloves, reckoned over the sordid accumulation\r\nof copper coin, not without silver intermixed, that had jingled into\r\nthe till.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must renew our stock, Cousin Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the little\r\nsaleswoman. \xe2\x80\x9cThe gingerbread figures are all gone, and so are those\r\nDutch wooden milkmaids, and most of our other playthings. There has\r\nbeen constant inquiry for cheap raisins, and a great cry for whistles,\r\nand trumpets, and jew\xe2\x80\x99s-harps; and at least a dozen little boys have\r\nasked for molasses-candy. And we must contrive to get a peck of russet\r\napples, late in the season as it is. But, dear cousin, what an enormous\r\nheap of copper! Positively a copper mountain!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell done! well done! well done!\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Uncle Venner, who had taken\r\noccasion to shuffle in and out of the shop several times in the course\r\nof the day. \xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s a girl that will never end her days at my farm!\r\nBless my eyes, what a brisk little soul!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Ph\xc5\x93be is a nice girl!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, with a scowl of austere\r\napprobation. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, Uncle Venner, you have known the family a great many\r\nyears. Can you tell me whether there ever was a Pyncheon whom she takes\r\nafter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe there ever was,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the venerable man. \xe2\x80\x9cAt any\r\nrate, it never was my luck to see her like among them, nor, for that\r\nmatter, anywhere else. I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen a great deal of the world, not only in\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x99s kitchens and back-yards but at the street-corners, and on the\r\nwharves, and in other places where my business calls me; and I\xe2\x80\x99m free\r\nto say, Miss Hepzibah, that I never knew a human creature do her work\r\nso much like one of God\xe2\x80\x99s angels as this child Ph\xc5\x93be does!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUncle Venner\xe2\x80\x99s eulogium, if it appear rather too high-strained for the\r\nperson and occasion, had, nevertheless, a sense in which it was both\r\nsubtile and true. There was a spiritual quality in Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s activity.\r\nThe life of the long and busy day\xe2\x80\x94spent in occupations that might so\r\neasily have taken a squalid and ugly aspect\xe2\x80\x94had been made pleasant, and\r\neven lovely, by the spontaneous grace with which these homely duties\r\nseemed to bloom out of her character; so that labor, while she dealt\r\nwith it, had the easy and flexible charm of play. Angels do not toil,\r\nbut let their good works grow out of them; and so did Ph\xc5\x93be.\r\n\r\nThe two relatives\xe2\x80\x94the young maid and the old one\xe2\x80\x94found time before\r\nnightfall, in the intervals of trade, to make rapid advances towards\r\naffection and confidence. A recluse, like Hepzibah, usually displays\r\nremarkable frankness, and at least temporary affability, on being\r\nabsolutely cornered, and brought to the point of personal intercourse;\r\nlike the angel whom Jacob wrestled with, she is ready to bless you when\r\nonce overcome.\r\n\r\nThe old gentlewoman took a dreary and proud satisfaction in leading\r\nPh\xc5\x93be from room to room of the house, and recounting the traditions\r\nwith which, as we may say, the walls were lugubriously frescoed. She\r\nshowed the indentations made by the lieutenant-governor\xe2\x80\x99s sword-hilt in\r\nthe door-panels of the apartment where old Colonel Pyncheon, a dead\r\nhost, had received his affrighted visitors with an awful frown. The\r\ndusky terror of that frown, Hepzibah observed, was thought to be\r\nlingering ever since in the passageway. She bade Ph\xc5\x93be step into one of\r\nthe tall chairs, and inspect the ancient map of the Pyncheon territory\r\nat the eastward. In a tract of land on which she laid her finger, there\r\nexisted a silver mine, the locality of which was precisely pointed out\r\nin some memoranda of Colonel Pyncheon himself, but only to be made\r\nknown when the family claim should be recognized by government. Thus it\r\nwas for the interest of all New England that the Pyncheons should have\r\njustice done them. She told, too, how that there was undoubtedly an\r\nimmense treasure of English guineas hidden somewhere about the house,\r\nor in the cellar, or possibly in the garden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you should happen to find it, Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, glancing aside\r\nat her with a grim yet kindly smile, \xe2\x80\x9cwe will tie up the shop-bell for\r\ngood and all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, dear cousin,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be; \xe2\x80\x9cbut, in the mean time, I hear\r\nsomebody ringing it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the customer was gone, Hepzibah talked rather vaguely, and at\r\ngreat length, about a certain Alice Pyncheon, who had been exceedingly\r\nbeautiful and accomplished in her lifetime, a hundred years ago. The\r\nfragrance of her rich and delightful character still lingered about the\r\nplace where she had lived, as a dried rose-bud scents the drawer where\r\nit has withered and perished. This lovely Alice had met with some great\r\nand mysterious calamity, and had grown thin and white, and gradually\r\nfaded out of the world. But, even now, she was supposed to haunt the\r\nHouse of the Seven Gables, and, a great many times,\xe2\x80\x94especially when one\r\nof the Pyncheons was to die,\xe2\x80\x94she had been heard playing sadly and\r\nbeautifully on the harpsichord. One of these tunes, just as it had\r\nsounded from her spiritual touch, had been written down by an amateur\r\nof music; it was so exquisitely mournful that nobody, to this day,\r\ncould bear to hear it played, unless when a great sorrow had made them\r\nknow the still profounder sweetness of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it the same harpsichord that you showed me?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Ph\xc5\x93be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe very same,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was Alice Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s harpsichord.\r\nWhen I was learning music, my father would never let me open it. So, as\r\nI could only play on my teacher\xe2\x80\x99s instrument, I have forgotten all my\r\nmusic long ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLeaving these antique themes, the old lady began to talk about the\r\ndaguerreotypist, whom, as he seemed to be a well-meaning and orderly\r\nyoung man, and in narrow circumstances, she had permitted to take up\r\nhis residence in one of the seven gables. But, on seeing more of Mr.\r\nHolgrave, she hardly knew what to make of him. He had the strangest\r\ncompanions imaginable; men with long beards, and dressed in linen\r\nblouses, and other such new-fangled and ill-fitting garments;\r\nreformers, temperance lecturers, and all manner of cross-looking\r\nphilanthropists; community-men, and come-outers, as Hepzibah believed,\r\nwho acknowledged no law, and ate no solid food, but lived on the scent\r\nof other people\xe2\x80\x99s cookery, and turned up their noses at the fare. As\r\nfor the daguerreotypist, she had read a paragraph in a penny paper, the\r\nother day, accusing him of making a speech full of wild and\r\ndisorganizing matter, at a meeting of his banditti-like associates. For\r\nher own part, she had reason to believe that he practised animal\r\nmagnetism, and, if such things were in fashion nowadays, should be apt\r\nto suspect him of studying the Black Art up there in his lonesome\r\nchamber.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, dear cousin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, \xe2\x80\x9cif the young man is so dangerous, why\r\ndo you let him stay? If he does nothing worse, he may set the house on\r\nfire!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, sometimes,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Hepzibah, \xe2\x80\x9cI have seriously made it a\r\nquestion, whether I ought not to send him away. But, with all his\r\noddities, he is a quiet kind of a person, and has such a way of taking\r\nhold of one\xe2\x80\x99s mind, that, without exactly liking him (for I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nenough of the young man), I should be sorry to lose sight of him\r\nentirely. A woman clings to slight acquaintances when she lives so much\r\nalone as I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if Mr. Holgrave is a lawless person!\xe2\x80\x9d remonstrated Ph\xc5\x93be, a part\r\nof whose essence it was to keep within the limits of law.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah carelessly,\xe2\x80\x94for, formal as she was, still, in her\r\nlife\xe2\x80\x99s experience, she had gnashed her teeth against human law,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsuppose he has a law of his own!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVI.\r\nMaule\xe2\x80\x99s Well\r\n\r\n\r\nAfter an early tea, the little country-girl strayed into the garden.\r\nThe enclosure had formerly been very extensive, but was now contracted\r\nwithin small compass, and hemmed about, partly by high wooden fences,\r\nand partly by the outbuildings of houses that stood on another street.\r\nIn its centre was a grass-plat, surrounding a ruinous little structure,\r\nwhich showed just enough of its original design to indicate that it had\r\nonce been a summer-house. A hop-vine, springing from last year\xe2\x80\x99s root,\r\nwas beginning to clamber over it, but would be long in covering the\r\nroof with its green mantle. Three of the seven gables either fronted or\r\nlooked sideways, with a dark solemnity of aspect, down into the garden.\r\n\r\nThe black, rich soil had fed itself with the decay of a long period of\r\ntime; such as fallen leaves, the petals of flowers, and the stalks and\r\nseed-vessels of vagrant and lawless plants, more useful after their\r\ndeath than ever while flaunting in the sun. The evil of these departed\r\nyears would naturally have sprung up again, in such rank weeds\r\n(symbolic of the transmitted vices of society) as are always prone to\r\nroot themselves about human dwellings. Ph\xc5\x93be saw, however, that their\r\ngrowth must have been checked by a degree of careful labor, bestowed\r\ndaily and systematically on the garden. The white double rosebush had\r\nevidently been propped up anew against the house since the commencement\r\nof the season; and a pear-tree and three damson-trees, which, except a\r\nrow of currant-bushes, constituted the only varieties of fruit, bore\r\nmarks of the recent amputation of several superfluous or defective\r\nlimbs. There were also a few species of antique and hereditary flowers,\r\nin no very flourishing condition, but scrupulously weeded; as if some\r\nperson, either out of love or curiosity, had been anxious to bring them\r\nto such perfection as they were capable of attaining. The remainder of\r\nthe garden presented a well-selected assortment of esculent vegetables,\r\nin a praiseworthy state of advancement. Summer squashes almost in their\r\ngolden blossom; cucumbers, now evincing a tendency to spread away from\r\nthe main stock, and ramble far and wide; two or three rows of\r\nstring-beans and as many more that were about to festoon themselves on\r\npoles; tomatoes, occupying a site so sheltered and sunny that the\r\nplants were already gigantic, and promised an early and abundant\r\nharvest.\r\n\r\nPh\xc5\x93be wondered whose care and toil it could have been that had planted\r\nthese vegetables, and kept the soil so clean and orderly. Not surely\r\nher cousin Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s, who had no taste nor spirits for the lady-like\r\nemployment of cultivating flowers, and\xe2\x80\x94with her recluse habits, and\r\ntendency to shelter herself within the dismal shadow of the house\xe2\x80\x94would\r\nhardly have come forth under the speck of open sky to weed and hoe\r\namong the fraternity of beans and squashes.\r\n\r\nIt being her first day of complete estrangement from rural objects,\r\nPh\xc5\x93be found an unexpected charm in this little nook of grass, and\r\nfoliage, and aristocratic flowers, and plebeian vegetables. The eye of\r\nHeaven seemed to look down into it pleasantly, and with a peculiar\r\nsmile, as if glad to perceive that nature, elsewhere overwhelmed, and\r\ndriven out of the dusty town, had here been able to retain a\r\nbreathing-place. The spot acquired a somewhat wilder grace, and yet a\r\nvery gentle one, from the fact that a pair of robins had built their\r\nnest in the pear-tree, and were making themselves exceedingly busy and\r\nhappy in the dark intricacy of its boughs. Bees, too,\xe2\x80\x94strange to\r\nsay,\xe2\x80\x94had thought it worth their while to come hither, possibly from the\r\nrange of hives beside some farm-house miles away. How many aerial\r\nvoyages might they have made, in quest of honey, or honey-laden,\r\nbetwixt dawn and sunset! Yet, late as it now was, there still arose a\r\npleasant hum out of one or two of the squash-blossoms, in the depths of\r\nwhich these bees were plying their golden labor. There was one other\r\nobject in the garden which Nature might fairly claim as her inalienable\r\nproperty, in spite of whatever man could do to render it his own. This\r\nwas a fountain, set round with a rim of old mossy stones, and paved, in\r\nits bed, with what appeared to be a sort of mosaic-work of variously\r\ncolored pebbles. The play and slight agitation of the water, in its\r\nupward gush, wrought magically with these variegated pebbles, and made\r\na continually shifting apparition of quaint figures, vanishing too\r\nsuddenly to be definable. Thence, swelling over the rim of moss-grown\r\nstones, the water stole away under the fence, through what we regret to\r\ncall a gutter, rather than a channel. Nor must we forget to mention a\r\nhen-coop of very reverend antiquity that stood in the farther corner of\r\nthe garden, not a great way from the fountain. It now contained only\r\nChanticleer, his two wives, and a solitary chicken. All of them were\r\npure specimens of a breed which had been transmitted down as an\r\nheirloom in the Pyncheon family, and were said, while in their prime,\r\nto have attained almost the size of turkeys, and, on the score of\r\ndelicate flesh, to be fit for a prince\xe2\x80\x99s table. In proof of the\r\nauthenticity of this legendary renown, Hepzibah could have exhibited\r\nthe shell of a great egg, which an ostrich need hardly have been\r\nashamed of. Be that as it might, the hens were now scarcely larger than\r\npigeons, and had a queer, rusty, withered aspect, and a gouty kind of\r\nmovement, and a sleepy and melancholy tone throughout all the\r\nvariations of their clucking and cackling. It was evident that the race\r\nhad degenerated, like many a noble race besides, in consequence of too\r\nstrict a watchfulness to keep it pure. These feathered people had\r\nexisted too long in their distinct variety; a fact of which the present\r\nrepresentatives, judging by their lugubrious deportment, seemed to be\r\naware. They kept themselves alive, unquestionably, and laid now and\r\nthen an egg, and hatched a chicken; not for any pleasure of their own,\r\nbut that the world might not absolutely lose what had once been so\r\nadmirable a breed of fowls. The distinguishing mark of the hens was a\r\ncrest of lamentably scanty growth, in these latter days, but so oddly\r\nand wickedly analogous to Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s turban, that Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x94to the poignant\r\ndistress of her conscience, but inevitably\xe2\x80\x94was led to fancy a general\r\nresemblance betwixt these forlorn bipeds and her respectable relative.\r\n\r\nThe girl ran into the house to get some crumbs of bread, cold potatoes,\r\nand other such scraps as were suitable to the accommodating appetite of\r\nfowls. Returning, she gave a peculiar call, which they seemed to\r\nrecognize. The chicken crept through the pales of the coop and ran,\r\nwith some show of liveliness, to her feet; while Chanticleer and the\r\nladies of his household regarded her with queer, sidelong glances, and\r\nthen croaked one to another, as if communicating their sage opinions of\r\nher character. So wise, as well as antique, was their aspect, as to\r\ngive color to the idea, not merely that they were the descendants of a\r\ntime-honored race, but that they had existed, in their individual\r\ncapacity, ever since the House of the Seven Gables was founded, and\r\nwere somehow mixed up with its destiny. They were a species of tutelary\r\nsprite, or Banshee; although winged and feathered differently from most\r\nother guardian angels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, you odd little chicken!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be; \xe2\x80\x9chere are some nice crumbs\r\nfor you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe chicken, hereupon, though almost as venerable in appearance as its\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x94possessing, indeed, the whole antiquity of its progenitors in\r\nminiature,\xe2\x80\x94mustered vivacity enough to flutter upward and alight on\r\nPh\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat little fowl pays you a high compliment!\xe2\x80\x9d said a voice behind\r\nPh\xc5\x93be.\r\n\r\nTurning quickly, she was surprised at sight of a young man, who had\r\nfound access into the garden by a door opening out of another gable\r\nthan that whence she had emerged. He held a hoe in his hand, and, while\r\nPh\xc5\x93be was gone in quest of the crumbs, had begun to busy himself with\r\ndrawing up fresh earth about the roots of the tomatoes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe chicken really treats you like an old acquaintance,\xe2\x80\x9d continued he\r\nin a quiet way, while a smile made his face pleasanter than Ph\xc5\x93be at\r\nfirst fancied it. \xe2\x80\x9cThose venerable personages in the coop, too, seem\r\nvery affably disposed. You are lucky to be in their good graces so\r\nsoon! They have known me much longer, but never honor me with any\r\nfamiliarity, though hardly a day passes without my bringing them food.\r\nMiss Hepzibah, I suppose, will interweave the fact with her other\r\ntraditions, and set it down that the fowls know you to be a Pyncheon!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe secret is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I have learned how to talk\r\nwith hens and chickens.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but these hens,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the young man,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthese hens of\r\naristocratic lineage would scorn to understand the vulgar language of a\r\nbarn-yard fowl. I prefer to think\xe2\x80\x94and so would Miss Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x94that they\r\nrecognize the family tone. For you are a Pyncheon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy name is Ph\xc5\x93be Pyncheon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl, with a manner of some\r\nreserve; for she was aware that her new acquaintance could be no other\r\nthan the daguerreotypist, of whose lawless propensities the old maid\r\nhad given her a disagreeable idea. \xe2\x80\x9cI did not know that my cousin\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s garden was under another person\xe2\x80\x99s care.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holgrave, \xe2\x80\x9cI dig, and hoe, and weed, in this black old\r\nearth, for the sake of refreshing myself with what little nature and\r\nsimplicity may be left in it, after men have so long sown and reaped\r\nhere. I turn up the earth by way of pastime. My sober occupation, so\r\nfar as I have any, is with a lighter material. In short, I make\r\npictures out of sunshine; and, not to be too much dazzled with my own\r\ntrade, I have prevailed with Miss Hepzibah to let me lodge in one of\r\nthese dusky gables. It is like a bandage over one\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, to come into\r\nit. But would you like to see a specimen of my productions?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA daguerreotype likeness, do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ph\xc5\x93be with less reserve;\r\nfor, in spite of prejudice, her own youthfulness sprang forward to meet\r\nhis. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t much like pictures of that sort,\xe2\x80\x94they are so hard and\r\nstern; besides dodging away from the eye, and trying to escape\r\naltogether. They are conscious of looking very unamiable, I suppose,\r\nand therefore hate to be seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you would permit me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the artist, looking at Ph\xc5\x93be, \xe2\x80\x9cI should\r\nlike to try whether the daguerreotype can bring out disagreeable traits\r\non a perfectly amiable face. But there certainly is truth in what you\r\nhave said. Most of my likenesses do look unamiable; but the very\r\nsufficient reason, I fancy, is, because the originals are so. There is\r\na wonderful insight in Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s broad and simple sunshine. While we\r\ngive it credit only for depicting the merest surface, it actually\r\nbrings out the secret character with a truth that no painter would ever\r\nventure upon, even could he detect it. There is, at least, no flattery\r\nin my humble line of art. Now, here is a likeness which I have taken\r\nover and over again, and still with no better result. Yet the original\r\nwears, to common eyes, a very different expression. It would gratify me\r\nto have your judgment on this character.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe exhibited a daguerreotype miniature in a morocco case. Ph\xc5\x93be merely\r\nglanced at it, and gave it back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know the face,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied; \xe2\x80\x9cfor its stern eye has been following\r\nme about all day. It is my Puritan ancestor, who hangs yonder in the\r\nparlor. To be sure, you have found some way of copying the portrait\r\nwithout its black velvet cap and gray beard, and have given him a\r\nmodern coat and satin cravat, instead of his cloak and band. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink him improved by your alterations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou would have seen other differences had you looked a little longer,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Holgrave, laughing, yet apparently much struck. \xe2\x80\x9cI can assure you\r\nthat this is a modern face, and one which you will very probably meet.\r\nNow, the remarkable point is, that the original wears, to the world\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neye,\xe2\x80\x94and, for aught I know, to his most intimate friends,\xe2\x80\x94an\r\nexceedingly pleasant countenance, indicative of benevolence, openness\r\nof heart, sunny good-humor, and other praiseworthy qualities of that\r\ncast. The sun, as you see, tells quite another story, and will not be\r\ncoaxed out of it, after half a dozen patient attempts on my part. Here\r\nwe have the man, sly, subtle, hard, imperious, and, withal, cold as\r\nice. Look at that eye! Would you like to be at its mercy? At that\r\nmouth! Could it ever smile? And yet, if you could only see the benign\r\nsmile of the original! It is so much the more unfortunate, as he is a\r\npublic character of some eminence, and the likeness was intended to be\r\nengraved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to see it any more,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Ph\xc5\x93be, turning away\r\nher eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is certainly very like the old portrait. But my cousin\r\nHepzibah has another picture,\xe2\x80\x94a miniature. If the original is still in\r\nthe world, I think he might defy the sun to make him look stern and\r\nhard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have seen that picture, then!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the artist, with an\r\nexpression of much interest. \xe2\x80\x9cI never did, but have a great curiosity\r\nto do so. And you judge favorably of the face?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere never was a sweeter one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is almost too soft and\r\ngentle for a man\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there nothing wild in the eye?\xe2\x80\x9d continued Holgrave, so earnestly\r\nthat it embarrassed Ph\xc5\x93be, as did also the quiet freedom with which he\r\npresumed on their so recent acquaintance. \xe2\x80\x9cIs there nothing dark or\r\nsinister anywhere? Could you not conceive the original to have been\r\nguilty of a great crime?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is nonsense,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be a little impatiently, \xe2\x80\x9cfor us to talk\r\nabout a picture which you have never seen. You mistake it for some\r\nother. A crime, indeed! Since you are a friend of my cousin Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nyou should ask her to show you the picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will suit my purpose still better to see the original,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the\r\ndaguerreotypist coolly. \xe2\x80\x9cAs to his character, we need not discuss its\r\npoints; they have already been settled by a competent tribunal, or one\r\nwhich called itself competent. But, stay! Do not go yet, if you please!\r\nI have a proposition to make you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPh\xc5\x93be was on the point of retreating, but turned back, with some\r\nhesitation; for she did not exactly comprehend his manner, although, on\r\nbetter observation, its feature seemed rather to be lack of ceremony\r\nthan any approach to offensive rudeness. There was an odd kind of\r\nauthority, too, in what he now proceeded to say, rather as if the\r\ngarden were his own than a place to which he was admitted merely by\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s courtesy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf agreeable to you,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed, \xe2\x80\x9cit would give me pleasure to turn\r\nover these flowers, and those ancient and respectable fowls, to your\r\ncare. Coming fresh from country air and occupations, you will soon feel\r\nthe need of some such out-of-door employment. My own sphere does not so\r\nmuch lie among flowers. You can trim and tend them, therefore, as you\r\nplease; and I will ask only the least trifle of a blossom, now and\r\nthen, in exchange for all the good, honest kitchen vegetables with\r\nwhich I propose to enrich Miss Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s table. So we will be\r\nfellow-laborers, somewhat on the community system.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSilently, and rather surprised at her own compliance, Ph\xc5\x93be accordingly\r\nbetook herself to weeding a flower-bed, but busied herself still more\r\nwith cogitations respecting this young man, with whom she so\r\nunexpectedly found herself on terms approaching to familiarity. She did\r\nnot altogether like him. His character perplexed the little\r\ncountry-girl, as it might a more practised observer; for, while the\r\ntone of his conversation had generally been playful, the impression\r\nleft on her mind was that of gravity, and, except as his youth modified\r\nit, almost sternness. She rebelled, as it were, against a certain\r\nmagnetic element in the artist\xe2\x80\x99s nature, which he exercised towards\r\nher, possibly without being conscious of it.\r\n\r\nAfter a little while, the twilight, deepened by the shadows of the\r\nfruit-trees and the surrounding buildings, threw an obscurity over the\r\ngarden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holgrave, \xe2\x80\x9cit is time to give over work! That last stroke\r\nof the hoe has cut off a beanstalk. Good-night, Miss Ph\xc5\x93be Pyncheon!\r\nAny bright day, if you will put one of those rosebuds in your hair, and\r\ncome to my rooms in Central Street, I will seize the purest ray of\r\nsunshine, and make a picture of the flower and its wearer.\xe2\x80\x9d He retired\r\ntowards his own solitary gable, but turned his head, on reaching the\r\ndoor, and called to Ph\xc5\x93be, with a tone which certainly had laughter in\r\nit, yet which seemed to be more than half in earnest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe careful not to drink at Maule\xe2\x80\x99s well!\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cNeither drink nor\r\nbathe your face in it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaule\xe2\x80\x99s well!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cIs that it with the rim of mossy\r\nstones? I have no thought of drinking there,\xe2\x80\x94but why not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the daguerreotypist, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause, like an old lady\xe2\x80\x99s cup of\r\ntea, it is water bewitched!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe vanished; and Ph\xc5\x93be, lingering a moment, saw a glimmering light, and\r\nthen the steady beam of a lamp, in a chamber of the gable. On returning\r\ninto Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s apartment of the house, she found the low-studded\r\nparlor so dim and dusky that her eyes could not penetrate the interior.\r\nShe was indistinctly aware, however, that the gaunt figure of the old\r\ngentlewoman was sitting in one of the straight-backed chairs, a little\r\nwithdrawn from the window, the faint gleam of which showed the blanched\r\npaleness of her cheek, turned sideways towards a corner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I light a lamp, Cousin Hepzibah?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo, if you please, my dear child,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cBut put it on\r\nthe table in the corner of the passage. My eyes are weak; and I can\r\nseldom bear the lamplight on them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat an instrument is the human voice! How wonderfully responsive to\r\nevery emotion of the human soul! In Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s tone, at that moment,\r\nthere was a certain rich depth and moisture, as if the words,\r\ncommonplace as they were, had been steeped in the warmth of her heart.\r\nAgain, while lighting the lamp in the kitchen, Ph\xc5\x93be fancied that her\r\ncousin spoke to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a moment, cousin!\xe2\x80\x9d answered the girl. \xe2\x80\x9cThese matches just glimmer,\r\nand go out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut, instead of a response from Hepzibah, she seemed to hear the murmur\r\nof an unknown voice. It was strangely indistinct, however, and less\r\nlike articulate words than an unshaped sound, such as would be the\r\nutterance of feeling and sympathy, rather than of the intellect. So\r\nvague was it, that its impression or echo in Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s mind was that of\r\nunreality. She concluded that she must have mistaken some other sound\r\nfor that of the human voice; or else that it was altogether in her\r\nfancy.\r\n\r\nShe set the lighted lamp in the passage, and again entered the parlor.\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s form, though its sable outline mingled with the dusk, was\r\nnow less imperfectly visible. In the remoter parts of the room,\r\nhowever, its walls being so ill adapted to reflect light, there was\r\nnearly the same obscurity as before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCousin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, \xe2\x80\x9cdid you speak to me just now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, child!\xe2\x80\x9d replied Hepzibah.\r\n\r\nFewer words than before, but with the same mysterious music in them!\r\nMellow, melancholy, yet not mournful, the tone seemed to gush up out of\r\nthe deep well of Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s heart, all steeped in its profoundest\r\nemotion. There was a tremor in it, too, that\xe2\x80\x94as all strong feeling is\r\nelectric\xe2\x80\x94partly communicated itself to Ph\xc5\x93be. The girl sat silently for\r\na moment. But soon, her senses being very acute, she became conscious\r\nof an irregular respiration in an obscure corner of the room. Her\r\nphysical organization, moreover, being at once delicate and healthy,\r\ngave her a perception, operating with almost the effect of a spiritual\r\nmedium, that somebody was near at hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear cousin,\xe2\x80\x9d asked she, overcoming an indefinable reluctance, \xe2\x80\x9cis\r\nthere not some one in the room with us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPh\xc5\x93be, my dear little girl,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s pause,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou were up betimes, and have been busy all day. Pray go to bed; for I\r\nam sure you must need rest. I will sit in the parlor awhile, and\r\ncollect my thoughts. It has been my custom for more years, child, than\r\nyou have lived!\xe2\x80\x9d While thus dismissing her, the maiden lady stept\r\nforward, kissed Ph\xc5\x93be, and pressed her to her heart, which beat against\r\nthe girl\xe2\x80\x99s bosom with a strong, high, and tumultuous swell. How came\r\nthere to be so much love in this desolate old heart, that it could\r\nafford to well over thus abundantly?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodnight, cousin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, strangely affected by Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmanner. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you begin to love me, I am glad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe retired to her chamber, but did not soon fall asleep, nor then very\r\nprofoundly. At some uncertain period in the depths of night, and, as it\r\nwere, through the thin veil of a dream, she was conscious of a footstep\r\nmounting the stairs heavily, but not with force and decision. The voice\r\nof Hepzibah, with a hush through it, was going up along with the\r\nfootsteps; and, again, responsive to her cousin\xe2\x80\x99s voice, Ph\xc5\x93be heard\r\nthat strange, vague murmur, which might be likened to an indistinct\r\nshadow of human utterance.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII.\r\nThe Guest\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen Ph\xc5\x93be awoke,\xe2\x80\x94which she did with the early twittering of the\r\nconjugal couple of robins in the pear-tree,\xe2\x80\x94she heard movements below\r\nstairs, and, hastening down, found Hepzibah already in the kitchen. She\r\nstood by a window, holding a book in close contiguity to her nose, as\r\nif with the hope of gaining an olfactory acquaintance with its\r\ncontents, since her imperfect vision made it not very easy to read\r\nthem. If any volume could have manifested its essential wisdom in the\r\nmode suggested, it would certainly have been the one now in Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhand; and the kitchen, in such an event, would forthwith have streamed\r\nwith the fragrance of venison, turkeys, capons, larded partridges,\r\npuddings, cakes, and Christmas pies, in all manner of elaborate mixture\r\nand concoction. It was a cookery book, full of innumerable old fashions\r\nof English dishes, and illustrated with engravings, which represented\r\nthe arrangements of the table at such banquets as it might have\r\nbefitted a nobleman to give in the great hall of his castle. And, amid\r\nthese rich and potent devices of the culinary art (not one of which,\r\nprobably, had been tested, within the memory of any man\xe2\x80\x99s grandfather),\r\npoor Hepzibah was seeking for some nimble little titbit, which, with\r\nwhat skill she had, and such materials as were at hand, she might toss\r\nup for breakfast.\r\n\r\nSoon, with a deep sigh, she put aside the savory volume, and inquired\r\nof Ph\xc5\x93be whether old Speckle, as she called one of the hens, had laid\r\nan egg the preceding day. Ph\xc5\x93be ran to see, but returned without the\r\nexpected treasure in her hand. At that instant, however, the blast of a\r\nfish-dealer\xe2\x80\x99s conch was heard, announcing his approach along the\r\nstreet. With energetic raps at the shop-window, Hepzibah summoned the\r\nman in, and made purchase of what he warranted as the finest mackerel\r\nin his cart, and as fat a one as ever he felt with his finger so early\r\nin the season. Requesting Ph\xc5\x93be to roast some coffee,\xe2\x80\x94which she\r\ncasually observed was the real Mocha, and so long kept that each of the\r\nsmall berries ought to be worth its weight in gold,\xe2\x80\x94the maiden lady\r\nheaped fuel into the vast receptacle of the ancient fireplace in such\r\nquantity as soon to drive the lingering dusk out of the kitchen. The\r\ncountry-girl, willing to give her utmost assistance, proposed to make\r\nan Indian cake, after her mother\xe2\x80\x99s peculiar method, of easy\r\nmanufacture, and which she could vouch for as possessing a richness,\r\nand, if rightly prepared, a delicacy, unequalled by any other mode of\r\nbreakfast-cake. Hepzibah gladly assenting, the kitchen was soon the\r\nscene of savory preparation. Perchance, amid their proper element of\r\nsmoke, which eddied forth from the ill-constructed chimney, the ghosts\r\nof departed cook-maids looked wonderingly on, or peeped down the great\r\nbreadth of the flue, despising the simplicity of the projected meal,\r\nyet ineffectually pining to thrust their shadowy hands into each\r\ninchoate dish. The half-starved rats, at any rate, stole visibly out of\r\ntheir hiding-places, and sat on their hind-legs, snuffing the fumy\r\natmosphere, and wistfully awaiting an opportunity to nibble.\r\n\r\nHepzibah had no natural turn for cookery, and, to say the truth, had\r\nfairly incurred her present meagreness by often choosing to go without\r\nher dinner rather than be attendant on the rotation of the spit, or\r\nebullition of the pot. Her zeal over the fire, therefore, was quite an\r\nheroic test of sentiment. It was touching, and positively worthy of\r\ntears (if Ph\xc5\x93be, the only spectator, except the rats and ghosts\r\naforesaid, had not been better employed than in shedding them), to see\r\nher rake out a bed of fresh and glowing coals, and proceed to broil the\r\nmackerel. Her usually pale cheeks were all ablaze with heat and hurry.\r\nShe watched the fish with as much tender care and minuteness of\r\nattention as if,\xe2\x80\x94we know not how to express it otherwise,\xe2\x80\x94as if her own\r\nheart were on the gridiron, and her immortal happiness were involved in\r\nits being done precisely to a turn!\r\n\r\nLife, within doors, has few pleasanter prospects than a neatly arranged\r\nand well-provisioned breakfast-table. We come to it freshly, in the\r\ndewy youth of the day, and when our spiritual and sensual elements are\r\nin better accord than at a later period; so that the material delights\r\nof the morning meal are capable of being fully enjoyed, without any\r\nvery grievous reproaches, whether gastric or conscientious, for\r\nyielding even a trifle overmuch to the animal department of our nature.\r\nThe thoughts, too, that run around the ring of familiar guests have a\r\npiquancy and mirthfulness, and oftentimes a vivid truth, which more\r\nrarely find their way into the elaborate intercourse of dinner.\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s small and ancient table, supported on its slender and\r\ngraceful legs, and covered with a cloth of the richest damask, looked\r\nworthy to be the scene and centre of one of the cheerfullest of\r\nparties. The vapor of the broiled fish arose like incense from the\r\nshrine of a barbarian idol, while the fragrance of the Mocha might have\r\ngratified the nostrils of a tutelary Lar, or whatever power has scope\r\nover a modern breakfast-table. Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s Indian cakes were the sweetest\r\noffering of all,\xe2\x80\x94in their hue befitting the rustic altars of the\r\ninnocent and golden age,\xe2\x80\x94or, so brightly yellow were they, resembling\r\nsome of the bread which was changed to glistening gold when Midas tried\r\nto eat it. The butter must not be forgotten,\xe2\x80\x94butter which Ph\xc5\x93be herself\r\nhad churned, in her own rural home, and brought it to her cousin as a\r\npropitiatory gift,\xe2\x80\x94smelling of clover-blossoms, and diffusing the charm\r\nof pastoral scenery through the dark-panelled parlor. All this, with\r\nthe quaint gorgeousness of the old china cups and saucers, and the\r\ncrested spoons, and a silver cream-jug (Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s only other article\r\nof plate, and shaped like the rudest porringer), set out a board at\r\nwhich the stateliest of old Colonel Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s guests need not have\r\nscorned to take his place. But the Puritan\xe2\x80\x99s face scowled down out of\r\nthe picture, as if nothing on the table pleased his appetite.\r\n\r\nBy way of contributing what grace she could, Ph\xc5\x93be gathered some roses\r\nand a few other flowers, possessing either scent or beauty, and\r\narranged them in a glass pitcher, which, having long ago lost its\r\nhandle, was so much the fitter for a flower-vase. The early sunshine\xe2\x80\x94as\r\nfresh as that which peeped into Eve\xe2\x80\x99s bower while she and Adam sat at\r\nbreakfast there\xe2\x80\x94came twinkling through the branches of the pear-tree,\r\nand fell quite across the table. All was now ready. There were chairs\r\nand plates for three. A chair and plate for Hepzibah,\xe2\x80\x94the same for\r\nPh\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x94but what other guest did her cousin look for?\r\n\r\nThroughout this preparation there had been a constant tremor in\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s frame; an agitation so powerful that Ph\xc5\x93be could see the\r\nquivering of her gaunt shadow, as thrown by the firelight on the\r\nkitchen wall, or by the sunshine on the parlor floor. Its\r\nmanifestations were so various, and agreed so little with one another,\r\nthat the girl knew not what to make of it. Sometimes it seemed an\r\necstasy of delight and happiness. At such moments, Hepzibah would fling\r\nout her arms, and infold Ph\xc5\x93be in them, and kiss her cheek as tenderly\r\nas ever her mother had; she appeared to do so by an inevitable impulse,\r\nand as if her bosom were oppressed with tenderness, of which she must\r\nneeds pour out a little, in order to gain breathing-room. The next\r\nmoment, without any visible cause for the change, her unwonted joy\r\nshrank back, appalled, as it were, and clothed itself in mourning; or\r\nit ran and hid itself, so to speak, in the dungeon of her heart, where\r\nit had long lain chained, while a cold, spectral sorrow took the place\r\nof the imprisoned joy, that was afraid to be enfranchised,\xe2\x80\x94a sorrow as\r\nblack as that was bright. She often broke into a little, nervous,\r\nhysteric laugh, more touching than any tears could be; and forthwith,\r\nas if to try which was the most touching, a gush of tears would follow;\r\nor perhaps the laughter and tears came both at once, and surrounded our\r\npoor Hepzibah, in a moral sense, with a kind of pale, dim rainbow.\r\nTowards Ph\xc5\x93be, as we have said, she was affectionate,\xe2\x80\x94far tenderer than\r\never before, in their brief acquaintance, except for that one kiss on\r\nthe preceding night,\xe2\x80\x94yet with a continually recurring pettishness and\r\nirritability. She would speak sharply to her; then, throwing aside all\r\nthe starched reserve of her ordinary manner, ask pardon, and the next\r\ninstant renew the just-forgiven injury.\r\n\r\nAt last, when their mutual labor was all finished, she took Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhand in her own trembling one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBear with me, my dear child,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried; \xe2\x80\x9cfor truly my heart is full\r\nto the brim! Bear with me; for I love you, Ph\xc5\x93be, though I speak so\r\nroughly. Think nothing of it, dearest child! By and by, I shall be\r\nkind, and only kind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dearest cousin, cannot you tell me what has happened?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ph\xc5\x93be,\r\nwith a sunny and tearful sympathy. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it that moves you so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush! hush! He is coming!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Hepzibah, hastily wiping her\r\neyes. \xe2\x80\x9cLet him see you first, Ph\xc5\x93be; for you are young and rosy, and\r\ncannot help letting a smile break out whether or no. He always liked\r\nbright faces! And mine is old now, and the tears are hardly dry on it.\r\nHe never could abide tears. There; draw the curtain a little, so that\r\nthe shadow may fall across his side of the table! But let there be a\r\ngood deal of sunshine, too; for he never was fond of gloom, as some\r\npeople are. He has had but little sunshine in his life,\xe2\x80\x94poor\r\nClifford,\xe2\x80\x94and, oh, what a black shadow. Poor, poor Clifford!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus murmuring in an undertone, as if speaking rather to her own heart\r\nthan to Ph\xc5\x93be, the old gentlewoman stepped on tiptoe about the room,\r\nmaking such arrangements as suggested themselves at the crisis.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile there was a step in the passage-way, above stairs. Ph\xc5\x93be\r\nrecognized it as the same which had passed upward, as through her\r\ndream, in the night-time. The approaching guest, whoever it might be,\r\nappeared to pause at the head of the staircase; he paused twice or\r\nthrice in the descent; he paused again at the foot. Each time, the\r\ndelay seemed to be without purpose, but rather from a forgetfulness of\r\nthe purpose which had set him in motion, or as if the person\xe2\x80\x99s feet\r\ncame involuntarily to a stand-still because the motive-power was too\r\nfeeble to sustain his progress. Finally, he made a long pause at the\r\nthreshold of the parlor. He took hold of the knob of the door; then\r\nloosened his grasp without opening it. Hepzibah, her hands convulsively\r\nclasped, stood gazing at the entrance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Cousin Hepzibah, pray don\xe2\x80\x99t look so!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, trembling; for\r\nher cousin\xe2\x80\x99s emotion, and this mysteriously reluctant step, made her\r\nfeel as if a ghost were coming into the room. \xe2\x80\x9cYou really frighten me!\r\nIs something awful going to happen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cBe cheerful! whatever may happen, be\r\nnothing but cheerful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe final pause at the threshold proved so long, that Hepzibah, unable\r\nto endure the suspense, rushed forward, threw open the door, and led in\r\nthe stranger by the hand. At the first glance, Ph\xc5\x93be saw an elderly\r\npersonage, in an old-fashioned dressing-gown of faded damask, and\r\nwearing his gray or almost white hair of an unusual length. It quite\r\novershadowed his forehead, except when he thrust it back, and stared\r\nvaguely about the room. After a very brief inspection of his face, it\r\nwas easy to conceive that his footstep must necessarily be such an one\r\nas that which, slowly and with as indefinite an aim as a child\xe2\x80\x99s first\r\njourney across a floor, had just brought him hitherward. Yet there were\r\nno tokens that his physical strength might not have sufficed for a free\r\nand determined gait. It was the spirit of the man that could not walk.\r\nThe expression of his countenance\xe2\x80\x94while, notwithstanding it had the\r\nlight of reason in it\xe2\x80\x94seemed to waver, and glimmer, and nearly to die\r\naway, and feebly to recover itself again. It was like a flame which we\r\nsee twinkling among half-extinguished embers; we gaze at it more\r\nintently than if it were a positive blaze, gushing vividly upward,\xe2\x80\x94more\r\nintently, but with a certain impatience, as if it ought either to\r\nkindle itself into satisfactory splendor, or be at once extinguished.\r\n\r\nFor an instant after entering the room, the guest stood still,\r\nretaining Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s hand instinctively, as a child does that of the\r\ngrown person who guides it. He saw Ph\xc5\x93be, however, and caught an\r\nillumination from her youthful and pleasant aspect, which, indeed,\r\nthrew a cheerfulness about the parlor, like the circle of reflected\r\nbrilliancy around the glass vase of flowers that was standing in the\r\nsunshine. He made a salutation, or, to speak nearer the truth, an\r\nill-defined, abortive attempt at curtsy. Imperfect as it was, however,\r\nit conveyed an idea, or, at least, gave a hint, of indescribable grace,\r\nsuch as no practised art of external manners could have attained. It\r\nwas too slight to seize upon at the instant; yet, as recollected\r\nafterwards, seemed to transfigure the whole man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Clifford,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, in the tone with which one soothes a\r\nwayward infant, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is our cousin Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x94little Ph\xc5\x93be\r\nPyncheon,\xe2\x80\x94Arthur\xe2\x80\x99s only child, you know. She has come from the country\r\nto stay with us awhile; for our old house has grown to be very lonely\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPh\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x94Ph\xc5\x93be Pyncheon?\xe2\x80\x94Ph\xc5\x93be?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated the guest, with a strange,\r\nsluggish, ill-defined utterance. \xe2\x80\x9cArthur\xe2\x80\x99s child! Ah, I forget! No\r\nmatter. She is very welcome!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, dear Clifford, take this chair,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, leading him to\r\nhis place. \xe2\x80\x9cPray, Ph\xc5\x93be, lower the curtain a very little more. Now let\r\nus begin breakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe guest seated himself in the place assigned him, and looked\r\nstrangely around. He was evidently trying to grapple with the present\r\nscene, and bring it home to his mind with a more satisfactory\r\ndistinctness. He desired to be certain, at least, that he was here, in\r\nthe low-studded, cross-beamed, oaken-panelled parlor, and not in some\r\nother spot, which had stereotyped itself into his senses. But the\r\neffort was too great to be sustained with more than a fragmentary\r\nsuccess. Continually, as we may express it, he faded away out of his\r\nplace; or, in other words, his mind and consciousness took their\r\ndeparture, leaving his wasted, gray, and melancholy figure\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nsubstantial emptiness, a material ghost\xe2\x80\x94to occupy his seat at table.\r\nAgain, after a blank moment, there would be a flickering taper-gleam in\r\nhis eyeballs. It betokened that his spiritual part had returned, and\r\nwas doing its best to kindle the heart\xe2\x80\x99s household fire, and light up\r\nintellectual lamps in the dark and ruinous mansion, where it was doomed\r\nto be a forlorn inhabitant.\r\n\r\nAt one of these moments of less torpid, yet still imperfect animation,\r\nPh\xc5\x93be became convinced of what she had at first rejected as too\r\nextravagant and startling an idea. She saw that the person before her\r\nmust have been the original of the beautiful miniature in her cousin\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s possession. Indeed, with a feminine eye for costume, she had\r\nat once identified the damask dressing-gown, which enveloped him, as\r\nthe same in figure, material, and fashion, with that so elaborately\r\nrepresented in the picture. This old, faded garment, with all its\r\npristine brilliancy extinct, seemed, in some indescribable way, to\r\ntranslate the wearer\xe2\x80\x99s untold misfortune, and make it perceptible to\r\nthe beholder\xe2\x80\x99s eye. It was the better to be discerned, by this exterior\r\ntype, how worn and old were the soul\xe2\x80\x99s more immediate garments; that\r\nform and countenance, the beauty and grace of which had almost\r\ntranscended the skill of the most exquisite of artists. It could the\r\nmore adequately be known that the soul of the man must have suffered\r\nsome miserable wrong, from its earthly experience. There he seemed to\r\nsit, with a dim veil of decay and ruin betwixt him and the world, but\r\nthrough which, at flitting intervals, might be caught the same\r\nexpression, so refined, so softly imaginative, which Malbone\xe2\x80\x94venturing\r\na happy touch, with suspended breath\xe2\x80\x94had imparted to the miniature!\r\nThere had been something so innately characteristic in this look, that\r\nall the dusky years, and the burden of unfit calamity which had fallen\r\nupon him, did not suffice utterly to destroy it.\r\n\r\nHepzibah had now poured out a cup of deliciously fragrant coffee, and\r\npresented it to her guest. As his eyes met hers, he seemed bewildered\r\nand disquieted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this you, Hepzibah?\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured sadly; then, more apart, and\r\nperhaps unconscious that he was overheard, \xe2\x80\x9cHow changed! how changed!\r\nAnd is she angry with me? Why does she bend her brow so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor Hepzibah! It was that wretched scowl which time and her\r\nnear-sightedness, and the fret of inward discomfort, had rendered so\r\nhabitual that any vehemence of mood invariably evoked it. But at the\r\nindistinct murmur of his words her whole face grew tender, and even\r\nlovely, with sorrowful affection; the harshness of her features\r\ndisappeared, as it were, behind the warm and misty glow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAngry!\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated; \xe2\x80\x9cangry with you, Clifford!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer tone, as she uttered the exclamation, had a plaintive and really\r\nexquisite melody thrilling through it, yet without subduing a certain\r\nsomething which an obtuse auditor might still have mistaken for\r\nasperity. It was as if some transcendent musician should draw a\r\nsoul-thrilling sweetness out of a cracked instrument, which makes its\r\nphysical imperfection heard in the midst of ethereal harmony,\xe2\x80\x94so deep\r\nwas the sensibility that found an organ in Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s voice!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is nothing but love here, Clifford,\xe2\x80\x9d she added,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cnothing but\r\nlove! You are at home!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe guest responded to her tone by a smile, which did not half light up\r\nhis face. Feeble as it was, however, and gone in a moment, it had a\r\ncharm of wonderful beauty. It was followed by a coarser expression; or\r\none that had the effect of coarseness on the fine mould and outline of\r\nhis countenance, because there was nothing intellectual to temper it.\r\nIt was a look of appetite. He ate food with what might almost be termed\r\nvoracity; and seemed to forget himself, Hepzibah, the young girl, and\r\neverything else around him, in the sensual enjoyment which the\r\nbountifully spread table afforded. In his natural system, though\r\nhigh-wrought and delicately refined, a sensibility to the delights of\r\nthe palate was probably inherent. It would have been kept in check,\r\nhowever, and even converted into an accomplishment, and one of the\r\nthousand modes of intellectual culture, had his more ethereal\r\ncharacteristics retained their vigor. But as it existed now, the effect\r\nwas painful and made Ph\xc5\x93be droop her eyes.\r\n\r\nIn a little while the guest became sensible of the fragrance of the yet\r\nuntasted coffee. He quaffed it eagerly. The subtle essence acted on him\r\nlike a charmed draught, and caused the opaque substance of his animal\r\nbeing to grow transparent, or, at least, translucent; so that a\r\nspiritual gleam was transmitted through it, with a clearer lustre than\r\nhitherto.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore, more!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, with nervous haste in his utterance, as if\r\nanxious to retain his grasp of what sought to escape him. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is what\r\nI need! Give me more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUnder this delicate and powerful influence he sat more erect, and\r\nlooked out from his eyes with a glance that took note of what it rested\r\non. It was not so much that his expression grew more intellectual;\r\nthis, though it had its share, was not the most peculiar effect.\r\nNeither was what we call the moral nature so forcibly awakened as to\r\npresent itself in remarkable prominence. But a certain fine temper of\r\nbeing was now not brought out in full relief, but changeably and\r\nimperfectly betrayed, of which it was the function to deal with all\r\nbeautiful and enjoyable things. In a character where it should exist as\r\nthe chief attribute, it would bestow on its possessor an exquisite\r\ntaste, and an enviable susceptibility of happiness. Beauty would be his\r\nlife; his aspirations would all tend toward it; and, allowing his frame\r\nand physical organs to be in consonance, his own developments would\r\nlikewise be beautiful. Such a man should have nothing to do with\r\nsorrow; nothing with strife; nothing with the martyrdom which, in an\r\ninfinite variety of shapes, awaits those who have the heart, and will,\r\nand conscience, to fight a battle with the world. To these heroic\r\ntempers, such martyrdom is the richest meed in the world\xe2\x80\x99s gift. To the\r\nindividual before us, it could only be a grief, intense in due\r\nproportion with the severity of the infliction. He had no right to be a\r\nmartyr; and, beholding him so fit to be happy and so feeble for all\r\nother purposes, a generous, strong, and noble spirit would, methinks,\r\nhave been ready to sacrifice what little enjoyment it might have\r\nplanned for itself,\xe2\x80\x94it would have flung down the hopes, so paltry in\r\nits regard,\xe2\x80\x94if thereby the wintry blasts of our rude sphere might come\r\ntempered to such a man.\r\n\r\nNot to speak it harshly or scornfully, it seemed Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s nature to\r\nbe a Sybarite. It was perceptible, even there, in the dark old parlor,\r\nin the inevitable polarity with which his eyes were attracted towards\r\nthe quivering play of sunbeams through the shadowy foliage. It was seen\r\nin his appreciating notice of the vase of flowers, the scent of which\r\nhe inhaled with a zest almost peculiar to a physical organization so\r\nrefined that spiritual ingredients are moulded in with it. It was\r\nbetrayed in the unconscious smile with which he regarded Ph\xc5\x93be, whose\r\nfresh and maidenly figure was both sunshine and flowers,\xe2\x80\x94their essence,\r\nin a prettier and more agreeable mode of manifestation. Not less\r\nevident was this love and necessity for the Beautiful, in the\r\ninstinctive caution with which, even so soon, his eyes turned away from\r\nhis hostess, and wandered to any quarter rather than come back. It was\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s misfortune,\xe2\x80\x94not Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s fault. How could he,\xe2\x80\x94so yellow as\r\nshe was, so wrinkled, so sad of mien, with that odd uncouthness of a\r\nturban on her head, and that most perverse of scowls contorting her\r\nbrow,\xe2\x80\x94how could he love to gaze at her? But, did he owe her no\r\naffection for so much as she had silently given? He owed her nothing. A\r\nnature like Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s can contract no debts of that kind.
