It is\xe2\x80\x94we say\r\nit without censure, nor in diminution of the claim which it\r\nindefeasibly possesses on beings of another mould\xe2\x80\x94it is always selfish\r\nin its essence; and we must give it leave to be so, and heap up our\r\nheroic and disinterested love upon it so much the more, without a\r\nrecompense. Poor Hepzibah knew this truth, or, at least, acted on the\r\ninstinct of it. So long estranged from what was lovely as Clifford had\r\nbeen, she rejoiced\xe2\x80\x94rejoiced, though with a present sigh, and a secret\r\npurpose to shed tears in her own chamber that he had brighter objects\r\nnow before his eyes than her aged and uncomely features. They never\r\npossessed a charm; and if they had, the canker of her grief for him\r\nwould long since have destroyed it.\r\n\r\nThe guest leaned back in his chair. Mingled in his countenance with a\r\ndreamy delight, there was a troubled look of effort and unrest. He was\r\nseeking to make himself more fully sensible of the scene around him;\r\nor, perhaps, dreading it to be a dream, or a play of imagination, was\r\nvexing the fair moment with a struggle for some added brilliancy and\r\nmore durable illusion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow pleasant!\xe2\x80\x94How delightful!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, but not as if addressing\r\nany one. \xe2\x80\x9cWill it last? How balmy the atmosphere through that open\r\nwindow! An open window! How beautiful that play of sunshine! Those\r\nflowers, how very fragrant! That young girl\xe2\x80\x99s face, how cheerful, how\r\nblooming!\xe2\x80\x94a flower with the dew on it, and sunbeams in the dew-drops!\r\nAh! this must be all a dream! A dream! A dream! But it has quite hidden\r\nthe four stone walls!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen his face darkened, as if the shadow of a cavern or a dungeon had\r\ncome over it; there was no more light in its expression than might have\r\ncome through the iron grates of a prison-window\xe2\x80\x94still lessening, too,\r\nas if he were sinking farther into the depths. Ph\xc5\x93be (being of that\r\nquickness and activity of temperament that she seldom long refrained\r\nfrom taking a part, and generally a good one, in what was going\r\nforward) now felt herself moved to address the stranger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is a new kind of rose, which I found this morning in the garden,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid she, choosing a small crimson one from among the flowers in the\r\nvase. \xe2\x80\x9cThere will be but five or six on the bush this season. This is\r\nthe most perfect of them all; not a speck of blight or mildew in it.\r\nAnd how sweet it is!\xe2\x80\x94sweet like no other rose! One can never forget\r\nthat scent!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x94let me see!\xe2\x80\x94let me hold it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the guest, eagerly seizing the\r\nflower, which, by the spell peculiar to remembered odors, brought\r\ninnumerable associations along with the fragrance that it exhaled.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you! This has done me good. I remember how I used to prize this\r\nflower,\xe2\x80\x94long ago, I suppose, very long ago!\xe2\x80\x94or was it only yesterday?\r\nIt makes me feel young again! Am I young? Either this remembrance is\r\nsingularly distinct, or this consciousness strangely dim! But how kind\r\nof the fair young girl! Thank you! Thank you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe favorable excitement derived from this little crimson rose afforded\r\nClifford the brightest moment which he enjoyed at the breakfast-table.\r\nIt might have lasted longer, but that his eyes happened, soon\r\nafterwards, to rest on the face of the old Puritan, who, out of his\r\ndingy frame and lustreless canvas, was looking down on the scene like a\r\nghost, and a most ill-tempered and ungenial one. The guest made an\r\nimpatient gesture of the hand, and addressed Hepzibah with what might\r\neasily be recognized as the licensed irritability of a petted member of\r\nthe family.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHepzibah!\xe2\x80\x94Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x9d cried he with no little force and distinctness,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhy do you keep that odious picture on the wall? Yes, yes!\xe2\x80\x94that is\r\nprecisely your taste! I have told you, a thousand times, that it was\r\nthe evil genius of the house!\xe2\x80\x94my evil genius particularly! Take it\r\ndown, at once!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Clifford,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah sadly, \xe2\x80\x9cyou know it cannot be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, at all events,\xe2\x80\x9d continued he, still speaking with some energy,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cpray cover it with a crimson curtain, broad enough to hang in folds,\r\nand with a golden border and tassels. I cannot bear it! It must not\r\nstare me in the face!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, dear Clifford, the picture shall be covered,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah\r\nsoothingly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is a crimson curtain in a trunk above stairs,\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nlittle faded and moth-eaten, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid,\xe2\x80\x94but Ph\xc5\x93be and I will do\r\nwonders with it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis very day, remember\xe2\x80\x9d said he; and then added, in a low,\r\nself-communing voice, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should we live in this dismal house at all?\r\nWhy not go to the South of France?\xe2\x80\x94to Italy?\xe2\x80\x94Paris, Naples, Venice,\r\nRome? Hepzibah will say we have not the means. A droll idea that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe smiled to himself, and threw a glance of fine sarcastic meaning\r\ntowards Hepzibah.\r\n\r\nBut the several moods of feeling, faintly as they were marked, through\r\nwhich he had passed, occurring in so brief an interval of time, had\r\nevidently wearied the stranger. He was probably accustomed to a sad\r\nmonotony of life, not so much flowing in a stream, however sluggish, as\r\nstagnating in a pool around his feet. A slumberous veil diffused itself\r\nover his countenance, and had an effect, morally speaking, on its\r\nnaturally delicate and elegant outline, like that which a brooding\r\nmist, with no sunshine in it, throws over the features of a landscape.\r\nHe appeared to become grosser,\xe2\x80\x94almost cloddish. If aught of interest or\r\nbeauty\xe2\x80\x94even ruined beauty\xe2\x80\x94had heretofore been visible in this man, the\r\nbeholder might now begin to doubt it, and to accuse his own imagination\r\nof deluding him with whatever grace had flickered over that visage, and\r\nwhatever exquisite lustre had gleamed in those filmy eyes.\r\n\r\nBefore he had quite sunken away, however, the sharp and peevish tinkle\r\nof the shop-bell made itself audible. Striking most disagreeably on\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s auditory organs and the characteristic sensibility of his\r\nnerves, it caused him to start upright out of his chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood heavens, Hepzibah! what horrible disturbance have we now in the\r\nhouse?\xe2\x80\x9d cried he, wreaking his resentful impatience\xe2\x80\x94as a matter of\r\ncourse, and a custom of old\xe2\x80\x94on the one person in the world that loved\r\nhim. \xe2\x80\x9cI have never heard such a hateful clamor! Why do you permit it?\r\nIn the name of all dissonance, what can it be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was very remarkable into what prominent relief\xe2\x80\x94even as if a dim\r\npicture should leap suddenly from its canvas\xe2\x80\x94Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s character was\r\nthrown by this apparently trifling annoyance. The secret was, that an\r\nindividual of his temper can always be pricked more acutely through his\r\nsense of the beautiful and harmonious than through his heart. It is\r\neven possible\xe2\x80\x94for similar cases have often happened\xe2\x80\x94that if Clifford,\r\nin his foregoing life, had enjoyed the means of cultivating his taste\r\nto its utmost perfectibility, that subtile attribute might, before this\r\nperiod, have completely eaten out or filed away his affections. Shall\r\nwe venture to pronounce, therefore, that his long and black calamity\r\nmay not have had a redeeming drop of mercy at the bottom?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Clifford, I wish I could keep the sound from your ears,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nHepzibah, patiently, but reddening with a painful suffusion of shame.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very disagreeable even to me. But, do you know, Clifford, I have\r\nsomething to tell you? This ugly noise,\xe2\x80\x94pray run, Ph\xc5\x93be, and see who is\r\nthere!\xe2\x80\x94this naughty little tinkle is nothing but our shop-bell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShop-bell!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Clifford, with a bewildered stare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, our shop-bell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, a certain natural dignity, mingled\r\nwith deep emotion, now asserting itself in her manner. \xe2\x80\x9cFor you must\r\nknow, dearest Clifford, that we are very poor. And there was no other\r\nresource, but either to accept assistance from a hand that I would push\r\naside (and so would you!) were it to offer bread when we were dying for\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x94no help, save from him, or else to earn our subsistence with my own\r\nhands! Alone, I might have been content to starve. But you were to be\r\ngiven back to me! Do you think, then, dear Clifford,\xe2\x80\x9d added she, with a\r\nwretched smile, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I have brought an irretrievable disgrace on the\r\nold house, by opening a little shop in the front gable? Our\r\ngreat-great-grandfather did the same, when there was far less need! Are\r\nyou ashamed of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShame! Disgrace! Do you speak these words to me, Hepzibah?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nClifford,\xe2\x80\x94not angrily, however; for when a man\xe2\x80\x99s spirit has been\r\nthoroughly crushed, he may be peevish at small offences, but never\r\nresentful of great ones. So he spoke with only a grieved emotion. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nwas not kind to say so, Hepzibah! What shame can befall me now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then the unnerved man\xe2\x80\x94he that had been born for enjoyment, but had\r\nmet a doom so very wretched\xe2\x80\x94burst into a woman\xe2\x80\x99s passion of tears. It\r\nwas but of brief continuance, however; soon leaving him in a quiescent,\r\nand, to judge by his countenance, not an uncomfortable state. From this\r\nmood, too, he partially rallied for an instant, and looked at Hepzibah\r\nwith a smile, the keen, half-derisory purport of which was a puzzle to\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we so very poor, Hepzibah?\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nFinally, his chair being deep and softly cushioned, Clifford fell\r\nasleep. Hearing the more regular rise and fall of his breath (which,\r\nhowever, even then, instead of being strong and full, had a feeble kind\r\nof tremor, corresponding with the lack of vigor in his\r\ncharacter),\xe2\x80\x94hearing these tokens of settled slumber, Hepzibah seized\r\nthe opportunity to peruse his face more attentively than she had yet\r\ndared to do. Her heart melted away in tears; her profoundest spirit\r\nsent forth a moaning voice, low, gentle, but inexpressibly sad. In this\r\ndepth of grief and pity she felt that there was no irreverence in\r\ngazing at his altered, aged, faded, ruined face. But no sooner was she\r\na little relieved than her conscience smote her for gazing curiously at\r\nhim, now that he was so changed; and, turning hastily away, Hepzibah\r\nlet down the curtain over the sunny window, and left Clifford to\r\nslumber there.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII.\r\nThe Pyncheon of To-day\r\n\r\n\r\nPh\xc5\x93be, on entering the shop, beheld there the already familiar face of\r\nthe little devourer\xe2\x80\x94if we can reckon his mighty deeds aright\xe2\x80\x94of Jim\r\nCrow, the elephant, the camel, the dromedaries, and the locomotive.\r\nHaving expended his private fortune, on the two preceding days, in the\r\npurchase of the above unheard-of luxuries, the young gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npresent errand was on the part of his mother, in quest of three eggs\r\nand half a pound of raisins. These articles Ph\xc5\x93be accordingly supplied,\r\nand, as a mark of gratitude for his previous patronage, and a slight\r\nsuper-added morsel after breakfast, put likewise into his hand a whale!\r\nThe great fish, reversing his experience with the prophet of Nineveh,\r\nimmediately began his progress down the same red pathway of fate\r\nwhither so varied a caravan had preceded him. This remarkable urchin,\r\nin truth, was the very emblem of old Father Time, both in respect of\r\nhis all-devouring appetite for men and things, and because he, as well\r\nas Time, after ingulfing thus much of creation, looked almost as\r\nyouthful as if he had been just that moment made.\r\n\r\nAfter partly closing the door, the child turned back, and mumbled\r\nsomething to Ph\xc5\x93be, which, as the whale was but half disposed of, she\r\ncould not perfectly understand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you say, my little fellow?\xe2\x80\x9d asked she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMother wants to know\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Ned Higgins more distinctly, \xe2\x80\x9chow Old\r\nMaid Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s brother does? Folks say he has got home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy cousin Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s brother?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Ph\xc5\x93be, surprised at this\r\nsudden explanation of the relationship between Hepzibah and her guest.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer brother! And where can he have been?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe little boy only put his thumb to his broad snub-nose, with that\r\nlook of shrewdness which a child, spending much of his time in the\r\nstreet, so soon learns to throw over his features, however\r\nunintelligent in themselves. Then as Ph\xc5\x93be continued to gaze at him,\r\nwithout answering his mother\xe2\x80\x99s message, he took his departure.\r\n\r\nAs the child went down the steps, a gentleman ascended them, and made\r\nhis entrance into the shop. It was the portly, and, had it possessed\r\nthe advantage of a little more height, would have been the stately\r\nfigure of a man considerably in the decline of life, dressed in a black\r\nsuit of some thin stuff, resembling broadcloth as closely as possible.\r\nA gold-headed cane, of rare Oriental wood, added materially to the high\r\nrespectability of his aspect, as did also a neckcloth of the utmost\r\nsnowy purity, and the conscientious polish of his boots. His dark,\r\nsquare countenance, with its almost shaggy depth of eyebrows, was\r\nnaturally impressive, and would, perhaps, have been rather stern, had\r\nnot the gentleman considerately taken upon himself to mitigate the\r\nharsh effect by a look of exceeding good-humor and benevolence. Owing,\r\nhowever, to a somewhat massive accumulation of animal substance about\r\nthe lower region of his face, the look was, perhaps, unctuous rather\r\nthan spiritual, and had, so to speak, a kind of fleshly effulgence, not\r\naltogether so satisfactory as he doubtless intended it to be. A\r\nsusceptible observer, at any rate, might have regarded it as affording\r\nvery little evidence of the general benignity of soul whereof it\r\npurported to be the outward reflection. And if the observer chanced to\r\nbe ill-natured, as well as acute and susceptible, he would probably\r\nsuspect that the smile on the gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s face was a good deal akin to\r\nthe shine on his boots, and that each must have cost him and his\r\nboot-black, respectively, a good deal of hard labor to bring out and\r\npreserve them.\r\n\r\nAs the stranger entered the little shop, where the projection of the\r\nsecond story and the thick foliage of the elm-tree, as well as the\r\ncommodities at the window, created a sort of gray medium, his smile\r\ngrew as intense as if he had set his heart on counteracting the whole\r\ngloom of the atmosphere (besides any moral gloom pertaining to Hepzibah\r\nand her inmates) by the unassisted light of his countenance. On\r\nperceiving a young rose-bud of a girl, instead of the gaunt presence of\r\nthe old maid, a look of surprise was manifest. He at first knit his\r\nbrows; then smiled with more unctuous benignity than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I see how it is!\xe2\x80\x9d said he in a deep voice,\xe2\x80\x94a voice which, had it\r\ncome from the throat of an uncultivated man, would have been gruff,\r\nbut, by dint of careful training, was now sufficiently agreeable,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwas not aware that Miss Hepzibah Pyncheon had commenced business under\r\nsuch favorable auspices. You are her assistant, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly am,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be, and added, with a little air of\r\nlady-like assumption (for, civil as the gentleman was, he evidently\r\ntook her to be a young person serving for wages), \xe2\x80\x9cI am a cousin of\r\nMiss Hepzibah, on a visit to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer cousin?\xe2\x80\x94and from the country? Pray pardon me, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\ngentleman, bowing and smiling, as Ph\xc5\x93be never had been bowed to nor\r\nsmiled on before; \xe2\x80\x9cin that case, we must be better acquainted; for,\r\nunless I am sadly mistaken, you are my own little kinswoman likewise!\r\nLet me see,\xe2\x80\x94Mary?\xe2\x80\x94Dolly?\xe2\x80\x94Ph\xc5\x93be?\xe2\x80\x94yes, Ph\xc5\x93be is the name! Is it possible\r\nthat you are Ph\xc5\x93be Pyncheon, only child of my dear cousin and\r\nclassmate, Arthur? Ah, I see your father now, about your mouth! Yes,\r\nyes! we must be better acquainted! I am your kinsman, my dear. Surely\r\nyou must have heard of Judge Pyncheon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Ph\xc5\x93be curtsied in reply, the Judge bent forward, with the pardonable\r\nand even praiseworthy purpose\xe2\x80\x94considering the nearness of blood and the\r\ndifference of age\xe2\x80\x94of bestowing on his young relative a kiss of\r\nacknowledged kindred and natural affection. Unfortunately (without\r\ndesign, or only with such instinctive design as gives no account of\r\nitself to the intellect) Ph\xc5\x93be, just at the critical moment, drew back;\r\nso that her highly respectable kinsman, with his body bent over the\r\ncounter and his lips protruded, was betrayed into the rather absurd\r\npredicament of kissing the empty air. It was a modern parallel to the\r\ncase of Ixion embracing a cloud, and was so much the more ridiculous as\r\nthe Judge prided himself on eschewing all airy matter, and never\r\nmistaking a shadow for a substance. The truth was,\xe2\x80\x94and it is Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nonly excuse,\xe2\x80\x94that, although Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s glowing benignity might\r\nnot be absolutely unpleasant to the feminine beholder, with the width\r\nof a street, or even an ordinary-sized room, interposed between, yet it\r\nbecame quite too intense, when this dark, full-fed physiognomy (so\r\nroughly bearded, too, that no razor could ever make it smooth) sought\r\nto bring itself into actual contact with the object of its regards. The\r\nman, the sex, somehow or other, was entirely too prominent in the\r\nJudge\xe2\x80\x99s demonstrations of that sort. Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s eyes sank, and, without\r\nknowing why, she felt herself blushing deeply under his look. Yet she\r\nhad been kissed before, and without any particular squeamishness, by\r\nperhaps half a dozen different cousins, younger as well as older than\r\nthis dark-browned, grisly-bearded, white-neck-clothed, and\r\nunctuously-benevolent Judge! Then, why not by him?\r\n\r\nOn raising her eyes, Ph\xc5\x93be was startled by the change in Judge\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s face. It was quite as striking, allowing for the difference\r\nof scale, as that betwixt a landscape under a broad sunshine and just\r\nbefore a thunder-storm; not that it had the passionate intensity of the\r\nlatter aspect, but was cold, hard, immitigable, like a day-long\r\nbrooding cloud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me! what is to be done now?\xe2\x80\x9d thought the country-girl to herself.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe looks as if there were nothing softer in him than a rock, nor\r\nmilder than the east wind! I meant no harm! Since he is really my\r\ncousin, I would have let him kiss me, if I could!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen, all at once, it struck Ph\xc5\x93be that this very Judge Pyncheon was\r\nthe original of the miniature which the daguerreotypist had shown her\r\nin the garden, and that the hard, stern, relentless look, now on his\r\nface, was the same that the sun had so inflexibly persisted in bringing\r\nout. Was it, therefore, no momentary mood, but, however skilfully\r\nconcealed, the settled temper of his life? And not merely so, but was\r\nit hereditary in him, and transmitted down, as a precious heirloom,\r\nfrom that bearded ancestor, in whose picture both the expression and,\r\nto a singular degree, the features of the modern Judge were shown as by\r\na kind of prophecy? A deeper philosopher than Ph\xc5\x93be might have found\r\nsomething very terrible in this idea. It implied that the weaknesses\r\nand defects, the bad passions, the mean tendencies, and the moral\r\ndiseases which lead to crime are handed down from one generation to\r\nanother, by a far surer process of transmission than human law has been\r\nable to establish in respect to the riches and honors which it seeks to\r\nentail upon posterity.\r\n\r\nBut, as it happened, scarcely had Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s eyes rested again on the\r\nJudge\xe2\x80\x99s countenance than all its ugly sternness vanished; and she found\r\nherself quite overpowered by the sultry, dog-day heat, as it were, of\r\nbenevolence, which this excellent man diffused out of his great heart\r\ninto the surrounding atmosphere,\xe2\x80\x94very much like a serpent, which, as a\r\npreliminary to fascination, is said to fill the air with his peculiar\r\nodor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like that, Cousin Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x9d cried he, with an emphatic nod of\r\napprobation. \xe2\x80\x9cI like it much, my little cousin! You are a good child,\r\nand know how to take care of yourself. A young girl\xe2\x80\x94especially if she\r\nbe a very pretty one\xe2\x80\x94can never be too chary of her lips.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, trying to laugh the matter off, \xe2\x80\x9cI did not\r\nmean to be unkind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNevertheless, whether or no it were entirely owing to the inauspicious\r\ncommencement of their acquaintance, she still acted under a certain\r\nreserve, which was by no means customary to her frank and genial\r\nnature. The fantasy would not quit her, that the original Puritan, of\r\nwhom she had heard so many sombre traditions,\xe2\x80\x94the progenitor of the\r\nwhole race of New England Pyncheons, the founder of the House of the\r\nSeven Gables, and who had died so strangely in it,\xe2\x80\x94had now stept into\r\nthe shop. In these days of off-hand equipment, the matter was easily\r\nenough arranged. On his arrival from the other world, he had merely\r\nfound it necessary to spend a quarter of an hour at a barber\xe2\x80\x99s, who had\r\ntrimmed down the Puritan\xe2\x80\x99s full beard into a pair of grizzled whiskers,\r\nthen, patronizing a ready-made clothing establishment, he had exchanged\r\nhis velvet doublet and sable cloak, with the richly worked band under\r\nhis chin, for a white collar and cravat, coat, vest, and pantaloons;\r\nand lastly, putting aside his steel-hilted broadsword to take up a\r\ngold-headed cane, the Colonel Pyncheon of two centuries ago steps\r\nforward as the Judge of the passing moment!\r\n\r\nOf course, Ph\xc5\x93be was far too sensible a girl to entertain this idea in\r\nany other way than as matter for a smile. Possibly, also, could the two\r\npersonages have stood together before her eye, many points of\r\ndifference would have been perceptible, and perhaps only a general\r\nresemblance. The long lapse of intervening years, in a climate so\r\nunlike that which had fostered the ancestral Englishman, must\r\ninevitably have wrought important changes in the physical system of his\r\ndescendant. The Judge\xe2\x80\x99s volume of muscle could hardly be the same as\r\nthe Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s; there was undoubtedly less beef in him. Though looked\r\nupon as a weighty man among his contemporaries in respect of animal\r\nsubstance, and as favored with a remarkable degree of fundamental\r\ndevelopment, well adapting him for the judicial bench, we conceive that\r\nthe modern Judge Pyncheon, if weighed in the same balance with his\r\nancestor, would have required at least an old-fashioned fifty-six to\r\nkeep the scale in equilibrio. Then the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s face had lost the ruddy\r\nEnglish hue that showed its warmth through all the duskiness of the\r\nColonel\xe2\x80\x99s weather-beaten cheek, and had taken a sallow shade, the\r\nestablished complexion of his countrymen. If we mistake not, moreover,\r\na certain quality of nervousness had become more or less manifest, even\r\nin so solid a specimen of Puritan descent as the gentleman now under\r\ndiscussion. As one of its effects, it bestowed on his countenance a\r\nquicker mobility than the old Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s had possessed, and keener\r\nvivacity, but at the expense of a sturdier something, on which these\r\nacute endowments seemed to act like dissolving acids. This process, for\r\naught we know, may belong to the great system of human progress, which,\r\nwith every ascending footstep, as it diminishes the necessity for\r\nanimal force, may be destined gradually to spiritualize us, by refining\r\naway our grosser attributes of body. If so, Judge Pyncheon could endure\r\na century or two more of such refinement as well as most other men.\r\n\r\nThe similarity, intellectual and moral, between the Judge and his\r\nancestor appears to have been at least as strong as the resemblance of\r\nmien and feature would afford reason to anticipate. In old Colonel\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s funeral discourse the clergyman absolutely canonized his\r\ndeceased parishioner, and opening, as it were, a vista through the roof\r\nof the church, and thence through the firmament above, showed him\r\nseated, harp in hand, among the crowned choristers of the spiritual\r\nworld. On his tombstone, too, the record is highly eulogistic; nor does\r\nhistory, so far as he holds a place upon its page, assail the\r\nconsistency and uprightness of his character. So also, as regards the\r\nJudge Pyncheon of to-day, neither clergyman, nor legal critic, nor\r\ninscriber of tombstones, nor historian of general or local politics,\r\nwould venture a word against this eminent person\xe2\x80\x99s sincerity as a\r\nChristian, or respectability as a man, or integrity as a judge, or\r\ncourage and faithfulness as the often-tried representative of his\r\npolitical party. But, besides these cold, formal, and empty words of\r\nthe chisel that inscribes, the voice that speaks, and the pen that\r\nwrites, for the public eye and for distant time,\xe2\x80\x94and which inevitably\r\nlose much of their truth and freedom by the fatal consciousness of so\r\ndoing,\xe2\x80\x94there were traditions about the ancestor, and private diurnal\r\ngossip about the Judge, remarkably accordant in their testimony. It is\r\noften instructive to take the woman\xe2\x80\x99s, the private and domestic, view\r\nof a public man; nor can anything be more curious than the vast\r\ndiscrepancy between portraits intended for engraving and the\r\npencil-sketches that pass from hand to hand behind the original\xe2\x80\x99s back.\r\n\r\nFor example: tradition affirmed that the Puritan had been greedy of\r\nwealth; the Judge, too, with all the show of liberal expenditure, was\r\nsaid to be as close-fisted as if his gripe were of iron. The ancestor\r\nhad clothed himself in a grim assumption of kindliness, a rough\r\nheartiness of word and manner, which most people took to be the genuine\r\nwarmth of nature, making its way through the thick and inflexible hide\r\nof a manly character. His descendant, in compliance with the\r\nrequirements of a nicer age, had etherealized this rude benevolence\r\ninto that broad benignity of smile wherewith he shone like a noonday\r\nsun along the streets, or glowed like a household fire in the\r\ndrawing-rooms of his private acquaintance. The Puritan\xe2\x80\x94if not belied by\r\nsome singular stories, murmured, even at this day, under the narrator\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbreath\xe2\x80\x94had fallen into certain transgressions to which men of his great\r\nanimal development, whatever their faith or principles, must continue\r\nliable, until they put off impurity, along with the gross earthly\r\nsubstance that involves it. We must not stain our page with any\r\ncontemporary scandal, to a similar purport, that may have been\r\nwhispered against the Judge. The Puritan, again, an autocrat in his own\r\nhousehold, had worn out three wives, and, merely by the remorseless\r\nweight and hardness of his character in the conjugal relation, had sent\r\nthem, one after another, broken-hearted, to their graves. Here the\r\nparallel, in some sort, fails. The Judge had wedded but a single wife,\r\nand lost her in the third or fourth year of their marriage. There was a\r\nfable, however,\xe2\x80\x94for such we choose to consider it, though, not\r\nimpossibly, typical of Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s marital deportment,\xe2\x80\x94that the\r\nlady got her death-blow in the honeymoon, and never smiled again,\r\nbecause her husband compelled her to serve him with coffee every\r\nmorning at his bedside, in token of fealty to her liege-lord and\r\nmaster.\r\n\r\nBut it is too fruitful a subject, this of hereditary resemblances,\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nfrequent recurrence of which, in a direct line, is truly unaccountable,\r\nwhen we consider how large an accumulation of ancestry lies behind\r\nevery man at the distance of one or two centuries. We shall only add,\r\ntherefore, that the Puritan\xe2\x80\x94so, at least, says chimney-corner\r\ntradition, which often preserves traits of character with marvellous\r\nfidelity\xe2\x80\x94was bold, imperious, relentless, crafty; laying his purposes\r\ndeep, and following them out with an inveteracy of pursuit that knew\r\nneither rest nor conscience; trampling on the weak, and, when essential\r\nto his ends, doing his utmost to beat down the strong. Whether the\r\nJudge in any degree resembled him, the further progress of our\r\nnarrative may show.\r\n\r\nScarcely any of the items in the above-drawn parallel occurred to\r\nPh\xc5\x93be, whose country birth and residence, in truth, had left her\r\npitifully ignorant of most of the family traditions, which lingered,\r\nlike cobwebs and incrustations of smoke, about the rooms and\r\nchimney-corners of the House of the Seven Gables. Yet there was a\r\ncircumstance, very trifling in itself, which impressed her with an odd\r\ndegree of horror. She had heard of the anathema flung by Maule, the\r\nexecuted wizard, against Colonel Pyncheon and his posterity,\xe2\x80\x94that God\r\nwould give them blood to drink,\xe2\x80\x94and likewise of the popular notion,\r\nthat this miraculous blood might now and then be heard gurgling in\r\ntheir throats. The latter scandal\xe2\x80\x94as became a person of sense, and,\r\nmore especially, a member of the Pyncheon family\xe2\x80\x94Ph\xc5\x93be had set down for\r\nthe absurdity which it unquestionably was. But ancient superstitions,\r\nafter being steeped in human hearts and embodied in human breath, and\r\npassing from lip to ear in manifold repetition, through a series of\r\ngenerations, become imbued with an effect of homely truth. The smoke of\r\nthe domestic hearth has scented them through and through. By long\r\ntransmission among household facts, they grow to look like them, and\r\nhave such a familiar way of making themselves at home that their\r\ninfluence is usually greater than we suspect. Thus it happened, that\r\nwhen Ph\xc5\x93be heard a certain noise in Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s throat,\xe2\x80\x94rather\r\nhabitual with him, not altogether voluntary, yet indicative of nothing,\r\nunless it were a slight bronchial complaint, or, as some people hinted,\r\nan apoplectic symptom,\xe2\x80\x94when the girl heard this queer and awkward\r\ningurgitation (which the writer never did hear, and therefore cannot\r\ndescribe), she very foolishly started, and clasped her hands.\r\n\r\nOf course, it was exceedingly ridiculous in Ph\xc5\x93be to be discomposed by\r\nsuch a trifle, and still more unpardonable to show her discomposure to\r\nthe individual most concerned in it. But the incident chimed in so\r\noddly with her previous fancies about the Colonel and the Judge, that,\r\nfor the moment, it seemed quite to mingle their identity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter with you, young woman?\xe2\x80\x9d said Judge Pyncheon, giving\r\nher one of his harsh looks. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you afraid of anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, nothing, sir\xe2\x80\x94nothing in the world!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be, with a little\r\nlaugh of vexation at herself. \xe2\x80\x9cBut perhaps you wish to speak with my\r\ncousin Hepzibah. Shall I call her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay a moment, if you please,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Judge, again beaming sunshine\r\nout of his face. \xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to be a little nervous this morning. The\r\ntown air, Cousin Ph\xc5\x93be, does not agree with your good, wholesome\r\ncountry habits. Or has anything happened to disturb you?\xe2\x80\x94anything\r\nremarkable in Cousin Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s family?\xe2\x80\x94 An arrival, eh? I thought so!\r\nNo wonder you are out of sorts, my little cousin. To be an inmate with\r\nsuch a guest may well startle an innocent young girl!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou quite puzzle me, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ph\xc5\x93be, gazing inquiringly at the\r\nJudge. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no frightful guest in the house, but only a poor,\r\ngentle, childlike man, whom I believe to be Cousin Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s brother.\r\nI am afraid (but you, sir, will know better than I) that he is not\r\nquite in his sound senses; but so mild and quiet he seems to be, that a\r\nmother might trust her baby with him; and I think he would play with\r\nthe baby as if he were only a few years older than itself. He startle\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x94Oh, no indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI rejoice to hear so favorable and so ingenuous an account of my\r\ncousin Clifford,\xe2\x80\x9d said the benevolent Judge. \xe2\x80\x9cMany years ago, when we\r\nwere boys and young men together, I had a great affection for him, and\r\nstill feel a tender interest in all his concerns. You say, Cousin\r\nPh\xc5\x93be, he appears to be weak minded. Heaven grant him at least enough\r\nof intellect to repent of his past sins!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody, I fancy,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Ph\xc5\x93be, \xe2\x80\x9ccan have fewer to repent of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is it possible, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the Judge, with a commiserating\r\nlook, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you have never heard of Clifford Pyncheon?\xe2\x80\x94that you know\r\nnothing of his history? Well, it is all right; and your mother has\r\nshown a very proper regard for the good name of the family with which\r\nshe connected herself. Believe the best you can of this unfortunate\r\nperson, and hope the best! It is a rule which Christians should always\r\nfollow, in their judgments of one another; and especially is it right\r\nand wise among near relatives, whose characters have necessarily a\r\ndegree of mutual dependence. But is Clifford in the parlor? I will just\r\nstep in and see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps, sir, I had better call my cousin Hepzibah,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be;\r\nhardly knowing, however, whether she ought to obstruct the entrance of\r\nso affectionate a kinsman into the private regions of the house. \xe2\x80\x9cHer\r\nbrother seemed to be just falling asleep after breakfast; and I am sure\r\nshe would not like him to be disturbed. Pray, sir, let me give her\r\nnotice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the Judge showed a singular determination to enter unannounced; and\r\nas Ph\xc5\x93be, with the vivacity of a person whose movements unconsciously\r\nanswer to her thoughts, had stepped towards the door, he used little or\r\nno ceremony in putting her aside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, Miss Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x9d said Judge Pyncheon in a voice as deep as a\r\nthunder-growl, and with a frown as black as the cloud whence it issues.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay you here! I know the house, and know my cousin Hepzibah, and know\r\nher brother Clifford likewise.\xe2\x80\x94nor need my little country cousin put\r\nherself to the trouble of announcing me!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94in these latter words, by the\r\nbye, there were symptoms of a change from his sudden harshness into his\r\nprevious benignity of manner. \xe2\x80\x9cI am at home here, Ph\xc5\x93be, you must\r\nrecollect, and you are the stranger. I will just step in, therefore,\r\nand see for myself how Clifford is, and assure him and Hepzibah of my\r\nkindly feelings and best wishes. It is right, at this juncture, that\r\nthey should both hear from my own lips how much I desire to serve them.\r\nHa! here is Hepzibah herself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuch was the case. The vibrations of the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s voice had reached the\r\nold gentlewoman in the parlor, where she sat, with face averted,\r\nwaiting on her brother\xe2\x80\x99s slumber. She now issued forth, as would\r\nappear, to defend the entrance, looking, we must needs say, amazingly\r\nlike the dragon which, in fairy tales, is wont to be the guardian over\r\nan enchanted beauty. The habitual scowl of her brow was undeniably too\r\nfierce, at this moment, to pass itself off on the innocent score of\r\nnear-sightedness; and it was bent on Judge Pyncheon in a way that\r\nseemed to confound, if not alarm him, so inadequately had he estimated\r\nthe moral force of a deeply grounded antipathy. She made a repelling\r\ngesture with her hand, and stood a perfect picture of prohibition, at\r\nfull length, in the dark frame of the doorway. But we must betray\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s secret, and confess that the native timorousness of her\r\ncharacter even now developed itself in a quick tremor, which, to her\r\nown perception, set each of her joints at variance with its fellows.\r\n\r\nPossibly, the Judge was aware how little true hardihood lay behind\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s formidable front. At any rate, being a gentleman of steady\r\nnerves, he soon recovered himself, and failed not to approach his\r\ncousin with outstretched hand; adopting the sensible precaution,\r\nhowever, to cover his advance with a smile, so broad and sultry, that,\r\nhad it been only half as warm as it looked, a trellis of grapes might\r\nat once have turned purple under its summer-like exposure. It may have\r\nbeen his purpose, indeed, to melt poor Hepzibah on the spot, as if she\r\nwere a figure of yellow wax.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHepzibah, my beloved cousin, I am rejoiced!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the Judge most\r\nemphatically. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, at length, you have something to live for. Yes, and\r\nall of us, let me say, your friends and kindred, have more to live for\r\nthan we had yesterday. I have lost no time in hastening to offer any\r\nassistance in my power towards making Clifford comfortable. He belongs\r\nto us all. I know how much he requires,\xe2\x80\x94how much he used to\r\nrequire,\xe2\x80\x94with his delicate taste, and his love of the beautiful.\r\nAnything in my house,\xe2\x80\x94pictures, books, wine, luxuries of the table,\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nmay command them all! It would afford me most heartfelt gratification\r\nto see him! Shall I step in, this moment?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Hepzibah, her voice quivering too painfully to allow of\r\nmany words. \xe2\x80\x9cHe cannot see visitors!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA visitor, my dear cousin!\xe2\x80\x94do you call me so?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Judge, whose\r\nsensibility, it seems, was hurt by the coldness of the phrase. \xe2\x80\x9cNay,\r\nthen, let me be Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s host, and your own likewise. Come at once to\r\nmy house. The country air, and all the conveniences,\xe2\x80\x94I may say\r\nluxuries,\xe2\x80\x94that I have gathered about me, will do wonders for him. And\r\nyou and I, dear Hepzibah, will consult together, and watch together,\r\nand labor together, to make our dear Clifford happy. Come! why should\r\nwe make more words about what is both a duty and a pleasure on my part?\r\nCome to me at once!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn hearing these so hospitable offers, and such generous recognition of\r\nthe claims of kindred, Ph\xc5\x93be felt very much in the mood of running up\r\nto Judge Pyncheon, and giving him, of her own accord, the kiss from\r\nwhich she had so recently shrunk away. It was quite otherwise with\r\nHepzibah; the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s smile seemed to operate on her acerbity of heart\r\nlike sunshine upon vinegar, making it ten times sourer than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClifford,\xe2\x80\x9d said she,\xe2\x80\x94still too agitated to utter more than an abrupt\r\nsentence,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cClifford has a home here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay Heaven forgive you, Hepzibah,\xe2\x80\x9d said Judge Pyncheon,\xe2\x80\x94reverently\r\nlifting his eyes towards that high court of equity to which he\r\nappealed,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cif you suffer any ancient prejudice or animosity to weigh\r\nwith you in this matter. I stand here with an open heart, willing and\r\nanxious to receive yourself and Clifford into it. Do not refuse my good\r\noffices,\xe2\x80\x94my earnest propositions for your welfare! They are such, in\r\nall respects, as it behooves your nearest kinsman to make. It will be a\r\nheavy responsibility, cousin, if you confine your brother to this\r\ndismal house and stifled air, when the delightful freedom of my\r\ncountry-seat is at his command.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would never suit Clifford,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, as briefly as before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWoman!\xe2\x80\x9d broke forth the Judge, giving way to his resentment, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is\r\nthe meaning of all this? Have you other resources? Nay, I suspected as\r\nmuch! Take care, Hepzibah, take care! Clifford is on the brink of as\r\nblack a ruin as ever befell him yet! But why do I talk with you, woman\r\nas you are? Make way!\xe2\x80\x94I must see Clifford!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHepzibah spread out her gaunt figure across the door, and seemed really\r\nto increase in bulk; looking the more terrible, also, because there was\r\nso much terror and agitation in her heart. But Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s evident\r\npurpose of forcing a passage was interrupted by a voice from the inner\r\nroom; a weak, tremulous, wailing voice, indicating helpless alarm, with\r\nno more energy for self-defence than belongs to a frightened infant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHepzibah, Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the voice; \xe2\x80\x9cgo down on your knees to him!\r\nKiss his feet! Entreat him not to come in! Oh, let him have mercy on\r\nme! Mercy! mercy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor the instant, it appeared doubtful whether it were not the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nresolute purpose to set Hepzibah aside, and step across the threshold\r\ninto the parlor, whence issued that broken and miserable murmur of\r\nentreaty. It was not pity that restrained him, for, at the first sound\r\nof the enfeebled voice, a red fire kindled in his eyes, and he made a\r\nquick pace forward, with something inexpressibly fierce and grim\r\ndarkening forth, as it were, out of the whole man. To know Judge\r\nPyncheon was to see him at that moment. After such a revelation, let\r\nhim smile with what sultriness he would, he could much sooner turn\r\ngrapes purple, or pumpkins yellow, than melt the iron-branded\r\nimpression out of the beholder\xe2\x80\x99s memory. And it rendered his aspect not\r\nthe less, but more frightful, that it seemed not to express wrath or\r\nhatred, but a certain hot fellness of purpose, which annihilated\r\neverything but itself.\r\n\r\nYet, after all, are we not slandering an excellent and amiable man?\r\nLook at the Judge now! He is apparently conscious of having erred, in\r\ntoo energetically pressing his deeds of loving-kindness on persons\r\nunable to appreciate them. He will await their better mood, and hold\r\nhimself as ready to assist them then as at this moment. As he draws\r\nback from the door, an all-comprehensive benignity blazes from his\r\nvisage, indicating that he gathers Hepzibah, little Ph\xc5\x93be, and the\r\ninvisible Clifford, all three, together with the whole world besides,\r\ninto his immense heart, and gives them a warm bath in its flood of\r\naffection.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do me great wrong, dear Cousin Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x9d said he, first kindly\r\noffering her his hand, and then drawing on his glove preparatory to\r\ndeparture. \xe2\x80\x9cVery great wrong! But I forgive it, and will study to make\r\nyou think better of me. Of course, our poor Clifford being in so\r\nunhappy a state of mind, I cannot think of urging an interview at\r\npresent. But I shall watch over his welfare as if he were my own\r\nbeloved brother; nor do I at all despair, my dear cousin, of\r\nconstraining both him and you to acknowledge your injustice. When that\r\nshall happen, I desire no other revenge than your acceptance of the\r\nbest offices in my power to do you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a bow to Hepzibah, and a degree of paternal benevolence in his\r\nparting nod to Ph\xc5\x93be, the Judge left the shop, and went smiling along\r\nthe street. As is customary with the rich, when they aim at the honors\r\nof a republic, he apologized, as it were, to the people, for his\r\nwealth, prosperity, and elevated station, by a free and hearty manner\r\ntowards those who knew him; putting off the more of his dignity in due\r\nproportion with the humbleness of the man whom he saluted, and thereby\r\nproving a haughty consciousness of his advantages as irrefragably as if\r\nhe had marched forth preceded by a troop of lackeys to clear the way.\r\nOn this particular forenoon, so excessive was the warmth of Judge\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s kindly aspect, that (such, at least, was the rumor about\r\ntown) an extra passage of the water-carts was found essential, in order\r\nto lay the dust occasioned by so much extra sunshine!\r\n\r\nNo sooner had he disappeared than Hepzibah grew deadly white, and,\r\nstaggering towards Ph\xc5\x93be, let her head fall on the young girl\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured she, \xe2\x80\x9cthat man has been the horror of my life!\r\nShall I never, never have the courage,\xe2\x80\x94will my voice never cease from\r\ntrembling long enough to let me tell him what he is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he so very wicked?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cYet his offers were surely kind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not speak of them,\xe2\x80\x94he has a heart of iron!\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cGo,\r\nnow, and talk to Clifford! Amuse and keep him quiet! It would disturb\r\nhim wretchedly to see me so agitated as I am. There, go, dear child,\r\nand I will try to look after the shop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPh\xc5\x93be went accordingly, but perplexed herself, meanwhile, with queries\r\nas to the purport of the scene which she had just witnessed, and also\r\nwhether judges, clergymen, and other characters of that eminent stamp\r\nand respectability, could really, in any single instance, be otherwise\r\nthan just and upright men. A doubt of this nature has a most disturbing\r\ninfluence, and, if shown to be a fact, comes with fearful and startling\r\neffect on minds of the trim, orderly, and limit-loving class, in which\r\nwe find our little country-girl. Dispositions more boldly speculative\r\nmay derive a stern enjoyment from the discovery, since there must be\r\nevil in the world, that a high man is as likely to grasp his share of\r\nit as a low one. A wider scope of view, and a deeper insight, may see\r\nrank, dignity, and station, all proved illusory, so far as regards\r\ntheir claim to human reverence, and yet not feel as if the universe\r\nwere thereby tumbled headlong into chaos. But Ph\xc5\x93be, in order to keep\r\nthe universe in its old place, was fain to smother, in some degree, her\r\nown intuitions as to Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s character. And as for her\r\ncousin\xe2\x80\x99s testimony in disparagement of it, she concluded that\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s judgment was embittered by one of those family feuds which\r\nrender hatred the more deadly by the dead and corrupted love that they\r\nintermingle with its native poison.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIX.\r\nClifford and Ph\xc5\x93be\r\n\r\n\r\nTruly was there something high, generous, and noble in the native\r\ncomposition of our poor old Hepzibah! Or else,\xe2\x80\x94and it was quite as\r\nprobably the case,\xe2\x80\x94she had been enriched by poverty, developed by\r\nsorrow, elevated by the strong and solitary affection of her life, and\r\nthus endowed with heroism, which never could have characterized her in\r\nwhat are called happier circumstances. Through dreary years Hepzibah\r\nhad looked forward\xe2\x80\x94for the most part despairingly, never with any\r\nconfidence of hope, but always with the feeling that it was her\r\nbrightest possibility\xe2\x80\x94to the very position in which she now found\r\nherself. In her own behalf, she had asked nothing of Providence but the\r\nopportunity of devoting herself to this brother, whom she had so\r\nloved,\xe2\x80\x94so admired for what he was, or might have been,\xe2\x80\x94and to whom she\r\nhad kept her faith, alone of all the world, wholly, unfalteringly, at\r\nevery instant, and throughout life. And here, in his late decline, the\r\nlost one had come back out of his long and strange misfortune, and was\r\nthrown on her sympathy, as it seemed, not merely for the bread of his\r\nphysical existence, but for everything that should keep him morally\r\nalive. She had responded to the call. She had come forward,\xe2\x80\x94our poor,\r\ngaunt Hepzibah, in her rusty silks, with her rigid joints, and the sad\r\nperversity of her scowl,\xe2\x80\x94ready to do her utmost; and with affection\r\nenough, if that were all, to do a hundred times as much! There could be\r\nfew more tearful sights,\xe2\x80\x94and Heaven forgive us if a smile insist on\r\nmingling with our conception of it!\xe2\x80\x94few sights with truer pathos in\r\nthem, than Hepzibah presented on that first afternoon.\r\n\r\nHow patiently did she endeavor to wrap Clifford up in her great, warm\r\nlove, and make it all the world to him, so that he should retain no\r\ntorturing sense of the coldness and dreariness without! Her little\r\nefforts to amuse him! How pitiful, yet magnanimous, they were!\r\n\r\nRemembering his early love of poetry and fiction, she unlocked a\r\nbookcase, and took down several books that had been excellent reading\r\nin their day. There was a volume of Pope, with the Rape of the Lock in\r\nit, and another of the Tatler, and an odd one of Dryden\xe2\x80\x99s Miscellanies,\r\nall with tarnished gilding on their covers, and thoughts of tarnished\r\nbrilliancy inside. They had no success with Clifford. These, and all\r\nsuch writers of society, whose new works glow like the rich texture of\r\na just-woven carpet, must be content to relinquish their charm, for\r\nevery reader, after an age or two, and could hardly be supposed to\r\nretain any portion of it for a mind that had utterly lost its estimate\r\nof modes and manners. Hepzibah then took up Rasselas, and began to read\r\nof the Happy Valley, with a vague idea that some secret of a contented\r\nlife had there been elaborated, which might at least serve Clifford and\r\nherself for this one day. But the Happy Valley had a cloud over it.\r\nHepzibah troubled her auditor, moreover, by innumerable sins of\r\nemphasis, which he seemed to detect, without any reference to the\r\nmeaning; nor, in fact, did he appear to take much note of the sense of\r\nwhat she read, but evidently felt the tedium of the lecture, without\r\nharvesting its profit. His sister\xe2\x80\x99s voice, too, naturally harsh, had,\r\nin the course of her sorrowful lifetime, contracted a kind of croak,\r\nwhich, when it once gets into the human throat, is as ineradicable as\r\nsin. In both sexes, occasionally, this lifelong croak, accompanying\r\neach word of joy or sorrow, is one of the symptoms of a settled\r\nmelancholy; and wherever it occurs, the whole history of misfortune is\r\nconveyed in its slightest accent. The effect is as if the voice had\r\nbeen dyed black; or,\xe2\x80\x94if we must use a more moderate simile,\xe2\x80\x94this\r\nmiserable croak, running through all the variations of the voice, is\r\nlike a black silken thread, on which the crystal beads of speech are\r\nstrung, and whence they take their hue. Such voices have put on\r\nmourning for dead hopes; and they ought to die and be buried along with\r\nthem!\r\n\r\nDiscerning that Clifford was not gladdened by her efforts, Hepzibah\r\nsearched about the house for the means of more exhilarating pastime. At\r\none time, her eyes chanced to rest on Alice Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s harpsichord. It\r\nwas a moment of great peril; for,\xe2\x80\x94despite the traditionary awe that had\r\ngathered over this instrument of music, and the dirges which spiritual\r\nfingers were said to play on it,\xe2\x80\x94the devoted sister had solemn thoughts\r\nof thrumming on its chords for Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s benefit, and accompanying the\r\nperformance with her voice. Poor Clifford! Poor Hepzibah! Poor\r\nharpsichord! All three would have been miserable together. By some good\r\nagency,\xe2\x80\x94possibly, by the unrecognized interposition of the long-buried\r\nAlice herself,\xe2\x80\x94the threatening calamity was averted.\r\n\r\nBut the worst of all\xe2\x80\x94the hardest stroke of fate for Hepzibah to endure,\r\nand perhaps for Clifford, too was his invincible distaste for her\r\nappearance. Her features, never the most agreeable, and now harsh with\r\nage and grief, and resentment against the world for his sake; her\r\ndress, and especially her turban; the queer and quaint manners, which\r\nhad unconsciously grown upon her in solitude,\xe2\x80\x94such being the poor\r\ngentlewoman\xe2\x80\x99s outward characteristics, it is no great marvel, although\r\nthe mournfullest of pities, that the instinctive lover of the Beautiful\r\nwas fain to turn away his eyes. There was no help for it. It would be\r\nthe latest impulse to die within him. In his last extremity, the\r\nexpiring breath stealing faintly through Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s lips, he would\r\ndoubtless press Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s hand, in fervent recognition of all her\r\nlavished love, and close his eyes,\xe2\x80\x94but not so much to die, as to be\r\nconstrained to look no longer on her face! Poor Hepzibah! She took\r\ncounsel with herself what might be done, and thought of putting ribbons\r\non her turban; but, by the instant rush of several guardian angels, was\r\nwithheld from an experiment that could hardly have proved less than\r\nfatal to the beloved object of her anxiety.\r\n\r\nTo be brief, besides Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s disadvantages of person, there was an\r\nuncouthness pervading all her deeds; a clumsy something, that could but\r\nill adapt itself for use, and not at all for ornament. She was a grief\r\nto Clifford, and she knew it. In this extremity, the antiquated virgin\r\nturned to Ph\xc5\x93be. No grovelling jealousy was in her heart. Had it\r\npleased Heaven to crown the heroic fidelity of her life by making her\r\npersonally the medium of Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s happiness, it would have rewarded\r\nher for all the past, by a joy with no bright tints, indeed, but deep\r\nand true, and worth a thousand gayer ecstasies. This could not be. She\r\ntherefore turned to Ph\xc5\x93be, and resigned the task into the young girl\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhands. The latter took it up cheerfully, as she did everything, but\r\nwith no sense of a mission to perform, and succeeding all the better\r\nfor that same simplicity.\r\n\r\nBy the involuntary effect of a genial temperament, Ph\xc5\x93be soon grew to\r\nbe absolutely essential to the daily comfort, if not the daily life, of\r\nher two forlorn companions. The grime and sordidness of the House of\r\nthe Seven Gables seemed to have vanished since her appearance there;\r\nthe gnawing tooth of the dry-rot was stayed among the old timbers of\r\nits skeleton frame; the dust had ceased to settle down so densely, from\r\nthe antique ceilings, upon the floors and furniture of the rooms\r\nbelow,\xe2\x80\x94or, at any rate, there was a little housewife, as light-footed\r\nas the breeze that sweeps a garden walk, gliding hither and thither to\r\nbrush it all away. The shadows of gloomy events that haunted the else\r\nlonely and desolate apartments; the heavy, breathless scent which death\r\nhad left in more than one of the bedchambers, ever since his visits of\r\nlong ago,\xe2\x80\x94these were less powerful than the purifying influence\r\nscattered throughout the atmosphere of the household by the presence of\r\none youthful, fresh, and thoroughly wholesome heart. There was no\r\nmorbidness in Ph\xc5\x93be; if there had been, the old Pyncheon House was the\r\nvery locality to ripen it into incurable disease. But now her spirit\r\nresembled, in its potency, a minute quantity of ottar of rose in one of\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s huge, iron-bound trunks, diffusing its fragrance through the\r\nvarious articles of linen and wrought-lace, kerchiefs, caps, stockings,\r\nfolded dresses, gloves, and whatever else was treasured there. As every\r\narticle in the great trunk was the sweeter for the rose-scent, so did\r\nall the thoughts and emotions of Hepzibah and Clifford, sombre as they\r\nmight seem, acquire a subtle attribute of happiness from Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nintermixture with them. Her activity of body, intellect, and heart\r\nimpelled her continually to perform the ordinary little toils that\r\noffered themselves around her, and to think the thought proper for the\r\nmoment, and to sympathize,\xe2\x80\x94now with the twittering gayety of the robins\r\nin the pear-tree, and now to such a depth as she could with Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndark anxiety, or the vague moan of her brother. This facile adaptation\r\nwas at once the symptom of perfect health and its best preservative.\r\n\r\nA nature like Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s has invariably its due influence, but is seldom\r\nregarded with due honor. Its spiritual force, however, may be partially\r\nestimated by the fact of her having found a place for herself, amid\r\ncircumstances so stern as those which surrounded the mistress of the\r\nhouse; and also by the effect which she produced on a character of so\r\nmuch more mass than her own. For the gaunt, bony frame and limbs of\r\nHepzibah, as compared with the tiny lightsomeness of Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s figure,\r\nwere perhaps in some fit proportion with the moral weight and\r\nsubstance, respectively, of the woman and the girl.\r\n\r\nTo the guest,\xe2\x80\x94to Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s brother,\xe2\x80\x94or Cousin Clifford, as Ph\xc5\x93be now\r\nbegan to call him,\xe2\x80\x94she was especially necessary. Not that he could ever\r\nbe said to converse with her, or often manifest, in any other very\r\ndefinite mode, his sense of a charm in her society. But if she were a\r\nlong while absent he became pettish and nervously restless, pacing the\r\nroom to and fro with the uncertainty that characterized all his\r\nmovements; or else would sit broodingly in his great chair, resting his\r\nhead on his hands, and evincing life only by an electric sparkle of\r\nill-humor, whenever Hepzibah endeavored to arouse him. Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npresence, and the contiguity of her fresh life to his blighted one, was\r\nusually all that he required. Indeed, such was the native gush and play\r\nof her spirit, that she was seldom perfectly quiet and undemonstrative,\r\nany more than a fountain ever ceases to dimple and warble with its\r\nflow. She possessed the gift of song, and that, too, so naturally, that\r\nyou would as little think of inquiring whence she had caught it, or\r\nwhat master had taught her, as of asking the same questions about a\r\nbird, in whose small strain of music we recognize the voice of the\r\nCreator as distinctly as in the loudest accents of his thunder. So long\r\nas Ph\xc5\x93be sang, she might stray at her own will about the house.\r\nClifford was content, whether the sweet, airy homeliness of her tones\r\ncame down from the upper chambers, or along the passageway from the\r\nshop, or was sprinkled through the foliage of the pear-tree, inward\r\nfrom the garden, with the twinkling sunbeams. He would sit quietly,\r\nwith a gentle pleasure gleaming over his face, brighter now, and now a\r\nlittle dimmer, as the song happened to float near him, or was more\r\nremotely heard. It pleased him best, however, when she sat on a low\r\nfootstool at his knee.\r\n\r\nIt is perhaps remarkable, considering her temperament, that Ph\xc5\x93be\r\noftener chose a strain of pathos than of gayety. But the young and\r\nhappy are not ill pleased to temper their life with a transparent\r\nshadow. The deepest pathos of Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s voice and song, moreover, came\r\nsifted through the golden texture of a cheery spirit, and was somehow\r\nso interfused with the quality thence acquired, that one\xe2\x80\x99s heart felt\r\nall the lighter for having wept at it. Broad mirth, in the sacred\r\npresence of dark misfortune, would have jarred harshly and irreverently\r\nwith the solemn symphony that rolled its undertone through Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nand her brother\xe2\x80\x99s life. Therefore, it was well that Ph\xc5\x93be so often\r\nchose sad themes, and not amiss that they ceased to be so sad while she\r\nwas singing them.\r\n\r\nBecoming habituated to her companionship, Clifford readily showed how\r\ncapable of imbibing pleasant tints and gleams of cheerful light from\r\nall quarters his nature must originally have been. He grew youthful\r\nwhile she sat by him. A beauty,\xe2\x80\x94not precisely real, even in its utmost\r\nmanifestation, and which a painter would have watched long to seize and\r\nfix upon his canvas, and, after all, in vain,\xe2\x80\x94beauty, nevertheless,\r\nthat was not a mere dream, would sometimes play upon and illuminate his\r\nface. It did more than to illuminate; it transfigured him with an\r\nexpression that could only be interpreted as the glow of an exquisite\r\nand happy spirit. That gray hair, and those furrows,\xe2\x80\x94with their record\r\nof infinite sorrow so deeply written across his brow, and so\r\ncompressed, as with a futile effort to crowd in all the tale, that the\r\nwhole inscription was made illegible,\xe2\x80\x94these, for the moment, vanished.\r\nAn eye at once tender and acute might have beheld in the man some\r\nshadow of what he was meant to be. Anon, as age came stealing, like a\r\nsad twilight, back over his figure, you would have felt tempted to hold\r\nan argument with Destiny, and affirm, that either this being should not\r\nhave been made mortal, or mortal existence should have been tempered to\r\nhis qualities. There seemed no necessity for his having drawn breath at\r\nall; the world never wanted him; but, as he had breathed, it ought\r\nalways to have been the balmiest of summer air. The same perplexity\r\nwill invariably haunt us with regard to natures that tend to feed\r\nexclusively upon the Beautiful, let their earthly fate be as lenient as\r\nit may.\r\n\r\nPh\xc5\x93be, it is probable, had but a very imperfect comprehension of the\r\ncharacter over which she had thrown so beneficent a spell. Nor was it\r\nnecessary. The fire upon the hearth can gladden a whole semicircle of\r\nfaces round about it, but need not know the individuality of one among\r\nthem all. Indeed, there was something too fine and delicate in\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s traits to be perfectly appreciated by one whose sphere lay\r\nso much in the Actual as Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s did. For Clifford, however, the\r\nreality, and simplicity, and thorough homeliness of the girl\xe2\x80\x99s nature\r\nwere as powerful a charm as any that she possessed. Beauty, it is true,\r\nand beauty almost perfect in its own style, was indispensable. Had\r\nPh\xc5\x93be been coarse in feature, shaped clumsily, of a harsh voice, and\r\nuncouthly mannered, she might have been rich with all good gifts,\r\nbeneath this unfortunate exterior, and still, so long as she wore the\r\nguise of woman, she would have shocked Clifford, and depressed him by\r\nher lack of beauty. But nothing more beautiful\xe2\x80\x94nothing prettier, at\r\nleast\xe2\x80\x94was ever made than Ph\xc5\x93be. And, therefore, to this man,\xe2\x80\x94whose\r\nwhole poor and impalpable enjoyment of existence heretofore, and until\r\nboth his heart and fancy died within him, had been a dream,\xe2\x80\x94whose\r\nimages of women had more and more lost their warmth and substance, and\r\nbeen frozen, like the pictures of secluded artists, into the chillest\r\nideality,\xe2\x80\x94to him, this little figure of the cheeriest household life\r\nwas just what he required to bring him back into the breathing world.\r\nPersons who have wandered, or been expelled, out of the common track of\r\nthings, even were it for a better system, desire nothing so much as to\r\nbe led back. They shiver in their loneliness, be it on a mountain-top\r\nor in a dungeon. Now, Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s presence made a home about her,\xe2\x80\x94that very\r\nsphere which the outcast, the prisoner, the potentate,\xe2\x80\x94the wretch\r\nbeneath mankind, the wretch aside from it, or the wretch above\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x94instinctively pines after,\xe2\x80\x94a home! She was real! Holding her hand,\r\nyou felt something; a tender something; a substance, and a warm one:\r\nand so long as you should feel its grasp, soft as it was, you might be\r\ncertain that your place was good in the whole sympathetic chain of\r\nhuman nature. The world was no longer a delusion.\r\n\r\nBy looking a little further in this direction, we might suggest an\r\nexplanation of an often-suggested mystery. Why are poets so apt to\r\nchoose their mates, not for any similarity of poetic endowment, but for\r\nqualities which might make the happiness of the rudest handicraftsman\r\nas well as that of the ideal craftsman of the spirit? Because,\r\nprobably, at his highest elevation, the poet needs no human\r\nintercourse; but he finds it dreary to descend, and be a stranger.\r\n\r\nThere was something very beautiful in the relation that grew up between\r\nthis pair, so closely and constantly linked together, yet with such a\r\nwaste of gloomy and mysterious years from his birthday to hers. On\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s part it was the feeling of a man naturally endowed with the\r\nliveliest sensibility to feminine influence, but who had never quaffed\r\nthe cup of passionate love, and knew that it was now too late. He knew\r\nit, with the instinctive delicacy that had survived his intellectual\r\ndecay. Thus, his sentiment for Ph\xc5\x93be, without being paternal, was not\r\nless chaste than if she had been his daughter. He was a man, it is\r\ntrue, and recognized her as a woman. She was his only representative of\r\nwomankind. He took unfailing note of every charm that appertained to\r\nher sex, and saw the ripeness of her lips, and the virginal development\r\nof her bosom. All her little womanly ways, budding out of her like\r\nblossoms on a young fruit-tree, had their effect on him, and sometimes\r\ncaused his very heart to tingle with the keenest thrills of pleasure.\r\nAt such moments,\xe2\x80\x94for the effect was seldom more than momentary,\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nhalf-torpid man would be full of harmonious life, just as a long-silent\r\nharp is full of sound, when the musician\xe2\x80\x99s fingers sweep across it.\r\nBut, after all, it seemed rather a perception, or a sympathy, than a\r\nsentiment belonging to himself as an individual. He read Ph\xc5\x93be as he\r\nwould a sweet and simple story; he listened to her as if she were a\r\nverse of household poetry, which God, in requital of his bleak and\r\ndismal lot, had permitted some angel, that most pitied him, to warble\r\nthrough the house. She was not an actual fact for him, but the\r\ninterpretation of all that he lacked on earth brought warmly home to\r\nhis conception; so that this mere symbol, or life-like picture, had\r\nalmost the comfort of reality.\r\n\r\nBut we strive in vain to put the idea into words. No adequate\r\nexpression of the beauty and profound pathos with which it impresses us\r\nis attainable. This being, made only for happiness, and heretofore so\r\nmiserably failing to be happy,\xe2\x80\x94his tendencies so hideously thwarted,\r\nthat, some unknown time ago, the delicate springs of his character,\r\nnever morally or intellectually strong, had given way, and he was now\r\nimbecile,\xe2\x80\x94this poor, forlorn voyager from the Islands of the Blest, in\r\na frail bark, on a tempestuous sea, had been flung, by the last\r\nmountain-wave of his shipwreck, into a quiet harbor. There, as he lay\r\nmore than half lifeless on the strand, the fragrance of an earthly\r\nrose-bud had come to his nostrils, and, as odors will, had summoned up\r\nreminiscences or visions of all the living and breathing beauty amid\r\nwhich he should have had his home. With his native susceptibility of\r\nhappy influences, he inhales the slight, ethereal rapture into his\r\nsoul, and expires!\r\n\r\nAnd how did Ph\xc5\x93be regard Clifford? The girl\xe2\x80\x99s was not one of those\r\nnatures which are most attracted by what is strange and exceptional in\r\nhuman character. The path which would best have suited her was the\r\nwell-worn track of ordinary life; the companions in whom she would most\r\nhave delighted were such as one encounters at every turn. The mystery\r\nwhich enveloped Clifford, so far as it affected her at all, was an\r\nannoyance, rather than the piquant charm which many women might have\r\nfound in it. Still, her native kindliness was brought strongly into\r\nplay, not by what was darkly picturesque in his situation, nor so much,\r\neven, by the finer graces of his character, as by the simple appeal of\r\na heart so forlorn as his to one so full of genuine sympathy as hers.\r\nShe gave him an affectionate regard, because he needed so much love,\r\nand seemed to have received so little. With a ready tact, the result of\r\never-active and wholesome sensibility, she discerned what was good for\r\nhim, and did it. Whatever was morbid in his mind and experience she\r\nignored; and thereby kept their intercourse healthy, by the incautious,\r\nbut, as it were, heaven-directed freedom of her whole conduct. The sick\r\nin mind, and, perhaps, in body, are rendered more darkly and hopelessly\r\nso by the manifold reflection of their disease, mirrored back from all\r\nquarters in the deportment of those about them; they are compelled to\r\ninhale the poison of their own breath, in infinite repetition. But\r\nPh\xc5\x93be afforded her poor patient a supply of purer air. She impregnated\r\nit, too, not with a wild-flower scent,\xe2\x80\x94for wildness was no trait of\r\nhers,\xe2\x80\x94but with the perfume of garden-roses, pinks, and other blossoms\r\nof much sweetness, which nature and man have consented together in\r\nmaking grow from summer to summer, and from century to century. Such a\r\nflower was Ph\xc5\x93be in her relation with Clifford, and such the delight\r\nthat he inhaled from her.\r\n\r\nYet, it must be said, her petals sometimes drooped a little, in\r\nconsequence of the heavy atmosphere about her. She grew more thoughtful\r\nthan heretofore. Looking aside at Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s face, and seeing the dim,\r\nunsatisfactory elegance and the intellect almost quenched, she would\r\ntry to inquire what had been his life. Was he always thus? Had this\r\nveil been over him from his birth?\xe2\x80\x94this veil, under which far more of\r\nhis spirit was hidden than revealed, and through which he so\r\nimperfectly discerned the actual world,\xe2\x80\x94or was its gray texture woven\r\nof some dark calamity? Ph\xc5\x93be loved no riddles, and would have been glad\r\nto escape the perplexity of this one. Nevertheless, there was so far a\r\ngood result of her meditations on Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s character, that, when her\r\ninvoluntary conjectures, together with the tendency of every strange\r\ncircumstance to tell its own story, had gradually taught her the fact,\r\nit had no terrible effect upon her. Let the world have done him what\r\nvast wrong it might, she knew Cousin Clifford too well\xe2\x80\x94or fancied\r\nso\xe2\x80\x94ever to shudder at the touch of his thin, delicate fingers.\r\n\r\nWithin a few days after the appearance of this remarkable inmate, the\r\nroutine of life had established itself with a good deal of uniformity\r\nin the old house of our narrative. In the morning, very shortly after\r\nbreakfast, it was Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s custom to fall asleep in his chair; nor,\r\nunless accidentally disturbed, would he emerge from a dense cloud of\r\nslumber or the thinner mists that flitted to and fro, until well\r\ntowards noonday. These hours of drowsihead were the season of the old\r\ngentlewoman\xe2\x80\x99s attendance on her brother, while Ph\xc5\x93be took charge of the\r\nshop; an arrangement which the public speedily understood, and evinced\r\ntheir decided preference of the younger shopwoman by the multiplicity\r\nof their calls during her administration of affairs. Dinner over,\r\nHepzibah took her knitting-work,\xe2\x80\x94a long stocking of gray yarn, for her\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s winter wear,\xe2\x80\x94and with a sigh, and a scowl of affectionate\r\nfarewell to Clifford, and a gesture enjoining watchfulness on Ph\xc5\x93be,\r\nwent to take her seat behind the counter. It was now the young girl\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nturn to be the nurse,\xe2\x80\x94the guardian, the playmate,\xe2\x80\x94or whatever is the\r\nfitter phrase,\xe2\x80\x94of the gray-haired man.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nX.\r\nThe Pyncheon Garden\r\n\r\n\r\nClifford, except for Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s more active instigation would ordinarily\r\nhave yielded to the torpor which had crept through all his modes of\r\nbeing, and which sluggishly counselled him to sit in his morning chair\r\ntill eventide. But the girl seldom failed to propose a removal to the\r\ngarden, where Uncle Venner and the daguerreotypist had made such\r\nrepairs on the roof of the ruinous arbor, or summer-house, that it was\r\nnow a sufficient shelter from sunshine and casual showers. The\r\nhop-vine, too, had begun to grow luxuriantly over the sides of the\r\nlittle edifice, and made an interior of verdant seclusion, with\r\ninnumerable peeps and glimpses into the wider solitude of the garden.\r\n\r\nHere, sometimes, in this green play-place of flickering light, Ph\xc5\x93be\r\nread to Clifford. Her acquaintance, the artist, who appeared to have a\r\nliterary turn, had supplied her with works of fiction, in pamphlet\r\nform,\xe2\x80\x94and a few volumes of poetry, in altogether a different style and\r\ntaste from those which Hepzibah selected for his amusement. Small\r\nthanks were due to the books, however, if the girl\xe2\x80\x99s readings were in\r\nany degree more successful than her elderly cousin\xe2\x80\x99s. Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s voice had\r\nalways a pretty music in it, and could either enliven Clifford by its\r\nsparkle and gayety of tone, or soothe him by a continued flow of pebbly\r\nand brook-like cadences. But the fictions\xe2\x80\x94in which the country-girl,\r\nunused to works of that nature, often became deeply absorbed\xe2\x80\x94interested\r\nher strange auditor very little, or not at all. Pictures of life,\r\nscenes of passion or sentiment, wit, humor, and pathos, were all thrown\r\naway, or worse than thrown away, on Clifford; either because he lacked\r\nan experience by which to test their truth, or because his own griefs\r\nwere a touch-stone of reality that few feigned emotions could\r\nwithstand. When Ph\xc5\x93be broke into a peal of merry laughter at what she\r\nread, he would now and then laugh for sympathy, but oftener respond\r\nwith a troubled, questioning look. If a tear\xe2\x80\x94a maiden\xe2\x80\x99s sunshiny tear\r\nover imaginary woe\xe2\x80\x94dropped upon some melancholy page, Clifford either\r\ntook it as a token of actual calamity, or else grew peevish, and\r\nangrily motioned her to close the volume. And wisely too! Is not the\r\nworld sad enough, in genuine earnest, without making a pastime of mock\r\nsorrows?\r\n\r\nWith poetry it was rather better. He delighted in the swell and\r\nsubsidence of the rhythm, and the happily recurring rhyme. Nor was\r\nClifford incapable of feeling the sentiment of poetry,\xe2\x80\x94not, perhaps,\r\nwhere it was highest or deepest, but where it was most flitting and\r\nethereal. It was impossible to foretell in what exquisite verse the\r\nawakening spell might lurk; but, on raising her eyes from the page to\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s face, Ph\xc5\x93be would be made aware, by the light breaking\r\nthrough it, that a more delicate intelligence than her own had caught a\r\nlambent flame from what she read. One glow of this kind, however, was\r\noften the precursor of gloom for many hours afterward; because, when\r\nthe glow left him, he seemed conscious of a missing sense and power,\r\nand groped about for them, as if a blind man should go seeking his lost\r\neyesight.\r\n\r\nIt pleased him more, and was better for his inward welfare, that Ph\xc5\x93be\r\nshould talk, and make passing occurrences vivid to his mind by her\r\naccompanying description and remarks. The life of the garden offered\r\ntopics enough for such discourse as suited Clifford best. He never\r\nfailed to inquire what flowers had bloomed since yesterday. His feeling\r\nfor flowers was very exquisite, and seemed not so much a taste as an\r\nemotion; he was fond of sitting with one in his hand, intently\r\nobserving it, and looking from its petals into Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s face, as if the\r\ngarden flower were the sister of the household maiden. Not merely was\r\nthere a delight in the flower\xe2\x80\x99s perfume, or pleasure in its beautiful\r\nform, and the delicacy or brightness of its hue; but Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nenjoyment was accompanied with a perception of life, character, and\r\nindividuality, that made him love these blossoms of the garden, as if\r\nthey were endowed with sentiment and intelligence. This affection and\r\nsympathy for flowers is almost exclusively a woman\xe2\x80\x99s trait. Men, if\r\nendowed with it by nature, soon lose, forget, and learn to despise it,\r\nin their contact with coarser things than flowers. Clifford, too, had\r\nlong forgotten it; but found it again now, as he slowly revived from\r\nthe chill torpor of his life.\r\n\r\nIt is wonderful how many pleasant incidents continually came to pass in\r\nthat secluded garden-spot when once Ph\xc5\x93be had set herself to look for\r\nthem. She had seen or heard a bee there, on the first day of her\r\nacquaintance with the place.
