And often,\xe2\x80\x94almost continually,\r\nindeed,\xe2\x80\x94since then, the bees kept coming thither, Heaven knows why, or\r\nby what pertinacious desire, for far-fetched sweets, when, no doubt,\r\nthere were broad clover-fields, and all kinds of garden growth, much\r\nnearer home than this. Thither the bees came, however, and plunged into\r\nthe squash-blossoms, as if there were no other squash-vines within a\r\nlong day\xe2\x80\x99s flight, or as if the soil of Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s garden gave its\r\nproductions just the very quality which these laborious little wizards\r\nwanted, in order to impart the Hymettus odor to their whole hive of New\r\nEngland honey. When Clifford heard their sunny, buzzing murmur, in the\r\nheart of the great yellow blossoms, he looked about him with a joyful\r\nsense of warmth, and blue sky, and green grass, and of God\xe2\x80\x99s free air\r\nin the whole height from earth to heaven. After all, there need be no\r\nquestion why the bees came to that one green nook in the dusty town.\r\nGod sent them thither to gladden our poor Clifford. They brought the\r\nrich summer with them, in requital of a little honey.\r\n\r\nWhen the bean-vines began to flower on the poles, there was one\r\nparticular variety which bore a vivid scarlet blossom. The\r\ndaguerreotypist had found these beans in a garret, over one of the\r\nseven gables, treasured up in an old chest of drawers by some\r\nhorticultural Pyncheon of days gone by, who doubtless meant to sow them\r\nthe next summer, but was himself first sown in Death\xe2\x80\x99s garden-ground.\r\nBy way of testing whether there were still a living germ in such\r\nancient seeds, Holgrave had planted some of them; and the result of his\r\nexperiment was a splendid row of bean-vines, clambering, early, to the\r\nfull height of the poles, and arraying them, from top to bottom, in a\r\nspiral profusion of red blossoms. And, ever since the unfolding of the\r\nfirst bud, a multitude of humming-birds had been attracted thither. At\r\ntimes, it seemed as if for every one of the hundred blossoms there was\r\none of these tiniest fowls of the air,\xe2\x80\x94a thumb\xe2\x80\x99s bigness of burnished\r\nplumage, hovering and vibrating about the bean-poles. It was with\r\nindescribable interest, and even more than childish delight, that\r\nClifford watched the humming-birds. He used to thrust his head softly\r\nout of the arbor to see them the better; all the while, too, motioning\r\nPh\xc5\x93be to be quiet, and snatching glimpses of the smile upon her face,\r\nso as to heap his enjoyment up the higher with her sympathy. He had not\r\nmerely grown young;\xe2\x80\x94he was a child again.\r\n\r\nHepzibah, whenever she happened to witness one of these fits of\r\nminiature enthusiasm, would shake her head, with a strange mingling of\r\nthe mother and sister, and of pleasure and sadness, in her aspect. She\r\nsaid that it had always been thus with Clifford when the humming-birds\r\ncame,\xe2\x80\x94always, from his babyhood,\xe2\x80\x94and that his delight in them had been\r\none of the earliest tokens by which he showed his love for beautiful\r\nthings. And it was a wonderful coincidence, the good lady thought, that\r\nthe artist should have planted these scarlet-flowering beans\xe2\x80\x94which the\r\nhumming-birds sought far and wide, and which had not grown in the\r\nPyncheon garden before for forty years\xe2\x80\x94on the very summer of Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreturn.\r\n\r\nThen would the tears stand in poor Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, or overflow them\r\nwith a too abundant gush, so that she was fain to betake herself into\r\nsome corner, lest Clifford should espy her agitation. Indeed, all the\r\nenjoyments of this period were provocative of tears. Coming so late as\r\nit did, it was a kind of Indian summer, with a mist in its balmiest\r\nsunshine, and decay and death in its gaudiest delight. The more\r\nClifford seemed to taste the happiness of a child, the sadder was the\r\ndifference to be recognized. With a mysterious and terrible Past, which\r\nhad annihilated his memory, and a blank Future before him, he had only\r\nthis visionary and impalpable Now, which, if you once look closely at\r\nit, is nothing. He himself, as was perceptible by many symptoms, lay\r\ndarkly behind his pleasure, and knew it to be a baby-play, which he was\r\nto toy and trifle with, instead of thoroughly believing. Clifford saw,\r\nit may be, in the mirror of his deeper consciousness, that he was an\r\nexample and representative of that great class of people whom an\r\ninexplicable Providence is continually putting at cross-purposes with\r\nthe world: breaking what seems its own promise in their nature;\r\nwithholding their proper food, and setting poison before them for a\r\nbanquet; and thus\xe2\x80\x94when it might so easily, as one would think, have\r\nbeen adjusted otherwise\xe2\x80\x94making their existence a strangeness, a\r\nsolitude, and torment. All his life long, he had been learning how to\r\nbe wretched, as one learns a foreign tongue; and now, with the lesson\r\nthoroughly by heart, he could with difficulty comprehend his little\r\nairy happiness. Frequently there was a dim shadow of doubt in his eyes.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake my hand, Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d he would say, \xe2\x80\x9cand pinch it hard with your\r\nlittle fingers! Give me a rose, that I may press its thorns, and prove\r\nmyself awake by the sharp touch of pain!\xe2\x80\x9d Evidently, he desired this\r\nprick of a trifling anguish, in order to assure himself, by that\r\nquality which he best knew to be real, that the garden, and the seven\r\nweather-beaten gables, and Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s scowl, and Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s smile, were\r\nreal likewise. Without this signet in his flesh, he could have\r\nattributed no more substance to them than to the empty confusion of\r\nimaginary scenes with which he had fed his spirit, until even that poor\r\nsustenance was exhausted.\r\n\r\nThe author needs great faith in his reader\xe2\x80\x99s sympathy; else he must\r\nhesitate to give details so minute, and incidents apparently so\r\ntrifling, as are essential to make up the idea of this garden-life. It\r\nwas the Eden of a thunder-smitten Adam, who had fled for refuge thither\r\nout of the same dreary and perilous wilderness into which the original\r\nAdam was expelled.\r\n\r\nOne of the available means of amusement, of which Ph\xc5\x93be made the most\r\nin Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s behalf, was that feathered society, the hens, a breed of\r\nwhom, as we have already said, was an immemorial heirloom in the\r\nPyncheon family. In compliance with a whim of Clifford, as it troubled\r\nhim to see them in confinement, they had been set at liberty, and now\r\nroamed at will about the garden; doing some little mischief, but\r\nhindered from escape by buildings on three sides, and the difficult\r\npeaks of a wooden fence on the other. They spent much of their abundant\r\nleisure on the margin of Maule\xe2\x80\x99s well, which was haunted by a kind of\r\nsnail, evidently a titbit to their palates; and the brackish water\r\nitself, however nauseous to the rest of the world, was so greatly\r\nesteemed by these fowls, that they might be seen tasting, turning up\r\ntheir heads, and smacking their bills, with precisely the air of\r\nwine-bibbers round a probationary cask. Their generally quiet, yet\r\noften brisk, and constantly diversified talk, one to another, or\r\nsometimes in soliloquy,\xe2\x80\x94as they scratched worms out of the rich, black\r\nsoil, or pecked at such plants as suited their taste,\xe2\x80\x94had such a\r\ndomestic tone, that it was almost a wonder why you could not establish\r\na regular interchange of ideas about household matters, human and\r\ngallinaceous. All hens are well worth studying for the piquancy and\r\nrich variety of their manners; but by no possibility can there have\r\nbeen other fowls of such odd appearance and deportment as these\r\nancestral ones. They probably embodied the traditionary peculiarities\r\nof their whole line of progenitors, derived through an unbroken\r\nsuccession of eggs; or else this individual Chanticleer and his two\r\nwives had grown to be humorists, and a little crack-brained withal, on\r\naccount of their solitary way of life, and out of sympathy for\r\nHepzibah, their lady-patroness.\r\n\r\nQueer, indeed, they looked! Chanticleer himself, though stalking on two\r\nstilt-like legs, with the dignity of interminable descent in all his\r\ngestures, was hardly bigger than an ordinary partridge; his two wives\r\nwere about the size of quails; and as for the one chicken, it looked\r\nsmall enough to be still in the egg, and, at the same time,\r\nsufficiently old, withered, wizened, and experienced, to have been\r\nfounder of the antiquated race. Instead of being the youngest of the\r\nfamily, it rather seemed to have aggregated into itself the ages, not\r\nonly of these living specimens of the breed, but of all its forefathers\r\nand foremothers, whose united excellences and oddities were squeezed\r\ninto its little body. Its mother evidently regarded it as the one\r\nchicken of the world, and as necessary, in fact, to the world\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncontinuance, or, at any rate, to the equilibrium of the present system\r\nof affairs, whether in church or state. No lesser sense of the infant\r\nfowl\xe2\x80\x99s importance could have justified, even in a mother\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, the\r\nperseverance with which she watched over its safety, ruffling her small\r\nperson to twice its proper size, and flying in everybody\xe2\x80\x99s face that so\r\nmuch as looked towards her hopeful progeny. No lower estimate could\r\nhave vindicated the indefatigable zeal with which she scratched, and\r\nher unscrupulousness in digging up the choicest flower or vegetable,\r\nfor the sake of the fat earthworm at its root. Her nervous cluck, when\r\nthe chicken happened to be hidden in the long grass or under the\r\nsquash-leaves; her gentle croak of satisfaction, while sure of it\r\nbeneath her wing; her note of ill-concealed fear and obstreperous\r\ndefiance, when she saw her arch-enemy, a neighbor\xe2\x80\x99s cat, on the top of\r\nthe high fence,\xe2\x80\x94one or other of these sounds was to be heard at almost\r\nevery moment of the day. By degrees, the observer came to feel nearly\r\nas much interest in this chicken of illustrious race as the mother-hen\r\ndid.\r\n\r\nPh\xc5\x93be, after getting well acquainted with the old hen, was sometimes\r\npermitted to take the chicken in her hand, which was quite capable of\r\ngrasping its cubic inch or two of body. While she curiously examined\r\nits hereditary marks,\xe2\x80\x94the peculiar speckle of its plumage, the funny\r\ntuft on its head, and a knob on each of its legs,\xe2\x80\x94the little biped, as\r\nshe insisted, kept giving her a sagacious wink. The daguerreotypist\r\nonce whispered her that these marks betokened the oddities of the\r\nPyncheon family, and that the chicken itself was a symbol of the life\r\nof the old house, embodying its interpretation, likewise, although an\r\nunintelligible one, as such clews generally are. It was a feathered\r\nriddle; a mystery hatched out of an egg, and just as mysterious as if\r\nthe egg had been addle!\r\n\r\nThe second of Chanticleer\xe2\x80\x99s two wives, ever since Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s arrival, had\r\nbeen in a state of heavy despondency, caused, as it afterwards\r\nappeared, by her inability to lay an egg. One day, however, by her\r\nself-important gait, the sideways turn of her head, and the cock of her\r\neye, as she pried into one and another nook of the garden,\xe2\x80\x94croaking to\r\nherself, all the while, with inexpressible complacency,\xe2\x80\x94it was made\r\nevident that this identical hen, much as mankind undervalued her,\r\ncarried something about her person the worth of which was not to be\r\nestimated either in gold or precious stones. Shortly after, there was a\r\nprodigious cackling and gratulation of Chanticleer and all his family,\r\nincluding the wizened chicken, who appeared to understand the matter\r\nquite as well as did his sire, his mother, or his aunt. That afternoon\r\nPh\xc5\x93be found a diminutive egg,\xe2\x80\x94not in the regular nest, it was far too\r\nprecious to be trusted there,\xe2\x80\x94but cunningly hidden under the\r\ncurrant-bushes, on some dry stalks of last year\xe2\x80\x99s grass. Hepzibah, on\r\nlearning the fact, took possession of the egg and appropriated it to\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s breakfast, on account of a certain delicacy of flavor, for\r\nwhich, as she affirmed, these eggs had always been famous. Thus\r\nunscrupulously did the old gentlewoman sacrifice the continuance,\r\nperhaps, of an ancient feathered race, with no better end than to\r\nsupply her brother with a dainty that hardly filled the bowl of a\r\ntea-spoon! It must have been in reference to this outrage that\r\nChanticleer, the next day, accompanied by the bereaved mother of the\r\negg, took his post in front of Ph\xc5\x93be and Clifford, and delivered\r\nhimself of a harangue that might have proved as long as his own\r\npedigree, but for a fit of merriment on Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s part. Hereupon, the\r\noffended fowl stalked away on his long stilts, and utterly withdrew his\r\nnotice from Ph\xc5\x93be and the rest of human nature, until she made her\r\npeace with an offering of spice-cake, which, next to snails, was the\r\ndelicacy most in favor with his aristocratic taste.\r\n\r\nWe linger too long, no doubt, beside this paltry rivulet of life that\r\nflowed through the garden of the Pyncheon House. But we deem it\r\npardonable to record these mean incidents and poor delights, because\r\nthey proved so greatly to Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s benefit. They had the earth-smell\r\nin them, and contributed to give him health and substance. Some of his\r\noccupations wrought less desirably upon him. He had a singular\r\npropensity, for example, to hang over Maule\xe2\x80\x99s well, and look at the\r\nconstantly shifting phantasmagoria of figures produced by the agitation\r\nof the water over the mosaic-work of colored pebbles at the bottom. He\r\nsaid that faces looked upward to him there,\xe2\x80\x94beautiful faces, arrayed in\r\nbewitching smiles,\xe2\x80\x94each momentary face so fair and rosy, and every\r\nsmile so sunny, that he felt wronged at its departure, until the same\r\nflitting witchcraft made a new one. But sometimes he would suddenly cry\r\nout, \xe2\x80\x9cThe dark face gazes at me!\xe2\x80\x9d and be miserable the whole day\r\nafterwards. Ph\xc5\x93be, when she hung over the fountain by Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s side,\r\ncould see nothing of all this,\xe2\x80\x94neither the beauty nor the ugliness,\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nonly the colored pebbles, looking as if the gush of the waters shook\r\nand disarranged them. And the dark face, that so troubled Clifford, was\r\nno more than the shadow thrown from a branch of one of the\r\ndamson-trees, and breaking the inner light of Maule\xe2\x80\x99s well. The truth\r\nwas, however, that his fancy\xe2\x80\x94reviving faster than his will and\r\njudgment, and always stronger than they\xe2\x80\x94created shapes of loveliness\r\nthat were symbolic of his native character, and now and then a stern\r\nand dreadful shape that typified his fate.\r\n\r\nOn Sundays, after Ph\xc5\x93be had been at church,\xe2\x80\x94for the girl had a\r\nchurch-going conscience, and would hardly have been at ease had she\r\nmissed either prayer, singing, sermon, or benediction,\xe2\x80\x94after\r\nchurch-time, therefore, there was, ordinarily, a sober little festival\r\nin the garden. In addition to Clifford, Hepzibah, and Ph\xc5\x93be, two guests\r\nmade up the company. One was the artist Holgrave, who, in spite of his\r\nconsociation with reformers, and his other queer and questionable\r\ntraits, continued to hold an elevated place in Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s regard. The\r\nother, we are almost ashamed to say, was the venerable Uncle Venner, in\r\na clean shirt, and a broadcloth coat, more respectable than his\r\nordinary wear, inasmuch as it was neatly patched on each elbow, and\r\nmight be called an entire garment, except for a slight inequality in\r\nthe length of its skirts. Clifford, on several occasions, had seemed to\r\nenjoy the old man\xe2\x80\x99s intercourse, for the sake of his mellow, cheerful\r\nvein, which was like the sweet flavor of a frost-bitten apple, such as\r\none picks up under the tree in December. A man at the very lowest point\r\nof the social scale was easier and more agreeable for the fallen\r\ngentleman to encounter than a person at any of the intermediate\r\ndegrees; and, moreover, as Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s young manhood had been lost, he\r\nwas fond of feeling himself comparatively youthful, now, in apposition\r\nwith the patriarchal age of Uncle Venner. In fact, it was sometimes\r\nobservable that Clifford half wilfully hid from himself the\r\nconsciousness of being stricken in years, and cherished visions of an\r\nearthly future still before him; visions, however, too indistinctly\r\ndrawn to be followed by disappointment\xe2\x80\x94though, doubtless, by\r\ndepression\xe2\x80\x94when any casual incident or recollection made him sensible\r\nof the withered leaf.\r\n\r\nSo this oddly composed little social party used to assemble under the\r\nruinous arbor. Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x94stately as ever at heart, and yielding not an\r\ninch of her old gentility, but resting upon it so much the more, as\r\njustifying a princess-like condescension\xe2\x80\x94exhibited a not ungraceful\r\nhospitality. She talked kindly to the vagrant artist, and took sage\r\ncounsel\xe2\x80\x94lady as she was\xe2\x80\x94with the wood-sawyer, the messenger of\r\neverybody\xe2\x80\x99s petty errands, the patched philosopher. And Uncle Venner,\r\nwho had studied the world at street-corners, and other posts equally\r\nwell adapted for just observation, was as ready to give out his wisdom\r\nas a town-pump to give water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Hepzibah, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d said he once, after they had all been cheerful\r\ntogether, \xe2\x80\x9cI really enjoy these quiet little meetings of a Sabbath\r\nafternoon. They are very much like what I expect to have after I retire\r\nto my farm!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle Venner\xe2\x80\x9d observed Clifford in a drowsy, inward tone, \xe2\x80\x9cis always\r\ntalking about his farm. But I have a better scheme for him, by and by.\r\nWe shall see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Mr. Clifford Pyncheon!\xe2\x80\x9d said the man of patches, \xe2\x80\x9cyou may scheme\r\nfor me as much as you please; but I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to give up this one\r\nscheme of my own, even if I never bring it really to pass. It does seem\r\nto me that men make a wonderful mistake in trying to heap up property\r\nupon property. If I had done so, I should feel as if Providence was not\r\nbound to take care of me; and, at all events, the city wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be! I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\none of those people who think that infinity is big enough for us\r\nall\xe2\x80\x94and eternity long enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, so they are, Uncle Venner,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Ph\xc5\x93be after a pause; for she\r\nhad been trying to fathom the profundity and appositeness of this\r\nconcluding apothegm. \xe2\x80\x9cBut for this short life of ours, one would like a\r\nhouse and a moderate garden-spot of one\xe2\x80\x99s own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt appears to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the daguerreotypist, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cthat Uncle\r\nVenner has the principles of Fourier at the bottom of his wisdom; only\r\nthey have not quite so much distinctness in his mind as in that of the\r\nsystematizing Frenchman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, \xe2\x80\x9cit is time to bring the currants.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then, while the yellow richness of the declining sunshine still\r\nfell into the open space of the garden, Ph\xc5\x93be brought out a loaf of\r\nbread and a china bowl of currants, freshly gathered from the bushes,\r\nand crushed with sugar. These, with water,\xe2\x80\x94but not from the fountain of\r\nill omen, close at hand,\xe2\x80\x94constituted all the entertainment. Meanwhile,\r\nHolgrave took some pains to establish an intercourse with Clifford,\r\nactuated, it might seem, entirely by an impulse of kindliness, in order\r\nthat the present hour might be cheerfuller than most which the poor\r\nrecluse had spent, or was destined yet to spend. Nevertheless, in the\r\nartist\xe2\x80\x99s deep, thoughtful, all-observant eyes, there was, now and then,\r\nan expression, not sinister, but questionable; as if he had some other\r\ninterest in the scene than a stranger, a youthful and unconnected\r\nadventurer, might be supposed to have. With great mobility of outward\r\nmood, however, he applied himself to the task of enlivening the party;\r\nand with so much success, that even dark-hued Hepzibah threw off one\r\ntint of melancholy, and made what shift she could with the remaining\r\nportion. Ph\xc5\x93be said to herself,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cHow pleasant he can be!\xe2\x80\x9d As for Uncle\r\nVenner, as a mark of friendship and approbation, he readily consented\r\nto afford the young man his countenance in the way of his\r\nprofession,\xe2\x80\x94not metaphorically, be it understood, but literally, by\r\nallowing a daguerreotype of his face, so familiar to the town, to be\r\nexhibited at the entrance of Holgrave\xe2\x80\x99s studio.\r\n\r\nClifford, as the company partook of their little banquet, grew to be\r\nthe gayest of them all. Either it was one of those up-quivering flashes\r\nof the spirit, to which minds in an abnormal state are liable, or else\r\nthe artist had subtly touched some chord that made musical vibration.\r\nIndeed, what with the pleasant summer evening, and the sympathy of this\r\nlittle circle of not unkindly souls, it was perhaps natural that a\r\ncharacter so susceptible as Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s should become animated, and show\r\nitself readily responsive to what was said around him. But he gave out\r\nhis own thoughts, likewise, with an airy and fanciful glow; so that\r\nthey glistened, as it were, through the arbor, and made their escape\r\namong the interstices of the foliage. He had been as cheerful, no\r\ndoubt, while alone with Ph\xc5\x93be, but never with such tokens of acute,\r\nalthough partial intelligence.\r\n\r\nBut, as the sunlight left the peaks of the Seven Gables, so did the\r\nexcitement fade out of Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s eyes. He gazed vaguely and mournfully\r\nabout him, as if he missed something precious, and missed it the more\r\ndrearily for not knowing precisely what it was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want my happiness!\xe2\x80\x9d at last he murmured hoarsely and indistinctly,\r\nhardly shaping out the words. \xe2\x80\x9cMany, many years have I waited for it!\r\nIt is late! It is late! I want my happiness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlas, poor Clifford! You are old, and worn with troubles that ought\r\nnever to have befallen you. You are partly crazy and partly imbecile; a\r\nruin, a failure, as almost everybody is,\xe2\x80\x94though some in less degree, or\r\nless perceptibly, than their fellows. Fate has no happiness in store\r\nfor you; unless your quiet home in the old family residence with the\r\nfaithful Hepzibah, and your long summer afternoons with Ph\xc5\x93be, and\r\nthese Sabbath festivals with Uncle Venner and the daguerreotypist,\r\ndeserve to be called happiness! Why not? If not the thing itself, it is\r\nmarvellously like it, and the more so for that ethereal and intangible\r\nquality which causes it all to vanish at too close an introspection.\r\nTake it, therefore, while you may. Murmur not,\xe2\x80\x94question not,\xe2\x80\x94but make\r\nthe most of it!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXI.\r\nThe Arched Window\r\n\r\n\r\nFrom the inertness, or what we may term the vegetative character, of\r\nhis ordinary mood, Clifford would perhaps have been content to spend\r\none day after another, interminably,\xe2\x80\x94or, at least, throughout the\r\nsummer-time,\xe2\x80\x94in just the kind of life described in the preceding pages.\r\nFancying, however, that it might be for his benefit occasionally to\r\ndiversify the scene, Ph\xc5\x93be sometimes suggested that he should look out\r\nupon the life of the street. For this purpose, they used to mount the\r\nstaircase together, to the second story of the house, where, at the\r\ntermination of a wide entry, there was an arched window, of uncommonly\r\nlarge dimensions, shaded by a pair of curtains. It opened above the\r\nporch, where there had formerly been a balcony, the balustrade of which\r\nhad long since gone to decay, and been removed. At this arched window,\r\nthrowing it open, but keeping himself in comparative obscurity by means\r\nof the curtain, Clifford had an opportunity of witnessing such a\r\nportion of the great world\xe2\x80\x99s movement as might be supposed to roll\r\nthrough one of the retired streets of a not very populous city. But he\r\nand Ph\xc5\x93be made a sight as well worth seeing as any that the city could\r\nexhibit. The pale, gray, childish, aged, melancholy, yet often simply\r\ncheerful, and sometimes delicately intelligent aspect of Clifford,\r\npeering from behind the faded crimson of the curtain,\xe2\x80\x94watching the\r\nmonotony of every-day occurrences with a kind of inconsequential\r\ninterest and earnestness, and, at every petty throb of his sensibility,\r\nturning for sympathy to the eyes of the bright young girl!\r\n\r\nIf once he were fairly seated at the window, even Pyncheon Street would\r\nhardly be so dull and lonely but that, somewhere or other along its\r\nextent, Clifford might discover matter to occupy his eye, and\r\ntitillate, if not engross, his observation. Things familiar to the\r\nyoungest child that had begun its outlook at existence seemed strange\r\nto him. A cab; an omnibus, with its populous interior, dropping here\r\nand there a passenger, and picking up another, and thus typifying that\r\nvast rolling vehicle, the world, the end of whose journey is everywhere\r\nand nowhere; these objects he followed eagerly with his eyes, but\r\nforgot them before the dust raised by the horses and wheels had settled\r\nalong their track. As regarded novelties (among which cabs and\r\nomnibuses were to be reckoned), his mind appeared to have lost its\r\nproper gripe and retentiveness. Twice or thrice, for example, during\r\nthe sunny hours of the day, a water-cart went along by the Pyncheon\r\nHouse, leaving a broad wake of moistened earth, instead of the white\r\ndust that had risen at a lady\xe2\x80\x99s lightest footfall; it was like a summer\r\nshower, which the city authorities had caught and tamed, and compelled\r\nit into the commonest routine of their convenience. With the water-cart\r\nClifford could never grow familiar; it always affected him with just\r\nthe same surprise as at first. His mind took an apparently sharp\r\nimpression from it, but lost the recollection of this perambulatory\r\nshower, before its next reappearance, as completely as did the street\r\nitself, along which the heat so quickly strewed white dust again. It\r\nwas the same with the railroad. Clifford could hear the obstreperous\r\nhowl of the steam-devil, and, by leaning a little way from the arched\r\nwindow, could catch a glimpse of the trains of cars, flashing a brief\r\ntransit across the extremity of the street. The idea of terrible energy\r\nthus forced upon him was new at every recurrence, and seemed to affect\r\nhim as disagreeably, and with almost as much surprise, the hundredth\r\ntime as the first.\r\n\r\nNothing gives a sadder sense of decay than this loss or suspension of\r\nthe power to deal with unaccustomed things, and to keep up with the\r\nswiftness of the passing moment. It can merely be a suspended\r\nanimation; for, were the power actually to perish, there would be\r\nlittle use of immortality. We are less than ghosts, for the time being,\r\nwhenever this calamity befalls us.\r\n\r\nClifford was indeed the most inveterate of conservatives. All the\r\nantique fashions of the street were dear to him; even such as were\r\ncharacterized by a rudeness that would naturally have annoyed his\r\nfastidious senses. He loved the old rumbling and jolting carts, the\r\nformer track of which he still found in his long-buried remembrance, as\r\nthe observer of to-day finds the wheel-tracks of ancient vehicles in\r\nHerculaneum. The butcher\xe2\x80\x99s cart, with its snowy canopy, was an\r\nacceptable object; so was the fish-cart, heralded by its horn; so,\r\nlikewise, was the countryman\xe2\x80\x99s cart of vegetables, plodding from door\r\nto door, with long pauses of the patient horse, while his owner drove a\r\ntrade in turnips, carrots, summer-squashes, string-beans, green peas,\r\nand new potatoes, with half the housewives of the neighborhood. The\r\nbaker\xe2\x80\x99s cart, with the harsh music of its bells, had a pleasant effect\r\non Clifford, because, as few things else did, it jingled the very\r\ndissonance of yore. One afternoon a scissor-grinder chanced to set his\r\nwheel a-going under the Pyncheon Elm, and just in front of the arched\r\nwindow. Children came running with their mothers\xe2\x80\x99 scissors, or the\r\ncarving-knife, or the paternal razor, or anything else that lacked an\r\nedge (except, indeed, poor Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s wits), that the grinder might\r\napply the article to his magic wheel, and give it back as good as new.\r\nRound went the busily revolving machinery, kept in motion by the\r\nscissor-grinder\xe2\x80\x99s foot, and wore away the hard steel against the hard\r\nstone, whence issued an intense and spiteful prolongation of a hiss as\r\nfierce as those emitted by Satan and his compeers in Pandemonium,\r\nthough squeezed into smaller compass. It was an ugly, little, venomous\r\nserpent of a noise, as ever did petty violence to human ears. But\r\nClifford listened with rapturous delight. The sound, however\r\ndisagreeable, had very brisk life in it, and, together with the circle\r\nof curious children watching the revolutions of the wheel, appeared to\r\ngive him a more vivid sense of active, bustling, and sunshiny existence\r\nthan he had attained in almost any other way. Nevertheless, its charm\r\nlay chiefly in the past; for the scissor-grinder\xe2\x80\x99s wheel had hissed in\r\nhis childish ears.\r\n\r\nHe sometimes made doleful complaint that there were no stage-coaches\r\nnowadays. And he asked in an injured tone what had become of all those\r\nold square-topped chaises, with wings sticking out on either side, that\r\nused to be drawn by a plough-horse, and driven by a farmer\xe2\x80\x99s wife and\r\ndaughter, peddling whortle-berries and blackberries about the town.\r\nTheir disappearance made him doubt, he said, whether the berries had\r\nnot left off growing in the broad pastures and along the shady country\r\nlanes.\r\n\r\nBut anything that appealed to the sense of beauty, in however humble a\r\nway, did not require to be recommended by these old associations. This\r\nwas observable when one of those Italian boys (who are rather a modern\r\nfeature of our streets) came along with his barrel-organ, and stopped\r\nunder the wide and cool shadows of the elm. With his quick professional\r\neye he took note of the two faces watching him from the arched window,\r\nand, opening his instrument, began to scatter its melodies abroad. He\r\nhad a monkey on his shoulder, dressed in a Highland plaid; and, to\r\ncomplete the sum of splendid attractions wherewith he presented himself\r\nto the public, there was a company of little figures, whose sphere and\r\nhabitation was in the mahogany case of his organ, and whose principle\r\nof life was the music which the Italian made it his business to grind\r\nout. In all their variety of occupation,\xe2\x80\x94the cobbler, the blacksmith,\r\nthe soldier, the lady with her fan, the toper with his bottle, the\r\nmilk-maid sitting by her cow\xe2\x80\x94this fortunate little society might truly\r\nbe said to enjoy a harmonious existence, and to make life literally a\r\ndance. The Italian turned a crank; and, behold! every one of these\r\nsmall individuals started into the most curious vivacity. The cobbler\r\nwrought upon a shoe; the blacksmith hammered his iron, the soldier\r\nwaved his glittering blade; the lady raised a tiny breeze with her fan;\r\nthe jolly toper swigged lustily at his bottle; a scholar opened his\r\nbook with eager thirst for knowledge, and turned his head to and fro\r\nalong the page; the milkmaid energetically drained her cow; and a miser\r\ncounted gold into his strong-box,\xe2\x80\x94all at the same turning of a crank.\r\nYes; and, moved by the self-same impulse, a lover saluted his mistress\r\non her lips! Possibly some cynic, at once merry and bitter, had desired\r\nto signify, in this pantomimic scene, that we mortals, whatever our\r\nbusiness or amusement,\xe2\x80\x94however serious, however trifling,\xe2\x80\x94all dance to\r\none identical tune, and, in spite of our ridiculous activity, bring\r\nnothing finally to pass. For the most remarkable aspect of the affair\r\nwas, that, at the cessation of the music, everybody was petrified at\r\nonce, from the most extravagant life into a dead torpor. Neither was\r\nthe cobbler\xe2\x80\x99s shoe finished, nor the blacksmith\xe2\x80\x99s iron shaped out; nor\r\nwas there a drop less of brandy in the toper\xe2\x80\x99s bottle, nor a drop more\r\nof milk in the milkmaid\xe2\x80\x99s pail, nor one additional coin in the miser\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nstrong-box, nor was the scholar a page deeper in his book. All were\r\nprecisely in the same condition as before they made themselves so\r\nridiculous by their haste to toil, to enjoy, to accumulate gold, and to\r\nbecome wise. Saddest of all, moreover, the lover was none the happier\r\nfor the maiden\xe2\x80\x99s granted kiss! But, rather than swallow this last too\r\nacrid ingredient, we reject the whole moral of the show.\r\n\r\nThe monkey, meanwhile, with a thick tail curling out into preposterous\r\nprolixity from beneath his tartans, took his station at the Italian\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfeet. He turned a wrinkled and abominable little visage to every\r\npasser-by, and to the circle of children that soon gathered round, and\r\nto Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s shop-door, and upward to the arched window, whence Ph\xc5\x93be\r\nand Clifford were looking down. Every moment, also, he took off his\r\nHighland bonnet, and performed a bow and scrape. Sometimes, moreover,\r\nhe made personal application to individuals, holding out his small\r\nblack palm, and otherwise plainly signifying his excessive desire for\r\nwhatever filthy lucre might happen to be in anybody\xe2\x80\x99s pocket. The mean\r\nand low, yet strangely man-like expression of his wilted countenance;\r\nthe prying and crafty glance, that showed him ready to gripe at every\r\nmiserable advantage; his enormous tail (too enormous to be decently\r\nconcealed under his gabardine), and the deviltry of nature which it\r\nbetokened,\xe2\x80\x94take this monkey just as he was, in short, and you could\r\ndesire no better image of the Mammon of copper coin, symbolizing the\r\ngrossest form of the love of money. Neither was there any possibility\r\nof satisfying the covetous little devil. Ph\xc5\x93be threw down a whole\r\nhandful of cents, which he picked up with joyless eagerness, handed\r\nthem over to the Italian for safekeeping, and immediately recommenced a\r\nseries of pantomimic petitions for more.\r\n\r\nDoubtless, more than one New-Englander\xe2\x80\x94or, let him be of what country\r\nhe might, it is as likely to be the case\xe2\x80\x94passed by, and threw a look at\r\nthe monkey, and went on, without imagining how nearly his own moral\r\ncondition was here exemplified. Clifford, however, was a being of\r\nanother order. He had taken childish delight in the music, and smiled,\r\ntoo, at the figures which it set in motion. But, after looking awhile\r\nat the long-tailed imp, he was so shocked by his horrible ugliness,\r\nspiritual as well as physical, that he actually began to shed tears; a\r\nweakness which men of merely delicate endowments, and destitute of the\r\nfiercer, deeper, and more tragic power of laughter, can hardly avoid,\r\nwhen the worst and meanest aspect of life happens to be presented to\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nPyncheon Street was sometimes enlivened by spectacles of more imposing\r\npretensions than the above, and which brought the multitude along with\r\nthem. With a shivering repugnance at the idea of personal contact with\r\nthe world, a powerful impulse still seized on Clifford, whenever the\r\nrush and roar of the human tide grew strongly audible to him. This was\r\nmade evident, one day, when a political procession, with hundreds of\r\nflaunting banners, and drums, fifes, clarions, and cymbals,\r\nreverberating between the rows of buildings, marched all through town,\r\nand trailed its length of trampling footsteps, and most infrequent\r\nuproar, past the ordinarily quiet House of the Seven Gables. As a mere\r\nobject of sight, nothing is more deficient in picturesque features than\r\na procession seen in its passage through narrow streets. The spectator\r\nfeels it to be fool\xe2\x80\x99s play, when he can distinguish the tedious\r\ncommonplace of each man\xe2\x80\x99s visage, with the perspiration and weary\r\nself-importance on it, and the very cut of his pantaloons, and the\r\nstiffness or laxity of his shirt-collar, and the dust on the back of\r\nhis black coat. In order to become majestic, it should be viewed from\r\nsome vantage point, as it rolls its slow and long array through the\r\ncentre of a wide plain, or the stateliest public square of a city; for\r\nthen, by its remoteness, it melts all the petty personalities, of which\r\nit is made up, into one broad mass of existence,\xe2\x80\x94one great life,\xe2\x80\x94one\r\ncollected body of mankind, with a vast, homogeneous spirit animating\r\nit. But, on the other hand, if an impressible person, standing alone\r\nover the brink of one of these processions, should behold it, not in\r\nits atoms, but in its aggregate,\xe2\x80\x94as a mighty river of life, massive in\r\nits tide, and black with mystery, and, out of its depths, calling to\r\nthe kindred depth within him,\xe2\x80\x94then the contiguity would add to the\r\neffect. It might so fascinate him that he would hardly be restrained\r\nfrom plunging into the surging stream of human sympathies.\r\n\r\nSo it proved with Clifford. He shuddered; he grew pale; he threw an\r\nappealing look at Hepzibah and Ph\xc5\x93be, who were with him at the window.\r\nThey comprehended nothing of his emotions, and supposed him merely\r\ndisturbed by the unaccustomed tumult. At last, with tremulous limbs, he\r\nstarted up, set his foot on the window-sill, and in an instant more\r\nwould have been in the unguarded balcony. As it was, the whole\r\nprocession might have seen him, a wild, haggard figure, his gray locks\r\nfloating in the wind that waved their banners; a lonely being,\r\nestranged from his race, but now feeling himself man again, by virtue\r\nof the irrepressible instinct that possessed him. Had Clifford attained\r\nthe balcony, he would probably have leaped into the street; but whether\r\nimpelled by the species of terror that sometimes urges its victim over\r\nthe very precipice which he shrinks from, or by a natural magnetism,\r\ntending towards the great centre of humanity, it were not easy to\r\ndecide. Both impulses might have wrought on him at once.\r\n\r\nBut his companions, affrighted by his gesture,\xe2\x80\x94which was that of a man\r\nhurried away in spite of himself,\xe2\x80\x94seized Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s garment and held\r\nhim back. Hepzibah shrieked. Ph\xc5\x93be, to whom all extravagance was a\r\nhorror, burst into sobs and tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClifford, Clifford! are you crazy?\xe2\x80\x9d cried his sister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hardly know, Hepzibah,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, drawing a long breath. \xe2\x80\x9cFear\r\nnothing,\xe2\x80\x94it is over now,\xe2\x80\x94but had I taken that plunge, and survived it,\r\nmethinks it would have made me another man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPossibly, in some sense, Clifford may have been right. He needed a\r\nshock; or perhaps he required to take a deep, deep plunge into the\r\nocean of human life, and to sink down and be covered by its\r\nprofoundness, and then to emerge, sobered, invigorated, restored to the\r\nworld and to himself. Perhaps again, he required nothing less than the\r\ngreat final remedy\xe2\x80\x94death!\r\n\r\nA similar yearning to renew the broken links of brotherhood with his\r\nkind sometimes showed itself in a milder form; and once it was made\r\nbeautiful by the religion that lay even deeper than itself. In the\r\nincident now to be sketched, there was a touching recognition, on\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s part, of God\xe2\x80\x99s care and love towards him,\xe2\x80\x94towards this poor,\r\nforsaken man, who, if any mortal could, might have been pardoned for\r\nregarding himself as thrown aside, forgotten, and left to be the sport\r\nof some fiend, whose playfulness was an ecstasy of mischief.\r\n\r\nIt was the Sabbath morning; one of those bright, calm Sabbaths, with\r\nits own hallowed atmosphere, when Heaven seems to diffuse itself over\r\nthe earth\xe2\x80\x99s face in a solemn smile, no less sweet than solemn. On such\r\na Sabbath morn, were we pure enough to be its medium, we should be\r\nconscious of the earth\xe2\x80\x99s natural worship ascending through our frames,\r\non whatever spot of ground we stood. The church-bells, with various\r\ntones, but all in harmony, were calling out and responding to one\r\nanother,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the Sabbath!\xe2\x80\x94The Sabbath!\xe2\x80\x94Yea; the Sabbath!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and over\r\nthe whole city the bells scattered the blessed sounds, now slowly, now\r\nwith livelier joy, now one bell alone, now all the bells together,\r\ncrying earnestly,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the Sabbath!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and flinging their accents afar\r\noff, to melt into the air and pervade it with the holy word. The air\r\nwith God\xe2\x80\x99s sweetest and tenderest sunshine in it, was meet for mankind\r\nto breathe into their hearts, and send it forth again as the utterance\r\nof prayer.\r\n\r\nClifford sat at the window with Hepzibah, watching the neighbors as\r\nthey stepped into the street. All of them, however unspiritual on other\r\ndays, were transfigured by the Sabbath influence; so that their very\r\ngarments\xe2\x80\x94whether it were an old man\xe2\x80\x99s decent coat well brushed for the\r\nthousandth time, or a little boy\xe2\x80\x99s first sack and trousers finished\r\nyesterday by his mother\xe2\x80\x99s needle\xe2\x80\x94had somewhat of the quality of\r\nascension-robes. Forth, likewise, from the portal of the old house\r\nstepped Ph\xc5\x93be, putting up her small green sunshade, and throwing upward\r\na glance and smile of parting kindness to the faces at the arched\r\nwindow. In her aspect there was a familiar gladness, and a holiness\r\nthat you could play with, and yet reverence it as much as ever. She was\r\nlike a prayer, offered up in the homeliest beauty of one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmother-tongue. Fresh was Ph\xc5\x93be, moreover, and airy and sweet in her\r\napparel; as if nothing that she wore\xe2\x80\x94neither her gown, nor her small\r\nstraw bonnet, nor her little kerchief, any more than her snowy\r\nstockings\xe2\x80\x94had ever been put on before; or, if worn, were all the\r\nfresher for it, and with a fragrance as if they had lain among the\r\nrosebuds.\r\n\r\nThe girl waved her hand to Hepzibah and Clifford, and went up the\r\nstreet; a religion in herself, warm, simple, true, with a substance\r\nthat could walk on earth, and a spirit that was capable of heaven.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHepzibah,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Clifford, after watching Ph\xc5\x93be to the corner, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you\r\nnever go to church?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Clifford!\xe2\x80\x9d she replied,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cnot these many, many years!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere I to be there,\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined, \xe2\x80\x9cit seems to me that I could pray\r\nonce more, when so many human souls were praying all around me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked into Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s face, and beheld there a soft natural\r\neffusion; for his heart gushed out, as it were, and ran over at his\r\neyes, in delightful reverence for God, and kindly affection for his\r\nhuman brethren. The emotion communicated itself to Hepzibah. She\r\nyearned to take him by the hand, and go and kneel down, they two\r\ntogether,\xe2\x80\x94both so long separate from the world, and, as she now\r\nrecognized, scarcely friends with Him above,\xe2\x80\x94to kneel down among the\r\npeople, and be reconciled to God and man at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said she earnestly, \xe2\x80\x9clet us go! We belong nowhere. We\r\nhave not a foot of space in any church to kneel upon; but let us go to\r\nsome place of worship, even if we stand in the broad aisle. Poor and\r\nforsaken as we are, some pew-door will be opened to us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Hepzibah and her brother made themselves, ready\xe2\x80\x94as ready as they\r\ncould in the best of their old-fashioned garments, which had hung on\r\npegs, or been laid away in trunks, so long that the dampness and mouldy\r\nsmell of the past was on them,\xe2\x80\x94made themselves ready, in their faded\r\nbettermost, to go to church. They descended the staircase\r\ntogether,\xe2\x80\x94gaunt, sallow Hepzibah, and pale, emaciated, age-stricken\r\nClifford! They pulled open the front door, and stepped across the\r\nthreshold, and felt, both of them, as if they were standing in the\r\npresence of the whole world, and with mankind\xe2\x80\x99s great and terrible eye\r\non them alone. The eye of their Father seemed to be withdrawn, and gave\r\nthem no encouragement. The warm sunny air of the street made them\r\nshiver. Their hearts quaked within them at the idea of taking one step\r\nfarther.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt cannot be, Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x94it is too late,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford with deep\r\nsadness. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are ghosts! We have no right among human beings,\xe2\x80\x94no right\r\nanywhere but in this old house, which has a curse on it, and which,\r\ntherefore, we are doomed to haunt! And, besides,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, with a\r\nfastidious sensibility, inalienably characteristic of the man, \xe2\x80\x9cit\r\nwould not be fit nor beautiful to go! It is an ugly thought that I\r\nshould be frightful to my fellow-beings, and that children would cling\r\nto their mothers\xe2\x80\x99 gowns at sight of me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey shrank back into the dusky passage-way, and closed the door. But,\r\ngoing up the staircase again, they found the whole interior of the\r\nhouse tenfold more dismal, and the air closer and heavier, for the\r\nglimpse and breath of freedom which they had just snatched. They could\r\nnot flee; their jailer had but left the door ajar in mockery, and stood\r\nbehind it to watch them stealing out. At the threshold, they felt his\r\npitiless gripe upon them. For, what other dungeon is so dark as one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nown heart! What jailer so inexorable as one\xe2\x80\x99s self!\r\n\r\nBut it would be no fair picture of Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s state of mind were we to\r\nrepresent him as continually or prevailingly wretched. On the contrary,\r\nthere was no other man in the city, we are bold to affirm, of so much\r\nas half his years, who enjoyed so many lightsome and griefless moments\r\nas himself. He had no burden of care upon him; there were none of those\r\nquestions and contingencies with the future to be settled which wear\r\naway all other lives, and render them not worth having by the very\r\nprocess of providing for their support. In this respect he was a\r\nchild,\xe2\x80\x94a child for the whole term of his existence, be it long or\r\nshort. Indeed, his life seemed to be standing still at a period little\r\nin advance of childhood, and to cluster all his reminiscences about\r\nthat epoch; just as, after the torpor of a heavy blow, the sufferer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreviving consciousness goes back to a moment considerably behind the\r\naccident that stupefied him. He sometimes told Ph\xc5\x93be and Hepzibah his\r\ndreams, in which he invariably played the part of a child, or a very\r\nyoung man. So vivid were they, in his relation of them, that he once\r\nheld a dispute with his sister as to the particular figure or print of\r\na chintz morning-dress which he had seen their mother wear, in the\r\ndream of the preceding night. Hepzibah, piquing herself on a woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naccuracy in such matters, held it to be slightly different from what\r\nClifford described; but, producing the very gown from an old trunk, it\r\nproved to be identical with his remembrance of it. Had Clifford, every\r\ntime that he emerged out of dreams so lifelike, undergone the torture\r\nof transformation from a boy into an old and broken man, the daily\r\nrecurrence of the shock would have been too much to bear. It would have\r\ncaused an acute agony to thrill from the morning twilight, all the day\r\nthrough, until bedtime; and even then would have mingled a dull,\r\ninscrutable pain and pallid hue of misfortune with the visionary bloom\r\nand adolescence of his slumber. But the nightly moonshine interwove\r\nitself with the morning mist, and enveloped him as in a robe, which he\r\nhugged about his person, and seldom let realities pierce through; he\r\nwas not often quite awake, but slept open-eyed, and perhaps fancied\r\nhimself most dreaming then.\r\n\r\nThus, lingering always so near his childhood, he had sympathies with\r\nchildren, and kept his heart the fresher thereby, like a reservoir into\r\nwhich rivulets were pouring not far from the fountain-head. Though\r\nprevented, by a subtile sense of propriety, from desiring to associate\r\nwith them, he loved few things better than to look out of the arched\r\nwindow and see a little girl driving her hoop along the sidewalk, or\r\nschoolboys at a game of ball. Their voices, also, were very pleasant to\r\nhim, heard at a distance, all swarming and intermingling together as\r\nflies do in a sunny room.\r\n\r\nClifford would, doubtless, have been glad to share their sports. One\r\nafternoon he was seized with an irresistible desire to blow\r\nsoap-bubbles; an amusement, as Hepzibah told Ph\xc5\x93be apart, that had been\r\na favorite one with her brother when they were both children. Behold\r\nhim, therefore, at the arched window, with an earthen pipe in his\r\nmouth! Behold him, with his gray hair, and a wan, unreal smile over his\r\ncountenance, where still hovered a beautiful grace, which his worst\r\nenemy must have acknowledged to be spiritual and immortal, since it had\r\nsurvived so long! Behold him, scattering airy spheres abroad from the\r\nwindow into the street! Little impalpable worlds were those\r\nsoap-bubbles, with the big world depicted, in hues bright as\r\nimagination, on the nothing of their surface. It was curious to see how\r\nthe passers-by regarded these brilliant fantasies, as they came\r\nfloating down, and made the dull atmosphere imaginative about them.\r\nSome stopped to gaze, and perhaps, carried a pleasant recollection of\r\nthe bubbles onward as far as the street-corner; some looked angrily\r\nupward, as if poor Clifford wronged them by setting an image of beauty\r\nafloat so near their dusty pathway. A great many put out their fingers\r\nor their walking-sticks to touch, withal; and were perversely\r\ngratified, no doubt, when the bubble, with all its pictured earth and\r\nsky scene, vanished as if it had never been.\r\n\r\nAt length, just as an elderly gentleman of very dignified presence\r\nhappened to be passing, a large bubble sailed majestically down, and\r\nburst right against his nose! He looked up,\xe2\x80\x94at first with a stern, keen\r\nglance, which penetrated at once into the obscurity behind the arched\r\nwindow,\xe2\x80\x94then with a smile which might be conceived as diffusing a\r\ndog-day sultriness for the space of several yards about him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha, Cousin Clifford!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Judge Pyncheon. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat! Still blowing\r\nsoap-bubbles!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe tone seemed as if meant to be kind and soothing, but yet had a\r\nbitterness of sarcasm in it. As for Clifford, an absolute palsy of fear\r\ncame over him. Apart from any definite cause of dread which his past\r\nexperience might have given him, he felt that native and original\r\nhorror of the excellent Judge which is proper to a weak, delicate, and\r\napprehensive character in the presence of massive strength. Strength is\r\nincomprehensible by weakness, and, therefore, the more terrible. There\r\nis no greater bugbear than a strong-willed relative in the circle of\r\nhis own connections.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXII.\r\nThe Daguerreotypist\r\n\r\n\r\nIt must not be supposed that the life of a personage naturally so\r\nactive as Ph\xc5\x93be could be wholly confined within the precincts of the\r\nold Pyncheon House. Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s demands upon her time were usually\r\nsatisfied, in those long days, considerably earlier than sunset. Quiet\r\nas his daily existence seemed, it nevertheless drained all the\r\nresources by which he lived. It was not physical exercise that\r\noverwearied him,\xe2\x80\x94for except that he sometimes wrought a little with a\r\nhoe, or paced the garden-walk, or, in rainy weather, traversed a large\r\nunoccupied room,\xe2\x80\x94it was his tendency to remain only too quiescent, as\r\nregarded any toil of the limbs and muscles. But, either there was a\r\nsmouldering fire within him that consumed his vital energy, or the\r\nmonotony that would have dragged itself with benumbing effect over a\r\nmind differently situated was no monotony to Clifford. Possibly, he was\r\nin a state of second growth and recovery, and was constantly\r\nassimilating nutriment for his spirit and intellect from sights,\r\nsounds, and events which passed as a perfect void to persons more\r\npractised with the world. As all is activity and vicissitude to the new\r\nmind of a child, so might it be, likewise, to a mind that had undergone\r\na kind of new creation, after its long-suspended life.\r\n\r\nBe the cause what it might, Clifford commonly retired to rest,\r\nthoroughly exhausted, while the sunbeams were still melting through his\r\nwindow-curtains, or were thrown with late lustre on the chamber wall.\r\nAnd while he thus slept early, as other children do, and dreamed of\r\nchildhood, Ph\xc5\x93be was free to follow her own tastes for the remainder of\r\nthe day and evening.\r\n\r\nThis was a freedom essential to the health even of a character so\r\nlittle susceptible of morbid influences as that of Ph\xc5\x93be. The old\r\nhouse, as we have already said, had both the dry-rot and the damp-rot\r\nin its walls; it was not good to breathe no other atmosphere than that.\r\nHepzibah, though she had her valuable and redeeming traits, had grown\r\nto be a kind of lunatic by imprisoning herself so long in one place,\r\nwith no other company than a single series of ideas, and but one\r\naffection, and one bitter sense of wrong. Clifford, the reader may\r\nperhaps imagine, was too inert to operate morally on his\r\nfellow-creatures, however intimate and exclusive their relations with\r\nhim. But the sympathy or magnetism among human beings is more subtile\r\nand universal than we think; it exists, indeed, among different classes\r\nof organized life, and vibrates from one to another. A flower, for\r\ninstance, as Ph\xc5\x93be herself observed, always began to droop sooner in\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s hand, or Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s, than in her own; and by the same law,\r\nconverting her whole daily life into a flower fragrance for these two\r\nsickly spirits, the blooming girl must inevitably droop and fade much\r\nsooner than if worn on a younger and happier breast. Unless she had now\r\nand then indulged her brisk impulses, and breathed rural air in a\r\nsuburban walk, or ocean breezes along the shore,\xe2\x80\x94had occasionally\r\nobeyed the impulse of Nature, in New England girls, by attending a\r\nmetaphysical or philosophical lecture, or viewing a seven-mile\r\npanorama, or listening to a concert,\xe2\x80\x94had gone shopping about the city,\r\nransacking entire depots of splendid merchandise, and bringing home a\r\nribbon,\xe2\x80\x94had employed, likewise, a little time to read the Bible in her\r\nchamber, and had stolen a little more to think of her mother and her\r\nnative place\xe2\x80\x94unless for such moral medicines as the above, we should\r\nsoon have beheld our poor Ph\xc5\x93be grow thin and put on a bleached,\r\nunwholesome aspect, and assume strange, shy ways, prophetic of\r\nold-maidenhood and a cheerless future.\r\n\r\nEven as it was, a change grew visible; a change partly to be regretted,\r\nalthough whatever charm it infringed upon was repaired by another,\r\nperhaps more precious. She was not so constantly gay, but had her moods\r\nof thought, which Clifford, on the whole, liked better than her former\r\nphase of unmingled cheerfulness; because now she understood him better\r\nand more delicately, and sometimes even interpreted him to himself. Her\r\neyes looked larger, and darker, and deeper; so deep, at some silent\r\nmoments, that they seemed like Artesian wells, down, down, into the\r\ninfinite. She was less girlish than when we first beheld her alighting\r\nfrom the omnibus; less girlish, but more a woman.\r\n\r\nThe only youthful mind with which Ph\xc5\x93be had an opportunity of frequent\r\nintercourse was that of the daguerreotypist. Inevitably, by the\r\npressure of the seclusion about them, they had been brought into habits\r\nof some familiarity. Had they met under different circumstances,\r\nneither of these young persons would have been likely to bestow much\r\nthought upon the other, unless, indeed, their extreme dissimilarity\r\nshould have proved a principle of mutual attraction. Both, it is true,\r\nwere characters proper to New England life, and possessing a common\r\nground, therefore, in their more external developments; but as unlike,\r\nin their respective interiors, as if their native climes had been at\r\nworld-wide distance. During the early part of their acquaintance, Ph\xc5\x93be\r\nhad held back rather more than was customary with her frank and simple\r\nmanners from Holgrave\xe2\x80\x99s not very marked advances. Nor was she yet\r\nsatisfied that she knew him well, although they almost daily met and\r\ntalked together, in a kind, friendly, and what seemed to be a familiar\r\nway.\r\n\r\nThe artist, in a desultory manner, had imparted to Ph\xc5\x93be something of\r\nhis history. Young as he was, and had his career terminated at the\r\npoint already attained, there had been enough of incident to fill, very\r\ncreditably, an autobiographic volume. A romance on the plan of Gil\r\nBlas, adapted to American society and manners, would cease to be a\r\nromance. The experience of many individuals among us, who think it\r\nhardly worth the telling, would equal the vicissitudes of the\r\nSpaniard\xe2\x80\x99s earlier life; while their ultimate success, or the point\r\nwhither they tend, may be incomparably higher than any that a novelist\r\nwould imagine for his hero. Holgrave, as he told Ph\xc5\x93be somewhat\r\nproudly, could not boast of his origin, unless as being exceedingly\r\nhumble, nor of his education, except that it had been the scantiest\r\npossible, and obtained by a few winter-months\xe2\x80\x99 attendance at a district\r\nschool. Left early to his own guidance, he had begun to be\r\nself-dependent while yet a boy; and it was a condition aptly suited to\r\nhis natural force of will. Though now but twenty-two years old (lacking\r\nsome months, which are years in such a life), he had already been,\r\nfirst, a country schoolmaster; next, a salesman in a country store;\r\nand, either at the same time or afterwards, the political editor of a\r\ncountry newspaper. He had subsequently travelled New England and the\r\nMiddle States, as a peddler, in the employment of a Connecticut\r\nmanufactory of cologne-water and other essences. In an episodical way\r\nhe had studied and practised dentistry, and with very flattering\r\nsuccess, especially in many of the factory-towns along our inland\r\nstreams. As a supernumerary official, of some kind or other, aboard a\r\npacket-ship, he had visited Europe, and found means, before his return,\r\nto see Italy, and part of France and Germany. At a later period he had\r\nspent some months in a community of Fourierists. Still more recently he\r\nhad been a public lecturer on Mesmerism, for which science (as he\r\nassured Ph\xc5\x93be, and, indeed, satisfactorily proved, by putting\r\nChanticleer, who happened to be scratching near by, to sleep) he had\r\nvery remarkable endowments.\r\n\r\nHis present phase, as a daguerreotypist, was of no more importance in\r\nhis own view, nor likely to be more permanent, than any of the\r\npreceding ones. It had been taken up with the careless alacrity of an\r\nadventurer, who had his bread to earn. It would be thrown aside as\r\ncarelessly, whenever he should choose to earn his bread by some other\r\nequally digressive means. But what was most remarkable, and, perhaps,\r\nshowed a more than common poise in the young man, was the fact that,\r\namid all these personal vicissitudes, he had never lost his identity.\r\nHomeless as he had been,\xe2\x80\x94continually changing his whereabout, and,\r\ntherefore, responsible neither to public opinion nor to\r\nindividuals,\xe2\x80\x94putting off one exterior, and snatching up another, to be\r\nsoon shifted for a third,\xe2\x80\x94he had never violated the innermost man, but\r\nhad carried his conscience along with him. It was impossible to know\r\nHolgrave without recognizing this to be the fact. Hepzibah had seen it.\r\nPh\xc5\x93be soon saw it likewise, and gave him the sort of confidence which\r\nsuch a certainty inspires. She was startled, however, and sometimes\r\nrepelled,\xe2\x80\x94not by any doubt of his integrity to whatever law he\r\nacknowledged, but by a sense that his law differed from her own. He\r\nmade her uneasy, and seemed to unsettle everything around her, by his\r\nlack of reverence for what was fixed, unless, at a moment\xe2\x80\x99s warning, it\r\ncould establish its right to hold its ground.\r\n\r\nThen, moreover, she scarcely thought him affectionate in his nature. He\r\nwas too calm and cool an observer. Ph\xc5\x93be felt his eye, often; his\r\nheart, seldom or never. He took a certain kind of interest in Hepzibah\r\nand her brother, and Ph\xc5\x93be herself. He studied them attentively, and\r\nallowed no slightest circumstance of their individualities to escape\r\nhim. He was ready to do them whatever good he might; but, after all, he\r\nnever exactly made common cause with them, nor gave any reliable\r\nevidence that he loved them better in proportion as he knew them more.\r\nIn his relations with them, he seemed to be in quest of mental food,\r\nnot heart-sustenance. Ph\xc5\x93be could not conceive what interested him so\r\nmuch in her friends and herself, intellectually, since he cared nothing\r\nfor them, or, comparatively, so little, as objects of human affection.\r\n\r\nAlways, in his interviews with Ph\xc5\x93be, the artist made especial inquiry\r\nas to the welfare of Clifford, whom, except at the Sunday festival, he\r\nseldom saw.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he still seem happy?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked one day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs happy as a child,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be; \xe2\x80\x9cbut\xe2\x80\x94like a child, too\xe2\x80\x94very\r\neasily disturbed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow disturbed?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cBy things without, or by thoughts\r\nwithin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot see his thoughts! How should I?\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ph\xc5\x93be with simple\r\npiquancy. \xe2\x80\x9cVery often his humor changes without any reason that can be\r\nguessed at, just as a cloud comes over the sun. Latterly, since I have\r\nbegun to know him better, I feel it to be not quite right to look\r\nclosely into his moods. He has had such a great sorrow, that his heart\r\nis made all solemn and sacred by it. When he is cheerful,\xe2\x80\x94when the sun\r\nshines into his mind,\xe2\x80\x94then I venture to peep in, just as far as the\r\nlight reaches, but no further. It is holy ground where the shadow\r\nfalls!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow prettily you express this sentiment!\xe2\x80\x9d said the artist. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\r\nunderstand the feeling, without possessing it. Had I your\r\nopportunities, no scruples would prevent me from fathoming Clifford to\r\nthe full depth of my plummet-line!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow strange that you should wish it!\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Ph\xc5\x93be involuntarily.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is Cousin Clifford to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, nothing,\xe2\x80\x94of course, nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Holgrave with a smile.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly this is such an odd and incomprehensible world! The more I look\r\nat it, the more it puzzles me, and I begin to suspect that a man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbewilderment is the measure of his wisdom. Men and women, and children,\r\ntoo, are such strange creatures, that one never can be certain that he\r\nreally knows them; nor ever guess what they have been from what he sees\r\nthem to be now. Judge Pyncheon! Clifford! What a complex riddle\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ncomplexity of complexities\xe2\x80\x94do they present! It requires intuitive\r\nsympathy, like a young girl\xe2\x80\x99s, to solve it. A mere observer, like\r\nmyself (who never have any intuitions, and am, at best, only subtile\r\nand acute), is pretty certain to go astray.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe artist now turned the conversation to themes less dark than that\r\nwhich they had touched upon. Ph\xc5\x93be and he were young together; nor had\r\nHolgrave, in his premature experience of life, wasted entirely that\r\nbeautiful spirit of youth, which, gushing forth from one small heart\r\nand fancy, may diffuse itself over the universe, making it all as\r\nbright as on the first day of creation. Man\xe2\x80\x99s own youth is the world\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nyouth; at least, he feels as if it were, and imagines that the earth\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngranite substance is something not yet hardened, and which he can mould\r\ninto whatever shape he likes. So it was with Holgrave. He could talk\r\nsagely about the world\xe2\x80\x99s old age, but never actually believed what he\r\nsaid; he was a young man still, and therefore looked upon the\r\nworld\xe2\x80\x94that gray-bearded and wrinkled profligate, decrepit, without\r\nbeing venerable\xe2\x80\x94as a tender stripling, capable of being improved into\r\nall that it ought to be, but scarcely yet had shown the remotest\r\npromise of becoming. He had that sense, or inward prophecy,\xe2\x80\x94which a\r\nyoung man had better never have been born than not to have, and a\r\nmature man had better die at once than utterly to relinquish,\xe2\x80\x94that we\r\nare not doomed to creep on forever in the old bad way, but that, this\r\nvery now, there are the harbingers abroad of a golden era, to be\r\naccomplished in his own lifetime. It seemed to Holgrave,\xe2\x80\x94as doubtless\r\nit has seemed to the hopeful of every century since the epoch of Adam\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngrandchildren,\xe2\x80\x94that in this age, more than ever before, the moss-grown\r\nand rotten Past is to be torn down, and lifeless institutions to be\r\nthrust out of the way, and their dead corpses buried, and everything to\r\nbegin anew.\r\n\r\nAs to the main point,\xe2\x80\x94may we never live to doubt it!\xe2\x80\x94as to the better\r\ncenturies that are coming, the artist was surely right. His error lay\r\nin supposing that this age, more than any past or future one, is\r\ndestined to see the tattered garments of Antiquity exchanged for a new\r\nsuit, instead of gradually renewing themselves by patchwork; in\r\napplying his own little life-span as the measure of an interminable\r\nachievement; and, more than all, in fancying that it mattered anything\r\nto the great end in view whether he himself should contend for it or\r\nagainst it. Yet it was well for him to think so. This enthusiasm,\r\ninfusing itself through the calmness of his character, and thus taking\r\nan aspect of settled thought and wisdom, would serve to keep his youth\r\npure, and make his aspirations high. And when, with the years settling\r\ndown more weightily upon him, his early faith should be modified by\r\ninevitable experience, it would be with no harsh and sudden revolution\r\nof his sentiments. He would still have faith in man\xe2\x80\x99s brightening\r\ndestiny, and perhaps love him all the better, as he should recognize\r\nhis helplessness in his own behalf; and the haughty faith, with which\r\nhe began life, would be well bartered for a far humbler one at its\r\nclose, in discerning that man\xe2\x80\x99s best directed effort accomplishes a\r\nkind of dream, while God is the sole worker of realities.\r\n\r\nHolgrave had read very little, and that little in passing through the\r\nthoroughfare of life, where the mystic language of his books was\r\nnecessarily mixed up with the babble of the multitude, so that both one\r\nand the other were apt to lose any sense that might have been properly\r\ntheir own. He considered himself a thinker, and was certainly of a\r\nthoughtful turn, but, with his own path to discover, had perhaps hardly\r\nyet reached the point where an educated man begins to think. The true\r\nvalue of his character lay in that deep consciousness of inward\r\nstrength, which made all his past vicissitudes seem merely like a\r\nchange of garments; in that enthusiasm, so quiet that he scarcely knew\r\nof its existence, but which gave a warmth to everything that he laid\r\nhis hand on; in that personal ambition, hidden\xe2\x80\x94from his own as well as\r\nother eyes\xe2\x80\x94among his more generous impulses, but in which lurked a\r\ncertain efficacy, that might solidify him from a theorist into the\r\nchampion of some practicable cause. Altogether in his culture and want\r\nof culture,\xe2\x80\x94in his crude, wild, and misty philosophy, and the practical\r\nexperience that counteracted some of its tendencies; in his magnanimous\r\nzeal for man\xe2\x80\x99s welfare, and his recklessness of whatever the ages had\r\nestablished in man\xe2\x80\x99s behalf; in his faith, and in his infidelity; in\r\nwhat he had, and in what he lacked,\xe2\x80\x94the artist might fitly enough stand\r\nforth as the representative of many compeers in his native land.\r\n\r\nHis career it would be difficult to prefigure. There appeared to be\r\nqualities in Holgrave, such as, in a country where everything is free\r\nto the hand that can grasp it, could hardly fail to put some of the\r\nworld\xe2\x80\x99s prizes within his reach. But these matters are delightfully\r\nuncertain. At almost every step in life, we meet with young men of just\r\nabout Holgrave\xe2\x80\x99s age, for whom we anticipate wonderful things, but of\r\nwhom, even after much and careful inquiry, we never happen to hear\r\nanother word. The effervescence of youth and passion, and the fresh\r\ngloss of the intellect and imagination, endow them with a false\r\nbrilliancy, which makes fools of themselves and other people. Like\r\ncertain chintzes, calicoes, and ginghams, they show finely in their\r\nfirst newness, but cannot stand the sun and rain, and assume a very\r\nsober aspect after washing-day.\r\n\r\nBut our business is with Holgrave as we find him on this particular\r\nafternoon, and in the arbor of the Pyncheon garden. In that point of\r\nview, it was a pleasant sight to behold this young man, with so much\r\nfaith in himself, and so fair an appearance of admirable powers,\xe2\x80\x94so\r\nlittle harmed, too, by the many tests that had tried his metal,\xe2\x80\x94it was\r\npleasant to see him in his kindly intercourse with Ph\xc5\x93be. Her thought\r\nhad scarcely done him justice when it pronounced him cold; or, if so,\r\nhe had grown warmer now. Without such purpose on her part, and\r\nunconsciously on his, she made the House of the Seven Gables like a\r\nhome to him, and the garden a familiar precinct. With the insight on\r\nwhich he prided himself, he fancied that he could look through Ph\xc5\x93be,\r\nand all around her, and could read her off like a page of a child\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nstory-book. But these transparent natures are often deceptive in their\r\ndepth; those pebbles at the bottom of the fountain are farther from us\r\nthan we think. Thus the artist, whatever he might judge of Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncapacity, was beguiled, by some silent charm of hers, to talk freely of\r\nwhat he dreamed of doing in the world. He poured himself out as to\r\nanother self. Very possibly, he forgot Ph\xc5\x93be while he talked to her,\r\nand was moved only by the inevitable tendency of thought, when rendered\r\nsympathetic by enthusiasm and emotion, to flow into the first safe\r\nreservoir which it finds. But, had you peeped at them through the\r\nchinks of the garden-fence, the young man\xe2\x80\x99s earnestness and heightened\r\ncolor might have led you to suppose that he was making love to the\r\nyoung girl!\r\n\r\nAt length, something was said by Holgrave that made it apposite for\r\nPh\xc5\x93be to inquire what had first brought him acquainted with her cousin\r\nHepzibah, and why he now chose to lodge in the desolate old Pyncheon\r\nHouse. Without directly answering her, he turned from the Future, which\r\nhad heretofore been the theme of his discourse, and began to speak of\r\nthe influences of the Past. One subject, indeed, is but the\r\nreverberation of the other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we never, never get rid of this Past?\xe2\x80\x9d cried he, keeping up the\r\nearnest tone of his preceding conversation. \xe2\x80\x9cIt lies upon the Present\r\nlike a giant\xe2\x80\x99s dead body! In fact, the case is just as if a young giant\r\nwere compelled to waste all his strength in carrying about the corpse\r\nof the old giant, his grandfather, who died a long while ago, and only\r\nneeds to be decently buried. Just think a moment, and it will startle\r\nyou to see what slaves we are to bygone times,\xe2\x80\x94to Death, if we give the\r\nmatter the right word!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I do not see it,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Ph\xc5\x93be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor example, then,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Holgrave: \xe2\x80\x9ca dead man, if he happens to\r\nhave made a will, disposes of wealth no longer his own; or, if he die\r\nintestate, it is distributed in accordance with the notions of men much\r\nlonger dead than he. A dead man sits on all our judgment-seats; and\r\nliving judges do but search out and repeat his decisions. We read in\r\ndead men\xe2\x80\x99s books! We laugh at dead men\xe2\x80\x99s jokes, and cry at dead men\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npathos! We are sick of dead men\xe2\x80\x99s diseases, physical and moral, and die\r\nof the same remedies with which dead doctors killed their patients! We\r\nworship the living Deity according to dead men\xe2\x80\x99s forms and creeds.\r\nWhatever we seek to do, of our own free motion, a dead man\xe2\x80\x99s icy hand\r\nobstructs us! Turn our eyes to what point we may, a dead man\xe2\x80\x99s white,\r\nimmitigable face encounters them, and freezes our very heart! And we\r\nmust be dead ourselves before we can begin to have our proper influence\r\non our own world, which will then be no longer our world, but the world\r\nof another generation, with which we shall have no shadow of a right to\r\ninterfere. I ought to have said, too, that we live in dead men\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouses; as, for instance, in this of the Seven Gables!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, \xe2\x80\x9cso long as we can be comfortable in them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we shall live to see the day, I trust,\xe2\x80\x9d went on the artist, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen\r\nno man shall build his house for posterity. Why should he? He might\r\njust as reasonably order a durable suit of clothes,\xe2\x80\x94leather, or\r\nguttapercha, or whatever else lasts longest,\xe2\x80\x94so that his\r\ngreat-grandchildren should have the benefit of them, and cut precisely\r\nthe same figure in the world that he himself does. If each generation\r\nwere allowed and expected to build its own houses, that single change,\r\ncomparatively unimportant in itself, would imply almost every reform\r\nwhich society is now suffering for. I doubt whether even our public\r\nedifices\xe2\x80\x94our capitols, state-houses, court-houses, city-hall, and\r\nchurches,\xe2\x80\x94ought to be built of such permanent materials as stone or\r\nbrick. It were better that they should crumble to ruin once in twenty\r\nyears, or thereabouts, as a hint to the people to examine into and\r\nreform the institutions which they symbolize.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you hate everything old!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be in dismay. \xe2\x80\x9cIt makes me dizzy\r\nto think of such a shifting world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly love nothing mouldy,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, this old\r\nPyncheon House! Is it a wholesome place to live in, with its black\r\nshingles, and the green moss that shows how damp they are?\xe2\x80\x94its dark,\r\nlow-studded rooms\xe2\x80\x94its grime and sordidness, which are the\r\ncrystallization on its walls of the human breath, that has been drawn\r\nand exhaled here in discontent and anguish? The house ought to be\r\npurified with fire,\xe2\x80\x94purified till only its ashes remain!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why do you live in it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ph\xc5\x93be, a little piqued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am pursuing my studies here; not in books, however,\xe2\x80\x9d replied\r\nHolgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cThe house, in my view, is expressive of that odious and\r\nabominable Past, with all its bad influences, against which I have just\r\nbeen declaiming. I dwell in it for a while, that I may know the better\r\nhow to hate it. By the bye, did you ever hear the story of Maule, the\r\nwizard, and what happened between him and your immeasurably\r\ngreat-grandfather?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be; \xe2\x80\x9cI heard it long ago, from my father, and\r\ntwo or three times from my cousin Hepzibah, in the month that I have\r\nbeen here. She seems to think that all the calamities of the Pyncheons\r\nbegan from that quarrel with the wizard, as you call him. And you, Mr.\r\nHolgrave look as if you thought so too! How singular that you should\r\nbelieve what is so very absurd, when you reject many things that are a\r\ngreat deal worthier of credit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do believe it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the artist seriously; \xe2\x80\x9cnot as a superstition,\r\nhowever, but as proved by unquestionable facts, and as exemplifying a\r\ntheory.
