Now, see: under those seven gables, at which we now look\r\nup,\xe2\x80\x94and which old Colonel Pyncheon meant to be the house of his\r\ndescendants, in prosperity and happiness, down to an epoch far beyond\r\nthe present,\xe2\x80\x94under that roof, through a portion of three centuries,\r\nthere has been perpetual remorse of conscience, a constantly defeated\r\nhope, strife amongst kindred, various misery, a strange form of death,\r\ndark suspicion, unspeakable disgrace,\xe2\x80\x94all, or most of which calamity I\r\nhave the means of tracing to the old Puritan\xe2\x80\x99s inordinate desire to\r\nplant and endow a family. To plant a family! This idea is at the bottom\r\nof most of the wrong and mischief which men do. The truth is, that,\r\nonce in every half-century, at longest, a family should be merged into\r\nthe great, obscure mass of humanity, and forget all about its\r\nancestors. Human blood, in order to keep its freshness, should run in\r\nhidden streams, as the water of an aqueduct is conveyed in subterranean\r\npipes. In the family existence of these Pyncheons, for\r\ninstance,\xe2\x80\x94forgive me Ph\xc5\x93be, but I cannot think of you as one of\r\nthem,\xe2\x80\x94in their brief New England pedigree, there has been time enough\r\nto infect them all with one kind of lunacy or another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou speak very unceremoniously of my kindred,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, debating\r\nwith herself whether she ought to take offence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI speak true thoughts to a true mind!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Holgrave, with a\r\nvehemence which Ph\xc5\x93be had not before witnessed in him. \xe2\x80\x9cThe truth is as\r\nI say! Furthermore, the original perpetrator and father of this\r\nmischief appears to have perpetuated himself, and still walks the\r\nstreet,\xe2\x80\x94at least, his very image, in mind and body,\xe2\x80\x94with the fairest\r\nprospect of transmitting to posterity as rich and as wretched an\r\ninheritance as he has received! Do you remember the daguerreotype, and\r\nits resemblance to the old portrait?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow strangely in earnest you are!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Ph\xc5\x93be, looking at him\r\nwith surprise and perplexity; half alarmed and partly inclined to\r\nlaugh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou talk of the lunacy of the Pyncheons; is it contagious?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand you!\xe2\x80\x9d said the artist, coloring and laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe\r\nI am a little mad. This subject has taken hold of my mind with the\r\nstrangest tenacity of clutch since I have lodged in yonder old gable.\r\nAs one method of throwing it off, I have put an incident of the\r\nPyncheon family history, with which I happen to be acquainted, into the\r\nform of a legend, and mean to publish it in a magazine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you write for the magazines?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Ph\xc5\x93be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible you did not know it?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, such is\r\nliterary fame! Yes. Miss Ph\xc5\x93be Pyncheon, among the multitude of my\r\nmarvellous gifts I have that of writing stories; and my name has\r\nfigured, I can assure you, on the covers of Graham and Godey, making as\r\nrespectable an appearance, for aught I could see, as any of the\r\ncanonized bead-roll with which it was associated. In the humorous line,\r\nI am thought to have a very pretty way with me; and as for pathos, I am\r\nas provocative of tears as an onion. But shall I read you my story?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if it is not very long,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x94and added laughingly,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cnor\r\nvery dull.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs this latter point was one which the daguerreotypist could not decide\r\nfor himself, he forthwith produced his roll of manuscript, and, while\r\nthe late sunbeams gilded the seven gables, began to read.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIII.\r\nAlice Pyncheon\r\n\r\n\r\nThere was a message brought, one day, from the worshipful Gervayse\r\nPyncheon to young Matthew Maule, the carpenter, desiring his immediate\r\npresence at the House of the Seven Gables.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what does your master want with me?\xe2\x80\x9d said the carpenter to Mr.\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s black servant. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes the house need any repair? Well it may,\r\nby this time; and no blame to my father who built it, neither! I was\r\nreading the old Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s tombstone, no longer ago than last Sabbath;\r\nand, reckoning from that date, the house has stood seven-and-thirty\r\nyears. No wonder if there should be a job to do on the roof.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t know what massa wants,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cThe house is a berry\r\ngood house, and old Colonel Pyncheon think so too, I reckon;\xe2\x80\x94else why\r\nthe old man haunt it so, and frighten a poor nigga, As he does?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well, friend Scipio; let your master know that I\xe2\x80\x99m coming,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe carpenter with a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cFor a fair, workmanlike job, he\xe2\x80\x99ll find me\r\nhis man. And so the house is haunted, is it? It will take a tighter\r\nworkman than I am to keep the spirits out of the Seven Gables. Even if\r\nthe Colonel would be quiet,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, muttering to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cmy old\r\ngrandfather, the wizard, will be pretty sure to stick to the Pyncheons\r\nas long as their walls hold together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that you mutter to yourself, Matthew Maule?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nwhat for do you look so black at me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter, darky,\xe2\x80\x9d said the carpenter. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think nobody is to look\r\nblack but yourself? Go tell your master I\xe2\x80\x99m coming; and if you happen\r\nto see Mistress Alice, his daughter, give Matthew Maule\xe2\x80\x99s humble\r\nrespects to her. She has brought a fair face from Italy,\xe2\x80\x94fair, and\r\ngentle, and proud,\xe2\x80\x94has that same Alice Pyncheon!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe talk of Mistress Alice!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Scipio, as he returned from his\r\nerrand. \xe2\x80\x9cThe low carpenter-man! He no business so much as to look at\r\nher a great way off!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis young Matthew Maule, the carpenter, it must be observed, was a\r\nperson little understood, and not very generally liked, in the town\r\nwhere he resided; not that anything could be alleged against his\r\nintegrity, or his skill and diligence in the handicraft which he\r\nexercised. The aversion (as it might justly be called) with which many\r\npersons regarded him was partly the result of his own character and\r\ndeportment, and partly an inheritance.\r\n\r\nHe was the grandson of a former Matthew Maule, one of the early\r\nsettlers of the town, and who had been a famous and terrible wizard in\r\nhis day. This old reprobate was one of the sufferers when Cotton\r\nMather, and his brother ministers, and the learned judges, and other\r\nwise men, and Sir William Phipps, the sagacious governor, made such\r\nlaudable efforts to weaken the great enemy of souls, by sending a\r\nmultitude of his adherents up the rocky pathway of Gallows Hill. Since\r\nthose days, no doubt, it had grown to be suspected that, in consequence\r\nof an unfortunate overdoing of a work praiseworthy in itself, the\r\nproceedings against the witches had proved far less acceptable to the\r\nBeneficent Father than to that very Arch Enemy whom they were intended\r\nto distress and utterly overwhelm. It is not the less certain, however,\r\nthat awe and terror brooded over the memories of those who died for\r\nthis horrible crime of witchcraft. Their graves, in the crevices of the\r\nrocks, were supposed to be incapable of retaining the occupants who had\r\nbeen so hastily thrust into them. Old Matthew Maule, especially, was\r\nknown to have as little hesitation or difficulty in rising out of his\r\ngrave as an ordinary man in getting out of bed, and was as often seen\r\nat midnight as living people at noonday. This pestilent wizard (in whom\r\nhis just punishment seemed to have wrought no manner of amendment) had\r\nan inveterate habit of haunting a certain mansion, styled the House of\r\nthe Seven Gables, against the owner of which he pretended to hold an\r\nunsettled claim for ground-rent. The ghost, it appears,\xe2\x80\x94with the\r\npertinacity which was one of his distinguishing characteristics while\r\nalive,\xe2\x80\x94insisted that he was the rightful proprietor of the site upon\r\nwhich the house stood. His terms were, that either the aforesaid\r\nground-rent, from the day when the cellar began to be dug, should be\r\npaid down, or the mansion itself given up; else he, the ghostly\r\ncreditor, would have his finger in all the affairs of the Pyncheons,\r\nand make everything go wrong with them, though it should be a thousand\r\nyears after his death. It was a wild story, perhaps, but seemed not\r\naltogether so incredible to those who could remember what an inflexibly\r\nobstinate old fellow this wizard Maule had been.\r\n\r\nNow, the wizard\xe2\x80\x99s grandson, the young Matthew Maule of our story, was\r\npopularly supposed to have inherited some of his ancestor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nquestionable traits. It is wonderful how many absurdities were\r\npromulgated in reference to the young man. He was fabled, for example,\r\nto have a strange power of getting into people\xe2\x80\x99s dreams, and regulating\r\nmatters there according to his own fancy, pretty much like the\r\nstage-manager of a theatre. There was a great deal of talk among the\r\nneighbors, particularly the petticoated ones, about what they called\r\nthe witchcraft of Maule\xe2\x80\x99s eye. Some said that he could look into\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x99s minds; others, that, by the marvellous power of this eye, he\r\ncould draw people into his own mind, or send them, if he pleased, to do\r\nerrands to his grandfather, in the spiritual world; others, again, that\r\nit was what is termed an Evil Eye, and possessed the valuable faculty\r\nof blighting corn, and drying children into mummies with the heartburn.\r\nBut, after all, what worked most to the young carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s disadvantage\r\nwas, first, the reserve and sternness of his natural disposition, and\r\nnext, the fact of his not being a church-communicant, and the suspicion\r\nof his holding heretical tenets in matters of religion and polity.\r\n\r\nAfter receiving Mr. Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s message, the carpenter merely tarried to\r\nfinish a small job, which he happened to have in hand, and then took\r\nhis way towards the House of the Seven Gables. This noted edifice,\r\nthough its style might be getting a little out of fashion, was still as\r\nrespectable a family residence as that of any gentleman in town. The\r\npresent owner, Gervayse Pyncheon, was said to have contracted a dislike\r\nto the house, in consequence of a shock to his sensibility, in early\r\nchildhood, from the sudden death of his grandfather. In the very act of\r\nrunning to climb Colonel Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s knee, the boy had discovered the\r\nold Puritan to be a corpse. On arriving at manhood, Mr. Pyncheon had\r\nvisited England, where he married a lady of fortune, and had\r\nsubsequently spent many years, partly in the mother country, and partly\r\nin various cities on the continent of Europe. During this period, the\r\nfamily mansion had been consigned to the charge of a kinsman, who was\r\nallowed to make it his home for the time being, in consideration of\r\nkeeping the premises in thorough repair. So faithfully had this\r\ncontract been fulfilled, that now, as the carpenter approached the\r\nhouse, his practised eye could detect nothing to criticise in its\r\ncondition. The peaks of the seven gables rose up sharply; the shingled\r\nroof looked thoroughly water-tight; and the glittering plaster-work\r\nentirely covered the exterior walls, and sparkled in the October sun,\r\nas if it had been new only a week ago.\r\n\r\nThe house had that pleasant aspect of life which is like the cheery\r\nexpression of comfortable activity in the human countenance. You could\r\nsee, at once, that there was the stir of a large family within it. A\r\nhuge load of oak-wood was passing through the gateway, towards the\r\noutbuildings in the rear; the fat cook\xe2\x80\x94or probably it might be the\r\nhousekeeper\xe2\x80\x94stood at the side door, bargaining for some turkeys and\r\npoultry which a countryman had brought for sale. Now and then a\r\nmaid-servant, neatly dressed, and now the shining sable face of a\r\nslave, might be seen bustling across the windows, in the lower part of\r\nthe house. At an open window of a room in the second story, hanging\r\nover some pots of beautiful and delicate flowers,\xe2\x80\x94exotics, but which\r\nhad never known a more genial sunshine than that of the New England\r\nautumn,\xe2\x80\x94was the figure of a young lady, an exotic, like the flowers,\r\nand beautiful and delicate as they. Her presence imparted an\r\nindescribable grace and faint witchery to the whole edifice. In other\r\nrespects, it was a substantial, jolly-looking mansion, and seemed fit\r\nto be the residence of a patriarch, who might establish his own\r\nheadquarters in the front gable and assign one of the remainder to each\r\nof his six children, while the great chimney in the centre should\r\nsymbolize the old fellow\xe2\x80\x99s hospitable heart, which kept them all warm,\r\nand made a great whole of the seven smaller ones.\r\n\r\nThere was a vertical sundial on the front gable; and as the carpenter\r\npassed beneath it, he looked up and noted the hour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree o\xe2\x80\x99clock!\xe2\x80\x9d said he to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cMy father told me that dial was\r\nput up only an hour before the old Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s death. How truly it has\r\nkept time these seven-and-thirty years past! The shadow creeps and\r\ncreeps, and is always looking over the shoulder of the sunshine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt might have befitted a craftsman, like Matthew Maule, on being sent\r\nfor to a gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s house, to go to the back door, where servants and\r\nwork-people were usually admitted; or at least to the side entrance,\r\nwhere the better class of tradesmen made application. But the carpenter\r\nhad a great deal of pride and stiffness in his nature; and, at this\r\nmoment, moreover, his heart was bitter with the sense of hereditary\r\nwrong, because he considered the great Pyncheon House to be standing on\r\nsoil which should have been his own. On this very site, beside a spring\r\nof delicious water, his grandfather had felled the pine-trees and built\r\na cottage, in which children had been born to him; and it was only from\r\na dead man\xe2\x80\x99s stiffened fingers that Colonel Pyncheon had wrested away\r\nthe title-deeds. So young Maule went straight to the principal\r\nentrance, beneath a portal of carved oak, and gave such a peal of the\r\niron knocker that you would have imagined the stern old wizard himself\r\nto be standing at the threshold.\r\n\r\nBlack Scipio answered the summons in a prodigious hurry; but showed the\r\nwhites of his eyes, in amazement on beholding only the carpenter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord-a-mercy, what a great man he be, this carpenter fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d mumbled\r\nScipio, down in his throat. \xe2\x80\x9cAnybody think he beat on the door with his\r\nbiggest hammer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere I am!\xe2\x80\x9d said Maule sternly. \xe2\x80\x9cShow me the way to your master\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nparlor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he stept into the house, a note of sweet and melancholy music\r\nthrilled and vibrated along the passage-way, proceeding from one of the\r\nrooms above stairs. It was the harpsichord which Alice Pyncheon had\r\nbrought with her from beyond the sea. The fair Alice bestowed most of\r\nher maiden leisure between flowers and music, although the former were\r\napt to droop, and the melodies were often sad. She was of foreign\r\neducation, and could not take kindly to the New England modes of life,\r\nin which nothing beautiful had ever been developed.\r\n\r\nAs Mr. Pyncheon had been impatiently awaiting Maule\xe2\x80\x99s arrival, black\r\nScipio, of course, lost no time in ushering the carpenter into his\r\nmaster\xe2\x80\x99s presence. The room in which this gentleman sat was a parlor of\r\nmoderate size, looking out upon the garden of the house, and having its\r\nwindows partly shadowed by the foliage of fruit-trees. It was Mr.\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s peculiar apartment, and was provided with furniture, in an\r\nelegant and costly style, principally from Paris; the floor (which was\r\nunusual at that day) being covered with a carpet, so skilfully and\r\nrichly wrought that it seemed to glow as with living flowers. In one\r\ncorner stood a marble woman, to whom her own beauty was the sole and\r\nsufficient garment. Some pictures\xe2\x80\x94that looked old, and had a mellow\r\ntinge diffused through all their artful splendor\xe2\x80\x94hung on the walls.\r\nNear the fireplace was a large and very beautiful cabinet of ebony,\r\ninlaid with ivory; a piece of antique furniture, which Mr. Pyncheon had\r\nbought in Venice, and which he used as the treasure-place for medals,\r\nancient coins, and whatever small and valuable curiosities he had\r\npicked up on his travels. Through all this variety of decoration,\r\nhowever, the room showed its original characteristics; its low stud,\r\nits cross-beam, its chimney-piece, with the old-fashioned Dutch tiles;\r\nso that it was the emblem of a mind industriously stored with foreign\r\nideas, and elaborated into artificial refinement, but neither larger,\r\nnor, in its proper self, more elegant than before.\r\n\r\nThere were two objects that appeared rather out of place in this very\r\nhandsomely furnished room. One was a large map, or surveyor\xe2\x80\x99s plan, of\r\na tract of land, which looked as if it had been drawn a good many years\r\nago, and was now dingy with smoke, and soiled, here and there, with the\r\ntouch of fingers. The other was a portrait of a stern old man, in a\r\nPuritan garb, painted roughly, but with a bold effect, and a remarkably\r\nstrong expression of character.\r\n\r\nAt a small table, before a fire of English sea-coal, sat Mr. Pyncheon,\r\nsipping coffee, which had grown to be a very favorite beverage with him\r\nin France. He was a middle-aged and really handsome man, with a wig\r\nflowing down upon his shoulders; his coat was of blue velvet, with lace\r\non the borders and at the button-holes; and the firelight glistened on\r\nthe spacious breadth of his waistcoat, which was flowered all over with\r\ngold. On the entrance of Scipio, ushering in the carpenter, Mr.\r\nPyncheon turned partly round, but resumed his former position, and\r\nproceeded deliberately to finish his cup of coffee, without immediate\r\nnotice of the guest whom he had summoned to his presence. It was not\r\nthat he intended any rudeness or improper neglect,\xe2\x80\x94which, indeed, he\r\nwould have blushed to be guilty of,\xe2\x80\x94but it never occurred to him that a\r\nperson in Maule\xe2\x80\x99s station had a claim on his courtesy, or would trouble\r\nhimself about it one way or the other.\r\n\r\nThe carpenter, however, stepped at once to the hearth, and turned\r\nhimself about, so as to look Mr. Pyncheon in the face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou sent for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cBe pleased to explain your business, that\r\nI may go back to my own affairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! excuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Pyncheon quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cI did not mean to tax your\r\ntime without a recompense. Your name, I think, is Maule,\xe2\x80\x94Thomas or\r\nMatthew Maule,\xe2\x80\x94a son or grandson of the builder of this house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Maule,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the carpenter,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cson of him who built the\r\nhouse,\xe2\x80\x94grandson of the rightful proprietor of the soil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know the dispute to which you allude,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mr. Pyncheon with\r\nundisturbed equanimity. \xe2\x80\x9cI am well aware that my grandfather was\r\ncompelled to resort to a suit at law, in order to establish his claim\r\nto the foundation-site of this edifice. We will not, if you please,\r\nrenew the discussion. The matter was settled at the time, and by the\r\ncompetent authorities,\xe2\x80\x94equitably, it is to be presumed,\xe2\x80\x94and, at all\r\nevents, irrevocably. Yet, singularly enough, there is an incidental\r\nreference to this very subject in what I am now about to say to you.\r\nAnd this same inveterate grudge,\xe2\x80\x94excuse me, I mean no offence,\xe2\x80\x94this\r\nirritability, which you have just shown, is not entirely aside from the\r\nmatter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you can find anything for your purpose, Mr. Pyncheon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\ncarpenter, \xe2\x80\x9cin a man\xe2\x80\x99s natural resentment for the wrongs done to his\r\nblood, you are welcome to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take you at your word, Goodman Maule,\xe2\x80\x9d said the owner of the Seven\r\nGables, with a smile, \xe2\x80\x9cand will proceed to suggest a mode in which your\r\nhereditary resentments\xe2\x80\x94justifiable or otherwise\xe2\x80\x94may have had a bearing\r\non my affairs. You have heard, I suppose, that the Pyncheon family,\r\never since my grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s days, have been prosecuting a still\r\nunsettled claim to a very large extent of territory at the Eastward?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOften,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Maule,\xe2\x80\x94and it is said that a smile came over his\r\nface,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cvery often,\xe2\x80\x94from my father!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis claim,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Mr. Pyncheon, after pausing a moment, as if to\r\nconsider what the carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s smile might mean, \xe2\x80\x9cappeared to be on the\r\nvery verge of a settlement and full allowance, at the period of my\r\ngrandfather\xe2\x80\x99s decease. It was well known, to those in his confidence,\r\nthat he anticipated neither difficulty nor delay. Now, Colonel\r\nPyncheon, I need hardly say, was a practical man, well acquainted with\r\npublic and private business, and not at all the person to cherish\r\nill-founded hopes, or to attempt the following out of an impracticable\r\nscheme. It is obvious to conclude, therefore, that he had grounds, not\r\napparent to his heirs, for his confident anticipation of success in the\r\nmatter of this Eastern claim. In a word, I believe,\xe2\x80\x94and my legal\r\nadvisers coincide in the belief, which, moreover, is authorized, to a\r\ncertain extent, by the family traditions,\xe2\x80\x94that my grandfather was in\r\npossession of some deed, or other document, essential to this claim,\r\nbut which has since disappeared.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew Maule,\xe2\x80\x94and again, it is said, there was a\r\ndark smile on his face,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cbut what can a poor carpenter have to do with\r\nthe grand affairs of the Pyncheon family?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Mr. Pyncheon, \xe2\x80\x9cpossibly much!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere ensued a great many words between Matthew Maule and the proprietor\r\nof the Seven Gables, on the subject which the latter had thus broached.\r\nIt seems (although Mr. Pyncheon had some hesitation in referring to\r\nstories so exceedingly absurd in their aspect) that the popular belief\r\npointed to some mysterious connection and dependence, existing between\r\nthe family of the Maules and these vast unrealized possessions of the\r\nPyncheons. It was an ordinary saying that the old wizard, hanged though\r\nhe was, had obtained the best end of the bargain in his contest with\r\nColonel Pyncheon; inasmuch as he had got possession of the great\r\nEastern claim, in exchange for an acre or two of garden-ground. A very\r\naged woman, recently dead, had often used the metaphorical expression,\r\nin her fireside talk, that miles and miles of the Pyncheon lands had\r\nbeen shovelled into Maule\xe2\x80\x99s grave; which, by the bye, was but a very\r\nshallow nook, between two rocks, near the summit of Gallows Hill.\r\nAgain, when the lawyers were making inquiry for the missing document,\r\nit was a by-word that it would never be found, unless in the wizard\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nskeleton hand. So much weight had the shrewd lawyers assigned to these\r\nfables, that (but Mr. Pyncheon did not see fit to inform the carpenter\r\nof the fact) they had secretly caused the wizard\xe2\x80\x99s grave to be\r\nsearched. Nothing was discovered, however, except that, unaccountably,\r\nthe right hand of the skeleton was gone.\r\n\r\nNow, what was unquestionably important, a portion of these popular\r\nrumors could be traced, though rather doubtfully and indistinctly, to\r\nchance words and obscure hints of the executed wizard\xe2\x80\x99s son, and the\r\nfather of this present Matthew Maule. And here Mr. Pyncheon could bring\r\nan item of his own personal evidence into play. Though but a child at\r\nthe time, he either remembered or fancied that Matthew\xe2\x80\x99s father had had\r\nsome job to perform on the day before, or possibly the very morning of\r\nthe Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s decease, in the private room where he and the carpenter\r\nwere at this moment talking. Certain papers belonging to Colonel\r\nPyncheon, as his grandson distinctly recollected, had been spread out\r\non the table.\r\n\r\nMatthew Maule understood the insinuated suspicion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\xe2\x80\x94but still there was that dark smile, making a\r\nriddle of his countenance,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cmy father was an honester man than the\r\nbloody old Colonel! Not to get his rights back again would he have\r\ncarried off one of those papers!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not bandy words with you,\xe2\x80\x9d observed the foreign-bred Mr.\r\nPyncheon, with haughty composure. \xe2\x80\x9cNor will it become me to resent any\r\nrudeness towards either my grandfather or myself. A gentleman, before\r\nseeking intercourse with a person of your station and habits, will\r\nfirst consider whether the urgency of the end may compensate for the\r\ndisagreeableness of the means. It does so in the present instance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe then renewed the conversation, and made great pecuniary offers to\r\nthe carpenter, in case the latter should give information leading to\r\nthe discovery of the lost document, and the consequent success of the\r\nEastern claim. For a long time Matthew Maule is said to have turned a\r\ncold ear to these propositions. At last, however, with a strange kind\r\nof laugh, he inquired whether Mr. Pyncheon would make over to him the\r\nold wizard\xe2\x80\x99s homestead-ground, together with the House of the Seven\r\nGables, now standing on it, in requital of the documentary evidence so\r\nurgently required.\r\n\r\nThe wild, chimney-corner legend (which, without copying all its\r\nextravagances, my narrative essentially follows) here gives an account\r\nof some very strange behavior on the part of Colonel Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nportrait. This picture, it must be understood, was supposed to be so\r\nintimately connected with the fate of the house, and so magically built\r\ninto its walls, that, if once it should be removed, that very instant\r\nthe whole edifice would come thundering down in a heap of dusty ruin.\r\nAll through the foregoing conversation between Mr. Pyncheon and the\r\ncarpenter, the portrait had been frowning, clenching its fist, and\r\ngiving many such proofs of excessive discomposure, but without\r\nattracting the notice of either of the two colloquists. And finally, at\r\nMatthew Maule\xe2\x80\x99s audacious suggestion of a transfer of the seven-gabled\r\nstructure, the ghostly portrait is averred to have lost all patience,\r\nand to have shown itself on the point of descending bodily from its\r\nframe. But such incredible incidents are merely to be mentioned aside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive up this house!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mr. Pyncheon, in amazement at the\r\nproposal. \xe2\x80\x9cWere I to do so, my grandfather would not rest quiet in his\r\ngrave!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe never has, if all stories are true,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the carpenter\r\ncomposedly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut that matter concerns his grandson more than it does\r\nMatthew Maule. I have no other terms to propose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nImpossible as he at first thought it to comply with Maule\xe2\x80\x99s conditions,\r\nstill, on a second glance, Mr. Pyncheon was of opinion that they might\r\nat least be made matter of discussion. He himself had no personal\r\nattachment for the house, nor any pleasant associations connected with\r\nhis childish residence in it. On the contrary, after seven-and-thirty\r\nyears, the presence of his dead grandfather seemed still to pervade it,\r\nas on that morning when the affrighted boy had beheld him, with so\r\nghastly an aspect, stiffening in his chair. His long abode in foreign\r\nparts, moreover, and familiarity with many of the castles and ancestral\r\nhalls of England, and the marble palaces of Italy, had caused him to\r\nlook contemptuously at the House of the Seven Gables, whether in point\r\nof splendor or convenience. It was a mansion exceedingly inadequate to\r\nthe style of living which it would be incumbent on Mr. Pyncheon to\r\nsupport, after realizing his territorial rights. His steward might\r\ndeign to occupy it, but never, certainly, the great landed proprietor\r\nhimself. In the event of success, indeed, it was his purpose to return\r\nto England; nor, to say the truth, would he recently have quitted that\r\nmore congenial home, had not his own fortune, as well as his deceased\r\nwife\xe2\x80\x99s, begun to give symptoms of exhaustion. The Eastern claim once\r\nfairly settled, and put upon the firm basis of actual possession, Mr.\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s property\xe2\x80\x94to be measured by miles, not acres\xe2\x80\x94would be worth\r\nan earldom, and would reasonably entitle him to solicit, or enable him\r\nto purchase, that elevated dignity from the British monarch. Lord\r\nPyncheon!\xe2\x80\x94or the Earl of Waldo!\xe2\x80\x94how could such a magnate be expected to\r\ncontract his grandeur within the pitiful compass of seven shingled\r\ngables?\r\n\r\nIn short, on an enlarged view of the business, the carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s terms\r\nappeared so ridiculously easy that Mr. Pyncheon could scarcely forbear\r\nlaughing in his face. He was quite ashamed, after the foregoing\r\nreflections, to propose any diminution of so moderate a recompense for\r\nthe immense service to be rendered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI consent to your proposition, Maule!\xe2\x80\x9d cried he. \xe2\x80\x9cPut me in possession\r\nof the document essential to establish my rights, and the House of the\r\nSeven Gables is your own!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAccording to some versions of the story, a regular contract to the\r\nabove effect was drawn up by a lawyer, and signed and sealed in the\r\npresence of witnesses. Others say that Matthew Maule was contented with\r\na private written agreement, in which Mr. Pyncheon pledged his honor\r\nand integrity to the fulfillment of the terms concluded upon. The\r\ngentleman then ordered wine, which he and the carpenter drank together,\r\nin confirmation of their bargain. During the whole preceding discussion\r\nand subsequent formalities, the old Puritan\xe2\x80\x99s portrait seems to have\r\npersisted in its shadowy gestures of disapproval; but without effect,\r\nexcept that, as Mr. Pyncheon set down the emptied glass, he thought he\r\nbeheld his grandfather frown.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis sherry is too potent a wine for me; it has affected my brain\r\nalready,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed, after a somewhat startled look at the picture.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn returning to Europe, I shall confine myself to the more delicate\r\nvintages of Italy and France, the best of which will not bear\r\ntransportation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy Lord Pyncheon may drink what wine he will, and wherever he\r\npleases,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the carpenter, as if he had been privy to Mr.\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s ambitious projects. \xe2\x80\x9cBut first, sir, if you desire tidings\r\nof this lost document, I must crave the favor of a little talk with\r\nyour fair daughter Alice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are mad, Maule!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mr. Pyncheon haughtily; and now, at\r\nlast, there was anger mixed up with his pride. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat can my daughter\r\nhave to do with a business like this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIndeed, at this new demand on the carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s part, the proprietor of\r\nthe Seven Gables was even more thunder-struck than at the cool\r\nproposition to surrender his house. There was, at least, an assignable\r\nmotive for the first stipulation; there appeared to be none whatever\r\nfor the last. Nevertheless, Matthew Maule sturdily insisted on the\r\nyoung lady being summoned, and even gave her father to understand, in a\r\nmysterious kind of explanation,\xe2\x80\x94which made the matter considerably\r\ndarker than it looked before,\xe2\x80\x94that the only chance of acquiring the\r\nrequisite knowledge was through the clear, crystal medium of a pure and\r\nvirgin intelligence, like that of the fair Alice. Not to encumber our\r\nstory with Mr. Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s scruples, whether of conscience, pride, or\r\nfatherly affection, he at length ordered his daughter to be called. He\r\nwell knew that she was in her chamber, and engaged in no occupation\r\nthat could not readily be laid aside; for, as it happened, ever since\r\nAlice\xe2\x80\x99s name had been spoken, both her father and the carpenter had\r\nheard the sad and sweet music of her harpsichord, and the airier\r\nmelancholy of her accompanying voice.\r\n\r\nSo Alice Pyncheon was summoned, and appeared. A portrait of this young\r\nlady, painted by a Venetian artist, and left by her father in England,\r\nis said to have fallen into the hands of the present Duke of\r\nDevonshire, and to be now preserved at Chatsworth; not on account of\r\nany associations with the original, but for its value as a picture, and\r\nthe high character of beauty in the countenance. If ever there was a\r\nlady born, and set apart from the world\xe2\x80\x99s vulgar mass by a certain\r\ngentle and cold stateliness, it was this very Alice Pyncheon. Yet there\r\nwas the womanly mixture in her; the tenderness, or, at least, the\r\ntender capabilities. For the sake of that redeeming quality, a man of\r\ngenerous nature would have forgiven all her pride, and have been\r\ncontent, almost, to lie down in her path, and let Alice set her slender\r\nfoot upon his heart. All that he would have required was simply the\r\nacknowledgment that he was indeed a man, and a fellow-being, moulded of\r\nthe same elements as she.\r\n\r\nAs Alice came into the room, her eyes fell upon the carpenter, who was\r\nstanding near its centre, clad in green woollen jacket, a pair of loose\r\nbreeches, open at the knees, and with a long pocket for his rule, the\r\nend of which protruded; it was as proper a mark of the artisan\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncalling as Mr. Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s full-dress sword of that gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naristocratic pretensions. A glow of artistic approval brightened over\r\nAlice Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s face; she was struck with admiration\xe2\x80\x94which she made no\r\nattempt to conceal\xe2\x80\x94of the remarkable comeliness, strength, and energy\r\nof Maule\xe2\x80\x99s figure. But that admiring glance (which most other men,\r\nperhaps, would have cherished as a sweet recollection all through life)\r\nthe carpenter never forgave. It must have been the devil himself that\r\nmade Maule so subtile in his preception.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes the girl look at me as if I were a brute beast?\xe2\x80\x9d thought he,\r\nsetting his teeth. \xe2\x80\x9cShe shall know whether I have a human spirit; and\r\nthe worse for her, if it prove stronger than her own!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father, you sent for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alice, in her sweet and harp-like\r\nvoice. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, if you have business with this young man, pray let me go\r\nagain. You know I do not love this room, in spite of that Claude, with\r\nwhich you try to bring back sunny recollections.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay a moment, young lady, if you please!\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew Maule. \xe2\x80\x9cMy\r\nbusiness with your father is over. With yourself, it is now to begin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlice looked towards her father, in surprise and inquiry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Alice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Pyncheon, with some disturbance and confusion.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis young man\xe2\x80\x94his name is Matthew Maule\xe2\x80\x94professes, so far as I can\r\nunderstand him, to be able to discover, through your means, a certain\r\npaper or parchment, which was missing long before your birth. The\r\nimportance of the document in question renders it advisable to neglect\r\nno possible, even if improbable, method of regaining it. You will\r\ntherefore oblige me, my dear Alice, by answering this person\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninquiries, and complying with his lawful and reasonable requests, so\r\nfar as they may appear to have the aforesaid object in view. As I shall\r\nremain in the room, you need apprehend no rude nor unbecoming\r\ndeportment, on the young man\xe2\x80\x99s part; and, at your slightest wish, of\r\ncourse, the investigation, or whatever we may call it, shall\r\nimmediately be broken off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMistress Alice Pyncheon,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Matthew Maule, with the utmost\r\ndeference, but yet a half-hidden sarcasm in his look and tone, \xe2\x80\x9cwill no\r\ndoubt feel herself quite safe in her father\xe2\x80\x99s presence, and under his\r\nall-sufficient protection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly shall entertain no manner of apprehension, with my father\r\nat hand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alice with maidenly dignity. \xe2\x80\x9cNeither do I conceive that\r\na lady, while true to herself, can have aught to fear from whomsoever,\r\nor in any circumstances!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor Alice! By what unhappy impulse did she thus put herself at once on\r\nterms of defiance against a strength which she could not estimate?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, Mistress Alice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew Maule, handing a chair,\xe2\x80\x94gracefully\r\nenough, for a craftsman, \xe2\x80\x9cwill it please you only to sit down, and do\r\nme the favor (though altogether beyond a poor carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s deserts) to\r\nfix your eyes on mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlice complied, She was very proud. Setting aside all advantages of\r\nrank, this fair girl deemed herself conscious of a power\xe2\x80\x94combined of\r\nbeauty, high, unsullied purity, and the preservative force of\r\nwomanhood\xe2\x80\x94that could make her sphere impenetrable, unless betrayed by\r\ntreachery within. She instinctively knew, it may be, that some sinister\r\nor evil potency was now striving to pass her barriers; nor would she\r\ndecline the contest. So Alice put woman\xe2\x80\x99s might against man\xe2\x80\x99s might; a\r\nmatch not often equal on the part of woman.\r\n\r\nHer father meanwhile had turned away, and seemed absorbed in the\r\ncontemplation of a landscape by Claude, where a shadowy and\r\nsun-streaked vista penetrated so remotely into an ancient wood, that it\r\nwould have been no wonder if his fancy had lost itself in the picture\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbewildering depths. But, in truth, the picture was no more to him at\r\nthat moment than the blank wall against which it hung. His mind was\r\nhaunted with the many and strange tales which he had heard, attributing\r\nmysterious if not supernatural endowments to these Maules, as well the\r\ngrandson here present as his two immediate ancestors. Mr. Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlong residence abroad, and intercourse with men of wit and\r\nfashion,\xe2\x80\x94courtiers, worldlings, and free-thinkers,\xe2\x80\x94had done much\r\ntowards obliterating the grim Puritan superstitions, which no man of\r\nNew England birth at that early period could entirely escape. But, on\r\nthe other hand, had not a whole community believed Maule\xe2\x80\x99s grandfather\r\nto be a wizard? Had not the crime been proved? Had not the wizard died\r\nfor it? Had he not bequeathed a legacy of hatred against the Pyncheons\r\nto this only grandson, who, as it appeared, was now about to exercise a\r\nsubtle influence over the daughter of his enemy\xe2\x80\x99s house? Might not this\r\ninfluence be the same that was called witchcraft?\r\n\r\nTurning half around, he caught a glimpse of Maule\xe2\x80\x99s figure in the\r\nlooking-glass. At some paces from Alice, with his arms uplifted in the\r\nair, the carpenter made a gesture as if directing downward a slow,\r\nponderous, and invisible weight upon the maiden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay, Maule!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mr. Pyncheon, stepping forward. \xe2\x80\x9cI forbid your\r\nproceeding further!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray, my dear father, do not interrupt the young man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alice,\r\nwithout changing her position. \xe2\x80\x9cHis efforts, I assure you, will prove\r\nvery harmless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain Mr. Pyncheon turned his eyes towards the Claude. It was then his\r\ndaughter\xe2\x80\x99s will, in opposition to his own, that the experiment should\r\nbe fully tried. Henceforth, therefore, he did but consent, not urge it.\r\nAnd was it not for her sake far more than for his own that he desired\r\nits success? That lost parchment once restored, the beautiful Alice\r\nPyncheon, with the rich dowry which he could then bestow, might wed an\r\nEnglish duke or a German reigning-prince, instead of some New England\r\nclergyman or lawyer! At the thought, the ambitious father almost\r\nconsented, in his heart, that, if the devil\xe2\x80\x99s power were needed to the\r\naccomplishment of this great object, Maule might evoke him. Alice\xe2\x80\x99s own\r\npurity would be her safeguard.\r\n\r\nWith his mind full of imaginary magnificence, Mr. Pyncheon heard a\r\nhalf-uttered exclamation from his daughter. It was very faint and low;\r\nso indistinct that there seemed but half a will to shape out the words,\r\nand too undefined a purport to be intelligible. Yet it was a call for\r\nhelp!\xe2\x80\x94his conscience never doubted it;\xe2\x80\x94and, little more than a whisper\r\nto his ear, it was a dismal shriek, and long reechoed so, in the region\r\nround his heart! But this time the father did not turn.\r\n\r\nAfter a further interval, Maule spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBehold your daughter,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nMr. Pyncheon came hastily forward. The carpenter was standing erect in\r\nfront of Alice\xe2\x80\x99s chair, and pointing his finger towards the maiden with\r\nan expression of triumphant power, the limits of which could not be\r\ndefined, as, indeed, its scope stretched vaguely towards the unseen and\r\nthe infinite. Alice sat in an attitude of profound repose, with the\r\nlong brown lashes drooping over her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere she is!\xe2\x80\x9d said the carpenter. \xe2\x80\x9cSpeak to her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlice! My daughter!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mr. Pyncheon. \xe2\x80\x9cMy own Alice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not stir.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLouder!\xe2\x80\x9d said Maule, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlice! Awake!\xe2\x80\x9d cried her father. \xe2\x80\x9cIt troubles me to see you thus!\r\nAwake!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe spoke loudly, with terror in his voice, and close to that delicate\r\near which had always been so sensitive to every discord. But the sound\r\nevidently reached her not. It is indescribable what a sense of remote,\r\ndim, unattainable distance betwixt himself and Alice was impressed on\r\nthe father by this impossibility of reaching her with his voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBest touch her!\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew Maule \xe2\x80\x9cShake the girl, and roughly, too!\r\nMy hands are hardened with too much use of axe, saw, and plane,\xe2\x80\x94else I\r\nmight help you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Pyncheon took her hand, and pressed it with the earnestness of\r\nstartled emotion. He kissed her, with so great a heart-throb in the\r\nkiss, that he thought she must needs feel it. Then, in a gust of anger\r\nat her insensibility, he shook her maiden form with a violence which,\r\nthe next moment, it affrighted him to remember. He withdrew his\r\nencircling arms, and Alice\xe2\x80\x94whose figure, though flexible, had been\r\nwholly impassive\xe2\x80\x94relapsed into the same attitude as before these\r\nattempts to arouse her. Maule having shifted his position, her face was\r\nturned towards him slightly, but with what seemed to be a reference of\r\nher very slumber to his guidance.\r\n\r\nThen it was a strange sight to behold how the man of conventionalities\r\nshook the powder out of his periwig; how the reserved and stately\r\ngentleman forgot his dignity; how the gold-embroidered waistcoat\r\nflickered and glistened in the firelight with the convulsion of rage,\r\nterror, and sorrow in the human heart that was beating under it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVillain!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mr. Pyncheon, shaking his clenched fist at Maule. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nand the fiend together have robbed me of my daughter. Give her back,\r\nspawn of the old wizard, or you shall climb Gallows Hill in your\r\ngrandfather\xe2\x80\x99s footsteps!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSoftly, Mr. Pyncheon!\xe2\x80\x9d said the carpenter with scornful composure.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSoftly, an\xe2\x80\x99 it please your worship, else you will spoil those rich\r\nlace-ruffles at your wrists! Is it my crime if you have sold your\r\ndaughter for the mere hope of getting a sheet of yellow parchment into\r\nyour clutch? There sits Mistress Alice quietly asleep. Now let Matthew\r\nMaule try whether she be as proud as the carpenter found her awhile\r\nsince.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe spoke, and Alice responded, with a soft, subdued, inward\r\nacquiescence, and a bending of her form towards him, like the flame of\r\na torch when it indicates a gentle draught of air. He beckoned with his\r\nhand, and, rising from her chair,\xe2\x80\x94blindly, but undoubtingly, as tending\r\nto her sure and inevitable centre,\xe2\x80\x94the proud Alice approached him. He\r\nwaved her back, and, retreating, Alice sank again into her seat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is mine!\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew Maule. \xe2\x80\x9cMine, by the right of the strongest\r\nspirit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the further progress of the legend, there is a long, grotesque, and\r\noccasionally awe-striking account of the carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s incantations (if\r\nso they are to be called), with a view of discovering the lost\r\ndocument. It appears to have been his object to convert the mind of\r\nAlice into a kind of telescopic medium, through which Mr. Pyncheon and\r\nhimself might obtain a glimpse into the spiritual world. He succeeded,\r\naccordingly, in holding an imperfect sort of intercourse, at one\r\nremove, with the departed personages in whose custody the so much\r\nvalued secret had been carried beyond the precincts of earth. During\r\nher trance, Alice described three figures as being present to her\r\nspiritualized perception. One was an aged, dignified, stern-looking\r\ngentleman, clad as for a solemn festival in grave and costly attire,\r\nbut with a great blood-stain on his richly wrought band; the second, an\r\naged man, meanly dressed, with a dark and malign countenance, and a\r\nbroken halter about his neck; the third, a person not so advanced in\r\nlife as the former two, but beyond the middle age, wearing a coarse\r\nwoollen tunic and leather breeches, and with a carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s rule\r\nsticking out of his side pocket. These three visionary characters\r\npossessed a mutual knowledge of the missing document. One of them, in\r\ntruth,\xe2\x80\x94it was he with the blood-stain on his band,\xe2\x80\x94seemed, unless his\r\ngestures were misunderstood, to hold the parchment in his immediate\r\nkeeping, but was prevented by his two partners in the mystery from\r\ndisburdening himself of the trust. Finally, when he showed a purpose of\r\nshouting forth the secret loudly enough to be heard from his own sphere\r\ninto that of mortals, his companions struggled with him, and pressed\r\ntheir hands over his mouth; and forthwith\xe2\x80\x94whether that he were choked\r\nby it, or that the secret itself was of a crimson hue\xe2\x80\x94there was a fresh\r\nflow of blood upon his band. Upon this, the two meanly dressed figures\r\nmocked and jeered at the much-abashed old dignitary, and pointed their\r\nfingers at the stain.\r\n\r\nAt this juncture, Maule turned to Mr. Pyncheon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will never be allowed,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cThe custody of this secret, that\r\nwould so enrich his heirs, makes part of your grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nretribution. He must choke with it until it is no longer of any value.\r\nAnd keep you the House of the Seven Gables! It is too dear bought an\r\ninheritance, and too heavy with the curse upon it, to be shifted yet\r\nawhile from the Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s posterity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Pyncheon tried to speak, but\xe2\x80\x94what with fear and passion\xe2\x80\x94could make\r\nonly a gurgling murmur in his throat. The carpenter smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha, worshipful sir!\xe2\x80\x94so you have old Maule\xe2\x80\x99s blood to drink!\xe2\x80\x9d said he\r\njeeringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFiend in man\xe2\x80\x99s shape! why dost thou keep dominion over my child?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncried Mr. Pyncheon, when his choked utterance could make way. \xe2\x80\x9cGive me\r\nback my daughter. Then go thy ways; and may we never meet again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour daughter!\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew Maule. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, she is fairly mine!\r\nNevertheless, not to be too hard with fair Mistress Alice, I will leave\r\nher in your keeping; but I do not warrant you that she shall never have\r\noccasion to remember Maule, the carpenter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe waved his hands with an upward motion; and, after a few repetitions\r\nof similar gestures, the beautiful Alice Pyncheon awoke from her\r\nstrange trance. She awoke without the slightest recollection of her\r\nvisionary experience; but as one losing herself in a momentary reverie,\r\nand returning to the consciousness of actual life, in almost as brief\r\nan interval as the down-sinking flame of the hearth should quiver again\r\nup the chimney. On recognizing Matthew Maule, she assumed an air of\r\nsomewhat cold but gentle dignity, the rather, as there was a certain\r\npeculiar smile on the carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s visage that stirred the native pride\r\nof the fair Alice. So ended, for that time, the quest for the lost\r\ntitle-deed of the Pyncheon territory at the Eastward; nor, though often\r\nsubsequently renewed, has it ever yet befallen a Pyncheon to set his\r\neye upon that parchment.\r\n\r\nBut, alas for the beautiful, the gentle, yet too haughty Alice! A power\r\nthat she little dreamed of had laid its grasp upon her maiden soul. A\r\nwill, most unlike her own, constrained her to do its grotesque and\r\nfantastic bidding. Her father as it proved, had martyred his poor child\r\nto an inordinate desire for measuring his land by miles instead of\r\nacres. And, therefore, while Alice Pyncheon lived, she was Maule\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nslave, in a bondage more humiliating, a thousand-fold, than that which\r\nbinds its chain around the body. Seated by his humble fireside, Maule\r\nhad but to wave his hand; and, wherever the proud lady chanced to\r\nbe,\xe2\x80\x94whether in her chamber, or entertaining her father\xe2\x80\x99s stately\r\nguests, or worshipping at church,\xe2\x80\x94whatever her place or occupation, her\r\nspirit passed from beneath her own control, and bowed itself to Maule.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlice, laugh!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the carpenter, beside his hearth, would say; or perhaps\r\nintensely will it, without a spoken word. And, even were it\r\nprayer-time, or at a funeral, Alice must break into wild laughter.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlice, be sad!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and, at the instant, down would come her tears,\r\nquenching all the mirth of those around her like sudden rain upon a\r\nbonfire. \xe2\x80\x9cAlice, dance.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and dance she would, not in such court-like\r\nmeasures as she had learned abroad, but some high-paced jig, or\r\nhop-skip rigadoon, befitting the brisk lasses at a rustic merry-making.\r\nIt seemed to be Maule\xe2\x80\x99s impulse, not to ruin Alice, nor to visit her\r\nwith any black or gigantic mischief, which would have crowned her\r\nsorrows with the grace of tragedy, but to wreak a low, ungenerous scorn\r\nupon her. Thus all the dignity of life was lost. She felt herself too\r\nmuch abased, and longed to change natures with some worm!\r\n\r\nOne evening, at a bridal party (but not her own; for, so lost from\r\nself-control, she would have deemed it sin to marry), poor Alice was\r\nbeckoned forth by her unseen despot, and constrained, in her gossamer\r\nwhite dress and satin slippers, to hasten along the street to the mean\r\ndwelling of a laboring-man. There was laughter and good cheer within;\r\nfor Matthew Maule, that night, was to wed the laborer\xe2\x80\x99s daughter, and\r\nhad summoned proud Alice Pyncheon to wait upon his bride. And so she\r\ndid; and when the twain were one, Alice awoke out of her enchanted\r\nsleep. Yet, no longer proud,\xe2\x80\x94humbly, and with a smile all steeped in\r\nsadness,\xe2\x80\x94she kissed Maule\xe2\x80\x99s wife, and went her way. It was an inclement\r\nnight; the southeast wind drove the mingled snow and rain into her\r\nthinly sheltered bosom; her satin slippers were wet through and\r\nthrough, as she trod the muddy sidewalks. The next day a cold; soon, a\r\nsettled cough; anon, a hectic cheek, a wasted form, that sat beside the\r\nharpsichord, and filled the house with music! Music in which a strain\r\nof the heavenly choristers was echoed! Oh; joy! For Alice had borne her\r\nlast humiliation! Oh, greater joy! For Alice was penitent of her one\r\nearthly sin, and proud no more!\r\n\r\nThe Pyncheons made a great funeral for Alice. The kith and kin were\r\nthere, and the whole respectability of the town besides. But, last in\r\nthe procession, came Matthew Maule, gnashing his teeth, as if he would\r\nhave bitten his own heart in twain,\xe2\x80\x94the darkest and wofullest man that\r\never walked behind a corpse! He meant to humble Alice, not to kill her;\r\nbut he had taken a woman\xe2\x80\x99s delicate soul into his rude gripe, to play\r\nwith\xe2\x80\x94and she was dead!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIV.\r\nPh\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s Good-Bye\r\n\r\n\r\nHolgrave, plunging into his tale with the energy and absorption natural\r\nto a young author, had given a good deal of action to the parts capable\r\nof being developed and exemplified in that manner. He now observed that\r\na certain remarkable drowsiness (wholly unlike that with which the\r\nreader possibly feels himself affected) had been flung over the senses\r\nof his auditress. It was the effect, unquestionably, of the mystic\r\ngesticulations by which he had sought to bring bodily before Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nperception the figure of the mesmerizing carpenter. With the lids\r\ndrooping over her eyes,\xe2\x80\x94now lifted for an instant, and drawn down again\r\nas with leaden weights,\xe2\x80\x94she leaned slightly towards him, and seemed\r\nalmost to regulate her breath by his. Holgrave gazed at her, as he\r\nrolled up his manuscript, and recognized an incipient stage of that\r\ncurious psychological condition which, as he had himself told Ph\xc5\x93be, he\r\npossessed more than an ordinary faculty of producing. A veil was\r\nbeginning to be muffled about her, in which she could behold only him,\r\nand live only in his thoughts and emotions. His glance, as he fastened\r\nit on the young girl, grew involuntarily more concentrated; in his\r\nattitude there was the consciousness of power, investing his hardly\r\nmature figure with a dignity that did not belong to its physical\r\nmanifestation. It was evident, that, with but one wave of his hand and\r\na corresponding effort of his will, he could complete his mastery over\r\nPh\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s yet free and virgin spirit: he could establish an influence\r\nover this good, pure, and simple child, as dangerous, and perhaps as\r\ndisastrous, as that which the carpenter of his legend had acquired and\r\nexercised over the ill-fated Alice.\r\n\r\nTo a disposition like Holgrave\xe2\x80\x99s, at once speculative and active, there\r\nis no temptation so great as the opportunity of acquiring empire over\r\nthe human spirit; nor any idea more seductive to a young man than to\r\nbecome the arbiter of a young girl\xe2\x80\x99s destiny. Let us,\r\ntherefore,\xe2\x80\x94whatever his defects of nature and education, and in spite\r\nof his scorn for creeds and institutions,\xe2\x80\x94concede to the\r\ndaguerreotypist the rare and high quality of reverence for another\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nindividuality. Let us allow him integrity, also, forever after to be\r\nconfided in; since he forbade himself to twine that one link more which\r\nmight have rendered his spell over Ph\xc5\x93be indissoluble.\r\n\r\nHe made a slight gesture upward with his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou really mortify me, my dear Miss Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, smiling\r\nhalf-sarcastically at her. \xe2\x80\x9cMy poor story, it is but too evident, will\r\nnever do for Godey or Graham! Only think of your falling asleep at what\r\nI hoped the newspaper critics would pronounce a most brilliant,\r\npowerful, imaginative, pathetic, and original winding up! Well, the\r\nmanuscript must serve to light lamps with;\xe2\x80\x94if, indeed, being so imbued\r\nwith my gentle dulness, it is any longer capable of flame!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMe asleep! How can you say so?\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be, as unconscious of the\r\ncrisis through which she had passed as an infant of the precipice to\r\nthe verge of which it has rolled. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! I consider myself as having\r\nbeen very attentive; and, though I don\xe2\x80\x99t remember the incidents quite\r\ndistinctly, yet I have an impression of a vast deal of trouble and\r\ncalamity,\xe2\x80\x94so, no doubt, the story will prove exceedingly attractive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time the sun had gone down, and was tinting the clouds towards\r\nthe zenith with those bright hues which are not seen there until some\r\ntime after sunset, and when the horizon has quite lost its richer\r\nbrilliancy. The moon, too, which had long been climbing overhead, and\r\nunobtrusively melting its disk into the azure,\xe2\x80\x94like an ambitious\r\ndemagogue, who hides his aspiring purpose by assuming the prevalent hue\r\nof popular sentiment,\xe2\x80\x94now began to shine out, broad and oval, in its\r\nmiddle pathway. These silvery beams were already powerful enough to\r\nchange the character of the lingering daylight. They softened and\r\nembellished the aspect of the old house; although the shadows fell\r\ndeeper into the angles of its many gables, and lay brooding under the\r\nprojecting story, and within the half-open door. With the lapse of\r\nevery moment, the garden grew more picturesque; the fruit-trees,\r\nshrubbery, and flower-bushes had a dark obscurity among them. The\r\ncommonplace characteristics\xe2\x80\x94which, at noontide, it seemed to have taken\r\na century of sordid life to accumulate\xe2\x80\x94were now transfigured by a charm\r\nof romance. A hundred mysterious years were whispering among the\r\nleaves, whenever the slight sea-breeze found its way thither and\r\nstirred them. Through the foliage that roofed the little summer-house\r\nthe moonlight flickered to and fro, and fell silvery white on the dark\r\nfloor, the table, and the circular bench, with a continual shift and\r\nplay, according as the chinks and wayward crevices among the twigs\r\nadmitted or shut out the glimmer.\r\n\r\nSo sweetly cool was the atmosphere, after all the feverish day, that\r\nthe summer eve might be fancied as sprinkling dews and liquid\r\nmoonlight, with a dash of icy temper in them, out of a silver vase.\r\nHere and there, a few drops of this freshness were scattered on a human\r\nheart, and gave it youth again, and sympathy with the eternal youth of\r\nnature. The artist chanced to be one on whom the reviving influence\r\nfell. It made him feel\xe2\x80\x94what he sometimes almost forgot, thrust so early\r\nas he had been into the rude struggle of man with man\xe2\x80\x94how youthful he\r\nstill was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I never watched the coming of so\r\nbeautiful an eve, and never felt anything so very much like happiness\r\nas at this moment. After all, what a good world we live in! How good,\r\nand beautiful! How young it is, too, with nothing really rotten or\r\nage-worn in it! This old house, for example, which sometimes has\r\npositively oppressed my breath with its smell of decaying timber! And\r\nthis garden, where the black mould always clings to my spade, as if I\r\nwere a sexton delving in a graveyard! Could I keep the feeling that now\r\npossesses me, the garden would every day be virgin soil, with the\r\nearth\xe2\x80\x99s first freshness in the flavor of its beans and squashes; and\r\nthe house!\xe2\x80\x94it would be like a bower in Eden, blossoming with the\r\nearliest roses that God ever made. Moonlight, and the sentiment in\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s heart responsive to it, are the greatest of renovators and\r\nreformers. And all other reform and renovation, I suppose, will prove\r\nto be no better than moonshine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been happier than I am now; at least, much gayer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be\r\nthoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cYet I am sensible of a great charm in this brightening\r\nmoonlight; and I love to watch how the day, tired as it is, lags away\r\nreluctantly, and hates to be called yesterday so soon. I never cared\r\nmuch about moonlight before. What is there, I wonder, so beautiful in\r\nit, to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have never felt it before?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the artist, looking\r\nearnestly at the girl through the twilight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be; \xe2\x80\x9cand life does not look the same, now that I\r\nhave felt it so. It seems as if I had looked at everything, hitherto,\r\nin broad daylight, or else in the ruddy light of a cheerful fire,\r\nglimmering and dancing through a room. Ah, poor me!\xe2\x80\x9d she added, with a\r\nhalf-melancholy laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall never be so merry as before I knew\r\nCousin Hepzibah and poor Cousin Clifford. I have grown a great deal\r\nolder, in this little time. Older, and, I hope, wiser, and,\xe2\x80\x94not exactly\r\nsadder,\xe2\x80\x94but, certainly, with not half so much lightness in my spirits!\r\nI have given them my sunshine, and have been glad to give it; but, of\r\ncourse, I cannot both give and keep it. They are welcome,\r\nnotwithstanding!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have lost nothing, Ph\xc5\x93be, worth keeping, nor which it was possible\r\nto keep,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holgrave after a pause. \xe2\x80\x9cOur first youth is of no value;\r\nfor we are never conscious of it until after it is gone. But\r\nsometimes\xe2\x80\x94always, I suspect, unless one is exceedingly\r\nunfortunate\xe2\x80\x94there comes a sense of second youth, gushing out of the\r\nheart\xe2\x80\x99s joy at being in love; or, possibly, it may come to crown some\r\nother grand festival in life, if any other such there be. This\r\nbemoaning of one\xe2\x80\x99s self (as you do now) over the first, careless,\r\nshallow gayety of youth departed, and this profound happiness at youth\r\nregained,\xe2\x80\x94so much deeper and richer than that we lost,\xe2\x80\x94are essential to\r\nthe soul\xe2\x80\x99s development. In some cases, the two states come almost\r\nsimultaneously, and mingle the sadness and the rapture in one\r\nmysterious emotion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hardly think I understand you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo wonder,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Holgrave, smiling; \xe2\x80\x9cfor I have told you a secret\r\nwhich I hardly began to know before I found myself giving it utterance.\r\nRemember it, however; and when the truth becomes clear to you, then\r\nthink of this moonlight scene!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is entirely moonlight now, except only a little flush of faint\r\ncrimson, upward from the west, between those buildings,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked\r\nPh\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cI must go in. Cousin Hepzibah is not quick at figures, and will\r\ngive herself a headache over the day\xe2\x80\x99s accounts, unless I help her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Holgrave detained her a little longer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Hepzibah tells me,\xe2\x80\x9d observed he, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you return to the country\r\nin a few days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but only for a little while,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be; \xe2\x80\x9cfor I look upon\r\nthis as my present home. I go to make a few arrangements, and to take a\r\nmore deliberate leave of my mother and friends. It is pleasant to live\r\nwhere one is much desired and very useful; and I think I may have the\r\nsatisfaction of feeling myself so here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou surely may, and more than you imagine,\xe2\x80\x9d said the artist. \xe2\x80\x9cWhatever\r\nhealth, comfort, and natural life exists in the house is embodied in\r\nyour person. These blessings came along with you, and will vanish when\r\nyou leave the threshold. Miss Hepzibah, by secluding herself from\r\nsociety, has lost all true relation with it, and is, in fact, dead;\r\nalthough she galvanizes herself into a semblance of life, and stands\r\nbehind her counter, afflicting the world with a\r\ngreatly-to-be-deprecated scowl. Your poor cousin Clifford is another\r\ndead and long-buried person, on whom the governor and council have\r\nwrought a necromantic miracle. I should not wonder if he were to\r\ncrumble away, some morning, after you are gone, and nothing be seen of\r\nhim more, except a heap of dust. Miss Hepzibah, at any rate, will lose\r\nwhat little flexibility she has. They both exist by you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be very sorry to think so,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it\r\nis true that my small abilities were precisely what they needed; and I\r\nhave a real interest in their welfare,\xe2\x80\x94an odd kind of motherly\r\nsentiment,\xe2\x80\x94which I wish you would not laugh at! And let me tell you\r\nfrankly, Mr. Holgrave, I am sometimes puzzled to know whether you wish\r\nthem well or ill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly,\xe2\x80\x9d said the daguerreotypist, \xe2\x80\x9cI do feel an interest in this\r\nantiquated, poverty-stricken old maiden lady, and this degraded and\r\nshattered gentleman,\xe2\x80\x94this abortive lover of the beautiful. A kindly\r\ninterest, too, helpless old children that they are! But you have no\r\nconception what a different kind of heart mine is from your own. It is\r\nnot my impulse, as regards these two individuals, either to help or\r\nhinder; but to look on, to analyze, to explain matters to myself, and\r\nto comprehend the drama which, for almost two hundred years, has been\r\ndragging its slow length over the ground where you and I now tread. If\r\npermitted to witness the close, I doubt not to derive a moral\r\nsatisfaction from it, go matters how they may. There is a conviction\r\nwithin me that the end draws nigh. But, though Providence sent you\r\nhither to help, and sends me only as a privileged and meet spectator, I\r\npledge myself to lend these unfortunate beings whatever aid I can!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would speak more plainly,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Ph\xc5\x93be, perplexed and\r\ndispleased; \xe2\x80\x9cand, above all, that you would feel more like a Christian\r\nand a human being! How is it possible to see people in distress without\r\ndesiring, more than anything else, to help and comfort them? You talk\r\nas if this old house were a theatre; and you seem to look at Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nand Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s misfortunes, and those of generations before them, as a\r\ntragedy, such as I have seen acted in the hall of a country hotel, only\r\nthe present one appears to be played exclusively for your amusement. I\r\ndo not like this. The play costs the performers too much, and the\r\naudience is too cold-hearted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are severe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holgrave, compelled to recognize a degree of\r\ntruth in the piquant sketch of his own mood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Ph\xc5\x93be, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat can you mean by your conviction,\r\nwhich you tell me of, that the end is drawing near? Do you know of any\r\nnew trouble hanging over my poor relatives? If so, tell me at once, and\r\nI will not leave them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgive me, Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x9d said the daguerreotypist, holding out his hand, to\r\nwhich the girl was constrained to yield her own. \xe2\x80\x9cI am somewhat of a\r\nmystic, it must be confessed. The tendency is in my blood, together\r\nwith the faculty of mesmerism, which might have brought me to Gallows\r\nHill, in the good old times of witchcraft. Believe me, if I were really\r\naware of any secret, the disclosure of which would benefit your\r\nfriends,\xe2\x80\x94who are my own friends, likewise,\xe2\x80\x94you should learn it before\r\nwe part. But I have no such knowledge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou hold something back!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing,\xe2\x80\x94no secrets but my own,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cI can perceive,\r\nindeed, that Judge Pyncheon still keeps his eye on Clifford, in whose\r\nruin he had so large a share. His motives and intentions, however are a\r\nmystery to me. He is a determined and relentless man, with the genuine\r\ncharacter of an inquisitor; and had he any object to gain by putting\r\nClifford to the rack, I verily believe that he would wrench his joints\r\nfrom their sockets, in order to accomplish it. But, so wealthy and\r\neminent as he is,\xe2\x80\x94so powerful in his own strength, and in the support\r\nof society on all sides,\xe2\x80\x94what can Judge Pyncheon have to hope or fear\r\nfrom the imbecile, branded, half-torpid Clifford?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet,\xe2\x80\x9d urged Ph\xc5\x93be, \xe2\x80\x9cyou did speak as if misfortune were impending!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that was because I am morbid!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the artist. \xe2\x80\x9cMy mind has a\r\ntwist aside, like almost everybody\xe2\x80\x99s mind, except your own. Moreover,\r\nit is so strange to find myself an inmate of this old Pyncheon House,\r\nand sitting in this old garden\xe2\x80\x94(hark, how Maule\xe2\x80\x99s well is\r\nmurmuring!)\xe2\x80\x94that, were it only for this one circumstance, I cannot help\r\nfancying that Destiny is arranging its fifth act for a catastrophe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Ph\xc5\x93be with renewed vexation; for she was by nature as\r\nhostile to mystery as the sunshine to a dark corner. \xe2\x80\x9cYou puzzle me\r\nmore than ever!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen let us part friends!\xe2\x80\x9d said Holgrave, pressing her hand. \xe2\x80\x9cOr, if\r\nnot friends, let us part before you entirely hate me. You, who love\r\neverybody else in the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-by, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be frankly. \xe2\x80\x9cI do not mean to be angry a great\r\nwhile, and should be sorry to have you think so. There has Cousin\r\nHepzibah been standing in the shadow of the doorway, this quarter of an\r\nhour past! She thinks I stay too long in the damp garden. So,\r\ngood-night, and good-by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the second morning thereafter, Ph\xc5\x93be might have been seen, in her\r\nstraw bonnet, with a shawl on one arm and a little carpet-bag on the\r\nother, bidding adieu to Hepzibah and Cousin Clifford. She was to take a\r\nseat in the next train of cars, which would transport her to within\r\nhalf a dozen miles of her country village.\r\n\r\nThe tears were in Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s eyes; a smile, dewy with affectionate regret,\r\nwas glimmering around her pleasant mouth. She wondered how it came to\r\npass, that her life of a few weeks, here in this heavy-hearted old\r\nmansion, had taken such hold of her, and so melted into her\r\nassociations, as now to seem a more important centre-point of\r\nremembrance than all which had gone before. How had Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x94grim,\r\nsilent, and irresponsive to her overflow of cordial sentiment\xe2\x80\x94contrived\r\nto win so much love? And Clifford,\xe2\x80\x94in his abortive decay, with the\r\nmystery of fearful crime upon him, and the close prison-atmosphere yet\r\nlurking in his breath,\xe2\x80\x94how had he transformed himself into the simplest\r\nchild, whom Ph\xc5\x93be felt bound to watch over, and be, as it were, the\r\nprovidence of his unconsidered hours! Everything, at that instant of\r\nfarewell, stood out prominently to her view. Look where she would, lay\r\nher hand on what she might, the object responded to her consciousness,\r\nas if a moist human heart were in it.\r\n\r\nShe peeped from the window into the garden, and felt herself more\r\nregretful at leaving this spot of black earth, vitiated with such an\r\nage-long growth of weeds, than joyful at the idea of again scenting her\r\npine forests and fresh clover-fields. She called Chanticleer, his two\r\nwives, and the venerable chicken, and threw them some crumbs of bread\r\nfrom the breakfast-table. These being hastily gobbled up, the chicken\r\nspread its wings, and alighted close by Ph\xc5\x93be on the window-sill, where\r\nit looked gravely into her face and vented its emotions in a croak.\r\nPh\xc5\x93be bade it be a good old chicken during her absence, and promised to\r\nbring it a little bag of buckwheat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Hepzibah, \xe2\x80\x9cyou do not smile so naturally as when\r\nyou came to us! Then, the smile chose to shine out; now, you choose it\r\nshould. It is well that you are going back, for a little while, into\r\nyour native air. There has been too much weight on your spirits. The\r\nhouse is too gloomy and lonesome; the shop is full of vexations; and as\r\nfor me, I have no faculty of making things look brighter than they are.\r\nDear Clifford has been your only comfort!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome hither, Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d suddenly cried her cousin Clifford, who had said\r\nvery little all the morning. \xe2\x80\x9cClose!\xe2\x80\x94closer!\xe2\x80\x94and look me in the face!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPh\xc5\x93be put one of her small hands on each elbow of his chair, and leaned\r\nher face towards him, so that he might peruse it as carefully as he\r\nwould. It is probable that the latent emotions of this parting hour had\r\nrevived, in some degree, his bedimmed and enfeebled faculties. At any\r\nrate, Ph\xc5\x93be soon felt that, if not the profound insight of a seer, yet\r\na more than feminine delicacy of appreciation, was making her heart the\r\nsubject of its regard. A moment before, she had known nothing which she\r\nwould have sought to hide. Now, as if some secret were hinted to her\r\nown consciousness through the medium of another\xe2\x80\x99s perception, she was\r\nfain to let her eyelids droop beneath Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s gaze. A blush,\r\ntoo,\xe2\x80\x94the redder, because she strove hard to keep it down,\xe2\x80\x94ascended\r\nbigger and higher, in a tide of fitful progress, until even her brow\r\nwas all suffused with it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is enough, Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, with a melancholy smile. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I\r\nfirst saw you, you were the prettiest little maiden in the world; and\r\nnow you have deepened into beauty. Girlhood has passed into womanhood;\r\nthe bud is a bloom! Go, now\xe2\x80\x94I feel lonelier than I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPh\xc5\x93be took leave of the desolate couple, and passed through the shop,\r\ntwinkling her eyelids to shake off a dew-drop; for\xe2\x80\x94considering how\r\nbrief her absence was to be, and therefore the folly of being cast down\r\nabout it\xe2\x80\x94she would not so far acknowledge her tears as to dry them with\r\nher handkerchief. On the doorstep, she met the little urchin whose\r\nmarvellous feats of gastronomy have been recorded in the earlier pages\r\nof our narrative. She took from the window some specimen or other of\r\nnatural history,\xe2\x80\x94her eyes being too dim with moisture to inform her\r\naccurately whether it was a rabbit or a hippopotamus,\xe2\x80\x94put it into the\r\nchild\xe2\x80\x99s hand as a parting gift, and went her way. Old Uncle Venner was\r\njust coming out of his door, with a wood-horse and saw on his shoulder;\r\nand, trudging along the street, he scrupled not to keep company with\r\nPh\xc5\x93be, so far as their paths lay together; nor, in spite of his patched\r\ncoat and rusty beaver, and the curious fashion of his tow-cloth\r\ntrousers, could she find it in her heart to outwalk him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall miss you, next Sabbath afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d observed the street\r\nphilosopher. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is unaccountable how little while it takes some folks\r\nto grow just as natural to a man as his own breath; and, begging your\r\npardon, Miss Ph\xc5\x93be (though there can be no offence in an old man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsaying it), that\xe2\x80\x99s just what you\xe2\x80\x99ve grown to me! My years have been a\r\ngreat many, and your life is but just beginning; and yet, you are\r\nsomehow as familiar to me as if I had found you at my mother\xe2\x80\x99s door,\r\nand you had blossomed, like a running vine, all along my pathway since.\r\nCome back soon, or I shall be gone to my farm; for I begin to find\r\nthese wood-sawing jobs a little too tough for my back-ache.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery soon, Uncle Venner,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ph\xc5\x93be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd let it be all the sooner, Ph\xc5\x93be, for the sake of those poor souls\r\nyonder,\xe2\x80\x9d continued her companion. \xe2\x80\x9cThey can never do without you,\r\nnow,\xe2\x80\x94never, Ph\xc5\x93be; never\xe2\x80\x94no more than if one of God\xe2\x80\x99s angels had been\r\nliving with them, and making their dismal house pleasant and\r\ncomfortable! Don\xe2\x80\x99t it seem to you they\xe2\x80\x99d be in a sad case, if, some\r\npleasant summer morning like this, the angel should spread his wings,\r\nand fly to the place he came from? Well, just so they feel, now that\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re going home by the railroad!
