They can\xe2\x80\x99t bear it, Miss Ph\xc5\x93be; so\r\nbe sure to come back!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am no angel, Uncle Venner,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, smiling, as she offered him\r\nher hand at the street-corner. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, I suppose, people never feel so\r\nmuch like angels as when they are doing what little good they may. So I\r\nshall certainly come back!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus parted the old man and the rosy girl; and Ph\xc5\x93be took the wings of\r\nthe morning, and was soon flitting almost as rapidly away as if endowed\r\nwith the aerial locomotion of the angels to whom Uncle Venner had so\r\ngraciously compared her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXV.\r\nThe Scowl and Smile\r\n\r\n\r\nSeveral days passed over the Seven Gables, heavily and drearily enough.\r\nIn fact (not to attribute the whole gloom of sky and earth to the one\r\ninauspicious circumstance of Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s departure), an easterly storm had\r\nset in, and indefatigably apply itself to the task of making the black\r\nroof and walls of the old house look more cheerless than ever before.\r\nYet was the outside not half so cheerless as the interior. Poor\r\nClifford was cut off, at once, from all his scanty resources of\r\nenjoyment. Ph\xc5\x93be was not there; nor did the sunshine fall upon the\r\nfloor. The garden, with its muddy walks, and the chill, dripping\r\nfoliage of its summer-house, was an image to be shuddered at. Nothing\r\nflourished in the cold, moist, pitiless atmosphere, drifting with the\r\nbrackish scud of sea-breezes, except the moss along the joints of the\r\nshingle-roof, and the great bunch of weeds, that had lately been\r\nsuffering from drought, in the angle between the two front gables.\r\n\r\nAs for Hepzibah, she seemed not merely possessed with the east wind,\r\nbut to be, in her very person, only another phase of this gray and\r\nsullen spell of weather; the East-Wind itself, grim and disconsolate,\r\nin a rusty black silk gown, and with a turban of cloud-wreaths on its\r\nhead. The custom of the shop fell off, because a story got abroad that\r\nshe soured her small beer and other damageable commodities, by scowling\r\non them. It is, perhaps, true that the public had something reasonably\r\nto complain of in her deportment; but towards Clifford she was neither\r\nill-tempered nor unkind, nor felt less warmth of heart than always, had\r\nit been possible to make it reach him. The inutility of her best\r\nefforts, however, palsied the poor old gentlewoman. She could do little\r\nelse than sit silently in a corner of the room, when the wet pear-tree\r\nbranches, sweeping across the small windows, created a noonday dusk,\r\nwhich Hepzibah unconsciously darkened with her woe-begone aspect. It\r\nwas no fault of Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s. Everything\xe2\x80\x94even the old chairs and tables,\r\nthat had known what weather was for three or four such lifetimes as her\r\nown\xe2\x80\x94looked as damp and chill as if the present were their worst\r\nexperience. The picture of the Puritan Colonel shivered on the wall.\r\nThe house itself shivered, from every attic of its seven gables down to\r\nthe great kitchen fireplace, which served all the better as an emblem\r\nof the mansion\xe2\x80\x99s heart, because, though built for warmth, it was now so\r\ncomfortless and empty.\r\n\r\nHepzibah attempted to enliven matters by a fire in the parlor. But the\r\nstorm demon kept watch above, and, whenever a flame was kindled, drove\r\nthe smoke back again, choking the chimney\xe2\x80\x99s sooty throat with its own\r\nbreath. Nevertheless, during four days of this miserable storm,\r\nClifford wrapt himself in an old cloak, and occupied his customary\r\nchair. On the morning of the fifth, when summoned to breakfast, he\r\nresponded only by a broken-hearted murmur, expressive of a\r\ndetermination not to leave his bed. His sister made no attempt to\r\nchange his purpose. In fact, entirely as she loved him, Hepzibah could\r\nhardly have borne any longer the wretched duty\xe2\x80\x94so impracticable by her\r\nfew and rigid faculties\xe2\x80\x94of seeking pastime for a still sensitive, but\r\nruined mind, critical and fastidious, without force or volition. It was\r\nat least something short of positive despair, that to-day she might sit\r\nshivering alone, and not suffer continually a new grief, and\r\nunreasonable pang of remorse, at every fitful sigh of her fellow\r\nsufferer.\r\n\r\nBut Clifford, it seemed, though he did not make his appearance below\r\nstairs, had, after all, bestirred himself in quest of amusement. In the\r\ncourse of the forenoon, Hepzibah heard a note of music, which (there\r\nbeing no other tuneful contrivance in the House of the Seven Gables)\r\nshe knew must proceed from Alice Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s harpsichord. She was aware\r\nthat Clifford, in his youth, had possessed a cultivated taste for\r\nmusic, and a considerable degree of skill in its practice. It was\r\ndifficult, however, to conceive of his retaining an accomplishment to\r\nwhich daily exercise is so essential, in the measure indicated by the\r\nsweet, airy, and delicate, though most melancholy strain, that now\r\nstole upon her ear. Nor was it less marvellous that the long-silent\r\ninstrument should be capable of so much melody. Hepzibah involuntarily\r\nthought of the ghostly harmonies, prelusive of death in the family,\r\nwhich were attributed to the legendary Alice. But it was, perhaps,\r\nproof of the agency of other than spiritual fingers, that, after a few\r\ntouches, the chords seemed to snap asunder with their own vibrations,\r\nand the music ceased.\r\n\r\nBut a harsher sound succeeded to the mysterious notes; nor was the\r\neasterly day fated to pass without an event sufficient in itself to\r\npoison, for Hepzibah and Clifford, the balmiest air that ever brought\r\nthe humming-birds along with it. The final echoes of Alice Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nperformance (or Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s, if his we must consider it) were driven\r\naway by no less vulgar a dissonance than the ringing of the shop-bell.\r\nA foot was heard scraping itself on the threshold, and thence somewhat\r\nponderously stepping on the floor. Hepzibah delayed a moment, while\r\nmuffling herself in a faded shawl, which had been her defensive armor\r\nin a forty years\xe2\x80\x99 warfare against the east wind. A characteristic\r\nsound, however,\xe2\x80\x94neither a cough nor a hem, but a kind of rumbling and\r\nreverberating spasm in somebody\xe2\x80\x99s capacious depth of chest;\xe2\x80\x94impelled\r\nher to hurry forward, with that aspect of fierce faint-heartedness so\r\ncommon to women in cases of perilous emergency. Few of her sex, on such\r\noccasions, have ever looked so terrible as our poor scowling Hepzibah.\r\nBut the visitor quietly closed the shop-door behind him, stood up his\r\numbrella against the counter, and turned a visage of composed\r\nbenignity, to meet the alarm and anger which his appearance had\r\nexcited.\r\n\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s presentiment had not deceived her. It was no other than\r\nJudge Pyncheon, who, after in vain trying the front door, had now\r\neffected his entrance into the shop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you do, Cousin Hepzibah?\xe2\x80\x94and how does this most inclement\r\nweather affect our poor Clifford?\xe2\x80\x9d began the Judge; and wonderful it\r\nseemed, indeed, that the easterly storm was not put to shame, or, at\r\nany rate, a little mollified, by the genial benevolence of his smile.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could not rest without calling to ask, once more, whether I can in\r\nany manner promote his comfort, or your own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can do nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, controlling her agitation as well\r\nas she could. \xe2\x80\x9cI devote myself to Clifford. He has every comfort which\r\nhis situation admits of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut allow me to suggest, dear cousin,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the Judge, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\r\nerr,\xe2\x80\x94in all affection and kindness, no doubt, and with the very best\r\nintentions,\xe2\x80\x94but you do err, nevertheless, in keeping your brother so\r\nsecluded. Why insulate him thus from all sympathy and kindness?\r\nClifford, alas! has had too much of solitude. Now let him try\r\nsociety,\xe2\x80\x94the society, that is to say, of kindred and old friends. Let\r\nme, for instance, but see Clifford, and I will answer for the good\r\neffect of the interview.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou cannot see him,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cClifford has kept his bed\r\nsince yesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! How! Is he ill?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Judge Pyncheon, starting with what\r\nseemed to be angry alarm; for the very frown of the old Puritan\r\ndarkened through the room as he spoke. \xe2\x80\x9cNay, then, I must and will see\r\nhim! What if he should die?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is in no danger of death,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah,\xe2\x80\x94and added, with\r\nbitterness that she could repress no longer, \xe2\x80\x9cnone; unless he shall be\r\npersecuted to death, now, by the same man who long ago attempted it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCousin Hepzibah,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Judge, with an impressive earnestness of\r\nmanner, which grew even to tearful pathos as he proceeded, \xe2\x80\x9cis it\r\npossible that you do not perceive how unjust, how unkind, how\r\nunchristian, is this constant, this long-continued bitterness against\r\nme, for a part which I was constrained by duty and conscience, by the\r\nforce of law, and at my own peril, to act? What did I do, in detriment\r\nto Clifford, which it was possible to leave undone? How could you, his\r\nsister,\xe2\x80\x94if, for your never-ending sorrow, as it has been for mine, you\r\nhad known what I did,\xe2\x80\x94have, shown greater tenderness? And do you think,\r\ncousin, that it has cost me no pang?\xe2\x80\x94that it has left no anguish in my\r\nbosom, from that day to this, amidst all the prosperity with which\r\nHeaven has blessed me?\xe2\x80\x94or that I do not now rejoice, when it is deemed\r\nconsistent with the dues of public justice and the welfare of society\r\nthat this dear kinsman, this early friend, this nature so delicately\r\nand beautifully constituted,\xe2\x80\x94so unfortunate, let us pronounce him, and\r\nforbear to say, so guilty,\xe2\x80\x94that our own Clifford, in fine, should be\r\ngiven back to life, and its possibilities of enjoyment? Ah, you little\r\nknow me, Cousin Hepzibah! You little know this heart! It now throbs at\r\nthe thought of meeting him! There lives not the human being (except\r\nyourself,\xe2\x80\x94and you not more than I) who has shed so many tears for\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s calamity. You behold some of them now. There is none who\r\nwould so delight to promote his happiness! Try me, Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x94try me,\r\nCousin!\xe2\x80\x94try the man whom you have treated as your enemy and\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s!\xe2\x80\x94try Jaffrey Pyncheon, and you shall find him true, to the\r\nheart\xe2\x80\x99s core!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the name of Heaven,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hepzibah, provoked only to intenser\r\nindignation by this outgush of the inestimable tenderness of a stern\r\nnature,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cin God\xe2\x80\x99s name, whom you insult, and whose power I could almost\r\nquestion, since he hears you utter so many false words without palsying\r\nyour tongue,\xe2\x80\x94give over, I beseech you, this loathsome pretence of\r\naffection for your victim! You hate him! Say so, like a man! You\r\ncherish, at this moment, some black purpose against him in your heart!\r\nSpeak it out, at once!\xe2\x80\x94or, if you hope so to promote it better, hide it\r\ntill you can triumph in its success! But never speak again of your love\r\nfor my poor brother. I cannot bear it! It will drive me beyond a\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s decency! It will drive me mad! Forbear! Not another word! It\r\nwill make me spurn you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor once, Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s wrath had given her courage. She had spoken. But,\r\nafter all, was this unconquerable distrust of Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nintegrity, and this utter denial, apparently, of his claim to stand in\r\nthe ring of human sympathies,\xe2\x80\x94were they founded in any just perception\r\nof his character, or merely the offspring of a woman\xe2\x80\x99s unreasonable\r\nprejudice, deduced from nothing?\r\n\r\nThe Judge, beyond all question, was a man of eminent respectability.\r\nThe church acknowledged it; the state acknowledged it. It was denied by\r\nnobody. In all the very extensive sphere of those who knew him, whether\r\nin his public or private capacities, there was not an individual\xe2\x80\x94except\r\nHepzibah, and some lawless mystic, like the daguerreotypist, and,\r\npossibly, a few political opponents\xe2\x80\x94who would have dreamed of seriously\r\ndisputing his claim to a high and honorable place in the world\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nregard. Nor (we must do him the further justice to say) did Judge\r\nPyncheon himself, probably, entertain many or very frequent doubts,\r\nthat his enviable reputation accorded with his deserts. His conscience,\r\ntherefore, usually considered the surest witness to a man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nintegrity,\xe2\x80\x94his conscience, unless it might be for the little space of\r\nfive minutes in the twenty-four hours, or, now and then, some black day\r\nin the whole year\xe2\x80\x99s circle,\xe2\x80\x94his conscience bore an accordant testimony\r\nwith the world\xe2\x80\x99s laudatory voice. And yet, strong as this evidence may\r\nseem to be, we should hesitate to peril our own conscience on the\r\nassertion, that the Judge and the consenting world were right, and that\r\npoor Hepzibah with her solitary prejudice was wrong. Hidden from\r\nmankind,\xe2\x80\x94forgotten by himself, or buried so deeply under a sculptured\r\nand ornamented pile of ostentatious deeds that his daily life could\r\ntake no note of it,\xe2\x80\x94there may have lurked some evil and unsightly\r\nthing. Nay, we could almost venture to say, further, that a daily guilt\r\nmight have been acted by him, continually renewed, and reddening forth\r\nafresh, like the miraculous blood-stain of a murder, without his\r\nnecessarily and at every moment being aware of it.\r\n\r\nMen of strong minds, great force of character, and a hard texture of\r\nthe sensibilities, are very capable of falling into mistakes of this\r\nkind. They are ordinarily men to whom forms are of paramount\r\nimportance. Their field of action lies among the external phenomena of\r\nlife. They possess vast ability in grasping, and arranging, and\r\nappropriating to themselves, the big, heavy, solid unrealities, such as\r\ngold, landed estate, offices of trust and emolument, and public honors.\r\nWith these materials, and with deeds of goodly aspect, done in the\r\npublic eye, an individual of this class builds up, as it were, a tall\r\nand stately edifice, which, in the view of other people, and ultimately\r\nin his own view, is no other than the man\xe2\x80\x99s character, or the man\r\nhimself. Behold, therefore, a palace! Its splendid halls and suites of\r\nspacious apartments are floored with a mosaic-work of costly marbles;\r\nits windows, the whole height of each room, admit the sunshine through\r\nthe most transparent of plate-glass; its high cornices are gilded, and\r\nits ceilings gorgeously painted; and a lofty dome\xe2\x80\x94through which, from\r\nthe central pavement, you may gaze up to the sky, as with no\r\nobstructing medium between\xe2\x80\x94surmounts the whole. With what fairer and\r\nnobler emblem could any man desire to shadow forth his character? Ah!\r\nbut in some low and obscure nook,\xe2\x80\x94some narrow closet on the\r\nground-floor, shut, locked and bolted, and the key flung away,\xe2\x80\x94or\r\nbeneath the marble pavement, in a stagnant water-puddle, with the\r\nrichest pattern of mosaic-work above,\xe2\x80\x94may lie a corpse, half decayed,\r\nand still decaying, and diffusing its death-scent all through the\r\npalace! The inhabitant will not be conscious of it, for it has long\r\nbeen his daily breath! Neither will the visitors, for they smell only\r\nthe rich odors which the master sedulously scatters through the palace,\r\nand the incense which they bring, and delight to burn before him! Now\r\nand then, perchance, comes in a seer, before whose sadly gifted eye the\r\nwhole structure melts into thin air, leaving only the hidden nook, the\r\nbolted closet, with the cobwebs festooned over its forgotten door, or\r\nthe deadly hole under the pavement, and the decaying corpse within.\r\nHere, then, we are to seek the true emblem of the man\xe2\x80\x99s character, and\r\nof the deed that gives whatever reality it possesses to his life. And,\r\nbeneath the show of a marble palace, that pool of stagnant water, foul\r\nwith many impurities, and, perhaps, tinged with blood,\xe2\x80\x94that secret\r\nabomination, above which, possibly, he may say his prayers, without\r\nremembering it,\xe2\x80\x94is this man\xe2\x80\x99s miserable soul!\r\n\r\nTo apply this train of remark somewhat more closely to Judge Pyncheon.\r\nWe might say (without in the least imputing crime to a personage of his\r\neminent respectability) that there was enough of splendid rubbish in\r\nhis life to cover up and paralyze a more active and subtile conscience\r\nthan the Judge was ever troubled with. The purity of his judicial\r\ncharacter, while on the bench; the faithfulness of his public service\r\nin subsequent capacities; his devotedness to his party, and the rigid\r\nconsistency with which he had adhered to its principles, or, at all\r\nevents, kept pace with its organized movements; his remarkable zeal as\r\npresident of a Bible society; his unimpeachable integrity as treasurer\r\nof a widow\xe2\x80\x99s and orphan\xe2\x80\x99s fund; his benefits to horticulture, by\r\nproducing two much esteemed varieties of the pear and to agriculture,\r\nthrough the agency of the famous Pyncheon bull; the cleanliness of his\r\nmoral deportment, for a great many years past; the severity with which\r\nhe had frowned upon, and finally cast off, an expensive and dissipated\r\nson, delaying forgiveness until within the final quarter of an hour of\r\nthe young man\xe2\x80\x99s life; his prayers at morning and eventide, and graces\r\nat meal-time; his efforts in furtherance of the temperance cause; his\r\nconfining himself, since the last attack of the gout, to five diurnal\r\nglasses of old sherry wine; the snowy whiteness of his linen, the\r\npolish of his boots, the handsomeness of his gold-headed cane, the\r\nsquare and roomy fashion of his coat, and the fineness of its material,\r\nand, in general, the studied propriety of his dress and equipment; the\r\nscrupulousness with which he paid public notice, in the street, by a\r\nbow, a lifting of the hat, a nod, or a motion of the hand, to all and\r\nsundry of his acquaintances, rich or poor; the smile of broad\r\nbenevolence wherewith he made it a point to gladden the whole\r\nworld,\xe2\x80\x94what room could possibly be found for darker traits in a\r\nportrait made up of lineaments like these? This proper face was what he\r\nbeheld in the looking-glass. This admirably arranged life was what he\r\nwas conscious of in the progress of every day. Then might not he claim\r\nto be its result and sum, and say to himself and the community, \xe2\x80\x9cBehold\r\nJudge Pyncheon there\xe2\x80\x9d?\r\n\r\nAnd allowing that, many, many years ago, in his early and reckless\r\nyouth, he had committed some one wrong act,\xe2\x80\x94or that, even now, the\r\ninevitable force of circumstances should occasionally make him do one\r\nquestionable deed among a thousand praiseworthy, or, at least,\r\nblameless ones,\xe2\x80\x94would you characterize the Judge by that one necessary\r\ndeed, and that half-forgotten act, and let it overshadow the fair\r\naspect of a lifetime? What is there so ponderous in evil, that a\r\nthumb\xe2\x80\x99s bigness of it should outweigh the mass of things not evil which\r\nwere heaped into the other scale! This scale and balance system is a\r\nfavorite one with people of Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s brotherhood. A hard, cold\r\nman, thus unfortunately situated, seldom or never looking inward, and\r\nresolutely taking his idea of himself from what purports to be his\r\nimage as reflected in the mirror of public opinion, can scarcely arrive\r\nat true self-knowledge, except through loss of property and reputation.\r\nSickness will not always help him do it; not always the death-hour!\r\n\r\nBut our affair now is with Judge Pyncheon as he stood confronting the\r\nfierce outbreak of Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s wrath. Without premeditation, to her own\r\nsurprise, and indeed terror, she had given vent, for once, to the\r\ninveteracy of her resentment, cherished against this kinsman for thirty\r\nyears.\r\n\r\nThus far the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s countenance had expressed mild forbearance,\xe2\x80\x94grave\r\nand almost gentle deprecation of his cousin\xe2\x80\x99s unbecoming violence,\xe2\x80\x94free\r\nand Christian-like forgiveness of the wrong inflicted by her words. But\r\nwhen those words were irrevocably spoken, his look assumed sternness,\r\nthe sense of power, and immitigable resolve; and this with so natural\r\nand imperceptible a change, that it seemed as if the iron man had stood\r\nthere from the first, and the meek man not at all. The effect was as\r\nwhen the light, vapory clouds, with their soft coloring, suddenly\r\nvanish from the stony brow of a precipitous mountain, and leave there\r\nthe frown which you at once feel to be eternal. Hepzibah almost adopted\r\nthe insane belief that it was her old Puritan ancestor, and not the\r\nmodern Judge, on whom she had just been wreaking the bitterness of her\r\nheart. Never did a man show stronger proof of the lineage attributed to\r\nhim than Judge Pyncheon, at this crisis, by his unmistakable\r\nresemblance to the picture in the inner room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCousin Hepzibah,\xe2\x80\x9d said he very calmly, \xe2\x80\x9cit is time to have done with\r\nthis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith all my heart!\xe2\x80\x9d answered she. \xe2\x80\x9cThen, why do you persecute us any\r\nlonger? Leave poor Clifford and me in peace. Neither of us desires\r\nanything better!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my purpose to see Clifford before I leave this house,\xe2\x80\x9d continued\r\nthe Judge. \xe2\x80\x9cDo not act like a madwoman, Hepzibah! I am his only friend,\r\nand an all-powerful one. Has it never occurred to you,\xe2\x80\x94are you so blind\r\nas not to have seen,\xe2\x80\x94that, without not merely my consent, but my\r\nefforts, my representations, the exertion of my whole influence,\r\npolitical, official, personal, Clifford would never have been what you\r\ncall free? Did you think his release a triumph over me? Not so, my good\r\ncousin; not so, by any means! The furthest possible from that! No; but\r\nit was the accomplishment of a purpose long entertained on my part. I\r\nset him free!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cI never will believe it! He owed his dungeon\r\nto you; his freedom to God\xe2\x80\x99s providence!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI set him free!\xe2\x80\x9d reaffirmed Judge Pyncheon, with the calmest\r\ncomposure. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I came hither now to decide whether he shall retain his\r\nfreedom. It will depend upon himself. For this purpose, I must see\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever!\xe2\x80\x94it would drive him mad!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Hepzibah, but with an\r\nirresoluteness sufficiently perceptible to the keen eye of the Judge;\r\nfor, without the slightest faith in his good intentions, she knew not\r\nwhether there was most to dread in yielding or resistance. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd why\r\nshould you wish to see this wretched, broken man, who retains hardly a\r\nfraction of his intellect, and will hide even that from an eye which\r\nhas no love in it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe shall see love enough in mine, if that be all!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Judge,\r\nwith well-grounded confidence in the benignity of his aspect. \xe2\x80\x9cBut,\r\nCousin Hepzibah, you confess a great deal, and very much to the\r\npurpose. Now, listen, and I will frankly explain my reasons for\r\ninsisting on this interview. At the death, thirty years since, of our\r\nuncle Jaffrey, it was found,\xe2\x80\x94I know not whether the circumstance ever\r\nattracted much of your attention, among the sadder interests that\r\nclustered round that event,\xe2\x80\x94but it was found that his visible estate,\r\nof every kind, fell far short of any estimate ever made of it. He was\r\nsupposed to be immensely rich. Nobody doubted that he stood among the\r\nweightiest men of his day. It was one of his eccentricities,\r\nhowever,\xe2\x80\x94and not altogether a folly, neither,\xe2\x80\x94to conceal the amount of\r\nhis property by making distant and foreign investments, perhaps under\r\nother names than his own, and by various means, familiar enough to\r\ncapitalists, but unnecessary here to be specified. By Uncle Jaffrey\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlast will and testament, as you are aware, his entire property was\r\nbequeathed to me, with the single exception of a life interest to\r\nyourself in this old family mansion, and the strip of patrimonial\r\nestate remaining attached to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd do you seek to deprive us of that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hepzibah, unable to\r\nrestrain her bitter contempt. \xe2\x80\x9cIs this your price for ceasing to\r\npersecute poor Clifford?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not, my dear cousin!\xe2\x80\x9d answered the Judge, smiling\r\nbenevolently. \xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary, as you must do me the justice to own, I\r\nhave constantly expressed my readiness to double or treble your\r\nresources, whenever you should make up your mind to accept any kindness\r\nof that nature at the hands of your kinsman. No, no! But here lies the\r\ngist of the matter. Of my uncle\xe2\x80\x99s unquestionably great estate, as I\r\nhave said, not the half\xe2\x80\x94no, not one third, as I am fully convinced\xe2\x80\x94was\r\napparent after his death. Now, I have the best possible reasons for\r\nbelieving that your brother Clifford can give me a clew to the recovery\r\nof the remainder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClifford!\xe2\x80\x94Clifford know of any hidden wealth? Clifford have it in his\r\npower to make you rich?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the old gentlewoman, affected with a\r\nsense of something like ridicule at the idea. \xe2\x80\x9cImpossible! You deceive\r\nyourself! It is really a thing to laugh at!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is as certain as that I stand here!\xe2\x80\x9d said Judge Pyncheon, striking\r\nhis gold-headed cane on the floor, and at the same time stamping his\r\nfoot, as if to express his conviction the more forcibly by the whole\r\nemphasis of his substantial person. \xe2\x80\x9cClifford told me so himself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Hepzibah incredulously. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are dreaming, Cousin\r\nJaffrey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not belong to the dreaming class of men,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Judge quietly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome months before my uncle\xe2\x80\x99s death, Clifford boasted to me of the\r\npossession of the secret of incalculable wealth. His purpose was to\r\ntaunt me, and excite my curiosity. I know it well. But, from a pretty\r\ndistinct recollection of the particulars of our conversation, I am\r\nthoroughly convinced that there was truth in what he said. Clifford, at\r\nthis moment, if he chooses,\xe2\x80\x94and choose he must!\xe2\x80\x94can inform me where to\r\nfind the schedule, the documents, the evidences, in whatever shape they\r\nexist, of the vast amount of Uncle Jaffrey\xe2\x80\x99s missing property. He has\r\nthe secret. His boast was no idle word. It had a directness, an\r\nemphasis, a particularity, that showed a backbone of solid meaning\r\nwithin the mystery of his expression.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what could have been Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s object,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hepzibah, \xe2\x80\x9cin\r\nconcealing it so long?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was one of the bad impulses of our fallen nature,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the\r\nJudge, turning up his eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cHe looked upon me as his enemy. He\r\nconsidered me as the cause of his overwhelming disgrace, his imminent\r\nperil of death, his irretrievable ruin. There was no great probability,\r\ntherefore, of his volunteering information, out of his dungeon, that\r\nshould elevate me still higher on the ladder of prosperity. But the\r\nmoment has now come when he must give up his secret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what if he should refuse?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cOr,\xe2\x80\x94as I\r\nsteadfastly believe,\xe2\x80\x94what if he has no knowledge of this wealth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear cousin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Judge Pyncheon, with a quietude which he had the\r\npower of making more formidable than any violence, \xe2\x80\x9csince your\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s return, I have taken the precaution (a highly proper one in\r\nthe near kinsman and natural guardian of an individual so situated) to\r\nhave his deportment and habits constantly and carefully overlooked.\r\nYour neighbors have been eye-witnesses to whatever has passed in the\r\ngarden. The butcher, the baker, the fish-monger, some of the customers\r\nof your shop, and many a prying old woman, have told me several of the\r\nsecrets of your interior. A still larger circle\xe2\x80\x94I myself, among the\r\nrest\xe2\x80\x94can testify to his extravagances at the arched window. Thousands\r\nbeheld him, a week or two ago, on the point of flinging himself thence\r\ninto the street. From all this testimony, I am led to\r\napprehend\xe2\x80\x94reluctantly, and with deep grief\xe2\x80\x94that Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s misfortunes\r\nhave so affected his intellect, never very strong, that he cannot\r\nsafely remain at large. The alternative, you must be aware,\xe2\x80\x94and its\r\nadoption will depend entirely on the decision which I am now about to\r\nmake,\xe2\x80\x94the alternative is his confinement, probably for the remainder of\r\nhis life, in a public asylum for persons in his unfortunate state of\r\nmind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou cannot mean it!\xe2\x80\x9d shrieked Hepzibah.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould my cousin Clifford,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Judge Pyncheon, wholly\r\nundisturbed, \xe2\x80\x9cfrom mere malice, and hatred of one whose interests ought\r\nnaturally to be dear to him,\xe2\x80\x94a mode of passion that, as often as any\r\nother, indicates mental disease,\xe2\x80\x94should he refuse me the information so\r\nimportant to myself, and which he assuredly possesses, I shall consider\r\nit the one needed jot of evidence to satisfy my mind of his insanity.\r\nAnd, once sure of the course pointed out by conscience, you know me too\r\nwell, Cousin Hepzibah, to entertain a doubt that I shall pursue it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Jaffrey,\xe2\x80\x94Cousin Jaffrey,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hepzibah mournfully, not\r\npassionately, \xe2\x80\x9cit is you that are diseased in mind, not Clifford! You\r\nhave forgotten that a woman was your mother!\xe2\x80\x94that you have had sisters,\r\nbrothers, children of your own!\xe2\x80\x94or that there ever was affection\r\nbetween man and man, or pity from one man to another, in this miserable\r\nworld! Else, how could you have dreamed of this? You are not young,\r\nCousin Jaffrey!\xe2\x80\x94no, nor middle-aged,\xe2\x80\x94but already an old man! The hair\r\nis white upon your head! How many years have you to live? Are you not\r\nrich enough for that little time? Shall you be hungry,\xe2\x80\x94shall you lack\r\nclothes, or a roof to shelter you,\xe2\x80\x94between this point and the grave?\r\nNo! but, with the half of what you now possess, you could revel in\r\ncostly food and wines, and build a house twice as splendid as you now\r\ninhabit, and make a far greater show to the world,\xe2\x80\x94and yet leave riches\r\nto your only son, to make him bless the hour of your death! Then, why\r\nshould you do this cruel, cruel thing?\xe2\x80\x94so mad a thing, that I know not\r\nwhether to call it wicked! Alas, Cousin Jaffrey, this hard and grasping\r\nspirit has run in our blood these two hundred years. You are but doing\r\nover again, in another shape, what your ancestor before you did, and\r\nsending down to your posterity the curse inherited from him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTalk sense, Hepzibah, for Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the Judge, with\r\nthe impatience natural to a reasonable man, on hearing anything so\r\nutterly absurd as the above, in a discussion about matters of business.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have told you my determination. I am not apt to change. Clifford\r\nmust give up his secret, or take the consequences. And let him decide\r\nquickly; for I have several affairs to attend to this morning, and an\r\nimportant dinner engagement with some political friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClifford has no secret!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd God will not let you\r\ndo the thing you meditate!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall see,\xe2\x80\x9d said the unmoved Judge. \xe2\x80\x9cMeanwhile, choose whether you\r\nwill summon Clifford, and allow this business to be amicably settled by\r\nan interview between two kinsmen, or drive me to harsher measures,\r\nwhich I should be most happy to feel myself justified in avoiding. The\r\nresponsibility is altogether on your part.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are stronger than I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah, after a brief consideration;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand you have no pity in your strength! Clifford is not now insane; but\r\nthe interview which you insist upon may go far to make him so.\r\nNevertheless, knowing you as I do, I believe it to be my best course to\r\nallow you to judge for yourself as to the improbability of his\r\npossessing any valuable secret. I will call Clifford. Be merciful in\r\nyour dealings with him!\xe2\x80\x94be far more merciful than your heart bids you\r\nbe!\xe2\x80\x94for God is looking at you, Jaffrey Pyncheon!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Judge followed his cousin from the shop, where the foregoing\r\nconversation had passed, into the parlor, and flung himself heavily\r\ninto the great ancestral chair. Many a former Pyncheon had found repose\r\nin its capacious arms: rosy children, after their sports; young men,\r\ndreamy with love; grown men, weary with cares; old men, burdened with\r\nwinters,\xe2\x80\x94they had mused, and slumbered, and departed to a yet\r\nprofounder sleep. It had been a long tradition, though a doubtful one,\r\nthat this was the very chair, seated in which the earliest of the\r\nJudge\xe2\x80\x99s New England forefathers\xe2\x80\x94he whose picture still hung upon the\r\nwall\xe2\x80\x94had given a dead man\xe2\x80\x99s silent and stern reception to the throng of\r\ndistinguished guests. From that hour of evil omen until the present, it\r\nmay be,\xe2\x80\x94though we know not the secret of his heart,\xe2\x80\x94but it may be that\r\nno wearier and sadder man had ever sunk into the chair than this same\r\nJudge Pyncheon, whom we have just beheld so immitigably hard and\r\nresolute. Surely, it must have been at no slight cost that he had thus\r\nfortified his soul with iron. Such calmness is a mightier effort than\r\nthe violence of weaker men. And there was yet a heavy task for him to\r\ndo. Was it a little matter\xe2\x80\x94a trifle to be prepared for in a single\r\nmoment, and to be rested from in another moment,\xe2\x80\x94that he must now,\r\nafter thirty years, encounter a kinsman risen from a living tomb, and\r\nwrench a secret from him, or else consign him to a living tomb again?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you speak?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hepzibah, looking in from the threshold of the\r\nparlor; for she imagined that the Judge had uttered some sound which\r\nshe was anxious to interpret as a relenting impulse. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you\r\ncalled me back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no\xe2\x80\x9d gruffly answered Judge Pyncheon with a harsh frown, while his\r\nbrow grew almost a black purple, in the shadow of the room. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should\r\nI call you back? Time flies! Bid Clifford come to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Judge had taken his watch from his vest pocket and now held it in\r\nhis hand, measuring the interval which was to ensue before the\r\nappearance of Clifford.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVI.\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s Chamber\r\n\r\n\r\nNever had the old house appeared so dismal to poor Hepzibah as when she\r\ndeparted on that wretched errand. There was a strange aspect in it. As\r\nshe trode along the foot-worn passages, and opened one crazy door after\r\nanother, and ascended the creaking staircase, she gazed wistfully and\r\nfearfully around. It would have been no marvel, to her excited mind,\r\nif, behind or beside her, there had been the rustle of dead people\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngarments, or pale visages awaiting her on the landing-place above. Her\r\nnerves were set all ajar by the scene of passion and terror through\r\nwhich she had just struggled. Her colloquy with Judge Pyncheon, who so\r\nperfectly represented the person and attributes of the founder of the\r\nfamily, had called back the dreary past. It weighed upon her heart.\r\nWhatever she had heard, from legendary aunts and grandmothers,\r\nconcerning the good or evil fortunes of the Pyncheons,\xe2\x80\x94stories which\r\nhad heretofore been kept warm in her remembrance by the chimney-corner\r\nglow that was associated with them,\xe2\x80\x94now recurred to her, sombre,\r\nghastly, cold, like most passages of family history, when brooded over\r\nin melancholy mood. The whole seemed little else but a series of\r\ncalamity, reproducing itself in successive generations, with one\r\ngeneral hue, and varying in little, save the outline. But Hepzibah now\r\nfelt as if the Judge, and Clifford, and herself,\xe2\x80\x94they three\r\ntogether,\xe2\x80\x94were on the point of adding another incident to the annals of\r\nthe house, with a bolder relief of wrong and sorrow, which would cause\r\nit to stand out from all the rest. Thus it is that the grief of the\r\npassing moment takes upon itself an individuality, and a character of\r\nclimax, which it is destined to lose after a while, and to fade into\r\nthe dark gray tissue common to the grave or glad events of many years\r\nago. It is but for a moment, comparatively, that anything looks strange\r\nor startling,\xe2\x80\x94a truth that has the bitter and the sweet in it.\r\n\r\nBut Hepzibah could not rid herself of the sense of something\r\nunprecedented at that instant passing and soon to be accomplished. Her\r\nnerves were in a shake. Instinctively she paused before the arched\r\nwindow, and looked out upon the street, in order to seize its permanent\r\nobjects with her mental grasp, and thus to steady herself from the reel\r\nand vibration which affected her more immediate sphere. It brought her\r\nup, as we may say, with a kind of shock, when she beheld everything\r\nunder the same appearance as the day before, and numberless preceding\r\ndays, except for the difference between sunshine and sullen storm. Her\r\neyes travelled along the street, from doorstep to doorstep, noting the\r\nwet sidewalks, with here and there a puddle in hollows that had been\r\nimperceptible until filled with water. She screwed her dim optics to\r\ntheir acutest point, in the hope of making out, with greater\r\ndistinctness, a certain window, where she half saw, half guessed, that\r\na tailor\xe2\x80\x99s seamstress was sitting at her work. Hepzibah flung herself\r\nupon that unknown woman\xe2\x80\x99s companionship, even thus far off. Then she\r\nwas attracted by a chaise rapidly passing, and watched its moist and\r\nglistening top, and its splashing wheels, until it had turned the\r\ncorner, and refused to carry any further her idly trifling, because\r\nappalled and overburdened, mind. When the vehicle had disappeared, she\r\nallowed herself still another loitering moment; for the patched figure\r\nof good Uncle Venner was now visible, coming slowly from the head of\r\nthe street downward, with a rheumatic limp, because the east wind had\r\ngot into his joints. Hepzibah wished that he would pass yet more\r\nslowly, and befriend her shivering solitude a little longer. Anything\r\nthat would take her out of the grievous present, and interpose human\r\nbeings betwixt herself and what was nearest to her,\xe2\x80\x94whatever would\r\ndefer for an instant the inevitable errand on which she was bound,\xe2\x80\x94all\r\nsuch impediments were welcome. Next to the lightest heart, the heaviest\r\nis apt to be most playful.\r\n\r\nHepzibah had little hardihood for her own proper pain, and far less for\r\nwhat she must inflict on Clifford. Of so slight a nature, and so\r\nshattered by his previous calamities, it could not well be short of\r\nutter ruin to bring him face to face with the hard, relentless man who\r\nhad been his evil destiny through life. Even had there been no bitter\r\nrecollections, nor any hostile interest now at stake between them, the\r\nmere natural repugnance of the more sensitive system to the massive,\r\nweighty, and unimpressible one, must, in itself, have been disastrous\r\nto the former. It would be like flinging a porcelain vase, with already\r\na crack in it, against a granite column. Never before had Hepzibah so\r\nadequately estimated the powerful character of her cousin\r\nJaffrey,\xe2\x80\x94powerful by intellect, energy of will, the long habit of\r\nacting among men, and, as she believed, by his unscrupulous pursuit of\r\nselfish ends through evil means. It did but increase the difficulty\r\nthat Judge Pyncheon was under a delusion as to the secret which he\r\nsupposed Clifford to possess. Men of his strength of purpose and\r\ncustomary sagacity, if they chance to adopt a mistaken opinion in\r\npractical matters, so wedge it and fasten it among things known to be\r\ntrue, that to wrench it out of their minds is hardly less difficult\r\nthan pulling up an oak. Thus, as the Judge required an impossibility of\r\nClifford, the latter, as he could not perform it, must needs perish.\r\nFor what, in the grasp of a man like this, was to become of Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsoft poetic nature, that never should have had a task more stubborn\r\nthan to set a life of beautiful enjoyment to the flow and rhythm of\r\nmusical cadences! Indeed, what had become of it already? Broken!\r\nBlighted! All but annihilated! Soon to be wholly so!\r\n\r\nFor a moment, the thought crossed Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s mind, whether Clifford\r\nmight not really have such knowledge of their deceased uncle\xe2\x80\x99s vanished\r\nestate as the Judge imputed to him. She remembered some vague\r\nintimations, on her brother\xe2\x80\x99s part, which\xe2\x80\x94if the supposition were not\r\nessentially preposterous\xe2\x80\x94might have been so interpreted. There had been\r\nschemes of travel and residence abroad, day-dreams of brilliant life at\r\nhome, and splendid castles in the air, which it would have required\r\nboundless wealth to build and realize. Had this wealth been in her\r\npower, how gladly would Hepzibah have bestowed it all upon her\r\niron-hearted kinsman, to buy for Clifford the freedom and seclusion of\r\nthe desolate old house! But she believed that her brother\xe2\x80\x99s schemes\r\nwere as destitute of actual substance and purpose as a child\xe2\x80\x99s pictures\r\nof its future life, while sitting in a little chair by its mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nknee. Clifford had none but shadowy gold at his command; and it was not\r\nthe stuff to satisfy Judge Pyncheon!\r\n\r\nWas there no help in their extremity? It seemed strange that there\r\nshould be none, with a city round about her. It would be so easy to\r\nthrow up the window, and send forth a shriek, at the strange agony of\r\nwhich everybody would come hastening to the rescue, well understanding\r\nit to be the cry of a human soul, at some dreadful crisis! But how\r\nwild, how almost laughable, the fatality,\xe2\x80\x94and yet how continually it\r\ncomes to pass, thought Hepzibah, in this dull delirium of a world,\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nwhosoever, and with however kindly a purpose, should come to help, they\r\nwould be sure to help the strongest side! Might and wrong combined,\r\nlike iron magnetized, are endowed with irresistible attraction. There\r\nwould be Judge Pyncheon,\xe2\x80\x94a person eminent in the public view, of high\r\nstation and great wealth, a philanthropist, a member of Congress and of\r\nthe church, and intimately associated with whatever else bestows good\r\nname,\xe2\x80\x94so imposing, in these advantageous lights, that Hepzibah herself\r\ncould hardly help shrinking from her own conclusions as to his hollow\r\nintegrity. The Judge, on one side! And who, on the other? The guilty\r\nClifford! Once a byword! Now, an indistinctly remembered ignominy!\r\n\r\nNevertheless, in spite of this perception that the Judge would draw all\r\nhuman aid to his own behalf, Hepzibah was so unaccustomed to act for\r\nherself, that the least word of counsel would have swayed her to any\r\nmode of action. Little Ph\xc5\x93be Pyncheon would at once have lighted up the\r\nwhole scene, if not by any available suggestion, yet simply by the warm\r\nvivacity of her character. The idea of the artist occurred to Hepzibah.\r\nYoung and unknown, mere vagrant adventurer as he was, she had been\r\nconscious of a force in Holgrave which might well adapt him to be the\r\nchampion of a crisis. With this thought in her mind, she unbolted a\r\ndoor, cobwebbed and long disused, but which had served as a former\r\nmedium of communication between her own part of the house and the gable\r\nwhere the wandering daguerreotypist had now established his temporary\r\nhome. He was not there. A book, face downward, on the table, a roll of\r\nmanuscript, a half-written sheet, a newspaper, some tools of his\r\npresent occupation, and several rejected daguerreotypes, conveyed an\r\nimpression as if he were close at hand. But, at this period of the day,\r\nas Hepzibah might have anticipated, the artist was at his public rooms.\r\nWith an impulse of idle curiosity, that flickered among her heavy\r\nthoughts, she looked at one of the daguerreotypes, and beheld Judge\r\nPyncheon frowning at her. Fate stared her in the face. She turned back\r\nfrom her fruitless quest, with a heartsinking sense of disappointment.\r\nIn all her years of seclusion, she had never felt, as now, what it was\r\nto be alone. It seemed as if the house stood in a desert, or, by some\r\nspell, was made invisible to those who dwelt around, or passed beside\r\nit; so that any mode of misfortune, miserable accident, or crime might\r\nhappen in it without the possibility of aid. In her grief and wounded\r\npride, Hepzibah had spent her life in divesting herself of friends; she\r\nhad wilfully cast off the support which God has ordained his creatures\r\nto need from one another; and it was now her punishment, that Clifford\r\nand herself would fall the easier victims to their kindred enemy.\r\n\r\nReturning to the arched window, she lifted her eyes,\xe2\x80\x94scowling, poor,\r\ndim-sighted Hepzibah, in the face of Heaven!\xe2\x80\x94and strove hard to send up\r\na prayer through the dense gray pavement of clouds. Those mists had\r\ngathered, as if to symbolize a great, brooding mass of human trouble,\r\ndoubt, confusion, and chill indifference, between earth and the better\r\nregions. Her faith was too weak; the prayer too heavy to be thus\r\nuplifted. It fell back, a lump of lead, upon her heart. It smote her\r\nwith the wretched conviction that Providence intermeddled not in these\r\npetty wrongs of one individual to his fellow, nor had any balm for\r\nthese little agonies of a solitary soul; but shed its justice, and its\r\nmercy, in a broad, sunlike sweep, over half the universe at once. Its\r\nvastness made it nothing. But Hepzibah did not see that, just as there\r\ncomes a warm sunbeam into every cottage window, so comes a lovebeam of\r\nGod\xe2\x80\x99s care and pity for every separate need.\r\n\r\nAt last, finding no other pretext for deferring the torture that she\r\nwas to inflict on Clifford,\xe2\x80\x94her reluctance to which was the true cause\r\nof her loitering at the window, her search for the artist, and even her\r\nabortive prayer,\xe2\x80\x94dreading, also, to hear the stern voice of Judge\r\nPyncheon from below stairs, chiding her delay,\xe2\x80\x94she crept slowly, a\r\npale, grief-stricken figure, a dismal shape of woman, with almost\r\ntorpid limbs, slowly to her brother\xe2\x80\x99s door, and knocked!\r\n\r\nThere was no reply.\r\n\r\nAnd how should there have been? Her hand, tremulous with the shrinking\r\npurpose which directed it, had smitten so feebly against the door that\r\nthe sound could hardly have gone inward. She knocked again. Still no\r\nresponse! Nor was it to be wondered at. She had struck with the entire\r\nforce of her heart\xe2\x80\x99s vibration, communicating, by some subtile\r\nmagnetism, her own terror to the summons. Clifford would turn his face\r\nto the pillow, and cover his head beneath the bedclothes, like a\r\nstartled child at midnight. She knocked a third time, three regular\r\nstrokes, gentle, but perfectly distinct, and with meaning in them; for,\r\nmodulate it with what cautious art we will, the hand cannot help\r\nplaying some tune of what we feel upon the senseless wood.\r\n\r\nClifford returned no answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClifford! Dear brother!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cShall I come in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA silence.\r\n\r\nTwo or three times, and more, Hepzibah repeated his name, without\r\nresult; till, thinking her brother\xe2\x80\x99s sleep unwontedly profound, she\r\nundid the door, and entering, found the chamber vacant. How could he\r\nhave come forth, and when, without her knowledge? Was it possible that,\r\nin spite of the stormy day, and worn out with the irksomeness within\r\ndoors he had betaken himself to his customary haunt in the garden, and\r\nwas now shivering under the cheerless shelter of the summer-house? She\r\nhastily threw up a window, thrust forth her turbaned head and the half\r\nof her gaunt figure, and searched the whole garden through, as\r\ncompletely as her dim vision would allow. She could see the interior of\r\nthe summer-house, and its circular seat, kept moist by the droppings of\r\nthe roof. It had no occupant. Clifford was not thereabouts; unless,\r\nindeed, he had crept for concealment (as, for a moment, Hepzibah\r\nfancied might be the case) into a great, wet mass of tangled and\r\nbroad-leaved shadow, where the squash-vines were clambering\r\ntumultuously upon an old wooden framework, set casually aslant against\r\nthe fence. This could not be, however; he was not there; for, while\r\nHepzibah was looking, a strange grimalkin stole forth from the very\r\nspot, and picked his way across the garden. Twice he paused to snuff\r\nthe air, and then anew directed his course towards the parlor window.\r\nWhether it was only on account of the stealthy, prying manner common to\r\nthe race, or that this cat seemed to have more than ordinary mischief\r\nin his thoughts, the old gentlewoman, in spite of her much perplexity,\r\nfelt an impulse to drive the animal away, and accordingly flung down a\r\nwindow stick. The cat stared up at her, like a detected thief or\r\nmurderer, and, the next instant, took to flight. No other living\r\ncreature was visible in the garden. Chanticleer and his family had\r\neither not left their roost, disheartened by the interminable rain, or\r\nhad done the next wisest thing, by seasonably returning to it. Hepzibah\r\nclosed the window.\r\n\r\nBut where was Clifford? Could it be that, aware of the presence of his\r\nEvil Destiny, he had crept silently down the staircase, while the Judge\r\nand Hepzibah stood talking in the shop, and had softly undone the\r\nfastenings of the outer door, and made his escape into the street? With\r\nthat thought, she seemed to behold his gray, wrinkled, yet childlike\r\naspect, in the old-fashioned garments which he wore about the house; a\r\nfigure such as one sometimes imagines himself to be, with the world\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neye upon him, in a troubled dream. This figure of her wretched brother\r\nwould go wandering through the city, attracting all eyes, and\r\neverybody\xe2\x80\x99s wonder and repugnance, like a ghost, the more to be\r\nshuddered at because visible at noontide. To incur the ridicule of the\r\nyounger crowd, that knew him not,\xe2\x80\x94the harsher scorn and indignation of\r\na few old men, who might recall his once familiar features! To be the\r\nsport of boys, who, when old enough to run about the streets, have no\r\nmore reverence for what is beautiful and holy, nor pity for what is\r\nsad,\xe2\x80\x94no more sense of sacred misery, sanctifying the human shape in\r\nwhich it embodies itself,\xe2\x80\x94than if Satan were the father of them all!\r\nGoaded by their taunts, their loud, shrill cries, and cruel\r\nlaughter,\xe2\x80\x94insulted by the filth of the public ways, which they would\r\nfling upon him,\xe2\x80\x94or, as it might well be, distracted by the mere\r\nstrangeness of his situation, though nobody should afflict him with so\r\nmuch as a thoughtless word,\xe2\x80\x94what wonder if Clifford were to break into\r\nsome wild extravagance which was certain to be interpreted as lunacy?\r\nThus Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s fiendish scheme would be ready accomplished to\r\nhis hands!\r\n\r\nThen Hepzibah reflected that the town was almost completely\r\nwater-girdled. The wharves stretched out towards the centre of the\r\nharbor, and, in this inclement weather, were deserted by the ordinary\r\nthrong of merchants, laborers, and sea-faring men; each wharf a\r\nsolitude, with the vessels moored stem and stern, along its misty\r\nlength. Should her brother\xe2\x80\x99s aimless footsteps stray thitherward, and\r\nhe but bend, one moment, over the deep, black tide, would he not\r\nbethink himself that here was the sure refuge within his reach, and\r\nthat, with a single step, or the slightest overbalance of his body, he\r\nmight be forever beyond his kinsman\xe2\x80\x99s gripe? Oh, the temptation! To\r\nmake of his ponderous sorrow a security! To sink, with its leaden\r\nweight upon him, and never rise again!\r\n\r\nThe horror of this last conception was too much for Hepzibah. Even\r\nJaffrey Pyncheon must help her now! She hastened down the staircase,\r\nshrieking as she went.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClifford is gone!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cI cannot find my brother. Help, Jaffrey\r\nPyncheon! Some harm will happen to him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe threw open the parlor-door. But, what with the shade of branches\r\nacross the windows, and the smoke-blackened ceiling, and the dark\r\noak-panelling of the walls, there was hardly so much daylight in the\r\nroom that Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s imperfect sight could accurately distinguish the\r\nJudge\xe2\x80\x99s figure. She was certain, however, that she saw him sitting in\r\nthe ancestral arm-chair, near the centre of the floor, with his face\r\nsomewhat averted, and looking towards a window. So firm and quiet is\r\nthe nervous system of such men as Judge Pyncheon, that he had perhaps\r\nstirred not more than once since her departure, but, in the hard\r\ncomposure of his temperament, retained the position into which accident\r\nhad thrown him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you, Jaffrey,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hepzibah impatiently, as she turned from\r\nthe parlor-door to search other rooms, \xe2\x80\x9cmy brother is not in his\r\nchamber! You must help me seek him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Judge Pyncheon was not the man to let himself be startled from an\r\neasy-chair with haste ill-befitting either the dignity of his character\r\nor his broad personal basis, by the alarm of an hysteric woman. Yet,\r\nconsidering his own interest in the matter, he might have bestirred\r\nhimself with a little more alacrity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you hear me, Jaffrey Pyncheon?\xe2\x80\x9d screamed Hepzibah, as she again\r\napproached the parlor-door, after an ineffectual search elsewhere.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClifford is gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this instant, on the threshold of the parlor, emerging from within,\r\nappeared Clifford himself! His face was preternaturally pale; so deadly\r\nwhite, indeed, that, through all the glimmering indistinctness of the\r\npassageway, Hepzibah could discern his features, as if a light fell on\r\nthem alone. Their vivid and wild expression seemed likewise sufficient\r\nto illuminate them; it was an expression of scorn and mockery,\r\ncoinciding with the emotions indicated by his gesture. As Clifford\r\nstood on the threshold, partly turning back, he pointed his finger\r\nwithin the parlor, and shook it slowly as though he would have\r\nsummoned, not Hepzibah alone, but the whole world, to gaze at some\r\nobject inconceivably ridiculous. This action, so ill-timed and\r\nextravagant,\xe2\x80\x94accompanied, too, with a look that showed more like joy\r\nthan any other kind of excitement,\xe2\x80\x94compelled Hepzibah to dread that her\r\nstern kinsman\xe2\x80\x99s ominous visit had driven her poor brother to absolute\r\ninsanity. Nor could she otherwise account for the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s quiescent\r\nmood than by supposing him craftily on the watch, while Clifford\r\ndeveloped these symptoms of a distracted mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe quiet, Clifford!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered his sister, raising her hand to impress\r\ncaution. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, for Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake, be quiet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him be quiet! What can he do better?\xe2\x80\x9d answered Clifford, with a\r\nstill wilder gesture, pointing into the room which he had just quitted.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs for us, Hepzibah, we can dance now!\xe2\x80\x94we can sing, laugh, play, do\r\nwhat we will! The weight is gone, Hepzibah! It is gone off this weary\r\nold world, and we may be as light-hearted as little Ph\xc5\x93be herself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd, in accordance with his words, he began to laugh, still pointing\r\nhis finger at the object, invisible to Hepzibah, within the parlor. She\r\nwas seized with a sudden intuition of some horrible thing. She thrust\r\nherself past Clifford, and disappeared into the room; but almost\r\nimmediately returned, with a cry choking in her throat. Gazing at her\r\nbrother with an affrighted glance of inquiry, she beheld him all in a\r\ntremor and a quake, from head to foot, while, amid these commoted\r\nelements of passion or alarm, still flickered his gusty mirth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy God! what is to become of us?\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Hepzibah.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford in a tone of brief decision, most unlike what was\r\nusual with him. \xe2\x80\x9cWe stay here too long! Let us leave the old house to\r\nour cousin Jaffrey! He will take good care of it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHepzibah now noticed that Clifford had on a cloak,\xe2\x80\x94a garment of long\r\nago,\xe2\x80\x94in which he had constantly muffled himself during these days of\r\neasterly storm. He beckoned with his hand, and intimated, so far as she\r\ncould comprehend him, his purpose that they should go together from the\r\nhouse. There are chaotic, blind, or drunken moments, in the lives of\r\npersons who lack real force of character,\xe2\x80\x94moments of test, in which\r\ncourage would most assert itself,\xe2\x80\x94but where these individuals, if left\r\nto themselves, stagger aimlessly along, or follow implicitly whatever\r\nguidance may befall them, even if it be a child\xe2\x80\x99s. No matter how\r\npreposterous or insane, a purpose is a Godsend to them. Hepzibah had\r\nreached this point. Unaccustomed to action or responsibility,\xe2\x80\x94full of\r\nhorror at what she had seen, and afraid to inquire, or almost to\r\nimagine, how it had come to pass,\xe2\x80\x94affrighted at the fatality which\r\nseemed to pursue her brother,\xe2\x80\x94stupefied by the dim, thick, stifling\r\natmosphere of dread which filled the house as with a death-smell, and\r\nobliterated all definiteness of thought,\xe2\x80\x94she yielded without a\r\nquestion, and on the instant, to the will which Clifford expressed. For\r\nherself, she was like a person in a dream, when the will always sleeps.\r\nClifford, ordinarily so destitute of this faculty, had found it in the\r\ntension of the crisis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you delay so?\xe2\x80\x9d cried he sharply. \xe2\x80\x9cPut on your cloak and hood,\r\nor whatever it pleases you to wear! No matter what; you cannot look\r\nbeautiful nor brilliant, my poor Hepzibah! Take your purse, with money\r\nin it, and come along!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHepzibah obeyed these instructions, as if nothing else were to be done\r\nor thought of. She began to wonder, it is true, why she did not wake\r\nup, and at what still more intolerable pitch of dizzy trouble her\r\nspirit would struggle out of the maze, and make her conscious that\r\nnothing of all this had actually happened. Of course it was not real;\r\nno such black, easterly day as this had yet begun to be; Judge Pyncheon\r\nhad not talked with, her. Clifford had not laughed, pointed, beckoned\r\nher away with him; but she had merely been afflicted\xe2\x80\x94as lonely sleepers\r\noften are\xe2\x80\x94with a great deal of unreasonable misery, in a morning dream!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow\xe2\x80\x94now\xe2\x80\x94I shall certainly awake!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Hepzibah, as she went to and\r\nfro, making her little preparations. \xe2\x80\x9cI can bear it no longer I must\r\nwake up now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut it came not, that awakening moment! It came not, even when, just\r\nbefore they left the house, Clifford stole to the parlor-door, and made\r\na parting obeisance to the sole occupant of the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat an absurd figure the old fellow cuts now!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered he to\r\nHepzibah. \xe2\x80\x9cJust when he fancied he had me completely under his thumb!\r\nCome, come; make haste! or he will start up, like Giant Despair in\r\npursuit of Christian and Hopeful, and catch us yet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs they passed into the street, Clifford directed Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s attention\r\nto something on one of the posts of the front door. It was merely the\r\ninitials of his own name, which, with somewhat of his characteristic\r\ngrace about the forms of the letters, he had cut there when a boy. The\r\nbrother and sister departed, and left Judge Pyncheon sitting in the old\r\nhome of his forefathers, all by himself; so heavy and lumpish that we\r\ncan liken him to nothing better than a defunct nightmare, which had\r\nperished in the midst of its wickedness, and left its flabby corpse on\r\nthe breast of the tormented one, to be gotten rid of as it might!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVII.\r\nThe Flight of Two Owls\r\n\r\n\r\nSummer as it was, the east wind set poor Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s few remaining teeth\r\nchattering in her head, as she and Clifford faced it, on their way up\r\nPyncheon Street, and towards the centre of the town. Not merely was it\r\nthe shiver which this pitiless blast brought to her frame (although her\r\nfeet and hands, especially, had never seemed so death-a-cold as now),\r\nbut there was a moral sensation, mingling itself with the physical\r\nchill, and causing her to shake more in spirit than in body. The\r\nworld\xe2\x80\x99s broad, bleak atmosphere was all so comfortless! Such, indeed,\r\nis the impression which it makes on every new adventurer, even if he\r\nplunge into it while the warmest tide of life is bubbling through his\r\nveins. What, then, must it have been to Hepzibah and Clifford,\xe2\x80\x94so\r\ntime-stricken as they were, yet so like children in their\r\ninexperience,\xe2\x80\x94as they left the doorstep, and passed from beneath the\r\nwide shelter of the Pyncheon Elm! They were wandering all abroad, on\r\nprecisely such a pilgrimage as a child often meditates, to the world\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nend, with perhaps a sixpence and a biscuit in his pocket. In Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmind, there was the wretched consciousness of being adrift. She had\r\nlost the faculty of self-guidance; but, in view of the difficulties\r\naround her, felt it hardly worth an effort to regain it, and was,\r\nmoreover, incapable of making one.\r\n\r\nAs they proceeded on their strange expedition, she now and then cast a\r\nlook sidelong at Clifford, and could not but observe that he was\r\npossessed and swayed by a powerful excitement. It was this, indeed,\r\nthat gave him the control which he had at once, and so irresistibly,\r\nestablished over his movements. It not a little resembled the\r\nexhilaration of wine. Or, it might more fancifully be compared to a\r\njoyous piece of music, played with wild vivacity, but upon a disordered\r\ninstrument. As the cracked jarring note might always be heard, and as\r\nit jarred loudest amidst the loftiest exultation of the melody, so was\r\nthere a continual quake through Clifford, causing him most to quiver\r\nwhile he wore a triumphant smile, and seemed almost under a necessity\r\nto skip in his gait.\r\n\r\nThey met few people abroad, even on passing from the retired\r\nneighborhood of the House of the Seven Gables into what was ordinarily\r\nthe more thronged and busier portion of the town. Glistening sidewalks,\r\nwith little pools of rain, here and there, along their unequal surface;\r\numbrellas displayed ostentatiously in the shop-windows, as if the life\r\nof trade had concentrated itself in that one article; wet leaves of the\r\nhorse-chestnut or elm-trees, torn off untimely by the blast and\r\nscattered along the public way; an unsightly accumulation of mud in the\r\nmiddle of the street, which perversely grew the more unclean for its\r\nlong and laborious washing,\xe2\x80\x94these were the more definable points of a\r\nvery sombre picture. In the way of movement and human life, there was\r\nthe hasty rattle of a cab or coach, its driver protected by a\r\nwaterproof cap over his head and shoulders; the forlorn figure of an\r\nold man, who seemed to have crept out of some subterranean sewer, and\r\nwas stooping along the kennel, and poking the wet rubbish with a stick,\r\nin quest of rusty nails; a merchant or two, at the door of the\r\npost-office, together with an editor and a miscellaneous politician,\r\nawaiting a dilatory mail; a few visages of retired sea-captains at the\r\nwindow of an insurance office, looking out vacantly at the vacant\r\nstreet, blaspheming at the weather, and fretting at the dearth as well\r\nof public news as local gossip. What a treasure-trove to these\r\nvenerable quidnuncs, could they have guessed the secret which Hepzibah\r\nand Clifford were carrying along with them! But their two figures\r\nattracted hardly so much notice as that of a young girl, who passed at\r\nthe same instant, and happened to raise her skirt a trifle too high\r\nabove her ankles. Had it been a sunny and cheerful day, they could\r\nhardly have gone through the streets without making themselves\r\nobnoxious to remark. Now, probably, they were felt to be in keeping\r\nwith the dismal and bitter weather, and therefore did not stand out in\r\nstrong relief, as if the sun were shining on them, but melted into the\r\ngray gloom and were forgotten as soon as gone.\r\n\r\nPoor Hepzibah! Could she have understood this fact, it would have\r\nbrought her some little comfort; for, to all her other\r\ntroubles,\xe2\x80\x94strange to say!\xe2\x80\x94there was added the womanish and\r\nold-maiden-like misery arising from a sense of unseemliness in her\r\nattire. Thus, she was fain to shrink deeper into herself, as it were,\r\nas if in the hope of making people suppose that here was only a cloak\r\nand hood, threadbare and woefully faded, taking an airing in the midst\r\nof the storm, without any wearer!\r\n\r\nAs they went on, the feeling of indistinctness and unreality kept dimly\r\nhovering round about her, and so diffusing itself into her system that\r\none of her hands was hardly palpable to the touch of the other. Any\r\ncertainty would have been preferable to this. She whispered to herself,\r\nagain and again, \xe2\x80\x9cAm I awake?\xe2\x80\x94Am I awake?\xe2\x80\x9d and sometimes exposed her\r\nface to the chill spatter of the wind, for the sake of its rude\r\nassurance that she was. Whether it was Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s purpose, or only\r\nchance, had led them thither, they now found themselves passing beneath\r\nthe arched entrance of a large structure of gray stone. Within, there\r\nwas a spacious breadth, and an airy height from floor to roof, now\r\npartially filled with smoke and steam, which eddied voluminously upward\r\nand formed a mimic cloud-region over their heads. A train of cars was\r\njust ready for a start; the locomotive was fretting and fuming, like a\r\nsteed impatient for a headlong rush; and the bell rang out its hasty\r\npeal, so well expressing the brief summons which life vouchsafes to us\r\nin its hurried career. Without question or delay,\xe2\x80\x94with the irresistible\r\ndecision, if not rather to be called recklessness, which had so\r\nstrangely taken possession of him, and through him of\r\nHepzibah,\xe2\x80\x94Clifford impelled her towards the cars, and assisted her to\r\nenter. The signal was given; the engine puffed forth its short, quick\r\nbreaths; the train began its movement; and, along with a hundred other\r\npassengers, these two unwonted travellers sped onward like the wind.\r\n\r\nAt last, therefore, and after so long estrangement from everything that\r\nthe world acted or enjoyed, they had been drawn into the great current\r\nof human life, and were swept away with it, as by the suction of fate\r\nitself.\r\n\r\nStill haunted with the idea that not one of the past incidents,\r\ninclusive of Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s visit, could be real, the recluse of the\r\nSeven Gables murmured in her brother\xe2\x80\x99s ear,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClifford! Clifford! Is not this a dream?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA dream, Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated he, almost laughing in her face. \xe2\x80\x9cOn the\r\ncontrary, I have never been awake before!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMeanwhile, looking from the window, they could see the world racing\r\npast them. At one moment, they were rattling through a solitude; the\r\nnext, a village had grown up around them; a few breaths more, and it\r\nhad vanished, as if swallowed by an earthquake. The spires of\r\nmeeting-houses seemed set adrift from their foundations; the\r\nbroad-based hills glided away. Everything was unfixed from its age-long\r\nrest, and moving at whirlwind speed in a direction opposite to their\r\nown.\r\n\r\nWithin the car there was the usual interior life of the railroad,\r\noffering little to the observation of other passengers, but full of\r\nnovelty for this pair of strangely enfranchised prisoners. It was\r\nnovelty enough, indeed, that there were fifty human beings in close\r\nrelation with them, under one long and narrow roof, and drawn onward by\r\nthe same mighty influence that had taken their two selves into its\r\ngrasp. It seemed marvellous how all these people could remain so\r\nquietly in their seats, while so much noisy strength was at work in\r\ntheir behalf. Some, with tickets in their hats (long travellers these,\r\nbefore whom lay a hundred miles of railroad), had plunged into the\r\nEnglish scenery and adventures of pamphlet novels, and were keeping\r\ncompany with dukes and earls. Others, whose briefer span forbade their\r\ndevoting themselves to studies so abstruse, beguiled the little tedium\r\nof the way with penny-papers. A party of girls, and one young man, on\r\nopposite sides of the car, found huge amusement in a game of ball. They\r\ntossed it to and fro, with peals of laughter that might be measured by\r\nmile-lengths; for, faster than the nimble ball could fly, the merry\r\nplayers fled unconsciously along, leaving the trail of their mirth afar\r\nbehind, and ending their game under another sky than had witnessed its\r\ncommencement. Boys, with apples, cakes, candy, and rolls of variously\r\ntinctured lozenges,\xe2\x80\x94merchandise that reminded Hepzibah of her deserted\r\nshop,\xe2\x80\x94appeared at each momentary stopping-place, doing up their\r\nbusiness in a hurry, or breaking it short off, lest the market should\r\nravish them away with it. New people continually entered. Old\r\nacquaintances\xe2\x80\x94for such they soon grew to be, in this rapid current of\r\naffairs\xe2\x80\x94continually departed. Here and there, amid the rumble and the\r\ntumult, sat one asleep. Sleep; sport; business; graver or lighter\r\nstudy; and the common and inevitable movement onward! It was life\r\nitself!\r\n\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s naturally poignant sympathies were all aroused. He caught\r\nthe color of what was passing about him, and threw it back more vividly\r\nthan he received it, but mixed, nevertheless, with a lurid and\r\nportentous hue. Hepzibah, on the other hand, felt herself more apart\r\nfrom human kind than even in the seclusion which she had just quitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not happy, Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, apart, in a tone of\r\nreproach. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are thinking of that dismal old house, and of Cousin\r\nJaffrey\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94here came the quake through him,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand of Cousin Jaffrey\r\nsitting there, all by himself! Take my advice,\xe2\x80\x94follow my example,\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nlet such things slip aside. Here we are, in the world, Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x94in the\r\nmidst of life!\xe2\x80\x94in the throng of our fellow beings! Let you and I be\r\nhappy! As happy as that youth and those pretty girls, at their game of\r\nball!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHappy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d thought Hepzibah, bitterly conscious, at the word, of her dull\r\nand heavy heart, with the frozen pain in it,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9chappy. He is mad already;\r\nand, if I could once feel myself broad awake, I should go mad too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf a fixed idea be madness, she was perhaps not remote from it. Fast\r\nand far as they had rattled and clattered along the iron track, they\r\nmight just as well, as regarded Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s mental images, have been\r\npassing up and down Pyncheon Street. With miles and miles of varied\r\nscenery between, there was no scene for her save the seven old\r\ngable-peaks, with their moss, and the tuft of weeds in one of the\r\nangles, and the shop-window, and a customer shaking the door, and\r\ncompelling the little bell to jingle fiercely, but without disturbing\r\nJudge Pyncheon! This one old house was everywhere! It transported its\r\ngreat, lumbering bulk with more than railroad speed, and set itself\r\nphlegmatically down on whatever spot she glanced at. The quality of\r\nHepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s mind was too unmalleable to take new impressions so readily\r\nas Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s. He had a winged nature; she was rather of the vegetable\r\nkind, and could hardly be kept long alive, if drawn up by the roots.\r\nThus it happened that the relation heretofore existing between her\r\nbrother and herself was changed. At home, she was his guardian; here,\r\nClifford had become hers, and seemed to comprehend whatever belonged to\r\ntheir new position with a singular rapidity of intelligence. He had\r\nbeen startled into manhood and intellectual vigor; or, at least, into a\r\ncondition that resembled them, though it might be both diseased and\r\ntransitory.\r\n\r\nThe conductor now applied for their tickets; and Clifford, who had made\r\nhimself the purse-bearer, put a bank-note into his hand, as he had\r\nobserved others do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the lady and yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the conductor. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd how far?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs far as that will carry us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is no great matter.\r\nWe are riding for pleasure merely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou choose a strange day for it, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d remarked a gimlet-eyed old\r\ngentleman on the other side of the car, looking at Clifford and his\r\ncompanion, as if curious to make them out. \xe2\x80\x9cThe best chance of\r\npleasure, in an easterly rain, I take it, is in a man\xe2\x80\x99s own house, with\r\na nice little fire in the chimney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot precisely agree with you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, courteously bowing\r\nto the old gentleman, and at once taking up the clew of conversation\r\nwhich the latter had proffered. \xe2\x80\x9cIt had just occurred to me, on the\r\ncontrary, that this admirable invention of the railroad\xe2\x80\x94with the vast\r\nand inevitable improvements to be looked for, both as to speed and\r\nconvenience\xe2\x80\x94is destined to do away with those stale ideas of home and\r\nfireside, and substitute something better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the name of common-sense,\xe2\x80\x9d asked the old gentleman rather testily,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhat can be better for a man than his own parlor and chimney-corner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese things have not the merit which many good people attribute to\r\nthem,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cThey may be said, in few and pithy words, to\r\nhave ill served a poor purpose. My impression is, that our wonderfully\r\nincreased and still increasing facilities of locomotion are destined to\r\nbring us around again to the nomadic state. You are aware, my dear\r\nsir,\xe2\x80\x94you must have observed it in your own experience,\xe2\x80\x94that all human\r\nprogress is in a circle; or, to use a more accurate and beautiful\r\nfigure, in an ascending spiral curve. While we fancy ourselves going\r\nstraight forward, and attaining, at every step, an entirely new\r\nposition of affairs, we do actually return to something long ago tried\r\nand abandoned, but which we now find etherealized, refined, and\r\nperfected to its ideal. The past is but a coarse and sensual prophecy\r\nof the present and the future. To apply this truth to the topic now\r\nunder discussion. In the early epochs of our race, men dwelt in\r\ntemporary huts, of bowers of branches, as easily constructed as a\r\nbird\xe2\x80\x99s-nest, and which they built,\xe2\x80\x94if it should be called building,\r\nwhen such sweet homes of a summer solstice rather grew than were made\r\nwith hands,\xe2\x80\x94which Nature, we will say, assisted them to rear where\r\nfruit abounded, where fish and game were plentiful, or, most\r\nespecially, where the sense of beauty was to be gratified by a lovelier\r\nshade than elsewhere, and a more exquisite arrangement of lake, wood,\r\nand hill. This life possessed a charm which, ever since man quitted it,\r\nhas vanished from existence. And it typified something better than\r\nitself. It had its drawbacks; such as hunger and thirst, inclement\r\nweather, hot sunshine, and weary and foot-blistering marches over\r\nbarren and ugly tracts, that lay between the sites desirable for their\r\nfertility and beauty.
