But in our ascending spiral, we escape all this.\r\nThese railroads\xe2\x80\x94could but the whistle be made musical, and the rumble\r\nand the jar got rid of\xe2\x80\x94are positively the greatest blessing that the\r\nages have wrought out for us. They give us wings; they annihilate the\r\ntoil and dust of pilgrimage; they spiritualize travel! Transition being\r\nso facile, what can be any man\xe2\x80\x99s inducement to tarry in one spot? Why,\r\ntherefore, should he build a more cumbrous habitation than can readily\r\nbe carried off with him? Why should he make himself a prisoner for life\r\nin brick, and stone, and old worm-eaten timber, when he may just as\r\neasily dwell, in one sense, nowhere,\xe2\x80\x94in a better sense, wherever the\r\nfit and beautiful shall offer him a home?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s countenance glowed, as he divulged this theory; a youthful\r\ncharacter shone out from within, converting the wrinkles and pallid\r\nduskiness of age into an almost transparent mask. The merry girls let\r\ntheir ball drop upon the floor, and gazed at him. They said to\r\nthemselves, perhaps, that, before his hair was gray and the crow\xe2\x80\x99s-feet\r\ntracked his temples, this now decaying man must have stamped the\r\nimpress of his features on many a woman\xe2\x80\x99s heart. But, alas! no woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neye had seen his face while it was beautiful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should scarcely call it an improved state of things,\xe2\x80\x9d observed\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s new acquaintance, \xe2\x80\x9cto live everywhere and nowhere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould you not?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Clifford, with singular energy. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is as\r\nclear to me as sunshine,\xe2\x80\x94were there any in the sky,\xe2\x80\x94that the greatest\r\npossible stumbling-blocks in the path of human happiness and\r\nimprovement are these heaps of bricks and stones, consolidated with\r\nmortar, or hewn timber, fastened together with spike-nails, which men\r\npainfully contrive for their own torment, and call them house and home!\r\nThe soul needs air; a wide sweep and frequent change of it. Morbid\r\ninfluences, in a thousand-fold variety, gather about hearths, and\r\npollute the life of households. There is no such unwholesome atmosphere\r\nas that of an old home, rendered poisonous by one\xe2\x80\x99s defunct forefathers\r\nand relatives. I speak of what I know. There is a certain house within\r\nmy familiar recollection,\xe2\x80\x94one of those peaked-gable (there are seven of\r\nthem), projecting-storied edifices, such as you occasionally see in our\r\nolder towns,\xe2\x80\x94a rusty, crazy, creaky, dry-rotted, dingy, dark, and\r\nmiserable old dungeon, with an arched window over the porch, and a\r\nlittle shop-door on one side, and a great, melancholy elm before it!\r\nNow, sir, whenever my thoughts recur to this seven-gabled mansion (the\r\nfact is so very curious that I must needs mention it), immediately I\r\nhave a vision or image of an elderly man, of remarkably stern\r\ncountenance, sitting in an oaken elbow-chair, dead, stone-dead, with an\r\nugly flow of blood upon his shirt-bosom! Dead, but with open eyes! He\r\ntaints the whole house, as I remember it. I could never flourish there,\r\nnor be happy, nor do nor enjoy what God meant me to do and enjoy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis face darkened, and seemed to contract, and shrivel itself up, and\r\nwither into age.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cI could never draw cheerful breath there!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think not,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old gentleman, eyeing Clifford\r\nearnestly, and rather apprehensively. \xe2\x80\x9cI should conceive not, sir, with\r\nthat notion in your head!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely not,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Clifford; \xe2\x80\x9cand it were a relief to me if that\r\nhouse could be torn down, or burnt up, and so the earth be rid of it,\r\nand grass be sown abundantly over its foundation. Not that I should\r\never visit its site again! for, sir, the farther I get away from it,\r\nthe more does the joy, the lightsome freshness, the heart-leap, the\r\nintellectual dance, the youth, in short,\xe2\x80\x94yes, my youth, my youth!\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nmore does it come back to me. No longer ago than this morning, I was\r\nold. I remember looking in the glass, and wondering at my own gray\r\nhair, and the wrinkles, many and deep, right across my brow, and the\r\nfurrows down my cheeks, and the prodigious trampling of crow\xe2\x80\x99s-feet\r\nabout my temples! It was too soon! I could not bear it! Age had no\r\nright to come! I had not lived! But now do I look old? If so, my aspect\r\nbelies me strangely; for\xe2\x80\x94a great weight being off my mind\xe2\x80\x94I feel in the\r\nvery heyday of my youth, with the world and my best days before me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI trust you may find it so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old gentleman, who seemed rather\r\nembarrassed, and desirous of avoiding the observation which Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwild talk drew on them both. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have my best wishes for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake, dear Clifford, be quiet!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered his sister.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey think you mad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe quiet yourself, Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x9d returned her brother. \xe2\x80\x9cNo matter what\r\nthey think! I am not mad. For the first time in thirty years my\r\nthoughts gush up and find words ready for them. I must talk, and I\r\nwill!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned again towards the old gentleman, and renewed the\r\nconversation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my dear sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cit is my firm belief and hope that these\r\nterms of roof and hearth-stone, which have so long been held to embody\r\nsomething sacred, are soon to pass out of men\xe2\x80\x99s daily use, and be\r\nforgotten. Just imagine, for a moment, how much of human evil will\r\ncrumble away, with this one change! What we call real estate\xe2\x80\x94the solid\r\nground to build a house on\xe2\x80\x94is the broad foundation on which nearly all\r\nthe guilt of this world rests. A man will commit almost any wrong,\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nwill heap up an immense pile of wickedness, as hard as granite, and\r\nwhich will weigh as heavily upon his soul, to eternal ages,\xe2\x80\x94only to\r\nbuild a great, gloomy, dark-chambered mansion, for himself to die in,\r\nand for his posterity to be miserable in. He lays his own dead corpse\r\nbeneath the underpinning, as one may say, and hangs his frowning\r\npicture on the wall, and, after thus converting himself into an evil\r\ndestiny, expects his remotest great-grandchildren to be happy there. I\r\ndo not speak wildly. I have just such a house in my mind\xe2\x80\x99s eye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old gentleman, getting anxious to drop the\r\nsubject, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are not to blame for leaving it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWithin the lifetime of the child already born,\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford went on, \xe2\x80\x9call\r\nthis will be done away. The world is growing too ethereal and spiritual\r\nto bear these enormities a great while longer. To me, though, for a\r\nconsiderable period of time, I have lived chiefly in retirement, and\r\nknow less of such things than most men,\xe2\x80\x94even to me, the harbingers of a\r\nbetter era are unmistakable. Mesmerism, now! Will that effect nothing,\r\nthink you, towards purging away the grossness out of human life?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll a humbug!\xe2\x80\x9d growled the old gentleman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese rapping spirits, that little Ph\xc5\x93be told us of, the other day,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Clifford,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwhat are these but the messengers of the spiritual\r\nworld, knocking at the door of substance? And it shall be flung wide\r\nopen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA humbug, again!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the old gentleman, growing more and more testy\r\nat these glimpses of Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s metaphysics. \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to rap with\r\na good stick on the empty pates of the dolts who circulate such\r\nnonsense!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen there is electricity,\xe2\x80\x94the demon, the angel, the mighty physical\r\npower, the all-pervading intelligence!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cIs that a\r\nhumbug, too? Is it a fact\xe2\x80\x94or have I dreamt it\xe2\x80\x94that, by means of\r\nelectricity, the world of matter has become a great nerve, vibrating\r\nthousands of miles in a breathless point of time? Rather, the round\r\nglobe is a vast head, a brain, instinct with intelligence! Or, shall we\r\nsay, it is itself a thought, nothing but thought, and no longer the\r\nsubstance which we deemed it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you mean the telegraph,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old gentleman, glancing his eye\r\ntoward its wire, alongside the rail-track, \xe2\x80\x9cit is an excellent\r\nthing,\xe2\x80\x94that is, of course, if the speculators in cotton and politics\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t get possession of it. A great thing, indeed, sir, particularly as\r\nregards the detection of bank-robbers and murderers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t quite like it, in that point of view,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\nbank-robber, and what you call a murderer, likewise, has his rights,\r\nwhich men of enlightened humanity and conscience should regard in so\r\nmuch the more liberal spirit, because the bulk of society is prone to\r\ncontrovert their existence. An almost spiritual medium, like the\r\nelectric telegraph, should be consecrated to high, deep, joyful, and\r\nholy missions. Lovers, day by day\xe2\x80\x94hour by hour, if so often moved to do\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x94might send their heart-throbs from Maine to Florida, with some such\r\nwords as these \xe2\x80\x98I love you forever!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98My heart runs over with love!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98I\r\nlove you more than I can!\xe2\x80\x99 and, again, at the next message \xe2\x80\x98I have\r\nlived an hour longer, and love you twice as much!\xe2\x80\x99 Or, when a good man\r\nhas departed, his distant friend should be conscious of an electric\r\nthrill, as from the world of happy spirits, telling him \xe2\x80\x98Your dear\r\nfriend is in bliss!\xe2\x80\x99 Or, to an absent husband, should come tidings thus\r\n\xe2\x80\x98An immortal being, of whom you are the father, has this moment come\r\nfrom God!\xe2\x80\x99 and immediately its little voice would seem to have reached\r\nso far, and to be echoing in his heart. But for these poor rogues, the\r\nbank-robbers,\xe2\x80\x94who, after all, are about as honest as nine people in\r\nten, except that they disregard certain formalities, and prefer to\r\ntransact business at midnight rather than \xe2\x80\x99Change-hours,\xe2\x80\x94and for these\r\nmurderers, as you phrase it, who are often excusable in the motives of\r\ntheir deed, and deserve to be ranked among public benefactors, if we\r\nconsider only its result,\xe2\x80\x94for unfortunate individuals like these, I\r\nreally cannot applaud the enlistment of an immaterial and miraculous\r\npower in the universal world-hunt at their heels!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t, hey?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the old gentleman, with a hard look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPositively, no!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cIt puts them too miserably at\r\ndisadvantage. For example, sir, in a dark, low, cross-beamed, panelled\r\nroom of an old house, let us suppose a dead man, sitting in an\r\narm-chair, with a blood-stain on his shirt-bosom,\xe2\x80\x94and let us add to our\r\nhypothesis another man, issuing from the house, which he feels to be\r\nover-filled with the dead man\xe2\x80\x99s presence,\xe2\x80\x94and let us lastly imagine him\r\nfleeing, Heaven knows whither, at the speed of a hurricane, by\r\nrailroad! Now, sir, if the fugitive alight in some distant town, and\r\nfind all the people babbling about that self-same dead man, whom he has\r\nfled so far to avoid the sight and thought of, will you not allow that\r\nhis natural rights have been infringed? He has been deprived of his\r\ncity of refuge, and, in my humble opinion, has suffered infinite\r\nwrong!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a strange man; Sir!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old gentleman, bringing his\r\ngimlet-eye to a point on Clifford, as if determined to bore right into\r\nhim. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t see through you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be bound you can\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Clifford, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet, my\r\ndear sir, I am as transparent as the water of Maule\xe2\x80\x99s well! But come,\r\nHepzibah! We have flown far enough for once. Let us alight, as the\r\nbirds do, and perch ourselves on the nearest twig, and consult wither\r\nwe shall fly next!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJust then, as it happened, the train reached a solitary way-station.\r\nTaking advantage of the brief pause, Clifford left the car, and drew\r\nHepzibah along with him. A moment afterwards, the train\xe2\x80\x94with all the\r\nlife of its interior, amid which Clifford had made himself so\r\nconspicuous an object\xe2\x80\x94was gliding away in the distance, and rapidly\r\nlessening to a point which, in another moment, vanished. The world had\r\nfled away from these two wanderers. They gazed drearily about them. At\r\na little distance stood a wooden church, black with age, and in a\r\ndismal state of ruin and decay, with broken windows, a great rift\r\nthrough the main body of the edifice, and a rafter dangling from the\r\ntop of the square tower. Farther off was a farm-house, in the old\r\nstyle, as venerably black as the church, with a roof sloping downward\r\nfrom the three-story peak, to within a man\xe2\x80\x99s height of the ground. It\r\nseemed uninhabited. There were the relics of a wood-pile, indeed, near\r\nthe door, but with grass sprouting up among the chips and scattered\r\nlogs. The small rain-drops came down aslant; the wind was not\r\nturbulent, but sullen, and full of chilly moisture.\r\n\r\nClifford shivered from head to foot. The wild effervescence of his\r\nmood\xe2\x80\x94which had so readily supplied thoughts, fantasies, and a strange\r\naptitude of words, and impelled him to talk from the mere necessity of\r\ngiving vent to this bubbling-up gush of ideas had entirely subsided. A\r\npowerful excitement had given him energy and vivacity. Its operation\r\nover, he forthwith began to sink.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must take the lead now, Hepzibah!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured he, with a torpid and\r\nreluctant utterance. \xe2\x80\x9cDo with me as you will!\xe2\x80\x9d She knelt down upon the\r\nplatform where they were standing and lifted her clasped hands to the\r\nsky. The dull, gray weight of clouds made it invisible; but it was no\r\nhour for disbelief,\xe2\x80\x94no juncture this to question that there was a sky\r\nabove, and an Almighty Father looking from it!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO God!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94ejaculated poor, gaunt Hepzibah,\xe2\x80\x94then paused a moment, to\r\nconsider what her prayer should be,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cO God,\xe2\x80\x94our Father,\xe2\x80\x94are we not thy\r\nchildren? Have mercy on us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVIII.\r\nGovernor Pyncheon\r\n\r\n\r\nJudge Pyncheon, while his two relatives have fled away with such\r\nill-considered haste, still sits in the old parlor, keeping house, as\r\nthe familiar phrase is, in the absence of its ordinary occupants. To\r\nhim, and to the venerable House of the Seven Gables, does our story now\r\nbetake itself, like an owl, bewildered in the daylight, and hastening\r\nback to his hollow tree.\r\n\r\nThe Judge has not shifted his position for a long while now. He has not\r\nstirred hand or foot, nor withdrawn his eyes so much as a\r\nhair\xe2\x80\x99s-breadth from their fixed gaze towards the corner of the room,\r\nsince the footsteps of Hepzibah and Clifford creaked along the passage,\r\nand the outer door was closed cautiously behind their exit. He holds\r\nhis watch in his left hand, but clutched in such a manner that you\r\ncannot see the dial-plate. How profound a fit of meditation! Or,\r\nsupposing him asleep, how infantile a quietude of conscience, and what\r\nwholesome order in the gastric region, are betokened by slumber so\r\nentirely undisturbed with starts, cramp, twitches, muttered dreamtalk,\r\ntrumpet-blasts through the nasal organ, or any slightest irregularity\r\nof breath! You must hold your own breath, to satisfy yourself whether\r\nhe breathes at all. It is quite inaudible. You hear the ticking of his\r\nwatch; his breath you do not hear. A most refreshing slumber,\r\ndoubtless! And yet, the Judge cannot be asleep. His eyes are open! A\r\nveteran politician, such as he, would never fall asleep with wide-open\r\neyes, lest some enemy or mischief-maker, taking him thus at unawares,\r\nshould peep through these windows into his consciousness, and make\r\nstrange discoveries among the reminiscences, projects, hopes,\r\napprehensions, weaknesses, and strong points, which he has heretofore\r\nshared with nobody. A cautious man is proverbially said to sleep with\r\none eye open. That may be wisdom. But not with both; for this were\r\nheedlessness! No, no! Judge Pyncheon cannot be asleep.\r\n\r\nIt is odd, however, that a gentleman so burdened with engagements,\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nnoted, too, for punctuality,\xe2\x80\x94should linger thus in an old lonely\r\nmansion, which he has never seemed very fond of visiting. The oaken\r\nchair, to be sure, may tempt him with its roominess. It is, indeed, a\r\nspacious, and, allowing for the rude age that fashioned it, a\r\nmoderately easy seat, with capacity enough, at all events, and offering\r\nno restraint to the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s breadth of beam. A bigger man might find\r\nample accommodation in it. His ancestor, now pictured upon the wall,\r\nwith all his English beef about him, used hardly to present a front\r\nextending from elbow to elbow of this chair, or a base that would cover\r\nits whole cushion. But there are better chairs than this,\xe2\x80\x94mahogany,\r\nblack walnut, rosewood, spring-seated and damask-cushioned, with varied\r\nslopes, and innumerable artifices to make them easy, and obviate the\r\nirksomeness of too tame an ease,\xe2\x80\x94a score of such might be at Judge\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s service. Yes! in a score of drawing-rooms he would be more\r\nthan welcome. Mamma would advance to meet him, with outstretched hand;\r\nthe virgin daughter, elderly as he has now got to be,\xe2\x80\x94an old widower,\r\nas he smilingly describes himself,\xe2\x80\x94would shake up the cushion for the\r\nJudge, and do her pretty utmost to make him comfortable. For the Judge\r\nis a prosperous man. He cherishes his schemes, moreover, like other\r\npeople, and reasonably brighter than most others; or did so, at least,\r\nas he lay abed this morning, in an agreeable half-drowse, planning the\r\nbusiness of the day, and speculating on the probabilities of the next\r\nfifteen years. With his firm health, and the little inroad that age has\r\nmade upon him, fifteen years or twenty\xe2\x80\x94yes, or perhaps\r\nfive-and-twenty!\xe2\x80\x94are no more than he may fairly call his own.\r\nFive-and-twenty years for the enjoyment of his real estate in town and\r\ncountry, his railroad, bank, and insurance shares, his United States\r\nstock,\xe2\x80\x94his wealth, in short, however invested, now in possession, or\r\nsoon to be acquired; together with the public honors that have fallen\r\nupon him, and the weightier ones that are yet to fall! It is good! It\r\nis excellent! It is enough!\r\n\r\nStill lingering in the old chair! If the Judge has a little time to\r\nthrow away, why does not he visit the insurance office, as is his\r\nfrequent custom, and sit awhile in one of their leathern-cushioned\r\narm-chairs, listening to the gossip of the day, and dropping some\r\ndeeply designed chance-word, which will be certain to become the gossip\r\nof to-morrow. And have not the bank directors a meeting at which it was\r\nthe Judge\xe2\x80\x99s purpose to be present, and his office to preside? Indeed\r\nthey have; and the hour is noted on a card, which is, or ought to be,\r\nin Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s right vest-pocket. Let him go thither, and loll at\r\nease upon his moneybags! He has lounged long enough in the old chair!\r\n\r\nThis was to have been such a busy day. In the first place, the\r\ninterview with Clifford. Half an hour, by the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s reckoning, was to\r\nsuffice for that; it would probably be less, but\xe2\x80\x94taking into\r\nconsideration that Hepzibah was first to be dealt with, and that these\r\nwomen are apt to make many words where a few would do much better\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nmight be safest to allow half an hour. Half an hour? Why, Judge, it is\r\nalready two hours, by your own undeviatingly accurate chronometer.\r\nGlance your eye down at it and see! Ah; he will not give himself the\r\ntrouble either to bend his head, or elevate his hand, so as to bring\r\nthe faithful time-keeper within his range of vision! Time, all at once,\r\nappears to have become a matter of no moment with the Judge!\r\n\r\nAnd has he forgotten all the other items of his memoranda? Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naffair arranged, he was to meet a State Street broker, who has\r\nundertaken to procure a heavy percentage, and the best of paper, for a\r\nfew loose thousands which the Judge happens to have by him, uninvested.\r\nThe wrinkled note-shaver will have taken his railroad trip in vain.\r\nHalf an hour later, in the street next to this, there was to be an\r\nauction of real estate, including a portion of the old Pyncheon\r\nproperty, originally belonging to Maule\xe2\x80\x99s garden ground. It has been\r\nalienated from the Pyncheons these four-score years; but the Judge had\r\nkept it in his eye, and had set his heart on reannexing it to the small\r\ndemesne still left around the Seven Gables; and now, during this odd\r\nfit of oblivion, the fatal hammer must have fallen, and transferred our\r\nancient patrimony to some alien possessor. Possibly, indeed, the sale\r\nmay have been postponed till fairer weather. If so, will the Judge make\r\nit convenient to be present, and favor the auctioneer with his bid, on\r\nthe proximate occasion?\r\n\r\nThe next affair was to buy a horse for his own driving. The one\r\nheretofore his favorite stumbled, this very morning, on the road to\r\ntown, and must be at once discarded. Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s neck is too\r\nprecious to be risked on such a contingency as a stumbling steed.\r\nShould all the above business be seasonably got through with, he might\r\nattend the meeting of a charitable society; the very name of which,\r\nhowever, in the multiplicity of his benevolence, is quite forgotten; so\r\nthat this engagement may pass unfulfilled, and no great harm done. And\r\nif he have time, amid the press of more urgent matters, he must take\r\nmeasures for the renewal of Mrs. Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s tombstone, which, the\r\nsexton tells him, has fallen on its marble face, and is cracked quite\r\nin twain. She was a praiseworthy woman enough, thinks the Judge, in\r\nspite of her nervousness, and the tears that she was so oozy with, and\r\nher foolish behavior about the coffee; and as she took her departure so\r\nseasonably, he will not grudge the second tombstone. It is better, at\r\nleast, than if she had never needed any! The next item on his list was\r\nto give orders for some fruit-trees, of a rare variety, to be\r\ndeliverable at his country-seat in the ensuing autumn. Yes, buy them,\r\nby all means; and may the peaches be luscious in your mouth, Judge\r\nPyncheon! After this comes something more important. A committee of his\r\npolitical party has besought him for a hundred or two of dollars, in\r\naddition to his previous disbursements, towards carrying on the fall\r\ncampaign. The Judge is a patriot; the fate of the country is staked on\r\nthe November election; and besides, as will be shadowed forth in\r\nanother paragraph, he has no trifling stake of his own in the same\r\ngreat game. He will do what the committee asks; nay, he will be liberal\r\nbeyond their expectations; they shall have a check for five hundred\r\ndollars, and more anon, if it be needed. What next? A decayed widow,\r\nwhose husband was Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s early friend, has laid her case of\r\ndestitution before him, in a very moving letter. She and her fair\r\ndaughter have scarcely bread to eat. He partly intends to call on her\r\nto-day,\xe2\x80\x94perhaps so\xe2\x80\x94perhaps not,\xe2\x80\x94accordingly as he may happen to have\r\nleisure, and a small bank-note.\r\n\r\nAnother business, which, however, he puts no great weight on (it is\r\nwell, you know, to be heedful, but not over-anxious, as respects one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npersonal health),\xe2\x80\x94another business, then, was to consult his family\r\nphysician. About what, for Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake? Why, it is rather difficult\r\nto describe the symptoms. A mere dimness of sight and dizziness of\r\nbrain, was it?\xe2\x80\x94or disagreeable choking, or stifling, or gurgling, or\r\nbubbling, in the region of the thorax, as the anatomists say?\xe2\x80\x94or was it\r\na pretty severe throbbing and kicking of the heart, rather creditable\r\nto him than otherwise, as showing that the organ had not been left out\r\nof the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s physical contrivance? No matter what it was. The doctor\r\nprobably would smile at the statement of such trifles to his\r\nprofessional ear; the Judge would smile in his turn; and meeting one\r\nanother\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, they would enjoy a hearty laugh together! But a fig for\r\nmedical advice. The Judge will never need it.\r\n\r\nPray, pray, Judge Pyncheon, look at your watch, Now! What\xe2\x80\x94not a glance!\r\nIt is within ten minutes of the dinner hour! It surely cannot have\r\nslipped your memory that the dinner of to-day is to be the most\r\nimportant, in its consequences, of all the dinners you ever ate. Yes,\r\nprecisely the most important; although, in the course of your somewhat\r\neminent career, you have been placed high towards the head of the\r\ntable, at splendid banquets, and have poured out your festive eloquence\r\nto ears yet echoing with Webster\xe2\x80\x99s mighty organ-tones. No public dinner\r\nthis, however. It is merely a gathering of some dozen or so of friends\r\nfrom several districts of the State; men of distinguished character and\r\ninfluence, assembling, almost casually, at the house of a common\r\nfriend, likewise distinguished, who will make them welcome to a little\r\nbetter than his ordinary fare. Nothing in the way of French cookery,\r\nbut an excellent dinner, nevertheless. Real turtle, we understand, and\r\nsalmon, tautog, canvas-backs, pig, English mutton, good roast beef, or\r\ndainties of that serious kind, fit for substantial country gentlemen,\r\nas these honorable persons mostly are. The delicacies of the season, in\r\nshort, and flavored by a brand of old Madeira which has been the pride\r\nof many seasons. It is the Juno brand; a glorious wine, fragrant, and\r\nfull of gentle might; a bottled-up happiness, put by for use; a golden\r\nliquid, worth more than liquid gold; so rare and admirable, that\r\nveteran wine-bibbers count it among their epochs to have tasted it! It\r\ndrives away the heart-ache, and substitutes no head-ache! Could the\r\nJudge but quaff a glass, it might enable him to shake off the\r\nunaccountable lethargy which (for the ten intervening minutes, and five\r\nto boot, are already past) has made him such a laggard at this\r\nmomentous dinner. It would all but revive a dead man! Would you like to\r\nsip it now, Judge Pyncheon?\r\n\r\nAlas, this dinner. Have you really forgotten its true object? Then let\r\nus whisper it, that you may start at once out of the oaken chair, which\r\nreally seems to be enchanted, like the one in Comus, or that in which\r\nMoll Pitcher imprisoned your own grandfather. But ambition is a\r\ntalisman more powerful than witchcraft. Start up, then, and, hurrying\r\nthrough the streets, burst in upon the company, that they may begin\r\nbefore the fish is spoiled! They wait for you; and it is little for\r\nyour interest that they should wait. These gentlemen\xe2\x80\x94need you be told\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x94have assembled, not without purpose, from every quarter of the\r\nState. They are practised politicians, every man of them, and skilled\r\nto adjust those preliminary measures which steal from the people,\r\nwithout its knowledge, the power of choosing its own rulers. The\r\npopular voice, at the next gubernatorial election, though loud as\r\nthunder, will be really but an echo of what these gentlemen shall\r\nspeak, under their breath, at your friend\xe2\x80\x99s festive board. They meet to\r\ndecide upon their candidate. This little knot of subtle schemers will\r\ncontrol the convention, and, through it, dictate to the party. And what\r\nworthier candidate,\xe2\x80\x94more wise and learned, more noted for philanthropic\r\nliberality, truer to safe principles, tried oftener by public trusts,\r\nmore spotless in private character, with a larger stake in the common\r\nwelfare, and deeper grounded, by hereditary descent, in the faith and\r\npractice of the Puritans,\xe2\x80\x94what man can be presented for the suffrage of\r\nthe people, so eminently combining all these claims to the\r\nchief-rulership as Judge Pyncheon here before us?\r\n\r\nMake haste, then! Do your part! The meed for which you have toiled, and\r\nfought, and climbed, and crept, is ready for your grasp! Be present at\r\nthis dinner!\xe2\x80\x94drink a glass or two of that noble wine!\xe2\x80\x94make your pledges\r\nin as low a whisper as you will!\xe2\x80\x94and you rise up from table virtually\r\ngovernor of the glorious old State! Governor Pyncheon of Massachusetts!\r\n\r\nAnd is there no potent and exhilarating cordial in a certainty like\r\nthis? It has been the grand purpose of half your lifetime to obtain it.\r\nNow, when there needs little more than to signify your acceptance, why\r\ndo you sit so lumpishly in your great-great-grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s oaken chair,\r\nas if preferring it to the gubernatorial one? We have all heard of King\r\nLog; but, in these jostling times, one of that royal kindred will\r\nhardly win the race for an elective chief-magistracy.\r\n\r\nWell; it is absolutely too late for dinner! Turtle, salmon, tautog,\r\nwoodcock, boiled turkey, South-Down mutton, pig, roast-beef, have\r\nvanished, or exist only in fragments, with lukewarm potatoes, and\r\ngravies crusted over with cold fat. The Judge, had he done nothing\r\nelse, would have achieved wonders with his knife and fork. It was he,\r\nyou know, of whom it used to be said, in reference to his ogre-like\r\nappetite, that his Creator made him a great animal, but that the\r\ndinner-hour made him a great beast. Persons of his large sensual\r\nendowments must claim indulgence, at their feeding-time. But, for once,\r\nthe Judge is entirely too late for dinner! Too late, we fear, even to\r\njoin the party at their wine! The guests are warm and merry; they have\r\ngiven up the Judge; and, concluding that the Free-Soilers have him,\r\nthey will fix upon another candidate. Were our friend now to stalk in\r\namong them, with that wide-open stare, at once wild and stolid, his\r\nungenial presence would be apt to change their cheer. Neither would it\r\nbe seemly in Judge Pyncheon, generally so scrupulous in his attire, to\r\nshow himself at a dinner-table with that crimson stain upon his\r\nshirt-bosom. By the bye, how came it there? It is an ugly sight, at any\r\nrate; and the wisest way for the Judge is to button his coat closely\r\nover his breast, and, taking his horse and chaise from the livery\r\nstable, to make all speed to his own house. There, after a glass of\r\nbrandy and water, and a mutton-chop, a beefsteak, a broiled fowl, or\r\nsome such hasty little dinner and supper all in one, he had better\r\nspend the evening by the fireside. He must toast his slippers a long\r\nwhile, in order to get rid of the chilliness which the air of this vile\r\nold house has sent curdling through his veins.\r\n\r\nUp, therefore, Judge Pyncheon, up! You have lost a day. But to-morrow\r\nwill be here anon. Will you rise, betimes, and make the most of it?\r\nTo-morrow. To-morrow! To-morrow. We, that are alive, may rise betimes\r\nto-morrow. As for him that has died to-day, his morrow will be the\r\nresurrection morn.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile the twilight is glooming upward out of the corners of the\r\nroom. The shadows of the tall furniture grow deeper, and at first\r\nbecome more definite; then, spreading wider, they lose their\r\ndistinctness of outline in the dark gray tide of oblivion, as it were,\r\nthat creeps slowly over the various objects, and the one human figure\r\nsitting in the midst of them. The gloom has not entered from without;\r\nit has brooded here all day, and now, taking its own inevitable time,\r\nwill possess itself of everything. The Judge\xe2\x80\x99s face, indeed, rigid and\r\nsingularly white, refuses to melt into this universal solvent. Fainter\r\nand fainter grows the light. It is as if another double-handful of\r\ndarkness had been scattered through the air. Now it is no longer gray,\r\nbut sable. There is still a faint appearance at the window; neither a\r\nglow, nor a gleam, nor a glimmer,\xe2\x80\x94any phrase of light would express\r\nsomething far brighter than this doubtful perception, or sense, rather,\r\nthat there is a window there. Has it yet vanished? No!\xe2\x80\x94yes!\xe2\x80\x94not quite!\r\nAnd there is still the swarthy whiteness,\xe2\x80\x94we shall venture to marry\r\nthese ill-agreeing words,\xe2\x80\x94the swarthy whiteness of Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nface. The features are all gone: there is only the paleness of them\r\nleft. And how looks it now? There is no window! There is no face! An\r\ninfinite, inscrutable blackness has annihilated sight! Where is our\r\nuniverse? All crumbled away from us; and we, adrift in chaos, may\r\nhearken to the gusts of homeless wind, that go sighing and murmuring\r\nabout in quest of what was once a world!\r\n\r\nIs there no other sound? One other, and a fearful one. It is the\r\nticking of the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s watch, which, ever since Hepzibah left the room\r\nin search of Clifford, he has been holding in his hand. Be the cause\r\nwhat it may, this little, quiet, never-ceasing throb of Time\xe2\x80\x99s pulse,\r\nrepeating its small strokes with such busy regularity, in Judge\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s motionless hand, has an effect of terror, which we do not\r\nfind in any other accompaniment of the scene.\r\n\r\nBut, listen! That puff of the breeze was louder. It had a tone unlike\r\nthe dreary and sullen one which has bemoaned itself, and afflicted all\r\nmankind with miserable sympathy, for five days past. The wind has\r\nveered about! It now comes boisterously from the northwest, and, taking\r\nhold of the aged framework of the Seven Gables, gives it a shake, like\r\na wrestler that would try strength with his antagonist. Another and\r\nanother sturdy tussle with the blast! The old house creaks again, and\r\nmakes a vociferous but somewhat unintelligible bellowing in its sooty\r\nthroat (the big flue, we mean, of its wide chimney), partly in\r\ncomplaint at the rude wind, but rather, as befits their century and a\r\nhalf of hostile intimacy, in tough defiance. A rumbling kind of a\r\nbluster roars behind the fire-board. A door has slammed above stairs. A\r\nwindow, perhaps, has been left open, or else is driven in by an unruly\r\ngust. It is not to be conceived, before-hand, what wonderful\r\nwind-instruments are these old timber mansions, and how haunted with\r\nthe strangest noises, which immediately begin to sing, and sigh, and\r\nsob, and shriek,\xe2\x80\x94and to smite with sledge-hammers, airy but ponderous,\r\nin some distant chamber,\xe2\x80\x94and to tread along the entries as with stately\r\nfootsteps, and rustle up and down the staircase, as with silks\r\nmiraculously stiff,\xe2\x80\x94whenever the gale catches the house with a window\r\nopen, and gets fairly into it. Would that we were not an attendant\r\nspirit here! It is too awful! This clamor of the wind through the\r\nlonely house; the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s quietude, as he sits invisible; and that\r\npertinacious ticking of his watch!\r\n\r\nAs regards Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s invisibility, however, that matter will\r\nsoon be remedied. The northwest wind has swept the sky clear. The\r\nwindow is distinctly seen. Through its panes, moreover, we dimly catch\r\nthe sweep of the dark, clustering foliage outside, fluttering with a\r\nconstant irregularity of movement, and letting in a peep of starlight,\r\nnow here, now there. Oftener than any other object, these glimpses\r\nilluminate the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s face. But here comes more effectual light.\r\nObserve that silvery dance upon the upper branches of the pear-tree,\r\nand now a little lower, and now on the whole mass of boughs, while,\r\nthrough their shifting intricacies, the moonbeams fall aslant into the\r\nroom. They play over the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s figure and show that he has not\r\nstirred throughout the hours of darkness. They follow the shadows, in\r\nchangeful sport, across his unchanging features. They gleam upon his\r\nwatch. His grasp conceals the dial-plate,\xe2\x80\x94but we know that the faithful\r\nhands have met; for one of the city clocks tells midnight.\r\n\r\nA man of sturdy understanding, like Judge Pyncheon, cares no more for\r\ntwelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock at night than for the corresponding hour of noon.\r\nHowever just the parallel drawn, in some of the preceding pages,\r\nbetween his Puritan ancestor and himself, it fails in this point. The\r\nPyncheon of two centuries ago, in common with most of his\r\ncontemporaries, professed his full belief in spiritual ministrations,\r\nalthough reckoning them chiefly of a malignant character. The Pyncheon\r\nof to-night, who sits in yonder arm-chair, believes in no such\r\nnonsense. Such, at least, was his creed, some few hours since. His hair\r\nwill not bristle, therefore, at the stories which\xe2\x80\x94in times when\r\nchimney-corners had benches in them, where old people sat poking into\r\nthe ashes of the past, and raking out traditions like live coals\xe2\x80\x94used\r\nto be told about this very room of his ancestral house. In fact, these\r\ntales are too absurd to bristle even childhood\xe2\x80\x99s hair. What sense,\r\nmeaning, or moral, for example, such as even ghost-stories should be\r\nsusceptible of, can be traced in the ridiculous legend, that, at\r\nmidnight, all the dead Pyncheons are bound to assemble in this parlor?\r\nAnd, pray, for what? Why, to see whether the portrait of their ancestor\r\nstill keeps its place upon the wall, in compliance with his\r\ntestamentary directions! Is it worth while to come out of their graves\r\nfor that?\r\n\r\nWe are tempted to make a little sport with the idea. Ghost-stories are\r\nhardly to be treated seriously any longer. The family-party of the\r\ndefunct Pyncheons, we presume, goes off in this wise.\r\n\r\nFirst comes the ancestor himself, in his black cloak, steeple-hat, and\r\ntrunk-breeches, girt about the waist with a leathern belt, in which\r\nhangs his steel-hilted sword; he has a long staff in his hand, such as\r\ngentlemen in advanced life used to carry, as much for the dignity of\r\nthe thing as for the support to be derived from it. He looks up at the\r\nportrait; a thing of no substance, gazing at its own painted image! All\r\nis safe. The picture is still there. The purpose of his brain has been\r\nkept sacred thus long after the man himself has sprouted up in\r\ngraveyard grass. See! he lifts his ineffectual hand, and tries the\r\nframe. All safe! But is that a smile?\xe2\x80\x94is it not, rather a frown of\r\ndeadly import, that darkens over the shadow of his features? The stout\r\nColonel is dissatisfied! So decided is his look of discontent as to\r\nimpart additional distinctness to his features; through which,\r\nnevertheless, the moonlight passes, and flickers on the wall beyond.\r\nSomething has strangely vexed the ancestor! With a grim shake of the\r\nhead, he turns away. Here come other Pyncheons, the whole tribe, in\r\ntheir half a dozen generations, jostling and elbowing one another, to\r\nreach the picture. We behold aged men and grandames, a clergyman with\r\nthe Puritanic stiffness still in his garb and mien, and a red-coated\r\nofficer of the old French war; and there comes the shop-keeping\r\nPyncheon of a century ago, with the ruffles turned back from his\r\nwrists; and there the periwigged and brocaded gentleman of the artist\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlegend, with the beautiful and pensive Alice, who brings no pride out\r\nof her virgin grave. All try the picture-frame. What do these ghostly\r\npeople seek? A mother lifts her child, that his little hands may touch\r\nit! There is evidently a mystery about the picture, that perplexes\r\nthese poor Pyncheons when they ought to be at rest. In a corner,\r\nmeanwhile, stands the figure of an elderly man, in a leathern jerkin\r\nand breeches, with a carpenter\xe2\x80\x99s rule sticking out of his side pocket;\r\nhe points his finger at the bearded Colonel and his descendants,\r\nnodding, jeering, mocking, and finally bursting into obstreperous,\r\nthough inaudible laughter.\r\n\r\nIndulging our fancy in this freak, we have partly lost the power of\r\nrestraint and guidance. We distinguish an unlooked-for figure in our\r\nvisionary scene. Among those ancestral people there is a young man,\r\ndressed in the very fashion of to-day: he wears a dark frock-coat,\r\nalmost destitute of skirts, gray pantaloons, gaiter boots of patent\r\nleather, and has a finely wrought gold chain across his breast, and a\r\nlittle silver-headed whalebone stick in his hand. Were we to meet this\r\nfigure at noonday, we should greet him as young Jaffrey Pyncheon, the\r\nJudge\xe2\x80\x99s only surviving child, who has been spending the last two years\r\nin foreign travel. If still in life, how comes his shadow hither? If\r\ndead, what a misfortune! The old Pyncheon property, together with the\r\ngreat estate acquired by the young man\xe2\x80\x99s father, would devolve on whom?\r\nOn poor, foolish Clifford, gaunt Hepzibah, and rustic little Ph\xc5\x93be! But\r\nanother and a greater marvel greets us! Can we believe our eyes? A\r\nstout, elderly gentleman has made his appearance; he has an aspect of\r\neminent respectability, wears a black coat and pantaloons, of roomy\r\nwidth, and might be pronounced scrupulously neat in his attire, but for\r\na broad crimson stain across his snowy neckcloth and down his\r\nshirt-bosom. Is it the Judge, or no? How can it be Judge Pyncheon? We\r\ndiscern his figure, as plainly as the flickering moonbeams can show us\r\nanything, still seated in the oaken chair! Be the apparition whose it\r\nmay, it advances to the picture, seems to seize the frame, tries to\r\npeep behind it, and turns away, with a frown as black as the ancestral\r\none.\r\n\r\nThe fantastic scene just hinted at must by no means be considered as\r\nforming an actual portion of our story. We were betrayed into this\r\nbrief extravagance by the quiver of the moonbeams; they dance\r\nhand-in-hand with shadows, and are reflected in the looking-glass,\r\nwhich, you are aware, is always a kind of window or doorway into the\r\nspiritual world. We needed relief, moreover, from our too long and\r\nexclusive contemplation of that figure in the chair. This wild wind,\r\ntoo, has tossed our thoughts into strange confusion, but without\r\ntearing them away from their one determined centre. Yonder leaden Judge\r\nsits immovably upon our soul. Will he never stir again? We shall go mad\r\nunless he stirs! You may the better estimate his quietude by the\r\nfearlessness of a little mouse, which sits on its hind legs, in a\r\nstreak of moonlight, close by Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s foot, and seems to\r\nmeditate a journey of exploration over this great black bulk. Ha! what\r\nhas startled the nimble little mouse? It is the visage of grimalkin,\r\noutside of the window, where he appears to have posted himself for a\r\ndeliberate watch. This grimalkin has a very ugly look. Is it a cat\r\nwatching for a mouse, or the devil for a human soul? Would we could\r\nscare him from the window!\r\n\r\nThank Heaven, the night is well-nigh past! The moonbeams have no longer\r\nso silvery a gleam, nor contrast so strongly with the blackness of the\r\nshadows among which they fall. They are paler now; the shadows look\r\ngray, not black. The boisterous wind is hushed. What is the hour? Ah!\r\nthe watch has at last ceased to tick; for the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s forgetful fingers\r\nneglected to wind it up, as usual, at ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock, being half an hour\r\nor so before his ordinary bedtime,\xe2\x80\x94and it has run down, for the first\r\ntime in five years. But the great world-clock of Time still keeps its\r\nbeat. The dreary night\xe2\x80\x94for, oh, how dreary seems its haunted waste,\r\nbehind us!\xe2\x80\x94gives place to a fresh, transparent, cloudless morn.\r\nBlessed, blessed radiance! The daybeam\xe2\x80\x94even what little of it finds its\r\nway into this always dusky parlor\xe2\x80\x94seems part of the universal\r\nbenediction, annulling evil, and rendering all goodness possible, and\r\nhappiness attainable. Will Judge Pyncheon now rise up from his chair?\r\nWill he go forth, and receive the early sunbeams on his brow? Will he\r\nbegin this new day,\xe2\x80\x94which God has smiled upon, and blessed, and given\r\nto mankind,\xe2\x80\x94will he begin it with better purposes than the many that\r\nhave been spent amiss? Or are all the deep-laid schemes of yesterday as\r\nstubborn in his heart, and as busy in his brain, as ever?\r\n\r\nIn this latter case, there is much to do. Will the Judge still insist\r\nwith Hepzibah on the interview with Clifford? Will he buy a safe,\r\nelderly gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s horse? Will he persuade the purchaser of the old\r\nPyncheon property to relinquish the bargain in his favor? Will he see\r\nhis family physician, and obtain a medicine that shall preserve him, to\r\nbe an honor and blessing to his race, until the utmost term of\r\npatriarchal longevity? Will Judge Pyncheon, above all, make due\r\napologies to that company of honorable friends, and satisfy them that\r\nhis absence from the festive board was unavoidable, and so fully\r\nretrieve himself in their good opinion that he shall yet be Governor of\r\nMassachusetts? And all these great purposes accomplished, will he walk\r\nthe streets again, with that dog-day smile of elaborate benevolence,\r\nsultry enough to tempt flies to come and buzz in it? Or will he, after\r\nthe tomb-like seclusion of the past day and night, go forth a humbled\r\nand repentant man, sorrowful, gentle, seeking no profit, shrinking from\r\nworldly honor, hardly daring to love God, but bold to love his fellow\r\nman, and to do him what good he may? Will he bear about with him,\xe2\x80\x94no\r\nodious grin of feigned benignity, insolent in its pretence, and\r\nloathsome in its falsehood,\xe2\x80\x94but the tender sadness of a contrite heart,\r\nbroken, at last, beneath its own weight of sin? For it is our belief,\r\nwhatever show of honor he may have piled upon it, that there was heavy\r\nsin at the base of this man\xe2\x80\x99s being.\r\n\r\nRise up, Judge Pyncheon! The morning sunshine glimmers through the\r\nfoliage, and, beautiful and holy as it is, shuns not to kindle up your\r\nface. Rise up, thou subtle, worldly, selfish, iron-hearted hypocrite,\r\nand make thy choice whether still to be subtle, worldly, selfish,\r\niron-hearted, and hypocritical, or to tear these sins out of thy\r\nnature, though they bring the lifeblood with them! The Avenger is upon\r\nthee! Rise up, before it be too late!\r\n\r\nWhat! Thou art not stirred by this last appeal? No, not a jot! And\r\nthere we see a fly,\xe2\x80\x94one of your common house-flies, such as are always\r\nbuzzing on the window-pane,\xe2\x80\x94which has smelt out Governor Pyncheon, and\r\nalights, now on his forehead, now on his chin, and now, Heaven help us!\r\nis creeping over the bridge of his nose, towards the would-be\r\nchief-magistrate\xe2\x80\x99s wide-open eyes! Canst thou not brush the fly away?\r\nArt thou too sluggish? Thou man, that hadst so many busy projects\r\nyesterday! Art thou too weak, that wast so powerful? Not brush away a\r\nfly? Nay, then, we give thee up!\r\n\r\nAnd hark! the shop-bell rings. After hours like these latter ones,\r\nthrough which we have borne our heavy tale, it is good to be made\r\nsensible that there is a living world, and that even this old, lonely\r\nmansion retains some manner of connection with it. We breathe more\r\nfreely, emerging from Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s presence into the street before\r\nthe Seven Gables.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIX.\r\nAlice\xe2\x80\x99s Posies\r\n\r\n\r\nUncle Venner, trundling a wheelbarrow, was the earliest person stirring\r\nin the neighborhood the day after the storm.\r\n\r\nPyncheon Street, in front of the House of the Seven Gables, was a far\r\npleasanter scene than a by-lane, confined by shabby fences, and\r\nbordered with wooden dwellings of the meaner class, could reasonably be\r\nexpected to present. Nature made sweet amends, that morning, for the\r\nfive unkindly days which had preceded it. It would have been enough to\r\nlive for, merely to look up at the wide benediction of the sky, or as\r\nmuch of it as was visible between the houses, genial once more with\r\nsunshine. Every object was agreeable, whether to be gazed at in the\r\nbreadth, or examined more minutely. Such, for example, were the\r\nwell-washed pebbles and gravel of the sidewalk; even the sky-reflecting\r\npools in the centre of the street; and the grass, now freshly verdant,\r\nthat crept along the base of the fences, on the other side of which, if\r\none peeped over, was seen the multifarious growth of gardens. Vegetable\r\nproductions, of whatever kind, seemed more than negatively happy, in\r\nthe juicy warmth and abundance of their life. The Pyncheon Elm,\r\nthroughout its great circumference, was all alive, and full of the\r\nmorning sun and a sweet-tempered little breeze, which lingered within\r\nthis verdant sphere, and set a thousand leafy tongues a-whispering all\r\nat once. This aged tree appeared to have suffered nothing from the\r\ngale. It had kept its boughs unshattered, and its full complement of\r\nleaves; and the whole in perfect verdure, except a single branch, that,\r\nby the earlier change with which the elm-tree sometimes prophesies the\r\nautumn, had been transmuted to bright gold. It was like the golden\r\nbranch that gained Aeneas and the Sibyl admittance into Hades.\r\n\r\nThis one mystic branch hung down before the main entrance of the Seven\r\nGables, so nigh the ground that any passer-by might have stood on\r\ntiptoe and plucked it off. Presented at the door, it would have been a\r\nsymbol of his right to enter, and be made acquainted with all the\r\nsecrets of the house. So little faith is due to external appearance,\r\nthat there was really an inviting aspect over the venerable edifice,\r\nconveying an idea that its history must be a decorous and happy one,\r\nand such as would be delightful for a fireside tale. Its windows\r\ngleamed cheerfully in the slanting sunlight. The lines and tufts of\r\ngreen moss, here and there, seemed pledges of familiarity and\r\nsisterhood with Nature; as if this human dwelling-place, being of such\r\nold date, had established its prescriptive title among primeval oaks\r\nand whatever other objects, by virtue of their long continuance, have\r\nacquired a gracious right to be. A person of imaginative temperament,\r\nwhile passing by the house, would turn, once and again, and peruse it\r\nwell: its many peaks, consenting together in the clustered chimney; the\r\ndeep projection over its basement-story; the arched window, imparting a\r\nlook, if not of grandeur, yet of antique gentility, to the broken\r\nportal over which it opened; the luxuriance of gigantic burdocks, near\r\nthe threshold; he would note all these characteristics, and be\r\nconscious of something deeper than he saw. He would conceive the\r\nmansion to have been the residence of the stubborn old Puritan,\r\nIntegrity, who, dying in some forgotten generation, had left a blessing\r\nin all its rooms and chambers, the efficacy of which was to be seen in\r\nthe religion, honesty, moderate competence, or upright poverty and\r\nsolid happiness, of his descendants, to this day.\r\n\r\nOne object, above all others, would take root in the imaginative\r\nobserver\xe2\x80\x99s memory. It was the great tuft of flowers,\xe2\x80\x94weeds, you would\r\nhave called them, only a week ago,\xe2\x80\x94the tuft of crimson-spotted flowers,\r\nin the angle between the two front gables. The old people used to give\r\nthem the name of Alice\xe2\x80\x99s Posies, in remembrance of fair Alice Pyncheon,\r\nwho was believed to have brought their seeds from Italy. They were\r\nflaunting in rich beauty and full bloom to-day, and seemed, as it were,\r\na mystic expression that something within the house was consummated.\r\n\r\nIt was but little after sunrise, when Uncle Venner made his appearance,\r\nas aforesaid, impelling a wheelbarrow along the street. He was going\r\nhis matutinal rounds to collect cabbage-leaves, turnip-tops,\r\npotato-skins, and the miscellaneous refuse of the dinner-pot, which the\r\nthrifty housewives of the neighborhood were accustomed to put aside, as\r\nfit only to feed a pig. Uncle Venner\xe2\x80\x99s pig was fed entirely, and kept\r\nin prime order, on these eleemosynary contributions; insomuch that the\r\npatched philosopher used to promise that, before retiring to his farm,\r\nhe would make a feast of the portly grunter, and invite all his\r\nneighbors to partake of the joints and spare-ribs which they had helped\r\nto fatten. Miss Hepzibah Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s housekeeping had so greatly\r\nimproved, since Clifford became a member of the family, that her share\r\nof the banquet would have been no lean one; and Uncle Venner,\r\naccordingly, was a good deal disappointed not to find the large earthen\r\npan, full of fragmentary eatables, that ordinarily awaited his coming\r\nat the back doorstep of the Seven Gables.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never knew Miss Hepzibah so forgetful before,\xe2\x80\x9d said the patriarch to\r\nhimself. \xe2\x80\x9cShe must have had a dinner yesterday,\xe2\x80\x94no question of that!\r\nShe always has one, nowadays. So where\xe2\x80\x99s the pot-liquor and\r\npotato-skins, I ask? Shall I knock, and see if she\xe2\x80\x99s stirring yet? No,\r\nno,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99twon\xe2\x80\x99t do! If little Ph\xc5\x93be was about the house, I should not mind\r\nknocking; but Miss Hepzibah, likely as not, would scowl down at me out\r\nof the window, and look cross, even if she felt pleasantly. So, I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ncome back at noon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith these reflections, the old man was shutting the gate of the little\r\nback-yard. Creaking on its hinges, however, like every other gate and\r\ndoor about the premises, the sound reached the ears of the occupant of\r\nthe northern gable, one of the windows of which had a side-view towards\r\nthe gate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-morning, Uncle Venner!\xe2\x80\x9d said the daguerreotypist, leaning out of\r\nthe window. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you hear nobody stirring?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a soul,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man of patches. \xe2\x80\x9cBut that\xe2\x80\x99s no wonder. \xe2\x80\x99Tis\r\nbarely half an hour past sunrise, yet. But I\xe2\x80\x99m really glad to see you,\r\nMr. Holgrave! There\xe2\x80\x99s a strange, lonesome look about this side of the\r\nhouse; so that my heart misgave me, somehow or other, and I felt as if\r\nthere was nobody alive in it. The front of the house looks a good deal\r\ncheerier; and Alice\xe2\x80\x99s Posies are blooming there beautifully; and if I\r\nwere a young man, Mr. Holgrave, my sweetheart should have one of those\r\nflowers in her bosom, though I risked my neck climbing for it! Well,\r\nand did the wind keep you awake last night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt did, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d answered the artist, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I were a believer\r\nin ghosts,\xe2\x80\x94and I don\xe2\x80\x99t quite know whether I am or not,\xe2\x80\x94I should have\r\nconcluded that all the old Pyncheons were running riot in the lower\r\nrooms, especially in Miss Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s part of the house. But it is very\r\nquiet now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Miss Hepzibah will be apt to over-sleep herself, after being\r\ndisturbed, all night, with the racket,\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Venner. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it\r\nwould be odd, now, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it, if the Judge had taken both his cousins\r\ninto the country along with him? I saw him go into the shop yesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt what hour?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Holgrave.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, along in the forenoon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, well! I must go\r\nmy rounds, and so must my wheelbarrow. But I\xe2\x80\x99ll be back here at\r\ndinner-time; for my pig likes a dinner as well as a breakfast. No\r\nmeal-time, and no sort of victuals, ever seems to come amiss to my pig.\r\nGood morning to you! And, Mr. Holgrave, if I were a young man, like\r\nyou, I\xe2\x80\x99d get one of Alice\xe2\x80\x99s Posies, and keep it in water till Ph\xc5\x93be\r\ncomes back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have heard,\xe2\x80\x9d said the daguerreotypist, as he drew in his head, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nthe water of Maule\xe2\x80\x99s well suits those flowers best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the conversation ceased, and Uncle Venner went on his way. For\r\nhalf an hour longer, nothing disturbed the repose of the Seven Gables;\r\nnor was there any visitor, except a carrier-boy, who, as he passed the\r\nfront doorstep, threw down one of his newspapers; for Hepzibah, of\r\nlate, had regularly taken it in. After a while, there came a fat woman,\r\nmaking prodigious speed, and stumbling as she ran up the steps of the\r\nshop-door. Her face glowed with fire-heat, and, it being a pretty warm\r\nmorning, she bubbled and hissed, as it were, as if all a-fry with\r\nchimney-warmth, and summer-warmth, and the warmth of her own corpulent\r\nvelocity. She tried the shop-door; it was fast. She tried it again,\r\nwith so angry a jar that the bell tinkled angrily back at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce take Old Maid Pyncheon!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the irascible housewife.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink of her pretending to set up a cent-shop, and then lying abed\r\ntill noon! These are what she calls gentlefolk\xe2\x80\x99s airs, I suppose! But\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll either start her ladyship, or break the door down!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shook it accordingly, and the bell, having a spiteful little temper\r\nof its own, rang obstreperously, making its remonstrances heard,\xe2\x80\x94not,\r\nindeed, by the ears for which they were intended,\xe2\x80\x94but by a good lady on\r\nthe opposite side of the street. She opened the window, and addressed\r\nthe impatient applicant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll find nobody there, Mrs. Gubbins.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I must and will find somebody here!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Gubbins,\r\ninflicting another outrage on the bell. \xe2\x80\x9cI want a half-pound of pork,\r\nto fry some first-rate flounders for Mr. Gubbins\xe2\x80\x99s breakfast; and, lady\r\nor not, Old Maid Pyncheon shall get up and serve me with it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut do hear reason, Mrs. Gubbins!\xe2\x80\x9d responded the lady opposite. \xe2\x80\x9cShe,\r\nand her brother too, have both gone to their cousin\xe2\x80\x99s, Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nat his country-seat. There\xe2\x80\x99s not a soul in the house, but that young\r\ndaguerreotype-man that sleeps in the north gable. I saw old Hepzibah\r\nand Clifford go away yesterday; and a queer couple of ducks they were,\r\npaddling through the mud-puddles! They\xe2\x80\x99re gone, I\xe2\x80\x99ll assure you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how do you know they\xe2\x80\x99re gone to the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Gubbins.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a rich man; and there\xe2\x80\x99s been a quarrel between him and Hepzibah\r\nthis many a day, because he won\xe2\x80\x99t give her a living. That\xe2\x80\x99s the main\r\nreason of her setting up a cent-shop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that well enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said the neighbor. \xe2\x80\x9cBut they\xe2\x80\x99re gone,\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\none thing certain. And who but a blood relation, that couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nhimself, I ask you, would take in that awful-tempered old maid, and\r\nthat dreadful Clifford? That\xe2\x80\x99s it, you may be sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Gubbins took her departure, still brimming over with hot wrath\r\nagainst the absent Hepzibah. For another half-hour, or, perhaps,\r\nconsiderably more, there was almost as much quiet on the outside of the\r\nhouse as within. The elm, however, made a pleasant, cheerful, sunny\r\nsigh, responsive to the breeze that was elsewhere imperceptible; a\r\nswarm of insects buzzed merrily under its drooping shadow, and became\r\nspecks of light whenever they darted into the sunshine; a locust sang,\r\nonce or twice, in some inscrutable seclusion of the tree; and a\r\nsolitary little bird, with plumage of pale gold, came and hovered about\r\nAlice\xe2\x80\x99s Posies.\r\n\r\nAt last our small acquaintance, Ned Higgins, trudged up the street, on\r\nhis way to school; and happening, for the first time in a fortnight, to\r\nbe the possessor of a cent, he could by no means get past the shop-door\r\nof the Seven Gables. But it would not open. Again and again, however,\r\nand half a dozen other agains, with the inexorable pertinacity of a\r\nchild intent upon some object important to itself, did he renew his\r\nefforts for admittance. He had, doubtless, set his heart upon an\r\nelephant; or, possibly, with Hamlet, he meant to eat a crocodile. In\r\nresponse to his more violent attacks, the bell gave, now and then, a\r\nmoderate tinkle, but could not be stirred into clamor by any exertion\r\nof the little fellow\xe2\x80\x99s childish and tiptoe strength. Holding by the\r\ndoor-handle, he peeped through a crevice of the curtain, and saw that\r\nthe inner door, communicating with the passage towards the parlor, was\r\nclosed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Pyncheon!\xe2\x80\x9d screamed the child, rapping on the window-pane, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwant an elephant!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere being no answer to several repetitions of the summons, Ned began\r\nto grow impatient; and his little pot of passion quickly boiling over,\r\nhe picked up a stone, with a naughty purpose to fling it through the\r\nwindow; at the same time blubbering and sputtering with wrath. A\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94one of two who happened to be passing by\xe2\x80\x94caught the urchin\xe2\x80\x99s arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the trouble, old gentleman?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want old Hepzibah, or Ph\xc5\x93be, or any of them!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ned, sobbing.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey won\xe2\x80\x99t open the door; and I can\xe2\x80\x99t get my elephant!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo to school, you little scamp!\xe2\x80\x9d said the man. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s another\r\ncent-shop round the corner. \xe2\x80\x99Tis very strange, Dixey,\xe2\x80\x9d added he to his\r\ncompanion, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\xe2\x80\x99s become of all these Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s! Smith, the\r\nlivery-stable keeper, tells me Judge Pyncheon put his horse up\r\nyesterday, to stand till after dinner, and has not taken him away yet.\r\nAnd one of the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s hired men has been in, this morning, to make\r\ninquiry about him. He\xe2\x80\x99s a kind of person, they say, that seldom breaks\r\nhis habits, or stays out o\xe2\x80\x99 nights.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he\xe2\x80\x99ll turn up safe enough!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dixey. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd as for Old Maid\r\nPyncheon, take my word for it, she has run in debt, and gone off from\r\nher creditors. I foretold, you remember, the first morning she set up\r\nshop, that her devilish scowl would frighten away customers. They\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stand it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never thought she\xe2\x80\x99d make it go,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked his friend. \xe2\x80\x9cThis business\r\nof cent-shops is overdone among the women-folks. My wife tried it, and\r\nlost five dollars on her outlay!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor business!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dixey, shaking his head. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor business!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the course of the morning, there were various other attempts to open\r\na communication with the supposed inhabitants of this silent and\r\nimpenetrable mansion. The man of root-beer came, in his neatly painted\r\nwagon, with a couple of dozen full bottles, to be exchanged for empty\r\nones; the baker, with a lot of crackers which Hepzibah had ordered for\r\nher retail custom; the butcher, with a nice titbit which he fancied she\r\nwould be eager to secure for Clifford. Had any observer of these\r\nproceedings been aware of the fearful secret hidden within the house,\r\nit would have affected him with a singular shape and modification of\r\nhorror, to see the current of human life making this small eddy\r\nhereabouts,\xe2\x80\x94whirling sticks, straws and all such trifles, round and\r\nround, right over the black depth where a dead corpse lay unseen!\r\n\r\nThe butcher was so much in earnest with his sweetbread of lamb, or\r\nwhatever the dainty might be, that he tried every accessible door of\r\nthe Seven Gables, and at length came round again to the shop, where he\r\nordinarily found admittance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a nice article, and I know the old lady would jump at it,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cShe can\xe2\x80\x99t be gone away! In fifteen years that I have\r\ndriven my cart through Pyncheon Street, I\xe2\x80\x99ve never known her to be away\r\nfrom home; though often enough, to be sure, a man might knock all day\r\nwithout bringing her to the door. But that was when she\xe2\x80\x99d only herself\r\nto provide for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeeping through the same crevice of the curtain where, only a little\r\nwhile before, the urchin of elephantine appetite had peeped, the\r\nbutcher beheld the inner door, not closed, as the child had seen it,\r\nbut ajar, and almost wide open. However it might have happened, it was\r\nthe fact. Through the passage-way there was a dark vista into the\r\nlighter but still obscure interior of the parlor. It appeared to the\r\nbutcher that he could pretty clearly discern what seemed to be the\r\nstalwart legs, clad in black pantaloons, of a man sitting in a large\r\noaken chair, the back of which concealed all the remainder of his\r\nfigure. This contemptuous tranquillity on the part of an occupant of\r\nthe house, in response to the butcher\xe2\x80\x99s indefatigable efforts to\r\nattract notice, so piqued the man of flesh that he determined to\r\nwithdraw.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he, \xe2\x80\x9cthere sits Old Maid Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s bloody brother, while\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve been giving myself all this trouble! Why, if a hog hadn\xe2\x80\x99t more\r\nmanners, I\xe2\x80\x99d stick him! I call it demeaning a man\xe2\x80\x99s business to trade\r\nwith such people; and from this time forth, if they want a sausage or\r\nan ounce of liver, they shall run after the cart for it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe tossed the titbit angrily into his cart, and drove off in a pet.\r\n\r\nNot a great while afterwards there was a sound of music turning the\r\ncorner and approaching down the street, with several intervals of\r\nsilence, and then a renewed and nearer outbreak of brisk melody. A mob\r\nof children was seen moving onward, or stopping, in unison with the\r\nsound, which appeared to proceed from the centre of the throng; so that\r\nthey were loosely bound together by slender strains of harmony, and\r\ndrawn along captive; with ever and anon an accession of some little\r\nfellow in an apron and straw-hat, capering forth from door or gateway.\r\nArriving under the shadow of the Pyncheon Elm, it proved to be the\r\nItalian boy, who, with his monkey and show of puppets, had once before\r\nplayed his hurdy-gurdy beneath the arched window. The pleasant face of\r\nPh\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x94and doubtless, too, the liberal recompense which she had flung\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94still dwelt in his remembrance. His expressive features kindled up,\r\nas he recognized the spot where this trifling incident of his erratic\r\nlife had chanced. He entered the neglected yard (now wilder than ever,\r\nwith its growth of hog-weed and burdock), stationed himself on the\r\ndoorstep of the main entrance, and, opening his show-box, began to\r\nplay. Each individual of the automatic community forthwith set to work,\r\naccording to his or her proper vocation: the monkey, taking off his\r\nHighland bonnet, bowed and scraped to the by-standers most\r\nobsequiously, with ever an observant eye to pick up a stray cent; and\r\nthe young foreigner himself, as he turned the crank of his machine,\r\nglanced upward to the arched window, expectant of a presence that would\r\nmake his music the livelier and sweeter. The throng of children stood\r\nnear; some on the sidewalk; some within the yard; two or three\r\nestablishing themselves on the very door-step; and one squatting on the\r\nthreshold. Meanwhile, the locust kept singing in the great old Pyncheon\r\nElm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t hear anybody in the house,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the children to\r\nanother. \xe2\x80\x9cThe monkey won\xe2\x80\x99t pick up anything here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is somebody at home,\xe2\x80\x9d affirmed the urchin on the threshold. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nheard a step!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill the young Italian\xe2\x80\x99s eye turned sidelong upward; and it really\r\nseemed as if the touch of genuine, though slight and almost playful,\r\nemotion communicated a juicier sweetness to the dry, mechanical process\r\nof his minstrelsy. These wanderers are readily responsive to any\r\nnatural kindness\xe2\x80\x94be it no more than a smile, or a word itself not\r\nunderstood, but only a warmth in it\xe2\x80\x94which befalls them on the roadside\r\nof life. They remember these things, because they are the little\r\nenchantments which, for the instant,\xe2\x80\x94for the space that reflects a\r\nlandscape in a soap-bubble,\xe2\x80\x94build up a home about them. Therefore, the\r\nItalian boy would not be discouraged by the heavy silence with which\r\nthe old house seemed resolute to clog the vivacity of his instrument.\r\nHe persisted in his melodious appeals; he still looked upward, trusting\r\nthat his dark, alien countenance would soon be brightened by Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsunny aspect. Neither could he be willing to depart without again\r\nbeholding Clifford, whose sensibility, like Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s smile, had talked a\r\nkind of heart\xe2\x80\x99s language to the foreigner. He repeated all his music\r\nover and over again, until his auditors were getting weary. So were the\r\nlittle wooden people in his show-box, and the monkey most of all. There\r\nwas no response, save the singing of the locust.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo children live in this house,\xe2\x80\x9d said a schoolboy, at last. \xe2\x80\x9cNobody\r\nlives here but an old maid and an old man. You\xe2\x80\x99ll get nothing here! Why\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t you go along?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou fool, you, why do you tell him?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered a shrewd little Yankee,\r\ncaring nothing for the music, but a good deal for the cheap rate at\r\nwhich it was had. \xe2\x80\x9cLet him play as he likes! If there\xe2\x80\x99s nobody to pay\r\nhim, that\xe2\x80\x99s his own lookout!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce more, however, the Italian ran over his round of melodies. To the\r\ncommon observer\xe2\x80\x94who could understand nothing of the case, except the\r\nmusic and the sunshine on the hither side of the door\xe2\x80\x94it might have\r\nbeen amusing to watch the pertinacity of the street-performer. Will he\r\nsucceed at last? Will that stubborn door be suddenly flung open? Will a\r\ngroup of joyous children, the young ones of the house, come dancing,\r\nshouting, laughing, into the open air, and cluster round the show-box,\r\nlooking with eager merriment at the puppets, and tossing each a copper\r\nfor long-tailed Mammon, the monkey, to pick up?\r\n\r\nBut to us, who know the inner heart of the Seven Gables as well as its\r\nexterior face, there is a ghastly effect in this repetition of light\r\npopular tunes at its door-step. It would be an ugly business, indeed,\r\nif Judge Pyncheon (who would not have cared a fig for Paganini\xe2\x80\x99s fiddle\r\nin his most harmonious mood) should make his appearance at the door,\r\nwith a bloody shirt-bosom, and a grim frown on his swarthily white\r\nvisage, and motion the foreign vagabond away! Was ever before such a\r\ngrinding out of jigs and waltzes, where nobody was in the cue to dance?\r\nYes, very often. This contrast, or intermingling of tragedy with mirth,\r\nhappens daily, hourly, momently. The gloomy and desolate old house,\r\ndeserted of life, and with awful Death sitting sternly in its solitude,\r\nwas the emblem of many a human heart, which, nevertheless, is compelled\r\nto hear the thrill and echo of the world\xe2\x80\x99s gayety around it.\r\n\r\nBefore the conclusion of the Italian\xe2\x80\x99s performance, a couple of men\r\nhappened to be passing, On their way to dinner. \xe2\x80\x9cI say, you young\r\nFrench fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d called out one of them,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9ccome away from that doorstep,\r\nand go somewhere else with your nonsense! The Pyncheon family live\r\nthere; and they are in great trouble, just about this time. They don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nfeel musical to-day. It is reported all over town that Judge Pyncheon,\r\nwho owns the house, has been murdered; and the city marshal is going to\r\nlook into the matter. So be off with you, at once!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs the Italian shouldered his hurdy-gurdy, he saw on the doorstep a\r\ncard, which had been covered, all the morning, by the newspaper that\r\nthe carrier had flung upon it, but was now shuffled into sight. He\r\npicked it up, and perceiving something written in pencil, gave it to\r\nthe man to read. In fact, it was an engraved card of Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwith certain pencilled memoranda on the back, referring to various\r\nbusinesses which it had been his purpose to transact during the\r\npreceding day. It formed a prospective epitome of the day\xe2\x80\x99s history;\r\nonly that affairs had not turned out altogether in accordance with the\r\nprogramme. The card must have been lost from the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s vest-pocket in\r\nhis preliminary attempt to gain access by the main entrance of the\r\nhouse. Though well soaked with rain, it was still partially legible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here; Dixey!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the man. \xe2\x80\x9cThis has something to do with Judge\r\nPyncheon. See!\xe2\x80\x94here\xe2\x80\x99s his name printed on it; and here, I suppose, is\r\nsome of his handwriting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go to the city marshal with it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dixey. \xe2\x80\x9cIt may give him\r\njust the clew he wants. After all,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered he in his companion\xe2\x80\x99s\r\near, \xe2\x80\x9cit would be no wonder if the Judge has gone into that door and\r\nnever come out again! A certain cousin of his may have been at his old\r\ntricks. And Old Maid Pyncheon having got herself in debt by the\r\ncent-shop,\xe2\x80\x94and the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s pocket-book being well filled,\xe2\x80\x94and bad blood\r\namongst them already! Put all these things together and see what they\r\nmake!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush, hush!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered the other. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems like a sin to be the first\r\nto speak of such a thing. But I think, with you, that we had better go\r\nto the city marshal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dixey. \xe2\x80\x9cWell!\xe2\x80\x94I always said there was something\r\ndevilish in that woman\xe2\x80\x99s scowl!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe men wheeled about, accordingly, and retraced their steps up the\r\nstreet. The Italian, also, made the best of his way off, with a parting\r\nglance up at the arched window. As for the children, they took to their\r\nheels, with one accord, and scampered as if some giant or ogre were in\r\npursuit, until, at a good distance from the house, they stopped as\r\nsuddenly and simultaneously as they had set out. Their susceptible\r\nnerves took an indefinite alarm from what they had overheard. Looking\r\nback at the grotesque peaks and shadowy angles of the old mansion, they\r\nfancied a gloom diffused about it which no brightness of the sunshine\r\ncould dispel. An imaginary Hepzibah scowled and shook her finger at\r\nthem, from several windows at the same moment. An imaginary\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x94for (and it would have deeply wounded him to know it) he had\r\nalways been a horror to these small people\xe2\x80\x94stood behind the unreal\r\nHepzibah, making awful gestures, in a faded dressing-gown. Children are\r\neven more apt, if possible, than grown people, to catch the contagion\r\nof a panic terror. For the rest of the day, the more timid went whole\r\nstreets about, for the sake of avoiding the Seven Gables; while the\r\nbolder signalized their hardihood by challenging their comrades to race\r\npast the mansion at full speed.\r\n\r\nIt could not have been more than half an hour after the disappearance\r\nof the Italian boy, with his unseasonable melodies, when a cab drove\r\ndown the street. It stopped beneath the Pyncheon Elm; the cabman took a\r\ntrunk, a canvas bag, and a bandbox, from the top of his vehicle, and\r\ndeposited them on the doorstep of the old house; a straw bonnet, and\r\nthen the pretty figure of a young girl, came into view from the\r\ninterior of the cab. It was Ph\xc5\x93be! Though not altogether so blooming as\r\nwhen she first tripped into our story,\xe2\x80\x94for, in the few intervening\r\nweeks, her experiences had made her graver, more womanly, and\r\ndeeper-eyed, in token of a heart that had begun to suspect its\r\ndepths,\xe2\x80\x94still there was the quiet glow of natural sunshine over her.\r\nNeither had she forfeited her proper gift of making things look real,\r\nrather than fantastic, within her sphere. Yet we feel it to be a\r\nquestionable venture, even for Ph\xc5\x93be, at this juncture, to cross the\r\nthreshold of the Seven Gables. Is her healthful presence potent enough\r\nto chase away the crowd of pale, hideous, and sinful phantoms, that\r\nhave gained admittance there since her departure?
