Or will she,\r\nlikewise, fade, sicken, sadden, and grow into deformity, and be only\r\nanother pallid phantom, to glide noiselessly up and down the stairs,\r\nand affright children as she pauses at the window?\r\n\r\nAt least, we would gladly forewarn the unsuspecting girl that there is\r\nnothing in human shape or substance to receive her, unless it be the\r\nfigure of Judge Pyncheon, who\xe2\x80\x94wretched spectacle that he is, and\r\nfrightful in our remembrance, since our night-long vigil with\r\nhim!\xe2\x80\x94still keeps his place in the oaken chair.\r\n\r\nPh\xc5\x93be first tried the shop-door. It did not yield to her hand; and the\r\nwhite curtain, drawn across the window which formed the upper section\r\nof the door, struck her quick perceptive faculty as something unusual.\r\nWithout making another effort to enter here, she betook herself to the\r\ngreat portal, under the arched window. Finding it fastened, she\r\nknocked. A reverberation came from the emptiness within. She knocked\r\nagain, and a third time; and, listening intently, fancied that the\r\nfloor creaked, as if Hepzibah were coming, with her ordinary tiptoe\r\nmovement, to admit her. But so dead a silence ensued upon this\r\nimaginary sound, that she began to question whether she might not have\r\nmistaken the house, familiar as she thought herself with its exterior.\r\n\r\nHer notice was now attracted by a child\xe2\x80\x99s voice, at some distance. It\r\nappeared to call her name. Looking in the direction whence it\r\nproceeded, Ph\xc5\x93be saw little Ned Higgins, a good way down the street,\r\nstamping, shaking his head violently, making deprecatory gestures with\r\nboth hands, and shouting to her at mouth-wide screech.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x9d he screamed. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you go in! There\xe2\x80\x99s something\r\nwicked there! Don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t go in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut, as the little personage could not be induced to approach near\r\nenough to explain himself, Ph\xc5\x93be concluded that he had been frightened,\r\non some of his visits to the shop, by her cousin Hepzibah; for the good\r\nlady\xe2\x80\x99s manifestations, in truth, ran about an equal chance of scaring\r\nchildren out of their wits, or compelling them to unseemly laughter.\r\nStill, she felt the more, for this incident, how unaccountably silent\r\nand impenetrable the house had become. As her next resort, Ph\xc5\x93be made\r\nher way into the garden, where on so warm and bright a day as the\r\npresent, she had little doubt of finding Clifford, and perhaps Hepzibah\r\nalso, idling away the noontide in the shadow of the arbor. Immediately\r\non her entering the garden gate, the family of hens half ran, half flew\r\nto meet her; while a strange grimalkin, which was prowling under the\r\nparlor window, took to his heels, clambered hastily over the fence, and\r\nvanished. The arbor was vacant, and its floor, table, and circular\r\nbench were still damp, and bestrewn with twigs and the disarray of the\r\npast storm. The growth of the garden seemed to have got quite out of\r\nbounds; the weeds had taken advantage of Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s absence, and the\r\nlong-continued rain, to run rampant over the flowers and\r\nkitchen-vegetables. Maule\xe2\x80\x99s well had overflowed its stone border, and\r\nmade a pool of formidable breadth in that corner of the garden.\r\n\r\nThe impression of the whole scene was that of a spot where no human\r\nfoot had left its print for many preceding days,\xe2\x80\x94probably not since\r\nPh\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s departure,\xe2\x80\x94for she saw a side-comb of her own under the table\r\nof the arbor, where it must have fallen on the last afternoon when she\r\nand Clifford sat there.\r\n\r\nThe girl knew that her two relatives were capable of far greater\r\noddities than that of shutting themselves up in their old house, as\r\nthey appeared now to have done. Nevertheless, with indistinct\r\nmisgivings of something amiss, and apprehensions to which she could not\r\ngive shape, she approached the door that formed the customary\r\ncommunication between the house and garden. It was secured within, like\r\nthe two which she had already tried. She knocked, however; and\r\nimmediately, as if the application had been expected, the door was\r\ndrawn open, by a considerable exertion of some unseen person\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nstrength, not wide, but far enough to afford her a sidelong entrance.\r\nAs Hepzibah, in order not to expose herself to inspection from without,\r\ninvariably opened a door in this manner, Ph\xc5\x93be necessarily concluded\r\nthat it was her cousin who now admitted her.\r\n\r\nWithout hesitation, therefore, she stepped across the threshold, and\r\nhad no sooner entered than the door closed behind her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXX.\r\nThe Flower of Eden\r\n\r\n\r\nPh\xc5\x93be, coming so suddenly from the sunny daylight, was altogether\r\nbedimmed in such density of shadow as lurked in most of the passages of\r\nthe old house. She was not at first aware by whom she had been\r\nadmitted. Before her eyes had adapted themselves to the obscurity, a\r\nhand grasped her own with a firm but gentle and warm pressure, thus\r\nimparting a welcome which caused her heart to leap and thrill with an\r\nindefinable shiver of enjoyment. She felt herself drawn along, not\r\ntowards the parlor, but into a large and unoccupied apartment, which\r\nhad formerly been the grand reception-room of the Seven Gables. The\r\nsunshine came freely into all the uncurtained windows of this room, and\r\nfell upon the dusty floor; so that Ph\xc5\x93be now clearly saw\xe2\x80\x94what, indeed,\r\nhad been no secret, after the encounter of a warm hand with hers\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nit was not Hepzibah nor Clifford, but Holgrave, to whom she owed her\r\nreception. The subtile, intuitive communication, or, rather, the vague\r\nand formless impression of something to be told, had made her yield\r\nunresistingly to his impulse. Without taking away her hand, she looked\r\neagerly in his face, not quick to forebode evil, but unavoidably\r\nconscious that the state of the family had changed since her departure,\r\nand therefore anxious for an explanation.\r\n\r\nThe artist looked paler than ordinary; there was a thoughtful and\r\nsevere contraction of his forehead, tracing a deep, vertical line\r\nbetween the eyebrows. His smile, however, was full of genuine warmth,\r\nand had in it a joy, by far the most vivid expression that Ph\xc5\x93be had\r\never witnessed, shining out of the New England reserve with which\r\nHolgrave habitually masked whatever lay near his heart. It was the look\r\nwherewith a man, brooding alone over some fearful object, in a dreary\r\nforest or illimitable desert, would recognize the familiar aspect of\r\nhis dearest friend, bringing up all the peaceful ideas that belong to\r\nhome, and the gentle current of every-day affairs. And yet, as he felt\r\nthe necessity of responding to her look of inquiry, the smile\r\ndisappeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ought not to rejoice that you have come, Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cWe meet\r\nat a strange moment!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has happened!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy is the house so deserted?\r\nWhere are Hepzibah and Clifford?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGone! I cannot imagine where they are!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are\r\nalone in the house!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHepzibah and Clifford gone?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not possible! And why\r\nhave you brought me into this room, instead of the parlor? Ah,\r\nsomething terrible has happened! I must run and see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x9d said Holgrave holding her back. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is as I have told\r\nyou. They are gone, and I know not whither. A terrible event has,\r\nindeed happened, but not to them, nor, as I undoubtingly believe,\r\nthrough any agency of theirs. If I read your character rightly, Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe continued, fixing his eyes on hers with stern anxiety, intermixed\r\nwith tenderness, \xe2\x80\x9cgentle as you are, and seeming to have your sphere\r\namong common things, you yet possess remarkable strength. You have\r\nwonderful poise, and a faculty which, when tested, will prove itself\r\ncapable of dealing with matters that fall far out of the ordinary\r\nrule.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, I am very weak!\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ph\xc5\x93be, trembling. \xe2\x80\x9cBut tell me what\r\nhas happened!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are strong!\xe2\x80\x9d persisted Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must be both strong and\r\nwise; for I am all astray, and need your counsel. It may be you can\r\nsuggest the one right thing to do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me!\xe2\x80\x94tell me!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, all in a tremble. \xe2\x80\x9cIt oppresses,\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nterrifies me,\xe2\x80\x94this mystery! Anything else I can bear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe artist hesitated. Notwithstanding what he had just said, and most\r\nsincerely, in regard to the self-balancing power with which Ph\xc5\x93be\r\nimpressed him, it still seemed almost wicked to bring the awful secret\r\nof yesterday to her knowledge. It was like dragging a hideous shape of\r\ndeath into the cleanly and cheerful space before a household fire,\r\nwhere it would present all the uglier aspect, amid the decorousness of\r\neverything about it. Yet it could not be concealed from her; she must\r\nneeds know it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPh\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you remember this?\xe2\x80\x9d He put into her hand a\r\ndaguerreotype; the same that he had shown her at their first interview\r\nin the garden, and which so strikingly brought out the hard and\r\nrelentless traits of the original.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has this to do with Hepzibah and Clifford?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ph\xc5\x93be, with\r\nimpatient surprise that Holgrave should so trifle with her at such a\r\nmoment. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is Judge Pyncheon! You have shown it to me before!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut here is the same face, taken within this half-hour\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nartist, presenting her with another miniature. \xe2\x80\x9cI had just finished it\r\nwhen I heard you at the door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is death!\xe2\x80\x9d shuddered Ph\xc5\x93be, turning very pale. \xe2\x80\x9cJudge Pyncheon\r\ndead!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch as there represented,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holgrave, \xe2\x80\x9che sits in the next room.\r\nThe Judge is dead, and Clifford and Hepzibah have vanished! I know no\r\nmore. All beyond is conjecture. On returning to my solitary chamber,\r\nlast evening, I noticed no light, either in the parlor, or Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nroom, or Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s; no stir nor footstep about the house. This\r\nmorning, there was the same death-like quiet. From my window, I\r\noverheard the testimony of a neighbor, that your relatives were seen\r\nleaving the house in the midst of yesterday\xe2\x80\x99s storm. A rumor reached\r\nme, too, of Judge Pyncheon being missed. A feeling which I cannot\r\ndescribe\xe2\x80\x94an indefinite sense of some catastrophe, or\r\nconsummation\xe2\x80\x94impelled me to make my way into this part of the house,\r\nwhere I discovered what you see. As a point of evidence that may be\r\nuseful to Clifford, and also as a memorial valuable to myself,\xe2\x80\x94for,\r\nPh\xc5\x93be, there are hereditary reasons that connect me strangely with that\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s fate,\xe2\x80\x94I used the means at my disposal to preserve this pictorial\r\nrecord of Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEven in her agitation, Ph\xc5\x93be could not help remarking the calmness of\r\nHolgrave\xe2\x80\x99s demeanor. He appeared, it is true, to feel the whole\r\nawfulness of the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s death, yet had received the fact into his mind\r\nwithout any mixture of surprise, but as an event preordained, happening\r\ninevitably, and so fitting itself into past occurrences that it could\r\nalmost have been prophesied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy have you not thrown open the doors, and called in witnesses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ninquired she with a painful shudder. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is terrible to be here alone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Clifford!\xe2\x80\x9d suggested the artist. \xe2\x80\x9cClifford and Hepzibah! We must\r\nconsider what is best to be done in their behalf. It is a wretched\r\nfatality that they should have disappeared! Their flight will throw the\r\nworst coloring over this event of which it is susceptible. Yet how easy\r\nis the explanation, to those who know them! Bewildered and\r\nterror-stricken by the similarity of this death to a former one, which\r\nwas attended with such disastrous consequences to Clifford, they have\r\nhad no idea but of removing themselves from the scene. How miserably\r\nunfortunate! Had Hepzibah but shrieked aloud,\xe2\x80\x94had Clifford flung wide\r\nthe door, and proclaimed Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s death,\xe2\x80\x94it would have been,\r\nhowever awful in itself, an event fruitful of good consequences to\r\nthem. As I view it, it would have gone far towards obliterating the\r\nblack stain on Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s character.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ph\xc5\x93be, \xe2\x80\x9ccould any good come from what is so very\r\ndreadful?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause,\xe2\x80\x9d said the artist, \xe2\x80\x9cif the matter can be fairly considered and\r\ncandidly interpreted, it must be evident that Judge Pyncheon could not\r\nhave come unfairly to his end. This mode of death had been an\r\nidiosyncrasy with his family, for generations past; not often\r\noccurring, indeed, but, when it does occur, usually attacking\r\nindividuals about the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s time of life, and generally in the\r\ntension of some mental crisis, or, perhaps, in an access of wrath. Old\r\nMaule\xe2\x80\x99s prophecy was probably founded on a knowledge of this physical\r\npredisposition in the Pyncheon race. Now, there is a minute and almost\r\nexact similarity in the appearances connected with the death that\r\noccurred yesterday and those recorded of the death of Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s uncle\r\nthirty years ago. It is true, there was a certain arrangement of\r\ncircumstances, unnecessary to be recounted, which made it possible nay,\r\nas men look at these things, probable, or even certain\xe2\x80\x94that old Jaffrey\r\nPyncheon came to a violent death, and by Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhence came those circumstances?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cHe being innocent,\r\nas we know him to be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey were arranged,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holgrave,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cat least such has long been my\r\nconviction,\xe2\x80\x94they were arranged after the uncle\xe2\x80\x99s death, and before it\r\nwas made public, by the man who sits in yonder parlor. His own death,\r\nso like that former one, yet attended by none of those suspicious\r\ncircumstances, seems the stroke of God upon him, at once a punishment\r\nfor his wickedness, and making plain the innocence of Clifford. But\r\nthis flight,\xe2\x80\x94it distorts everything! He may be in concealment, near at\r\nhand. Could we but bring him back before the discovery of the Judge\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeath, the evil might be rectified.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must not hide this thing a moment longer!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\ndreadful to keep it so closely in our hearts. Clifford is innocent. God\r\nwill make it manifest! Let us throw open the doors, and call all the\r\nneighborhood to see the truth!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are right, Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cDoubtless you are right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYet the artist did not feel the horror, which was proper to Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsweet and order-loving character, at thus finding herself at issue with\r\nsociety, and brought in contact with an event that transcended ordinary\r\nrules. Neither was he in haste, like her, to betake himself within the\r\nprecincts of common life. On the contrary, he gathered a wild\r\nenjoyment,\xe2\x80\x94as it were, a flower of strange beauty, growing in a\r\ndesolate spot, and blossoming in the wind,\xe2\x80\x94such a flower of momentary\r\nhappiness he gathered from his present position. It separated Ph\xc5\x93be and\r\nhimself from the world, and bound them to each other, by their\r\nexclusive knowledge of Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s mysterious death, and the\r\ncounsel which they were forced to hold respecting it. The secret, so\r\nlong as it should continue such, kept them within the circle of a\r\nspell, a solitude in the midst of men, a remoteness as entire as that\r\nof an island in mid-ocean; once divulged, the ocean would flow betwixt\r\nthem, standing on its widely sundered shores. Meanwhile, all the\r\ncircumstances of their situation seemed to draw them together; they\r\nwere like two children who go hand in hand, pressing closely to one\r\nanother\xe2\x80\x99s side, through a shadow-haunted passage. The image of awful\r\nDeath, which filled the house, held them united by his stiffened grasp.\r\n\r\nThese influences hastened the development of emotions that might not\r\notherwise have flowered so. Possibly, indeed, it had been Holgrave\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npurpose to let them die in their undeveloped germs. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do we delay\r\nso?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cThis secret takes away my breath! Let us throw open\r\nthe doors!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn all our lives there can never come another moment like this!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nHolgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cPh\xc5\x93be, is it all terror?\xe2\x80\x94nothing but terror? Are you\r\nconscious of no joy, as I am, that has made this the only point of life\r\nworth living for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems a sin,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ph\xc5\x93be, trembling, \xe2\x80\x9cto think of joy at such a\r\ntime!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you but know, Ph\xc5\x93be, how it was with me the hour before you\r\ncame!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the artist. \xe2\x80\x9cA dark, cold, miserable hour! The\r\npresence of yonder dead man threw a great black shadow over everything;\r\nhe made the universe, so far as my perception could reach, a scene of\r\nguilt and of retribution more dreadful than the guilt. The sense of it\r\ntook away my youth. I never hoped to feel young again! The world looked\r\nstrange, wild, evil, hostile; my past life, so lonesome and dreary; my\r\nfuture, a shapeless gloom, which I must mould into gloomy shapes! But,\r\nPh\xc5\x93be, you crossed the threshold; and hope, warmth, and joy came in\r\nwith you! The black moment became at once a blissful one. It must not\r\npass without the spoken word. I love you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you love a simple girl like me?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ph\xc5\x93be, compelled by his\r\nearnestness to speak. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have many, many thoughts, with which I\r\nshould try in vain to sympathize. And I,\xe2\x80\x94I, too,\xe2\x80\x94I have tendencies with\r\nwhich you would sympathize as little. That is less matter. But I have\r\nnot scope enough to make you happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are my only possibility of happiness!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cI have\r\nno faith in it, except as you bestow it on me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then\xe2\x80\x94I am afraid!\xe2\x80\x9d continued Ph\xc5\x93be, shrinking towards Holgrave,\r\neven while she told him so frankly the doubts with which he affected\r\nher. \xe2\x80\x9cYou will lead me out of my own quiet path. You will make me\r\nstrive to follow you where it is pathless. I cannot do so. It is not my\r\nnature. I shall sink down and perish!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Holgrave, with almost a sigh, and a smile that\r\nwas burdened with thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be far otherwise than as you forebode. The world owes all its\r\nonward impulses to men ill at ease. The happy man inevitably confines\r\nhimself within ancient limits. I have a presentiment that, hereafter,\r\nit will be my lot to set out trees, to make fences,\xe2\x80\x94perhaps, even, in\r\ndue time, to build a house for another generation,\xe2\x80\x94in a word, to\r\nconform myself to laws and the peaceful practice of society. Your poise\r\nwill be more powerful than any oscillating tendency of mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would not have it so!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be earnestly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you love me?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cIf we love one another, the moment\r\nhas room for nothing more. Let us pause upon it, and be satisfied. Do\r\nyou love me, Ph\xc5\x93be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look into my heart,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, letting her eyes drop. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know I\r\nlove you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd it was in this hour, so full of doubt and awe, that the one miracle\r\nwas wrought, without which every human existence is a blank. The bliss\r\nwhich makes all things true, beautiful, and holy shone around this\r\nyouth and maiden. They were conscious of nothing sad nor old. They\r\ntransfigured the earth, and made it Eden again, and themselves the two\r\nfirst dwellers in it. The dead man, so close beside them, was\r\nforgotten. At such a crisis, there is no death; for immortality is\r\nrevealed anew, and embraces everything in its hallowed atmosphere.\r\n\r\nBut how soon the heavy earth-dream settled down again!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHark!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cSomebody is at the street door!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow let us meet the world!\xe2\x80\x9d said Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt, the rumor of\r\nJudge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s visit to this house, and the flight of Hepzibah and\r\nClifford, is about to lead to the investigation of the premises. We\r\nhave no way but to meet it. Let us open the door at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut, to their surprise, before they could reach the street door,\xe2\x80\x94even\r\nbefore they quitted the room in which the foregoing interview had\r\npassed,\xe2\x80\x94they heard footsteps in the farther passage. The door,\r\ntherefore, which they supposed to be securely locked,\xe2\x80\x94which Holgrave,\r\nindeed, had seen to be so, and at which Ph\xc5\x93be had vainly tried to\r\nenter,\xe2\x80\x94must have been opened from without. The sound of footsteps was\r\nnot harsh, bold, decided, and intrusive, as the gait of strangers would\r\nnaturally be, making authoritative entrance into a dwelling where they\r\nknew themselves unwelcome. It was feeble, as of persons either weak or\r\nweary; there was the mingled murmur of two voices, familiar to both the\r\nlisteners.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan it be?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Holgrave.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is they!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ph\xc5\x93be. \xe2\x80\x9cThank God!\xe2\x80\x94thank God!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then, as if in sympathy with Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s whispered ejaculation, they\r\nheard Hepzibah\xe2\x80\x99s voice more distinctly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank God, my brother, we are at home!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell!\xe2\x80\x94Yes!\xe2\x80\x94thank God!\xe2\x80\x9d responded Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cA dreary home, Hepzibah!\r\nBut you have done well to bring me hither! Stay! That parlor door is\r\nopen. I cannot pass by it! Let me go and rest me in the arbor, where I\r\nused,\xe2\x80\x94oh, very long ago, it seems to me, after what has befallen\r\nus,\xe2\x80\x94where I used to be so happy with little Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the house was not altogether so dreary as Clifford imagined it.\r\nThey had not made many steps,\xe2\x80\x94in truth, they were lingering in the\r\nentry, with the listlessness of an accomplished purpose, uncertain what\r\nto do next,\xe2\x80\x94when Ph\xc5\x93be ran to meet them. On beholding her, Hepzibah\r\nburst into tears. With all her might, she had staggered onward beneath\r\nthe burden of grief and responsibility, until now that it was safe to\r\nfling it down. Indeed, she had not energy to fling it down, but had\r\nceased to uphold it, and suffered it to press her to the earth.\r\nClifford appeared the stronger of the two.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is our own little Ph\xc5\x93be!\xe2\x80\x94Ah! and Holgrave with, her\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed he,\r\nwith a glance of keen and delicate insight, and a smile, beautiful,\r\nkind, but melancholy. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought of you both, as we came down the\r\nstreet, and beheld Alice\xe2\x80\x99s Posies in full bloom. And so the flower of\r\nEden has bloomed, likewise, in this old, darksome house to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXI.\r\nThe Departure\r\n\r\n\r\nThe sudden death of so prominent a member of the social world as the\r\nHonorable Judge Jaffrey Pyncheon created a sensation (at least, in the\r\ncircles more immediately connected with the deceased) which had hardly\r\nquite subsided in a fortnight.\r\n\r\nIt may be remarked, however, that, of all the events which constitute a\r\nperson\xe2\x80\x99s biography, there is scarcely one\xe2\x80\x94none, certainly, of anything\r\nlike a similar importance\xe2\x80\x94to which the world so easily reconciles\r\nitself as to his death. In most other cases and contingencies, the\r\nindividual is present among us, mixed up with the daily revolution of\r\naffairs, and affording a definite point for observation. At his\r\ndecease, there is only a vacancy, and a momentary eddy,\xe2\x80\x94very small, as\r\ncompared with the apparent magnitude of the ingurgitated object,\xe2\x80\x94and a\r\nbubble or two, ascending out of the black depth and bursting at the\r\nsurface. As regarded Judge Pyncheon, it seemed probable, at first\r\nblush, that the mode of his final departure might give him a larger and\r\nlonger posthumous vogue than ordinarily attends the memory of a\r\ndistinguished man. But when it came to be understood, on the highest\r\nprofessional authority, that the event was a natural, and\xe2\x80\x94except for\r\nsome unimportant particulars, denoting a slight idiosyncrasy\xe2\x80\x94by no\r\nmeans an unusual form of death, the public, with its customary\r\nalacrity, proceeded to forget that he had ever lived. In short, the\r\nhonorable Judge was beginning to be a stale subject before half the\r\ncountry newspapers had found time to put their columns in mourning, and\r\npublish his exceedingly eulogistic obituary.\r\n\r\nNevertheless, creeping darkly through the places which this excellent\r\nperson had haunted in his lifetime, there was a hidden stream of\r\nprivate talk, such as it would have shocked all decency to speak loudly\r\nat the street-corners. It is very singular, how the fact of a man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeath often seems to give people a truer idea of his character, whether\r\nfor good or evil, than they have ever possessed while he was living and\r\nacting among them. Death is so genuine a fact that it excludes\r\nfalsehood, or betrays its emptiness; it is a touchstone that proves the\r\ngold, and dishonors the baser metal. Could the departed, whoever he may\r\nbe, return in a week after his decease, he would almost invariably find\r\nhimself at a higher or lower point than he had formerly occupied, on\r\nthe scale of public appreciation. But the talk, or scandal, to which we\r\nnow allude, had reference to matters of no less old a date than the\r\nsupposed murder, thirty or forty years ago, of the late Judge\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s uncle. The medical opinion with regard to his own recent and\r\nregretted decease had almost entirely obviated the idea that a murder\r\nwas committed in the former case. Yet, as the record showed, there were\r\ncircumstances irrefragably indicating that some person had gained\r\naccess to old Jaffrey Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s private apartments, at or near the\r\nmoment of his death. His desk and private drawers, in a room contiguous\r\nto his bedchamber, had been ransacked; money and valuable articles were\r\nmissing; there was a bloody hand-print on the old man\xe2\x80\x99s linen; and, by\r\na powerfully welded chain of deductive evidence, the guilt of the\r\nrobbery and apparent murder had been fixed on Clifford, then residing\r\nwith his uncle in the House of the Seven Gables.\r\n\r\nWhencesoever originating, there now arose a theory that undertook so to\r\naccount for these circumstances as to exclude the idea of Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nagency. Many persons affirmed that the history and elucidation of the\r\nfacts, long so mysterious, had been obtained by the daguerreotypist\r\nfrom one of those mesmerical seers who, nowadays, so strangely perplex\r\nthe aspect of human affairs, and put everybody\xe2\x80\x99s natural vision to the\r\nblush, by the marvels which they see with their eyes shut.\r\n\r\nAccording to this version of the story, Judge Pyncheon, exemplary as we\r\nhave portrayed him in our narrative, was, in his youth, an apparently\r\nirreclaimable scapegrace. The brutish, the animal instincts, as is\r\noften the case, had been developed earlier than the intellectual\r\nqualities, and the force of character, for which he was afterwards\r\nremarkable. He had shown himself wild, dissipated, addicted to low\r\npleasures, little short of ruffianly in his propensities, and\r\nrecklessly expensive, with no other resources than the bounty of his\r\nuncle. This course of conduct had alienated the old bachelor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naffection, once strongly fixed upon him. Now it is averred,\xe2\x80\x94but whether\r\non authority available in a court of justice, we do not pretend to have\r\ninvestigated,\xe2\x80\x94that the young man was tempted by the devil, one night,\r\nto search his uncle\xe2\x80\x99s private drawers, to which he had unsuspected\r\nmeans of access. While thus criminally occupied, he was startled by the\r\nopening of the chamber-door. There stood old Jaffrey Pyncheon, in his\r\nnightclothes! The surprise of such a discovery, his agitation, alarm,\r\nand horror, brought on the crisis of a disorder to which the old\r\nbachelor had an hereditary liability; he seemed to choke with blood,\r\nand fell upon the floor, striking his temple a heavy blow against the\r\ncorner of a table. What was to be done? The old man was surely dead!\r\nAssistance would come too late! What a misfortune, indeed, should it\r\ncome too soon, since his reviving consciousness would bring the\r\nrecollection of the ignominious offence which he had beheld his nephew\r\nin the very act of committing!\r\n\r\nBut he never did revive. With the cool hardihood that always pertained\r\nto him, the young man continued his search of the drawers, and found a\r\nwill, of recent date, in favor of Clifford,\xe2\x80\x94which he destroyed,\xe2\x80\x94and an\r\nolder one, in his own favor, which he suffered to remain. But before\r\nretiring, Jaffrey bethought himself of the evidence, in these ransacked\r\ndrawers, that some one had visited the chamber with sinister purposes.\r\nSuspicion, unless averted, might fix upon the real offender. In the\r\nvery presence of the dead man, therefore, he laid a scheme that should\r\nfree himself at the expense of Clifford, his rival, for whose character\r\nhe had at once a contempt and a repugnance. It is not probable, be it\r\nsaid, that he acted with any set purpose of involving Clifford in a\r\ncharge of murder. Knowing that his uncle did not die by violence, it\r\nmay not have occurred to him, in the hurry of the crisis, that such an\r\ninference might be drawn. But, when the affair took this darker aspect,\r\nJaffrey\xe2\x80\x99s previous steps had already pledged him to those which\r\nremained. So craftily had he arranged the circumstances, that, at\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s trial, his cousin hardly found it necessary to swear to\r\nanything false, but only to withhold the one decisive explanation, by\r\nrefraining to state what he had himself done and witnessed.\r\n\r\nThus Jaffrey Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s inward criminality, as regarded Clifford, was,\r\nindeed, black and damnable; while its mere outward show and positive\r\ncommission was the smallest that could possibly consist with so great a\r\nsin. This is just the sort of guilt that a man of eminent\r\nrespectability finds it easiest to dispose of. It was suffered to fade\r\nout of sight or be reckoned a venial matter, in the Honorable Judge\r\nPyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s long subsequent survey of his own life. He shuffled it\r\naside, among the forgotten and forgiven frailties of his youth, and\r\nseldom thought of it again.\r\n\r\nWe leave the Judge to his repose. He could not be styled fortunate at\r\nthe hour of death. Unknowingly, he was a childless man, while striving\r\nto add more wealth to his only child\xe2\x80\x99s inheritance. Hardly a week after\r\nhis decease, one of the Cunard steamers brought intelligence of the\r\ndeath, by cholera, of Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s son, just at the point of\r\nembarkation for his native land. By this misfortune Clifford became\r\nrich; so did Hepzibah; so did our little village maiden, and, through\r\nher, that sworn foe of wealth and all manner of conservatism,\xe2\x80\x94the wild\r\nreformer,\xe2\x80\x94Holgrave!\r\n\r\nIt was now far too late in Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s life for the good opinion of\r\nsociety to be worth the trouble and anguish of a formal vindication.\r\nWhat he needed was the love of a very few; not the admiration, or even\r\nthe respect, of the unknown many. The latter might probably have been\r\nwon for him, had those on whom the guardianship of his welfare had\r\nfallen deemed it advisable to expose Clifford to a miserable\r\nresuscitation of past ideas, when the condition of whatever comfort he\r\nmight expect lay in the calm of forgetfulness. After such wrong as he\r\nhad suffered, there is no reparation. The pitiable mockery of it, which\r\nthe world might have been ready enough to offer, coming so long after\r\nthe agony had done its utmost work, would have been fit only to provoke\r\nbitterer laughter than poor Clifford was ever capable of. It is a truth\r\n(and it would be a very sad one but for the higher hopes which it\r\nsuggests) that no great mistake, whether acted or endured, in our\r\nmortal sphere, is ever really set right. Time, the continual\r\nvicissitude of circumstances, and the invariable inopportunity of\r\ndeath, render it impossible. If, after long lapse of years, the right\r\nseems to be in our power, we find no niche to set it in. The better\r\nremedy is for the sufferer to pass on, and leave what he once thought\r\nhis irreparable ruin far behind him.\r\n\r\nThe shock of Judge Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x99s death had a permanently invigorating and\r\nultimately beneficial effect on Clifford. That strong and ponderous man\r\nhad been Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s nightmare. There was no free breath to be drawn,\r\nwithin the sphere of so malevolent an influence. The first effect of\r\nfreedom, as we have witnessed in Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s aimless flight, was a\r\ntremulous exhilaration. Subsiding from it, he did not sink into his\r\nformer intellectual apathy. He never, it is true, attained to nearly\r\nthe full measure of what might have been his faculties. But he\r\nrecovered enough of them partially to light up his character, to\r\ndisplay some outline of the marvellous grace that was abortive in it,\r\nand to make him the object of no less deep, although less melancholy\r\ninterest than heretofore. He was evidently happy. Could we pause to\r\ngive another picture of his daily life, with all the appliances now at\r\ncommand to gratify his instinct for the Beautiful, the garden scenes,\r\nthat seemed so sweet to him, would look mean and trivial in comparison.\r\n\r\nVery soon after their change of fortune, Clifford, Hepzibah, and little\r\nPh\xc5\x93be, with the approval of the artist, concluded to remove from the\r\ndismal old House of the Seven Gables, and take up their abode, for the\r\npresent, at the elegant country-seat of the late Judge Pyncheon.\r\nChanticleer and his family had already been transported thither, where\r\nthe two hens had forthwith begun an indefatigable process of\r\negg-laying, with an evident design, as a matter of duty and conscience,\r\nto continue their illustrious breed under better auspices than for a\r\ncentury past. On the day set for their departure, the principal\r\npersonages of our story, including good Uncle Venner, were assembled in\r\nthe parlor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe country-house is certainly a very fine one, so far as the plan\r\ngoes,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Holgrave, as the party were discussing their future\r\narrangements. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I wonder that the late Judge\xe2\x80\x94being so opulent, and\r\nwith a reasonable prospect of transmitting his wealth to descendants of\r\nhis own\xe2\x80\x94should not have felt the propriety of embodying so excellent a\r\npiece of domestic architecture in stone, rather than in wood. Then,\r\nevery generation of the family might have altered the interior, to suit\r\nits own taste and convenience; while the exterior, through the lapse of\r\nyears, might have been adding venerableness to its original beauty, and\r\nthus giving that impression of permanence which I consider essential to\r\nthe happiness of any one moment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Ph\xc5\x93be, gazing into the artist\xe2\x80\x99s face with infinite\r\namazement, \xe2\x80\x9chow wonderfully your ideas are changed! A house of stone,\r\nindeed! It is but two or three weeks ago that you seemed to wish people\r\nto live in something as fragile and temporary as a bird\xe2\x80\x99s-nest!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Ph\xc5\x93be, I told you how it would be!\xe2\x80\x9d said the artist, with a\r\nhalf-melancholy laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou find me a conservative already! Little did\r\nI think ever to become one. It is especially unpardonable in this\r\ndwelling of so much hereditary misfortune, and under the eye of yonder\r\nportrait of a model conservative, who, in that very character, rendered\r\nhimself so long the evil destiny of his race.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat picture!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, seeming to shrink from its stern glance.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhenever I look at it, there is an old dreamy recollection haunting\r\nme, but keeping just beyond the grasp of my mind. Wealth, it seems to\r\nsay!\xe2\x80\x94boundless wealth!\xe2\x80\x94unimaginable wealth! I could fancy that, when I\r\nwas a child, or a youth, that portrait had spoken, and told me a rich\r\nsecret, or had held forth its hand, with the written record of hidden\r\nopulence. But those old matters are so dim with me, nowadays! What\r\ncould this dream have been?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I can recall it,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Holgrave. \xe2\x80\x9cSee! There are a hundred\r\nchances to one that no person, unacquainted with the secret, would ever\r\ntouch this spring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA secret spring!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, I remember now! I did discover\r\nit, one summer afternoon, when I was idling and dreaming about the\r\nhouse, long, long ago. But the mystery escapes me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe artist put his finger on the contrivance to which he had referred.\r\nIn former days, the effect would probably have been to cause the\r\npicture to start forward. But, in so long a period of concealment, the\r\nmachinery had been eaten through with rust; so that at Holgrave\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npressure, the portrait, frame and all, tumbled suddenly from its\r\nposition, and lay face downward on the floor. A recess in the wall was\r\nthus brought to light, in which lay an object so covered with a\r\ncentury\xe2\x80\x99s dust that it could not immediately be recognized as a folded\r\nsheet of parchment. Holgrave opened it, and displayed an ancient deed,\r\nsigned with the hieroglyphics of several Indian sagamores, and\r\nconveying to Colonel Pyncheon and his heirs, forever, a vast extent of\r\nterritory at the Eastward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the very parchment, the attempt to recover which cost the\r\nbeautiful Alice Pyncheon her happiness and life,\xe2\x80\x9d said the artist,\r\nalluding to his legend. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is what the Pyncheons sought in vain, while\r\nit was valuable; and now that they find the treasure, it has long been\r\nworthless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Cousin Jaffrey! This is what deceived him,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Hepzibah.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen they were young together, Clifford probably made a kind of\r\nfairy-tale of this discovery. He was always dreaming hither and thither\r\nabout the house, and lighting up its dark corners with beautiful\r\nstories. And poor Jaffrey, who took hold of everything as if it were\r\nreal, thought my brother had found out his uncle\xe2\x80\x99s wealth. He died with\r\nthis delusion in his mind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ph\xc5\x93be, apart to Holgrave, \xe2\x80\x9chow came you to know the\r\nsecret?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dearest Ph\xc5\x93be,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holgrave, \xe2\x80\x9chow will it please you to assume\r\nthe name of Maule? As for the secret, it is the only inheritance that\r\nhas come down to me from my ancestors. You should have known sooner\r\n(only that I was afraid of frightening you away) that, in this long\r\ndrama of wrong and retribution, I represent the old wizard, and am\r\nprobably as much a wizard as ever he was. The son of the executed\r\nMatthew Maule, while building this house, took the opportunity to\r\nconstruct that recess, and hide away the Indian deed, on which depended\r\nthe immense land-claim of the Pyncheons. Thus they bartered their\r\neastern territory for Maule\xe2\x80\x99s garden-ground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now\xe2\x80\x9d said Uncle Venner \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose their whole claim is not worth\r\none man\xe2\x80\x99s share in my farm yonder!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUncle Venner,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Ph\xc5\x93be, taking the patched philosopher\xe2\x80\x99s hand,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou must never talk any more about your farm! You shall never go\r\nthere, as long as you live! There is a cottage in our new garden,\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nprettiest little yellowish-brown cottage you ever saw; and the\r\nsweetest-looking place, for it looks just as if it were made of\r\ngingerbread,\xe2\x80\x94and we are going to fit it up and furnish it, on purpose\r\nfor you. And you shall do nothing but what you choose, and shall be as\r\nhappy as the day is long, and shall keep Cousin Clifford in spirits\r\nwith the wisdom and pleasantness which is always dropping from your\r\nlips!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! my dear child,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth good Uncle Venner, quite overcome, \xe2\x80\x9cif you\r\nwere to speak to a young man as you do to an old one, his chance of\r\nkeeping his heart another minute would not be worth one of the buttons\r\non my waistcoat! And\xe2\x80\x94soul alive!\xe2\x80\x94that great sigh, which you made me\r\nheave, has burst off the very last of them! But, never mind! It was the\r\nhappiest sigh I ever did heave; and it seems as if I must have drawn in\r\na gulp of heavenly breath, to make it with. Well, well, Miss Ph\xc5\x93be!\r\nThey\xe2\x80\x99ll miss me in the gardens hereabouts, and round by the back doors;\r\nand Pyncheon Street, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid, will hardly look the same without old\r\nUncle Venner, who remembers it with a mowing field on one side, and the\r\ngarden of the Seven Gables on the other. But either I must go to your\r\ncountry-seat, or you must come to my farm,\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s one of two things\r\ncertain; and I leave you to choose which!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come with us, by all means, Uncle Venner!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, who had\r\na remarkable enjoyment of the old man\xe2\x80\x99s mellow, quiet, and simple\r\nspirit. \xe2\x80\x9cI want you always to be within five minutes, saunter of my\r\nchair. You are the only philosopher I ever knew of whose wisdom has not\r\na drop of bitter essence at the bottom!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Uncle Venner, beginning partly to realize what manner\r\nof man he was. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet folks used to set me down among the simple\r\nones, in my younger days! But I suppose I am like a Roxbury russet,\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ngreat deal the better, the longer I can be kept. Yes; and my words of\r\nwisdom, that you and Ph\xc5\x93be tell me of, are like the golden dandelions,\r\nwhich never grow in the hot months, but may be seen glistening among\r\nthe withered grass, and under the dry leaves, sometimes as late as\r\nDecember. And you are welcome, friends, to my mess of dandelions, if\r\nthere were twice as many!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA plain, but handsome, dark-green barouche had now drawn up in front of\r\nthe ruinous portal of the old mansion-house. The party came forth, and\r\n(with the exception of good Uncle Venner, who was to follow in a few\r\ndays) proceeded to take their places. They were chatting and laughing\r\nvery pleasantly together; and\xe2\x80\x94as proves to be often the case, at\r\nmoments when we ought to palpitate with sensibility\xe2\x80\x94Clifford and\r\nHepzibah bade a final farewell to the abode of their forefathers, with\r\nhardly more emotion than if they had made it their arrangement to\r\nreturn thither at tea-time. Several children were drawn to the spot by\r\nso unusual a spectacle as the barouche and pair of gray horses.\r\nRecognizing little Ned Higgins among them, Hepzibah put her hand into\r\nher pocket, and presented the urchin, her earliest and staunchest\r\ncustomer, with silver enough to people the Domdaniel cavern of his\r\ninterior with as various a procession of quadrupeds as passed into the\r\nark.\r\n\r\nTwo men were passing, just as the barouche drove off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Dixey,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of them, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat do you think of this? My wife\r\nkept a cent-shop three months, and lost five dollars on her outlay. Old\r\nMaid Pyncheon has been in trade just about as long, and rides off in\r\nher carriage with a couple of hundred thousand,\xe2\x80\x94reckoning her share,\r\nand Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s, and Ph\xc5\x93be\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x94and some say twice as much! If you choose\r\nto call it luck, it is all very well; but if we are to take it as the\r\nwill of Providence, why, I can\xe2\x80\x99t exactly fathom it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty good business!\xe2\x80\x9d quoth the sagacious Dixey,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cpretty good\r\nbusiness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaule\xe2\x80\x99s well, all this time, though left in solitude, was throwing up a\r\nsuccession of kaleidoscopic pictures, in which a gifted eye might have\r\nseen foreshadowed the coming fortunes of Hepzibah and Clifford, and the\r\ndescendant of the legendary wizard, and the village maiden, over whom\r\nhe had thrown love\xe2\x80\x99s web of sorcery. The Pyncheon Elm, moreover, with\r\nwhat foliage the September gale had spared to it, whispered\r\nunintelligible prophecies. And wise Uncle Venner, passing slowly from\r\nthe ruinous porch, seemed to hear a strain of music, and fancied that\r\nsweet Alice Pyncheon\xe2\x80\x94after witnessing these deeds, this bygone woe and\r\nthis present happiness, of her kindred mortals\xe2\x80\x94had given one farewell\r\ntouch of a spirit\xe2\x80\x99s joy upon her harpsichord, as she floated heavenward\r\nfrom the HOUSE OF THE SEVEN GABLES!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE HOUSE OF THE SEVEN GABLES ***\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
